# 韩非子

**"Han Feizi (Han Fei’s Writings)**
*Authored by Han Fei*
Twenty scrolls in total.
Original source: *Zhengtong Yizang* (正统遗藏), Daoist Canon (*Taqing* 太清部).

**Han Feizi – Volume One**
#1 *Chū Jiàn Qín* (初見秦) – **First Meeting with Qin**

A loyal subject who lacks knowledge does not speak wisely, and one who knows but does not speak lacks virtue. For a subject not to possess virtue is deserving of death, and for a subject to speak without propriety is also deserving of death.

Though this be so, I dare to speak all I have heard, awaiting Your Majesty’s judgment on my offense.

I have heard that the realm of Qin is both shadowed and sheltered by Yan and Wei. Yan lies to the north, hence the term *shadowed*. Wei lies to the south, hence the term *sheltered*. Upending Jing and securing Qi, it subjugated Han and formed a coalition, intending to face west and challenge Qin’s strength—this I find laughable.

There are three ways to bring about downfall, yet the world may gain from them. He who knows these three ways may gain the world.

Is this not what I mean?

I have heard that:
One who attacks order with chaos perishes,
One who attacks rectitude with deceit perishes,
One who attacks harmony with rebellion perishes. #1

Now the granaries of the world are not full, and the storehouses of Qin are empty. Its soldiers and people, numbering in the hundreds of thousands, are all willing to die. They prostrate themselves, donning plumes as generals, and march to their deaths without hesitation—even a thousand may not be found, all dying by their words alone.

Blades flash before them, axes gleam behind, yet they retreat not in fear but press onward to death. It is not that the people cannot die; rather, it is that the ruler cannot command it.

When rewards are promised, they are not given; when punishments are decreed, they are not carried out. Rewards and punishments lack credibility, and thus the people do not die.

Now Qin issues commands and enforces rewards and punishments, and those with merit serve those without. It takes men from their parents’ embrace, born without ever having seen an enemy. Yet when they hear of battle, they stamp their feet in readiness, #2
Charge through blades, tread upon burning coals,
And march to their deaths without hesitation—all this is true.

To command death is different from commanding life, yet the people obey. This is because Qin values the resolve to die.

One man’s resolve to die can confront ten, ten can confront a hundred, a hundred can confront a thousand, a thousand can confront ten thousand, and ten thousand can conquer the world.

Now Qin’s territory is vast, stretching thousands of miles, and its armies number in the hundreds of thousands. Qin’s commands, rewards, punishments, terrain, and strategic advantages are unmatched in the world.

With this, Qin could conquer the world and hold it all—yet it is not enough. Thus its arms grow weary, its people exhausted, its stores depleted, its fields barren, its granaries empty, and the neighboring lords do not submit. The title of *Conqueror* is not achieved—this is no accident. Its ministers lack full virtue. #1

I dare to speak of this. Formerly, when Qi broke Jing to the south, shattered Song to the east, subdued Qin to the west, crushed Yan to the north, and dominated Han and Wei in the center, its lands were vast and its armies strong. It won battles, took cities, and issued commands to the world.

Qi’s clear waters of the Ji River and the muddy waters of the He River were sufficient as boundaries, while its grand walls and mighty defenses were enough to seal its borders.

Qi was a land of five defeated states—meaning five conquered nations. #2
Had it not won one battle, Qi would not have stood.

When Le Yi (樂毅) broke Qi at the Ji River’s west, this proves that war is the life or death of a great state.

Moreover, I have heard that:
To erase the traces of ruin without uprooting its roots is to leave a neighbor with calamity, and calamity will then persist.

This means that the remnants of defeat, if left unchecked, will breed future ruin. It is a warning to Qin: let Qi serve as a lesson.

When Qin fought with Jing’s men, it shattered Jing, raided Ying, and seized Dongting, Wuhu, and Jiangnan. The Jing king and his ministers fled in disarray, submitting to Chen in the east.

At that moment, if Qin had pursued Jing with its armies, Jing would have been utterly vanquished. Had Jing been utterly vanquished, the people would have had enough to covet, and the lands would have been fully exploited.

To the east, it could have weakened Qi and Yan, while in the center, it could have dominated the Three Jin. #3
Thus, with one decisive stroke, the title of *Conqueror* could have been achieved, and the neighboring lords could have been made to bow in homage.

Yet its ministers did not act, and Qin withdrew its armies. Instead, it made peace with the criminals of Jing, allowing them to restore their fallen kingdom, gather their scattered people, reinstate their ancestral rites, and rebuild their temples. It permitted them to rally the world against Qin, facing west once more to challenge its might—this is surely the loss of one path to *Conqueror*’s dominion.

Again, the world allied against Zhou and marched upon Hua Xia’s borders. The Great King issued a decree to break them, and the armies reached the gates of Liang. Had Liang been besieged for several decades, it would have fallen, and the fall of Liang would have meant the rise of Wei. The rise of Wei would have severed the ambitions of the Three Jin, and the severing of their ambitions would have left Zhao in peril. With Zhao in peril, Jing would hesitate and doubt, while to the east, Qi and Yan would be weakened, and in the center, the Three Jin would be dominated.

Thus, with one decisive stroke, the title of *Conqueror* could have been achieved, and the neighboring lords could have been made to bow in homage.

Yet its ministers did not act, and Qin withdrew its armies. Instead, it made peace with Wei, allowing them to restore their fallen kingdom, gather their scattered people, reinstate their ancestral rites, and rebuild their temples. It permitted them to rally the world against Qin—[facing west once more to challenge its might]. #3

「This is indeed the second way of losing the *Dao/Way* of the Conqueror. Earlier, when Lord Ráng governed Qin, he sought to accomplish the achievements of two states with the strength of just one. Lord Ráng established private domains for himself, scheming against Qin, and thus his words were not sincere when he spoke of two states. Therefore, the soldiers remained exposed for their entire lives, while the people within grew weary and exhausted, and the title of *Conqueror* was never realized—this is indeed the third way of losing the *Dao/Way* of the Conqueror.

The state of Zhao is a central realm, inhabited by a mixed populace. Zhao occupies Handan, to the south of Yan, to the west of Qi, to the north of Wei, and to the east of Han; hence, it is called *central*. It encompasses the people of four states, hence *mixed*. Its subjects are light-hearted and difficult to command. Orders are not enforced, rewards and punishments lack credibility, and its terrain is not advantageous. The rulers cannot fully utilize their people’s strength, and thus it is destined to perish. Yet Zhao did not fear the stirrings of rebellion, and mustered all its soldiers and civilians to fight at Changping, contending for Han’s Shangdang region. The Great King then shattered them, capturing Wū’ān. At that time, Zhao’s nobles and commoners were divided, and the high and low did not trust one another. Thus, Handan was unguarded, and if taken, the Great King would have seized the Yellow River’s eastern banks and left his forces there, then withdrawn westward to attack Xiūwǔ, crossing the Huá Pass and taking Shangdang. The forty-six counties of Dai and the seventy of Shangdang—without a single battle, without exhausting a single soldier or civilian—would all belong to Qin. Shangdang and Dai would have fallen to Qin without struggle, while Dongyang and the lands beyond the Hé River would have reverted to Qi without a fight, and Zhōngshān and the territories north of Hùzhuō would have belonged to Yan without resistance. Thus, Zhao’s defeat would have led to Han’s collapse, and with Han gone, Jing and Wei could not stand alone. Jing and Wei’s collapse would have meant the destruction of Han, the weakening of Wei, and the subjugation of Jing, while to the east, Qi and Yan would be weakened, and the Great King would have opened the White Horse Pass to flood Wei with his armies—resulting in the Three Jin’s annihilation and the defeat of their allies. The Great King could have remained passive, and the world would have submitted in awe; the title of *Conqueror* would have been achieved. Yet its ministers did not act, and Qin withdrew its forces, instead making peace with Zhao.

With the Great King’s wisdom and Qin’s formidable armies, to abandon the *Dao/Way* of the Conqueror and fail to secure even a portion of the land is to invite deceit from a dying state—this is the folly of the ministers. Moreover, if Zhao were to perish but does not, and if Qin were to become the Conqueror but does not, the world would already measure Qin’s ministers as incompetent. Now, Qin has again mustered its soldiers to attack Handan, yet cannot take it. Abandoning armor, weapons, and crossbows, they retreat in disarray, and the world has already measured Qin’s strength three times. Internally, the world sees our ministers’ incompetence, while externally, it witnesses the limits of our military power.

From this, I believe the world’s submission is nearly impossible. Why? Because the lords know that Qin’s armies exhaust the people, and thus their loyalty grows ever firmer. The Great King must consider this carefully.

Furthermore, I have heard it said: *"To tremble and fear, to be cautious each day—if one steadfastly follows the Dao, the world can be won."* How do I know this is true? In the past, King Zhou, as sovereign of the world, led his armies to drink from the Qí River on the left and the Yán River on the right. The waters of Qí dried up, and those of Yán did not flow, yet he still sought to challenge King Wǔ of the Zhou. King Wǔ, with his three thousand unarmored warriors, fought for one day and shattered Zhou’s realm, capturing his person and seizing his lands and people—none in the world dared to resist.

Zhībó led the combined forces of three states to attack Zhao’s Shèngzhǔ at Jìnyáng. He dammed the waters and flooded the city for three months, and the walls were nearly breached. Shèngzhǔ consulted the tortoise-shell divination and observed auspicious signs: *"Which state can be subdued?"* He then sent his minister Zhāng Mèngtán to secretly go among the enemy ranks and reveal Zhībó’s pact. With the help of two states, he attacked Zhībó, captured him, and restored Zhao’s original dominion.

Now, Qin’s lands are vast, stretching thousands of miles, with renowned armies numbering tens and hundreds of millions. Its decrees, rewards, punishments, and strategic advantages surpass all others in the world. With this, Qin could conquer the world—yet the ministers fail to break the world’s submission, to topple Zhao, to destroy Han, to subjugate Jing and Wei, and to win the favor of Qi and Yan, thereby achieving the title of *Conqueror* and making the neighboring lords bow in homage.

If the Great King truly heeds my counsel, yet still fails to break the world’s submission, to topple Zhao, to destroy Han, to subjugate Jing and Wei, or to win the favor of Qi and Yan—if the title of *Conqueror* is not achieved and the neighboring lords do not bow in homage—then I beg the Great King to behead me and display my corpse as a warning to the state, an example of a minister whose loyalty did not extend to its fullest.

**The Second Matter: Preserving Han**
Han has served Qin for over thirty years, offering its strength as a shield when needed and its wealth as a humble offering when not. It sends tribute to supply Qin’s demands, like a mat placed beneath a guest’s feet. Qin has now dispatched its elite troops to seize Han’s lands, and the resentment of the world will follow, with all the glory accruing to mighty Qin.

Moreover, Han’s contributions in tribute and service are no different from those of the counties and prefectures. Yet I have secretly heard the plans of the noble ministers: they intend to raise armies and attack Han. Zhao has already gathered its soldiers and trained its levies, desiring to add to the world’s armies—like a brooch or a pendant, linking and securing them. If Qin is not weakened, the lords will surely destroy their ancestral temples and turn westward to act against Qin, not a strategy for a single day.»

Now that Qin abandons Zhao’s troubles and instead repels Han as its own subject, the world will clearly recognize Zhao’s strategy. If Han becomes a subject of Qin, and Qin still annihilates it, then the lords will unite with Zhao to attack Qin, and the plan will succeed.

Han is a small state, yet it has endured four assaults from the world’s armies, with its ruler humiliated and its subjects suffering, all sharing in prolonged grief. It has fortified its defenses, guarded against powerful enemies, stockpiled provisions, and built city walls to secure its borders. If Qin attacks Han and cannot conquer it within a year, or even seizes just one city and withdraws, then Qin’s authority will be diminished in the eyes of the world, and the world’s armies will crush Qin’s forces.

If Han rebels, then Wei will respond in support. Zhao will rely on Qi as its base—like a mountain ridge. In this way, with Han and Wei as resources and Qi as an ally to solidify their ranks, Zhao will challenge Qin’s might, bringing fortune to Zhao and disaster to Qin.

If Qin advances but fails to take Zhao, and retreats yet cannot conquer Han, then its elite troops will be exhausted from field battles, its burdened forces will weary from internal campaigns, and those who supply provisions will be drained. Then, united with the weak and suffering, it will oppose the combined armies of the two great states—this is not the way to destroy Zhao.

If Qin follows the noble ministers’ plan—meaning, to apply their same strategy—then Qin will surely become the world’s military target. If it cannot advance or retreat effectively, it will be like a hostage for all.

Your Majesty, even if you exhaust your resources like gold and stone, and summon all your soldiers, the day when Qin conquers all under heaven will not yet come. Now, this humble servant’s foolish plan: send envoys to Jing, lavish gifts upon the officials who manage the affairs of Han, and make clear to Zhao the reasons for its deception of Qin. Offer Wei as a hostage to ease Zhao’s suspicions, then attack Zhao while it is preoccupied. Even if Zhao unites with Qi, it will not be a grave threat. Once the affairs of these two states are settled, Han can be secured by written agreement. This single move will make the fate of both states certain, and Jing and Wei will surely submit themselves.

Thus it is said: *"War is a dire instrument. Its use must be carefully considered."* To oppose Zhao with Qin’s strength, balanced by the support of Qi, yet now to betray Han without a means to secure Jing and Wei’s loyalty, is perilous. If Qin loses a single battle, then disaster will befall. Strategy is the means to resolve affairs; it must be examined with care. The strength or weakness of Han and Qin lies within this year alone.

Moreover, Zhao has long conspired with the other lords. If Qin acts and thereby weakens itself among the lords, it is a dangerous matter. To devise a plan that makes the lords harbor hostile intentions toward Qin is most perilous. The two states’ (Han and Wei) plight is not the way to grow stronger among the lords.

I humbly hope Your Majesty will carefully reconsider this matter. Once a campaign is launched and its allies hear of it, there will be no turning back. By Your Majesty’s decree, based on the book submitted by Han’s envoys, which states that Han cannot be taken immediately, this servant greatly disagrees. Qin’s possession of Han is like a man with a fatal illness in his vitals—if left untreated, it will fester and spread. If one remains in damp ground, letting the sickness linger without treatment, it will worsen until it erupts in a fatal outbreak.

If the illness is exposed to cold, it will suddenly flare up and worsen—this is certain. This illustrates that even though Qin bestows great favors upon Han, should there be an urgent matter, Han’s rebellious heart will surely reveal itself. Though Han submits to Qin, it has never ceased to be a source of trouble for Qin. Now, if there is a sudden matter requiring retaliation, Han cannot be trusted.

Qin and Zhao are at odds, Jing and Su are urging Qi, but it is unclear how this will unfold. In my view, the alliance between Qi and Zhao may not necessarily be severed by Jing and Su. If it is not severed, then this would mean attacking Zhao while responding to the two armies of Qi. Han does not submit to Qin out of righteousness, but out of coercion. Now, if Qin focuses solely on Qi and Zhao, then Han’s rebellious heart will surely erupt as a fatal illness in Qin’s vitals.

Han is plotting with Jing, and if the other lords respond to it, then Qin will again face the calamity of the Hangu Pass. Though Jing’s envoys may not come solely to preserve Han, they may prioritize their own interests over Han. Their arguments and persuasive rhetoric adorn falsehoods and deceptive schemes, aiming to lure Qin into advantage while observing Han’s best interests to gauge Your Majesty’s intentions. They seek to exploit any opportunity to enter and persuade, hoping to secure Han’s advantage.

The bond between Qin and Han is indeed close, yet Jing’s envoys are given greater weight by the two states. This is their own strategic advantage. I observe Jing’s envoys and their excessive flattery and polished rhetoric; Your Majesty must not be swayed by their deceitful arguments and overlook their treacherous intentions.

If Qin mobilizes troops without declaring their target, then Han’s officials will surely calculate how to deal with Qin, fearing they might be the next to be attacked. I therefore request permission to go and meet the Han king, bring him here, and have Your Majesty see him. Once he is inside, do not send him back, but gradually summon his ministers of state to bargain with the Han people. Then Han can be deeply humiliated.

If Your Majesty then orders Xiangwu to dispatch the soldiers from the Eastern Command to threaten the Han border without naming their destination, the Qi people will fear and follow Jing’s plan. This would mean that our troops have not even left before Han is weakened by intimidation and Qi is forced to submit through righteous persuasion.

When the other lords hear of this, Zhao will be broken in spirit, Jing will be in doubt, and there will surely be loyal strategies. If Jing does not act, then Wei is no threat, and the other lords can be consumed one by one, while Zhao can be made to stand as our enemy.

I humbly hope Your Majesty will carefully consider this servant’s humble plan and not dismiss it lightly.

Qin thus sent this servant to Han. Li Si went to deliver the decree to the Han king but was not granted an audience. He therefore submitted a letter, which read:

In the past, Qin and Han united in purpose and strength, never encroaching upon one another, and no one dared to violate this. Such has been the case for several generations. In earlier times, the Five Lords once jointly attacked Han, and Qin sent troops to rescue it. Though Han occupied the central lands, its territory could not fill a thousand *li*, yet it was able to secure its position among the lords of the world and ensure the safety of its king and ministers through the generations—all because of Qin’s enduring support.

Previously, the Five Lords jointly attacked Qin, and Han led the way, forming a vanguard to confront Qin’s army at the pass. The other lords’ forces were exhausted and desperate, with no recourse, and their campaign ultimately failed. Du Cang, Qin’s prime minister, raised an army and dispatched generals to retaliate against the world’s resentment, but failed to attack Jing. The prime minister of Jing, concerned by this, said: *"Han regards Qin’s actions as unjust and has allied with Qin as brothers to afflict the world. Yet now it has betrayed Qin, leading the vanguard to attack the pass. Han occupies the central lands, shifting its allegiance unpredictably. The other lords have collectively ceded ten Han cities to appease Qin and withdraw their forces. Since then, Han’s territory has been diminished, its army weakened, and it remains so to this day.*

*The reason for this is that Han heeded the empty words of deceitful ministers, failing to weigh the truth of the situation. Thus, even after executing those treacherous ministers, Han could not regain its former strength.*

*Now Zhao wants to gather its soldiers and troops, using Qin as its pretext, and sends envoys to borrow passage, claiming they intend to attack Qin. But their true intent must be to strike Han first and then Qin. Moreover, I have heard this saying: 'When the lips are gone, the teeth will grow cold.' Qin and Han cannot escape shared anxiety, and their mutual vulnerability is evident.*

*Wei wants to send troops to attack Han, and Qin has dispatched envoys to escort Han’s envoys. Now that Qin’s king has sent this servant here and he is not granted an audience, I fear that Your Majesty’s attendants may follow the old tactics of treacherous ministers, causing Han to again face the loss of its lands. As this servant is not granted an audience, I request permission to return and report. If Qin and Han’s alliance is severed, this servant’s mission will have failed.*

*This servant came to serve Qin’s king’s pleasure and offer convenient strategies. How could it be that Your Majesty would treat this lowly servant with such hostility? I wish only to be granted an audience, present my humble plans, and then submit to punishment if Your Majesty so desires. If Your Majesty has any regard for this servant’s words, I beg that you consider them carefully.*

*If Your Majesty executes this servant in Han, then the Han king will not be able to grow stronger. If Your Majesty does not heed this servant’s advice, then disaster will surely befall. If Qin mobilizes troops without delay, then Han’s state and its people will be in peril. If this servant is exposed in Han’s capital, then even if Your Majesty wishes to examine this lowly servant’s loyal and foolish plans, it will be too late. The borders will be ravaged, the state will be firmly defended, and the sound of battle will be heard—yet Your Majesty’s use of this servant’s plan will come too late.*

*Moreover, Han’s military strength in the world is known. Now it has again betrayed the mighty Qin. If it abandons its cities and loses its armies, then the enemy that turns its back upon it will surely attack its cities. When the cities are all lost, the troops will scatter, and when the troops scatter, there will be no army left. If the cities are firmly defended, then Qin will surely raise troops to besiege the Han king’s capital. If the roads are blocked, then the enemy will surely devise schemes to attack, and their situation will be unsalvageable. Your attendants’ plans will not be used, and I beg Your Majesty to carefully reconsider.*

*If any of this servant’s words do not align with the facts, I hope the Han king will grant me an audience to fully explain my reasoning before I am punished by the law. Qin’s king is unable to eat or enjoy his leisure, his thoughts fixed solely on attacking Zhao. He has sent this servant to speak, hoping for an audience so that we may urgently devise plans together."*

If Your Majesty does not grant this servant an audience, then the fate of the state of Han remains uncertain. Qin will surely abandon Zhao’s troubles and redirect its forces against Han. I beg Your Majesty to reconsider and grant me a reply.

**Difficulties of Speech (Third Section)**
This servant is not one who finds speech difficult, but the reason I find it difficult is this: when words flow smoothly and are polished like flowing water, rich and glossy in their eloquence, they are seen as empty and insubstantial. When speech is cautious and comparisons are disjointed, it is seen as clumsy and lacking coherence. When words are sincere and earnest, yet rigid and overly guarded, they are seen as awkward and unrefined. When speech is verbose and excessive, piling up comparisons and analogies, it is seen as hollow and useless. When arguments are subtle and concise, direct yet unadorned, they are seen as shallow and unpersuasive. When one eagerly probes the emotions of those in power, seeking to flatter and ingratiate, it is seen as slanderous and unprincipled. When speech is grand and far-reaching, profound yet inscrutable, it is seen as boastful and impractical. When one speaks of trivial household matters, enumerating petty details, it is seen as vulgar. When speech aligns with current trends, using conventional language without contradiction, it is seen as craven and sycophantic. When speech deviates from common usage, appearing strange and erratic, it is seen as fabrications. When one is quick-witted and eloquent, adorned with rhetorical flourishes, it is seen as mere sophistry. When one rejects literary refinement and speaks only of innate nature, it is seen as crude. When one cites poetry and classics, invoking ancient precedents, it is seen as mere recitation—recitation of old tales. These are the reasons why this servant finds speech so difficult and faces grave peril in doing so.

Thus, even if one’s reasoning is correct, it may not be heeded. Even if one’s principles are sound, they may not be applied. If Your Majesty remains unconvinced by this, then in minor matters, you may dismiss it as slanderous defamation, and in grave matters, you may face calamity, disaster, or even death. Hence, Ziyu was a master strategist yet was executed by Wu; Confucius was eloquent yet was besieged at Kuang; Guan Zhong was truly virtuous yet was imprisoned by Lu. Were these three not virtuous? Yet their rulers were unenlightened.

In ancient times, Tang was supremely virtuous, and Yi Yin was supremely wise. Yet even when the supremely wise spoke to the supremely virtuous, Tang required seventy attempts before accepting his counsel, and only then did he recognize Yi Yin’s worth and employ him as a humble cook. Thus, even when the supremely wise speaks to the supremely virtuous, there is no guarantee of immediate acceptance—such was Yi Yin’s case with Tang. When wisdom speaks to folly, it will surely not be heard—such was King Wen’s case with King Zhou. Hence, King Wen was imprisoned by Zhou, Yi Hu was flayed, Gui Hu was skinned alive, Bi Gan’s heart was cut out, Mei Bo was minced, Ji Wu was bound, and Cao Jiu fled to Chen. Bo Li Zi begged for his life, Fu Yao sold himself as a laborer, and was later hired as a servant—hence, "sold." Sun Bin was mutilated in Wei, Wu Qi wept bitterly at the gates of Lu, lamenting the loss of the Western River to Qin, and was ultimately dismembered in Chu. Gongzuo Zuo spoke treasonously against his own state, and Gongsun Yang fled to Qin. Guan Longfeng was beheaded, Chang Hong was torn apart—*[missing character]* split, pronounced *jie*. Yin Zhi was thrown into a pit of thorns. Sima Ziqian drowned in the Yangtze, Tian Ming was executed without cause, and Mo Zi’s followers were killed. Mozi himself and Ximen Bao died by others’ hands without resistance, Dong An died and was displayed in the market, Zai Yu was not spared by Tian Chang, and Fan Sui was broken in Wei. These ten or so men were all virtuous, loyal, and skilled in the Dao, yet they met with tyrannical and deluded rulers and died. Thus, even the wise and virtuous cannot escape death or humiliation. This is because the foolish are difficult to persuade, and thus the wise refrain from speaking.

Moreover, profound truths may offend the ears and contradict the heart—only the wise and virtuous can truly listen. I beg Your Majesty to carefully consider this.

**Loved Ministers (Fourth Section)**
When ministers are overly favored, their lives are in peril. When their authority threatens the sovereign, their lives are in peril. When ministers are too powerful, they risk usurping the throne. When the sovereign’s consorts are without distinction, the legitimate heir is endangered. When brothers do not submit to the ruler, the state is imperiled. I have heard that even a ruler of a thousand chariots, if unprepared, will have a minister of a hundred chariots at his side, ready to transfer the people’s allegiance and topple the state. A ruler of ten thousand chariots, if unprepared, will have a minister of a thousand chariots in his household, ready to undermine his authority and overthrow the state. Thus, when treacherous ministers flourish, the ruler’s path to governance declines and collapses. Therefore, the rise of powerful vassals is the ruin of the Son of Heaven, and the wealth of ministers leads to the downfall of the sovereign.

Generals and ministers who control the ruler and dominate the state are what rulers must exclude and distance themselves from. The four greatest dangers—a ruler’s wealth, his exalted status, his heavy authority, and his supreme influence—should not be sought from others or begged for; they must be secured through wise governance. Hence, if a ruler cannot harness his wealth, he will inevitably rely on others. This is what rulers must recognize.

In the past, the fall of Shang and the decline of Zhou both stemmed from the rise of powerful vassals—Zhou’s Duke Wen of Jin and Qin’s Duke Xian of Zhou. When Jin divided, it gave rise to Zhao, Wei, and Han. When Qi seized power, Chen Heng assassinated Duke Jian. All these were due to the excessive wealth of ministers. The regicides of Yan and Song were all of this kind. Thus, comparing the past to Shang and Zhou, and the present to Yan and Song, none escape this fate. Therefore, enlightened rulers must govern their ministers strictly by law, ensuring that regardless of rank, all are equal under the law.

「Test their loyalty by making them modest, meaning to bestow meager rewards and allowances. If ministers are impoverished, they are easily controlled.

Hence, do not pardon capital crimes, nor commute punishments. To pardon capital crimes and commute punishments is called *威淫* (Wei-yin, i.e., **the tyranny of lenient authority**). *淫* (Yin) means **excessive or scattered**. The ancestral altars and fields will then be in peril, and the state’s authority will become **one-sided**. When the ruler’s authority is scattered, the ministers’ authority grows—hence the term *偏* (pien, **one-sided**).

Therefore, even if great ministers receive vast stipends, they must not rely on their influence to dominate the marketplace. The marketplace is where the people gather; one fears they may exploit the crowd and harbor ambitions.

Though factions may be numerous, they must not control the soldiers. Hence, officials must not have private audiences in the state, nor private dealings in the military. Their treasuries must not lend money to their families, lest they accumulate **private wealth** (福, *fu*).

This is how enlightened rulers prevent their ministers from harboring **deviant intentions** (邪, *hsieh*).

Hence, they must not permit **four private allegiances** (四从, *ssu-tsung*), and neighboring states are considered **private dealings** (私交, *ssu-chiao*). They must not transport **unconventional troops** (奇兵, *ch’i-ping*), nor send them without proper authorization (非传非遽, *fei-ch’uan-fei-chu*). Transporting unconventional troops is punishable by death, with no reprieve.

This is how enlightened rulers prepare for **unforeseen contingencies** (不虞, *pu-yu*).

**Chapter Five on the Dao/Way**
The Dao/Way is the **origin of all things** (始, *shih*), for all things arise from the Dao/Way, hence it is called *始* (origin). It is the **standard for right and wrong** (是非之纪, *shih-fei-chih-chih*), for right and wrong are revealed through the Dao/Way, hence it is called *纪* (standard).

Thus, enlightened rulers uphold the origin to know the **source of all things** (万物之源, *wan-wu-chih-yuan*), and if they grasp the origin, they can fathom its source. They govern by the standard to discern the **beginning of success and failure** (善败之端, *shan-pai-chih-tuan*), and if they grasp the standard, they can foresee its outcome.

Hence, they maintain **emptiness and tranquility** (虚静, *hsü-ching*) to await the **mandate** (令, *ling*), for the mandate’s name comes from itself, and its affairs are self-defined. Emptiness reveals the **true nature of things** (实之情, *shih-chih-ching*), and tranquility discerns the **rightness of movement** (动者正, *tung-chih-cheng*).

Those who speak define their own **names** (名, *ming*), and those who act manifest their own **forms** (形, *hsing*). When form and name align, the ruler has no need for action, and all returns to its **natural disposition** (情, *ching*).

Hence it is said:
*"Do not reveal your desires."* If the ruler reveals his desires, the ministers will **engrave and embellish** (雕琢, *tiao-chu*) to match them.
The ministers, in turn, **engrave and embellish** (雕琢) their desires to conform to the ruler’s.

*"Do not reveal your intentions."* If the ruler reveals his intentions, the ministers will **display their own differences** (表异, *piao-i*). The ministers, in turn, **display their own differences** to conform to the ruler’s intentions.

Hence it is said:
*"Remove your preferences and your aversions; then the ministers will reveal their true nature."* Remove your old ways and your wisdom; then the ministers will **prepare themselves naturally**.

If preferences and aversions are not evident, the ministers have nothing to **imitate** (效, *hsiao*), and thus they **refrain and prepare themselves naturally** (戒而自备, *chieh-erh-tsu-pei*).

Hence, one who has wisdom but does not **deliberate** (虑, *lü*), allows all things to know their proper place. One who has virtue but does not **claim merit** (贤, *hsien*), observes the ministers’ **natural inclinations** (所因, *suo-yin*). One who has courage but does not **act in anger** (怒, *nü*), enables the ministers to **fulfill their martial strength** (尽其武, *chin-chih-wu*).

Therefore, to **remove wisdom** (去智, *chü-chih*) is to gain **clarity** (明, *ming*), for when the ruler removes his wisdom, the ministers’ wisdom naturally becomes clear. To **remove virtue** (去贤, *chü-hsien*) is to achieve **meritorious deeds** (功, *kung*), for when the ruler removes his virtue, the ministers’ actions naturally bear merit. To **remove courage** (去勇, *chü-yung*) is to foster **strength** (强, *chiang*), for when the ruler removes his courage, the ministers’ martial prowess naturally becomes strong.

When ministers uphold their duties and officials maintain their roles, the ruler **assigns tasks according to their abilities** (因能而使之, *in-neng-erh-shih-chih*), which is called *习常* (**establishing routine**).

Hence it is said:
*"Silent as if without position, yet floating as if nowhere can be found."* The enlightened ruler **acts without interference** (无为, *wu-wei*), while the ministers **stand in awe below** (竦惧, *sung-chü*).

The Dao/Way of the enlightened ruler is to **utilize ministers’ wisdom** (智者尽其虑, *chih-chih-chin-chih-lü*), yet the ruler **decides affairs accordingly** (因而断事, *chin-erh-tuan-shih*), so the ruler is **not constrained by wisdom** (穷於智, *chüng-yu-chih*). By employing the ministers’ wisdom, wisdom is **not exhausted**.

To **employ virtuous ministers according to their talents** (贤者勑其材, *hsien-chih-ch’ü-chih-ts’ai*), and the ruler **assigns them accordingly** (因而任之, *chin-erh-jen-chih*), so the ruler is **not constrained by ability** (穷於能, *chüng-yu-neng*). When there are meritorious deeds, the ruler **recognizes their virtue** (有其贤, *yü-chih-hsien*), and when there are faults, the ministers **bear responsibility for their crimes** (任其罪, *jen-chih-tsoi*), so the ruler is **not constrained by reputation** (穷於名, *chüng-yu-ming*).

Hence, though the ruler may not be virtuous, he is the **model for virtuous ministers** (贤者师, *hsien-chih-shih*). Though he may not be wise, he is the **arbiter for wise ministers** (智者正, *chih-chih-cheng*).

The ministers **bear the labor**, while the ruler **claims the success**. The ruler takes the ministers’ labor and **attributes it as his own success** (以为己功, *yü-wei-chih-kung*). This is called the **principle of the sagacious ruler** (贤主之经, *hsien-chiu-chih-ching*), for *经* (ching) means **constant law**.

The Dao/Way lies in what **cannot be seen**. The ruler’s Dao/Way must ensure that ministers **cannot be seen** (不可见, *pu-chien*). Authority must be **unfathomable** (不可知, *pu-chih*).

With emptiness and tranquility, there is no need for action, yet **flaws are revealed in darkness**. To **see without seeing**, to **hear without hearing**, to **know without knowing** (见而不见，闻而不闻，知而不知, *chien-erh-pu-chien, wen-erh-pu-wen, chih-erh-pu-chih*), yet to **know their words to act accordingly** (知其言以往, *chih-chih-yen-yi-wang*), without altering or amending, to **verify and cross-check** (参合阅, *ts’an-ho-yüeh*).

When an office has but one person, **do not allow them to communicate freely**; then all matters will be **fully revealed**. Each must **guard their duties** (守职, *shou-chih*), and **not be permitted to cross-interfere** (相通, *hsiao-t’ung). If their **intentions are mutually suspected** (情既相猜, *ching-chih-hsiao-tsoi), they will **exhaust themselves naturally** (自尽矣, *tsu-chin-yi).

They must **conceal their traces** (函掩其迹, *han-yen-chih-chih*), **hide their beginnings** (匿其端, *ni-chih-tuan*), lest the ministers **cannot forgive** (不能原, *pu-neng-yuan*). They must **remove their wisdom** (去其智, *chü-chih-chih*), **sever their abilities** (绝其能, *chüeh-chih-neng*), lest the ministers **cannot discern** (不能意, *pu-neng-i).*

They must **preserve the means by which they act** (保吾所以往, *pao-wu-sui-yi-wang*), and **cross-check their consistency** (稽同之, *chi-tung-chih*). They must **carefully hold their authority** (谨执其柄, *chin-chih-chih-ping*) and **firmly grasp it** (固握之, *ku-wo-chih*).

They must **sever all hopes of ability** (绝其能望, *chüeh-chih-neng-wang*), **shatter all intentions** (破其意, *po-chih-i*), lest people **desire to act** (人欲之, *jen-yü-chih). If the ruler **holds authority firmly** (执柄固, *chih-ping-ku), then all ministers’ intentions and hopes are **severed**.

If the ruler **does not secure his defenses** (不谨其闭, *pu-chin-chih-pih), nor **fortify his gates** (不固其门, *pu-ku-chih-men*), then **the usurping tiger will linger** (虎乃将存, *hü-nai-chiang-ch’ün*). If authority is not firmly held, the **tiger of usurpation** (篡国之虎, *chuan-kuo-chih-hü*) will **take root**.

If the ruler **does not guard his affairs** (不慎其事, *pu-shen-chih-shih*), nor **conceal his intentions** (不掩其情, *pu-yen-chih-ching*), then **the regicidal thief will arise** (贼乃将生, *chih-nai-chiang-sheng*). The thief **kills his sovereign** (弒其主, *chi-chih-chiu*) and **seizes his position** (代其所, *tai-chih-suo*). Since all people **will side with the thief** (莫不与, *mu-pu-yü*), he is called *虎* (**the tiger**).

He lurks beside his sovereign, acting as a **treacherous minister** (奸臣, *chien-chih*), and **hears his sovereign’s weaknesses** (闻其主之忒, *wen-chih-chiu-chih-t’ieh*), hence he is called *贼* (**the thief**).

To **scatter his faction** (散其党, *san-chih-t’ang*), **collect his remnants** (收其余, *shou-chih-yü*), **seal his gates** (闭其门, *pi-chih-men*), and **strip his support** (夺其辅, *t’o-chih-fu*), then the state will have **no tiger**.

To be **vast beyond measure** (不可量, *pu-ku-liang*), **profound beyond reckoning** (不可测, *pu-ku-ts’o*), yet to **unify laws and names** (同合刑名, *tung-ho-hsing-ming*), **examine and verify legal standards** (审验法式, *shen-yen-fa-shih*), and **punish those who act arbitrarily** (擅为者诛, *shan-wei-chih-chu), then the state will have **no thief**.

Thus, the sovereign has **five obstructions** (五壅, *wu-yen):
1. Ministers **blocking the sovereign** (臣闭其主, *chih-pi-chih-chiu*) is an obstruction.
2. Ministers **controlling wealth and resources** (臣制财利, *chih-chih-ts’ai-li*) is an obstruction.
3. Ministers **issuing orders unilaterally** (臣擅行令, *chih-shan-hsing-ling*) is an obstruction.
4. Ministers **acting in righteousness on their own** (臣得行义, *chih-têh-hsing-i*) is an obstruction.
5. Ministers **raising followers independently** (臣得树人, *chih-têh-shu-jen*) is an obstruction.

When ministers **block the sovereign**, the sovereign **loses his position** (失位, *shih-wei*).
When ministers **control wealth and resources**, the sovereign **loses his virtue/power** (失德, *shih-tê*).
When ministers **issue orders unilaterally**, the sovereign **loses his authority** (失制, *shih-chih*).
When ministers **act in righteousness on their own**, the sovereign **loses his reputation** (失名, *shih-ming*).
When ministers **raise followers independently**, the sovereign **loses his faction** (失党, *shih-t’ang*).

This is how the sovereign **must hold exclusive authority** (独擅, *tu-shên*), and not allow ministers to **seize control** (得操, *têh-ts’ao).

The sovereign’s Dao/Way is to **hold tranquility and withdrawal as treasures** (静退以为宝, *ching-t’ui-yü-wei-pao*). By **not meddling in affairs** (不自操事, *pu-tsu-ts’ao-shih*), he knows **what is clumsy and what is skillful** (拙与巧, *chüo-yü-chiao*). By **not calculating his own deliberations** (不自计虑, *pu-tsu-chih-lü*), he knows **what brings fortune and what brings blame** (福与咎, *fu-yü-chiu).

Hence, he **does not speak yet responds appropriately** (不言而善应, *pu-yen-erh-shan-ying*), **does not make agreements yet increases harmony** (不约而善增, *pu-yüeh-erh-shan-tsêng*). If his words are already **responded to**, he **holds the contract** (执其契, *chih-chih-ch’ih*). If his affairs are already **augmented** (增, *tsêng*), he **seizes the symbol of authority** (操其符, *ts’ao-chih-fu*).

The **seal and contract** are where **authority and punishment** converge. Thus, the ministers present their words, and the sovereign, by his words, entrusts them with affairs, demanding their fulfillment through deeds. When deeds match the entrusted affairs and the affairs match the spoken words, he grants **rewards**. When deeds do not match the entrusted affairs and the affairs do not match the spoken words, he imposes **punishment**. The **Dao/Way of a sage sovereign** is that ministers do not present their words yet still fulfill them appropriately.

Hence, the sage sovereign’s **bestowal of rewards** is **warm like timely rain**, and the people benefit from its **blessings**. His **infliction of punishments** is **feared like thunder and lightning**, so much so that even the **divine and holy** cannot **dissolve** it. Therefore, a sage sovereign **never withholds rewards** nor **pardons punishments**. Withholding rewards makes **meritorious ministers abandon their achievements**, while pardoning crimes encourages **wicked men to persist in evil**. Hence, if there is **true merit**, even those **distant and lowly** must be rewarded; if there is **true fault**, even those **close and beloved** must be punished. Punishing those close and beloved ensures that the distant and lowly do not **grow slack**, while the close and beloved do not **become arrogant**.

---

**Han Feizi (卷之一竟)**

The **Han Feizi** states: *"Those who attack the order with disorder perish."* #1

---

**Hanzi Xu (韓子序)**

Han Fei was one of the **princely sons of the Han state**. He delighted in the **school of penal law and merit-based governance**, yet rooted his principles in **Huang-Lao** thought. Though **tongue-tied** and unable to **eloquently debate**, he excelled in **writing**. He served alongside Li Si under Xun Qing, and Li Si believed himself **inferior** to Han Fei. #2

Seeing the **Han state’s decline**, he repeatedly **submitted memorials** urging the Han king to **adopt capable advisors**, but the king **failed to act**. Thus, Han Fei observed that **governance did not seek merit** but instead **burdened honest officials** with **frivolous tasks**. He believed that **Confucians** disrupted law with **empty rhetoric**, while **chivalrous men** violated prohibitions through **violent force**. In times of **leniency**, he favored those **praised for reputation**; in times of **urgency**, he employed **armed warriors**. What he **used** was not what he **nurtured**, and what he **nurtured** was not what he **used**. The **frugal and upright** could not **thrive among corrupt ministers**. #3

Witnessing the **changes in fortune** of past rulers, he composed *Gu Fèn* (孤愤), *Wu Du* (五蠹), *Nei-Wai Chu* (内外储), and *Shuò Nàn* (说难) — **fifty-five chapters**, **over ten thousand words**. Someone **transmitted his writings to Qin**. #4

The Qin king, upon seeing *Gu Fèn* and *Wu Du*, exclaimed: *"Ah! If I could meet this man and converse with him, I would not regret even death."* Li Si replied: *"This is the work of Han Fei."* Qin then **hurriedly launched an assault** against Han.

Initially, Han **did nothing**, but when pressed, they **sent Han Fei as an envoy to Qin**. The Qin king **took pleasure in him**, yet **did not yet employ him**. Li Si feared him, saying: *"Han Fei is a princely son of the Han state. Now that we seek to **unify the feudal lords**, he will ultimately **remain loyal to Han rather than serve Qin**—this is his **natural inclination**. If the king **does not use him** but keeps him **long in residence**, only to **return him**, this will **invite disaster** for himself. It would be better to **transgress the law** and **execute him**."* The Qin king agreed, **ordered his arrest**, and Li Si **had poison administered** to him, compelling him to **suicide early**. Han Fei wished to **present himself in person** but **could not gain an audience**. Later, the Qin king **regretted his decision**, sending someone to **pardon him**, but Han Fei was already dead. #5

---

**Han Feizi (卷之二 有度第六)**

The **Han Feizi** states: *"The **supreme authority of the sovereign’s position**—his **weighty majesty** and **overwhelming power**—is **unmatched**."* #6

The **Han Feizi** states: *"A state has no **constant strength**, nor **constant weakness**. When those who **uphold the Dao/Way** are strong, the state is strong; when they **act without bending the law** nor **following private interests**. When those who **uphold the Dao/Way** are weak, the state is weak. King Zheng of Chu **annexed twenty-six states**, **expanding his territory by three thousand li**, yet his people and **sacred altars** (社稷) perished with Chu’s fall. In Chu’s time of **prosperity** and its time of **ruin**, neither the people nor the **sacred altars** (社稷) changed, yet both **flourished** and **perished**—the difference lies in whether the **Dao/Way was upheld with strength or weakness**. Lord Huan of Qi **annexed thirty states**, **expanding his territory by three thousand li**, yet his people and **sacred altars** (社稷) perished with Qi’s fall. King Xiang of Yan **set the Yellow River as its border** and **Jixia as its capital**, **seized the cities of Zhaoxi and Fangcheng**—both **Yan’s vassals**—and **defeated Qi**, **subdued Zhongshan**. With Yan’s presence, neighboring states **grew heavy**; without it, they **grew light**—meaning those who **ally with Yan** (as a party) were favored, while others were slighted. Yet Yan’s people and **sacred altars** (社稷) perished with its fall. King Anli of Wei **assaulted Zhao to rescue Yan**, **seizing the land of Hedong**—once the domain of Southern Yan, now **Wei’s through Yan’s favor**. He **conquered all of Tao and Wei’s territory**, **laid siege to Lu’s capital** in private ambition, **plundered the city of Guan**—once the domain of **Guan Zhong**—and **triumphed at Qixia**. In the **affair of Suiyang**, Chu’s army **aged and fled** after prolonged resistance. In the **affair of Cai and Zhaoling**, Chu’s army was **defeated**. **Wei’s forces spread across the four quarters**, **dominating the states of the civilized world**. Yet when Anli died, Wei **perished**.

Thus, when Chu had **Zheng** and Qi had **Huan**, Chu and Qi **could become hegemons**. When Yan had **Xiang** and Wei had **Anli**, Yan and Wei **could grow strong**. Yet now they are all **ruined states**, for their **ministers and officials** all **pursue disorder** rather than **order**. Their states are **disordered and weak**, and they **abandon the state’s laws** while **privately enriching their own affairs**—meaning they **prioritize their own interests** over public duty. This is like **carrying fuel to extinguish a fire**—the disorder and weakness are **most severe**. Therefore, in the present age, if one **discards private interests** and **conforms to public law**, the people **will be secure** and the state **will flourish**. If one **discards private schemes** and **strictly follows public law**, then the **army will be strong** and the **enemies will be weak**.

Hence, if the sovereign **selects ministers** based on their **mastery of gain and loss** and **adherence to legal standards**, then the ruler **cannot be deceived by falsehoods**. If the sovereign **chooses advisors** who **weigh gain and loss with precision**, then the ruler **cannot be deceived by the lightness or gravity of matters under Heaven**. Now, if **merit is advanced through flattery**, then ministers **will abandon their lord** and **clique with inferiors**—for merit comes from flattery, and they **seek empty praise**. If **officials are appointed by factional influence**, then the people **will focus on currying favor** rather than **seeking merit through law**. For when officials are **elevated by faction**, they **pursue connections** to secure their patrons’ support. Thus, when officials **lose merit through factionalism**, the state **becomes disordered**.

If **flattery is rewarded** and **slander is punished**, then those who **love rewards and hate punishments** will **ignore public law**, **practice private schemes**, and **clique with inferiors** for mutual gain. They **forget the state’s affairs** and **pursue private alliances**, thus their **inferiors will regard them lightly**. Though their **allies and patrons may be many**, and though **factions flourish within and without**, even if they commit **great crimes**, their **cover-ups will be numerous**. When **factions multiply**, they **shield each other**, so even **great crimes go unnoticed**. Thus, **loyal ministers** **perish unjustly**, while **wicked ministers** **prosper without merit**. When **wicked ministers** **clique with like-minded allies**, **loyal ministers** are **wrongly condemned** and **wicked ministers** **gain unjust rewards**.

If **loyal ministers** **perish unjustly** without their **crimes being proven**, then **virtuous ministers** **will be suppressed**. If **ministers harm their own kind**, then **virtuous ministers** **will be suppressed**. If **wicked ministers** **prosper unjustly** without **merit**, then **wicked ministers** **will advance**. For **those of like *qi*** **seek each other out**—thus **wicked ministers** **will rise**. This is the **root of ruin**.

If this is so, then though the **ministers may be many**, the ruler **holds only the name** of sovereignty, while **reality rests in the hands of his ministers’ factions**. For **authority and power do not shift**—this is why. Therefore, the minister says: *"The courts of ruined states hold no true men."* No **men who care for the state** remain. The minister—**Han Fei himself**—speaks. When the **court holds no true men**, it is not because the **court has declined**, but because **each household seeks its own gain**, not the **state’s prosperity**; **great ministers vie for their own honor**, not the **lord’s**; **lowly ministers accept their salaries** and **nurture their connections**, not **fulfilling their duties**. This is why it is so—because the ruler **does not enforce law from above** but **trusts those below** to act for him. Therefore, the **enlightened sovereign** **uses law to select ministers**, not **self-appointment**; he **uses law to measure merit**, not **self-assessment**.

The **method of selecting ministers** and **measuring merit** is **established in the state’s ordinances**—the **foundational institutions of the realm**."

「The capable must not be exhausted, the unworthy must not be adorned, praise cannot advance them, and criticism cannot dismiss them. By measuring merit with law, the capable cannot be exhausted, nor the unworthy adorned. When law adorns men, praise cannot advance them, nor criticism dismiss them. Thus, between ruler and ministers, distinctions are clear and governance is easy—clear distinctions mean good and evil do not conceal each other.

Therefore, the sovereign’s **enforcement of law** is acceptable. **Enforcement of law** refers to **examining and confirming** what is right. The **virtuous ministers**, when serving their lord, kneel in submission with no divided loyalty; they do not dare refuse lowly tasks for the court, and thus the soldiers do not dare refuse perilous missions. If the court refuses lowly tasks, then those below will harbor resentment toward their superiors. If soldiers refuse perilous missions, then officials will harbor intentions of neglecting their duties. To act in accordance with the lord’s will, to follow the lord’s law, and to keep an open mind awaiting commands without harboring bias—this is the way.

Thus, they speak with no private words, only words for the lord. They see with no private eyes, only eyes for the lord. And the lord fully **deploys their virtue**.

For those who serve as ministers, consider them like hands: the lord uses them to tend the head, while they use their own to tend the feet. Cold and heat, cleanliness and warmth—these cannot but be shared. When cold, they share warmth; when hot, they share cleanliness. All these are done through the hands, hence the saying: *"they cannot but be shared."*

Even when **sharp tools** are pressed against the body, the hands must grasp them. When a blade is near the body, the hands will inevitably grasp it—there are no **private virtuous ministers** or **private capable officials**. **Virtuous ministers** and **capable officials** are all employed for **public use**. Hence, the people do not cross their own villages to make friends, nor do they have **affinities within a hundred li**.

Once ministers are entrusted with **public virtue**, governance becomes fair and the state orderly; the people have no divergent expectations or external ambitions, hence they do not cross villages to make friends, and thus there are no **affinities within a hundred li**.

High and low do not overstep their bounds; the **foolish and the wise** are balanced and appointed accordingly, each receiving their proper place—hence the saying: *"balanced and appointed accordingly."* This is the pinnacle of governance.

Now, those who **lightly value rank and salary**, easily abandon a failing state to seek another lord, are not called **liberal**. To abandon a failing state and seek another lord is merely the act of a **greedy heart**. Such ministers cannot be called **liberal**.

Those who **deceive with flattery**, oppose the law, betray their lord, yet persist in giving strong advice, are not called **loyal**. To oppose the law and give strong advice is to **overstep the lord’s authority**. Such ministers cannot be called **loyal**.

Those who **practice kindness and bestow favors**, yet **demand credit from below**, are not called **benevolent**. To practice kindness and demand credit is to **create merit for oneself**. Such ministers cannot be called **benevolent**.

Those who **withdraw from society** and take refuge in seclusion, yet **criticize their superiors**, are not called **righteous**. To withdraw and criticize one’s superiors is to **exalt the lord’s faults**. Such ministers cannot be called **righteous**.

Those who **engage foreign lords as envoys**, yet **drain their own state’s resources**, wait for moments of **national peril** to instill fear in their lord, saying: *"Resentment is not mine that I cannot resolve; grievances are not mine that I cannot untangle,"*—if the lord believes them and **entrusts the state to their judgment**, humiliating the lord’s prestige to aggrandize themselves, destroying the state’s strength to benefit their own households—such ministers are not called **wise**. To exploit national peril for the lord’s discredit and destroy the state for personal gain is the act of **crafty tyrants**. Such ministers cannot be called **wise**.

These five practices are **crafty sayings of a treacherous age**, yet they are what the **ancient sages** **discarded**. **Crafty sayings** seek **short-term advantage**, while the **ancient sages’ teachings** must ensure **eternal adherence**. The **ancient sages’ laws** state: *"Ministers must not create their own authority, must not create their own profits, and must follow the king’s commands. They must not commit evil and must follow the king’s path."*

In ancient times, the people of a **well-governed age** adhered to **public law**, discarded **private schemes**, and focused their intentions solely on fulfilling their duties. The people of a **well-governed age**, in all their intentions and actions, did not use them for private gain but only to await the lord’s **appointment**.

If the lord personally **inspects every official**, he will lack time and strength. Instead, he should **employ law** to examine them. Moreover, if the lord relies on his **eyes**, those below will **adorn their appearances**; if he relies on his **ears**, they will **adorn their words**; if he relies on his **thoughts**, they will **proliferate their arguments**. The **ancient sages** regarded these three methods as insufficient, hence they **restrained their own abilities**, **relied on the law’s standards**, and **carefully assessed rewards and punishments**. This is what the **ancient sages** **held as essential**.

Thus, the law is **concise yet not restrictive**. Within the **Four Seas**, the **crafty and cunning** cannot employ their deceit, the **restless and bold** cannot rely on their flattery, and the **wicked and perverse** have no foundation to stand on. Even if they are **thousands of li away**, they dare not alter their words. Though they stand **among the attendants**, they dare not **conceal the good or embellish the bad**. **Attendants** are officials in **close service**.

Though the **lowly and humble** may be **direct and honest**, even they are prevented from **overstepping their bounds**. Though they are direct and honest, they are still **assigned their proper duties**, and the **powerful and strong** dare not overstep.

Hence, governance is **never lacking**, yet the lord’s **duties are always in excess**, for the **lord’s authority and the law’s teachings** make it so. Once the **foundations of governance** are established, the **lord’s duties** remain abundant, and the **teachings of law** and **virtue** are already balanced, while the **ministers are already disciplined**—then the lord’s **authoritative deployment** does not conflict, and the law’s teachings ensure it.

The **encroachment of ministers upon their lord** is like the **shape of the earth**: it **gradually shifts**, like how the **land appears to be tilled away** over time, **slowly eroded and diminished**. The lord loses his bearings, **east and west reversed**, yet he does not realize it. Since the encroachment comes **gradually**, even when it reaches the point where the lord **loses his bearings entirely**, he still **cannot perceive it**.

Therefore, the **ancient sages established the compass** to **determine the directions of morning and evening**—the compass being the **south-pointing chariot**—to illustrate the **state’s correct law**. Hence, the **enlightened sovereign** ensures his **ministers do not wander in thought beyond the law**, nor **practice favors within the law’s scope**; he does not allow **wandering thoughts beyond the law** or **favors within the law**, all of which are measures to **prevent encroachment.**»

The movement of all things must conform to law. Law is what transcends and restrains private deviations beyond its scope. If all the actions of the ministers are governed by law, then any transgression beyond it is nothing but private scheming.

Harsh punishments are used to ensure and deter the subjects. The reason for harsh punishments is to ensure and deter the subjects. *Ensure* means to make thorough.

Authority does not delegate errors, and governance does not share decisions with ministers. Authority should be the sovereign’s to correct, so it does not delegate to ministers—governance and correction should be the sovereign’s alone. *Errors* means to place. If authority and governance are shared with ministers, then all evils will flourish. If governance and authority are shared with ministers, then the suppression of evil will be clearly demonstrated.

If law lacks credibility, the sovereign’s rule becomes perilous. If law lacks credibility, the heir cannot be established, hence the sovereign’s peril. If punishments are not decisive, evil cannot be subdued.

Thus it is said: A skilled carpenter aligns his vision with the plumb line, yet he must first use the compass and square as his measure. Though the carpenter’s eye aligns with the plumb line, it cannot be used without the compass and square as his standard. The wise ruler swiftly executes affairs in accordance with the laws of the ancient kings, yet even his wisdom cannot suffice without taking the ancient kings’ laws as his standard.

The plumb line straightens crooked wood, and the level plane shaves down high ground. *Equal* refers to leveling high ground to bring it down. The balance scale, when suspended, makes heavy things lighter and light things heavier—reducing the heavy and increasing the light until the scale is balanced. The measure for grain and stone, when set, makes abundance scarce—reducing the excess and increasing the deficit until the measure is full.

Thus, governing the state with law is simply a matter of applying and executing it. When law is applied and executed properly, governance naturally becomes balanced. Law does not favor the noble; the plumb line does not bend to crookedness. Where law is applied, the wise cannot evade it, nor the brave dare to contest it. Punishment for crimes does not spare high ministers, nor does reward for virtue neglect the commoner.

Thus, correcting the sovereign’s errors, reproving the subjects’ evils, resolving chaos and confusion, suppressing arrogance and aligning wrongdoing—suppressing the proud and arrogant, rectifying their wrongs. *Suppressing* is pronounced *chù*. No better guide for the people exists than law. Officials rely on authority to govern the people. *Rely on officials* means to make officials subordinate to oneself. To repel licentiousness and deceit, to stop fraud and falsehood, nothing is more effective than punishment.

Heavy punishments ensure that none dare exchange low status for high through nobility or use noble power to disdain the low. Precise law ensures the sovereign’s authority is exalted and unassailable, making the ruler strong and secure in his essentials—thus the ancient kings valued and transmitted it. *Transmitted* means passed down to posterity.

If the sovereign abandons law for private favoritism, then distinctions between ruler and subjects vanish.

**The Two Instruments (Chapter Seven)**
The enlightened sovereign guides and restrains his ministers through two instruments alone. *Guides* means to lead, speaking of how the Way leads. He instructs his ministers and judges them accordingly. The two instruments are punishment and virtue. What are punishment and virtue?

Punishment refers to killing and slaughter; virtue refers to rewards and honors. As a subject, one fears punishment and seeks rewards, so if the sovereign personally wields punishment and virtue, the ministers will fear his authority and seek his benefits.

Yet the deluded ministers of the world act otherwise: what they hate, they can persuade the sovereign to punish, and through deceit and flattery, they use the sovereign’s authority to inflict punishment. What they love, they can persuade the sovereign to reward, and through deceit and flattery, they use the sovereign’s favor to bestow rewards.

Now, if the sovereign does not ensure that the power of rewards and punishments originates from himself, but instead listens to his ministers and lets them execute rewards and punishments, then all the people in the state will fear the ministers and disregard the sovereign. If ministers mete out punishment, the people will fear the ministers and despise the sovereign. They will turn to the ministers and abandon the sovereign. If ministers bestow rewards, the people will turn to the ministers and abandon the sovereign. This is the sovereign’s peril of losing control over punishment and virtue.

The tiger’s power over dogs lies in its claws and teeth. If the tiger abandons its claws and teeth and lets the dogs use them, then the tiger will be subdued by the dogs. The sovereign uses punishment and virtue to restrain his ministers. If the sovereign abandons punishment and virtue and lets the ministers wield them, then the sovereign will be controlled by his ministers—subdued by his own subjects.

Thus, Tian Chang requested titles and emoluments from his lord and distributed them among the ministers, requesting the lord’s titles and emoluments to bestow upon the ministers, thereby establishing private virtue among the officials. He used large measures for grain and stone when distributing to the people, thereby establishing private favor among the commoners. This was how Duke Jian of Qi lost his virtue, allowing Tian Chang to exploit it—thus Duke Jian was assassinated.

Zi Han told the Duke of Song: "Rewards and honors are what the people delight in; the sovereign should personally administer them. Killing and punishment are what the people abhor; let me, your subject, bear this responsibility." Thus, the Duke of Song lost control over punishment, allowing Zi Han to exploit it—thus the Duke of Song was held hostage.

Tian Chang relied solely on virtue, meaning he did not combine it with punishment, yet Duke Jian was assassinated. Zi Han relied solely on punishment, meaning he did not combine it with virtue, yet the Duke of Song was held hostage.

Thus, today’s ministers, if they combine punishment and virtue and wield them, then the peril facing the sovereign is even greater than that of Duke Jian or the Duke of Song. Therefore, no sovereign who allows his ministers to wield punishment and virtue without peril or ruin has ever existed.

If the sovereign wishes to suppress deceit, he must carefully match punishments to their designated crimes. *Speaking* refers to naming, *affairs* refers to actions. If the words and deeds do not align, their correspondence cannot be known.

When a minister presents his words, the sovereign assigns him the corresponding task and holds him solely accountable for its outcome. If the outcome matches the task and the task matches the words, then reward. If the outcome does not match the task or the task does not match the words, then punish.

Thus, if ministers make grand claims but achieve little, they are punished—not for minor achievements, but for outcomes failing to match their claims. If ministers make modest claims but achieve greatly, they are also punished—not because great achievements are displeased, but because they do not match their claims. The harm of unmatched claims is greater than having great achievements, hence the punishment.

「The harm of failing to match the words is greater than having great achievements, and great achievements that #9[shock the sovereign] are also grounds for punishment.

In ancient times, the Lord of Zhao became drunk and fell asleep. The attendant who managed the crown saw that his lord was cold, so he added a robe to his lord’s body. When the lord awoke from his sleep, he was pleased, and upon waking, he realized the act.

He asked his attendants: "Who added the robe?"
The attendants replied: "The crown attendant."
The lord then punished both the robe attendant and the crown attendant together.

The robe attendant was blamed for neglecting his duty, while the crown attendant was blamed for overstepping his authority. The lord was not displeased because he disliked the cold—rather, he deemed the harm of encroaching on another’s office as greater than the cold itself.

Thus, a wise sovereign keeps his ministers in check: they may not overstep their duties to gain achievements, nor may they make claims that do not match their words. Overstepping duties results in death, failing to match claims results in punishment. Faithfully fulfilling their assigned duties and matching their words with their deeds is the true standard. By adhering to their proper duties and ensuring their words align with them, ministers cannot form factions.

A sovereign has two afflictions: favoring the virtuous may allow ministers to exploit the sovereign’s trust by seizing power. The virtuous must necessarily possess many talents and skills, thus enabling them to exploit their virtue and usurp the sovereign’s authority.

Or, reckless appointments may lead to failure and defeat. Reckless appointments refer to selecting ministers without regard to their merit, which inevitably results in failure and defeat. *Stū* (沮) means ruin and collapse. Therefore, when a sovereign favors the virtuous, ministers embellish their conduct to curry favor, and their true intentions remain concealed. Embellished conduct is mere pretense, so their inner motives are not revealed. *Xiǎn* (效) means to manifest or display. If ministers’ true intentions are not displayed, the sovereign cannot distinguish them.

Since all ministers embellish their conduct, the sovereign cannot tell true from false. Thus, the King of Yue loved valor, and his people often died lightly. The King of Chu loved slender waists, and his realm had many starving men. The Duke of Qi was jealous and fond of his women, so Jian Tiǎo (坚刁) took matters into his own hands to manage the inner quarters; the Duke of Qi loved fine flavors, so Yí Yá (易牙) steamed his own son’s head and offered it to him. Zi Qiao (子之) of Yan loved virtue, so Zi Ming (子之明) refused to accept the throne, claiming to let Zi Qiao take it instead, and thus seized power.

Hence, when the sovereign perceives evil, ministers conceal their true motives to avoid what he dislikes. When the sovereign perceives good, ministers falsely claim their abilities to seek favor. If the sovereign desires to see something, ministers’ true inclinations and behaviors provide the means to manipulate him. Ministers’ true inclinations are all aimed at seeking personal gain—when the sovereign sees their preferences, he knows where their interests lie, and thus they can exploit his desires.

Therefore, Zi Ming (子之明) relied on his virtue to deceive and take power from his lord, Jian Tiǎo (坚刁) and Yí Yá (易牙) exploited the lord’s desires to encroach upon his authority, and Zi Qiao (子哙) ultimately brought chaos to Yan. Zi Qiao (子哙) was the name of the Yan king. The Duke of Qi’s corpse was left unburied outside the gate—what was the reason for this?

The sovereign’s own preferences lend themselves to the ministers’ schemes, thereby inviting their misdeeds.»

The sovereign’s preferences and aversions, once revealed, become tools for ministers to exploit for their own gain—this is how desires invite ministers to seek advantage, thereby generating chaos. Ministers do not inherently love their sovereign; they act out of self-interest.

When the sovereign does not conceal his preferences nor obscure their origins, he unwittingly grants ministers the means to encroach upon him. By revealing his likes and dislikes, he allows them to exploit these very traits to undermine his authority. Thus, ministers would readily serve figures like Zi Ming (子之) or Tian Chang (田常).

Hence it is said: *"Abandon likes and dislikes, and ministers will show their true nature."* If the sovereign has no preferences, ministers cannot feign false abilities for his favor—truth will naturally manifest. When ministers reveal their true nature, the sovereign will not be misled.

**Yáng Quán** (扬权, *lit.* "Promoting Authority")—the eighth principle: **Yáng** (扬) means to uplift and clarify, **Quán** (权) means to measure matters and devise strategies. The great patterns of Heaven and Earth—day and night, the four seasons—are Heaven’s great **Quán** (命). The proper distinctions between sovereign and ministers, superiors and inferiors, are humanity’s great **Quán** (命).

Fragrant delicacies and tender flavors, rich wine and fatty meats, though pleasing to the palate, bring ruin to the body. Graceful demeanor and white teeth, though charming, drain vitality when indulged excessively. Acquired talents, though helpful, become perilous if misapplied—overuse leads to bodily harm, excessive indulgence wastes vital essence, and improper reliance on clever ministers endangers the sovereign. Thus, avoiding extremes preserves safety.

**Quán** (权) should not be wielded if the sovereign’s **Way** (道) remains natural and unforced. The affairs of the four directions (四方) depend on the center (中央). The four directions refer to the people, while the center refers to the sovereign. The sage holds the center, and the four directions come to serve him. If he remains empty and receptive, they will naturally align themselves.

**Yīn** (以, *lit.* "use") means to employ. The sovereign need only maintain an open heart and await their actions; then each will naturally apply their own abilities. When the four seas are contained, **Yīn** (道) reveals itself in **Yáng** (阳). The four seas are the four directions; to contain them is to let them remain unseen. If their abilities are thus, the sovereign must guide ministers’ **Yīn** (阴) to reveal his own **Yáng** (阳)—when **Yīn** and **Yáng** meet, sovereign and ministers will harmonize.

Once the left and right ministers (左右) are established, the sovereign must open the door to receive them. The left and right ministers are the **left assistant** (左辅) and **right minister** (右弼). When sovereign and ministers harmonize, these aiding ministers will stand firm—like echoes following sound, or voices resonating in unison. Then, all worthy talents from the four directions will gather.

The sovereign need only open the door to receive them, without obstruction. **Dāng** (当) means to accept. If he does not alter or shift their roles, letting the two ministers—**left assistant** and **right minister**—both fulfill their duties, then once their tasks are set in motion, if they prove capable and worthy, these are all ministers of **virtue** (贤), needing no removal. None will fail to transform and align.

This is called **treading the Way** (履理). When the sovereign can **tread the Way**, success will follow.

Things have their proper uses, talents have their fitting applications. If each is placed where it belongs, then superiors and inferiors need no forced intervention. To let a rooster guard the night or a weasel catch mice is to employ their abilities—yet the superior will have no work to do. If the superior has strengths, affairs will naturally proceed without obstruction.

To **boast** (矜) of abilities and **favor** (好) cleverness is to invite deception from inferiors. If those in high positions **boast** of their cleverness, inferiors will each embellish their own abilities to deceive them. To **cherish** (辩惠) and **love** (好生) cleverness is to exploit talents. If superiors **cherish** cleverness and **love** those who are skillful in **deception** (辩惠), inferiors will use their talents to flatter and curry favor—cleverness itself becomes a tool for **deception**.

When superiors and inferiors exchange roles, the state will inevitably fall into disarray. If superiors **boast** while inferiors manipulate, the state will not be well-ordered.

The **Way** of **unity** (一) begins with **proper naming** (名). **Yī** (一) refers to the **Way**. What can be perpetually applied, unchanged from ancient to modern times, is only **proper naming**. Thus it is said: *"The **Way** of **unity** begins with **proper naming**."* When names are correct, things are settled; when names are misaligned, things shift accordingly.

Therefore, the sage holds **unity** to remain **still** (静), letting names define themselves and affairs settle naturally. Once names and affairs are aligned, matters will naturally be settled. If the superior does not observe their forms, inferiors will naturally maintain correctness.

Following their actions, he entrusts them, letting them take charge. If he follows their actions and entrusts them, they will naturally assume their own responsibilities. Following their actions and granting them authority, they will naturally take charge. If he follows their actions and aligns with them, they will naturally settle matters themselves. The superior uses **proper naming** to guide them—all matters are left to their own definition. If the superior follows this method, then **punishments and names** will be clear and precise.

If he does not know their names, he will again examine their forms. Forms refer to affairs. By tracing affairs to seek their names, their names can be known. When forms and names correspond, he will use those who arise from them.

Those who arise from them—those who emerge through the alignment of forms and names—are the ones he will employ. If these two (forms and names) are **sincere** (诚) and **trustworthy** (信), inferiors will naturally contribute their true **emotions** (情).

**Gòng** (贡) means to present or reveal. The ruler who carefully attends to affairs will receive **Heaven’s response** (符应之命). He must not lose the **key points** (要), and then he will be a sage.

The sage’s **Way** abandons **wisdom** (智) and **cleverness** (巧). If **wisdom** and **cleverness** are not abandoned, it is difficult to maintain the **Way** as a constant. When **wisdom** and **cleverness** are present, they will inevitably lead to **deception** (诈) and deviation from the **Way**. Thus, they must be abandoned.

If common people employ them, their own lives will suffer many calamities. If the sovereign employs them, his state will face peril and ruin.

Following **Heaven’s **Way** (天之道), reversing **forms’ principles** (形之理), and **supervising** (督), **verifying** (参), and **examining** (鞠) them together, the end will return to the beginning. Once **wisdom** and **cleverness** are abandoned, the superior follows **Heaven’s **Way****, while inferiors reverse **forms’ principles**—the two together are **supervised**, **verified**, and **examined**. When their actions conclude, they return to their original state.

Remaining **empty** (虚) and **still** (静), he never uses himself first. The **Way** of the sage is always to defer to others after **emptiness** and **stillness**, never to act first by employing himself.
