The sovereign’s afflictions always arise from his own beginnings. **Beginnings** (端) refer to the first matters presented. If a minister presents affairs without distinguishing their merit or demerit, always aligning with them, then this is the peril of partial listening leading to calamity.

**Trust** (信) without alignment, and all the people will follow in unison. When matters are presented, first **trust** them, but do not immediately align with them. Then select their virtuous aspects to apply in governance, and all the people will be harmonized and follow in unison.

The **Dao/Way** is vast and boundless yet without form;
**Virtue/Power** is the essence of principle yet universally pervasive.
When applied to all beings, it weighs and measures their use—
All things flourish, yet it does not rest with them.
**Virtue/Power** does not rest with things, yet things themselves find rest.

The **Dao/Way** is not confined to affairs but follows examination to issue commands,
Giving life and death in accordance with the times.
This is saying: *You should examine the **Dao/Way** to determine your responses to their petitions.*
**While** (而), *you* (汝) also.
**Life and death** (死生) is akin to **abolishment and restoration** (废兴).
It means: *When their teachings can be abolished, abolish them; when they can be restored, restore them.*

**Verify** (参) different matters by their names,
Yet ensure they converge in one principle and shared sentiment.
**Verify** the names of different matters,
And command them to be unified yet resonate with shared sentiment.
Thus it is said: The **Dao/Way** does not align with all things, yet it can give birth to all things.
**Virtue/Power** does not align with yin and yang, yet it can perfect yin and yang.
The **balance** (衡) does not align with light and heavy, yet it can discern their lightness and weight.
The **measuring line** (绳) does not align with right and wrong, yet it can rectify right and wrong.
**Harmony** (和) does not align with dryness and moisture, yet it can equalize dryness and moisture.
The sovereign does not align with the multitude of ministers.
Thus he can govern the multitude of ministers.
These six principles are the **manifestations of the Dao** (道之出).
These six principles all arise from the **Dao/Way** itself, hence it is said: *the **manifestations of the Dao** (道之出).*

The **Dao/Way** has no duality, hence it is said: *one* (一).
Thus, the enlightened sovereign values the singular **Dao/Way**,
And the **Dao/Way** is valued for its singularity.

If the sovereign and ministers do not align in the **Dao/Way**,
The inferiors should present their names and petitions.
The inferiors should present their words and reasoning to petition the sovereign.
The sovereign holds their names, while the ministers embody their forms—
When **forms** and **names** are **verified** and aligned,
Superior and inferior are harmonized and attuned.

The **Dao/Way** of listening is to let their words emerge,
Yet use them to draw inward and discern.
The **Dao/Way** of listening is such:
If there are doubts, one must speak to challenge them,
And they will then seek their principles to align with yours.

Thus, **verify** the names to establish positions,
And clarify distinctions to differentiate categories.
**Verify** the names, and the matters and positions will be self-established.
Clarify their distinctions, and the types of things will be self-differentiated.

The **Dao/Way** of listening is like being deeply intoxicated.
**Dissolving** (溶) is the appearance of leisure and ease.
When listening to speech, one should appear **dissolved** (溶) as if deeply intoxicated,
So that the speaker will fully reveal their thoughts and words will be laid bare.

Lips or teeth—
I will not be the first to speak.
If lips or teeth speak,
The more I appear **dim-witted** (惛惛),
The more they will appear **clear and bright** (昭昭).
They will then separate themselves,
And I will thereby know them.

Right and wrong radiate outward,
The sovereign does not fabricate them.

**Separation** refers to analyzing what they say.
Once they have been analyzed, I thereby know it.
The words they present—whether right or wrong—
radiate outward like spokes converging on a hub,
all arising from sincere intent below,
while the sovereign does not contrive them.

*Contrive* (構) means *to bind* (結).
**Vacuity and stillness, non-action**—this is the essence of the Dao/Way.
**Comparing and aligning things**—this is the form of governance.
Compare by aligning with things,
and arrange in fives to harmonize with vacuity.
If the roots and trunks remain unshaken,
then movements and leaks will not be lost.

*Compare* (參) is three.
*Arrange* (伍) is five.
That is, the matters presented may be examined in threes through the alignment of things,
or in fives through the harmonization of vacuity’s numbers.
Always ensure the roots and trunks are firmly planted,
unmoved and unshaken—
thus, even if movements scatter,
nothing will be lost or leaked.

When movements **dissolve** (溶),
non-action governs their transformation.
All actions, when **dissolved** (溶) in leisure,
though altered, are done without contrivance.

If you take pleasure, there will be excess;
if you take dislike, resentment will arise.
That is, when ministers present their words,
if the sovereign favors them, they will flatter and multiply their deeds.
If the sovereign dislikes them, they will harbor resentment and cease.

Thus, abandoning favor and aversion,
emptying the heart as the Dao’s dwelling,
the heart, emptied of favor and aversion,
becomes the Dao’s resting place.
The sovereign does not interfere with them,
and the people thereby receive their due.

That is, when ministers act without the sovereign’s involvement,
they gain autonomy, and their deeds will succeed,
thus earning their honor and favor.

The sovereign does not share in their governance,
but instead closes the inner gates,
observing the court from within the chamber—
measuring with the span of a *zhi* (咫) and *cun* (寸),
all things already in their proper place.
Reward those who deserve reward,
punish those who deserve punishment.

*Closing the inner gates* means sealing the heart to scrutinize ministers.
From within, observing outward—
like viewing the courtyard from a chamber.
Eight *cun* make a *zhi*.
*Cun* and *inch* measure length—
once the heart is sealed and verified,
and measured with *zhi* and *cun*,
both are complete—
then great and small, long and short,
each finds its place without confusion.
Thus, if reward is due, reward is given;
if punishment is due, punishment is meted out—
no contradictions arise.

Following their actions,
each attains their own outcome.
Good and evil will surely be met—
who would dare not believe?

Once the *gu* (规) and *ju* (矩) are set,
the three corners align.
If rewards and punishments are applied correctly to one or two matters,
people will know the rest follow the same rule—
thus, the three corners align.

If the sovereign is not **divine** (神),
the ministers will exploit the opportunity.
*Divine* refers to what is hidden and inscrutable in its workings.
Since the sovereign is not divine,
it can be measured—
and if measured, it can be exploited—
thus, the ministers will take advantage.

If their actions are improper,
the ministers will examine the norms.
When governance is flawed,
the ministers will assess it against established principles
to expose its faults.

Like heaven or earth—
this is called **layered resolution** (累解).
Heaven and earth are vast and profound,
beyond measure.
If the sovereign’s intent mirrors heaven and earth,
then even layered scrutiny will reveal no flaw.

Like earth, like heaven—
who is distant, who is close?
Heaven covers without favor,
earth bears without preference—
thus, there is neither distance nor closeness.

To embody heaven and earth—
this is the sage.

To govern the inner realm,
appoint officials without personal favor.
*The inner realm* refers to the sovereign’s secrets.
To ensure no loss in confidential matters,
do not show private favor to the appointed ministers.

To govern the outer realm,
assign one official per office.
Do not allow them unchecked authority—
how else could they usurp or combine powers?

*The outer realm* refers to the administration of officials.
To ensure governance is not lost,
assign one person per office—
two strong figures will inevitably clash,
and if an office has two, it only breeds suspicion.
One ensures specialization without excess,
so no shifting or merging occurs.

The gates of great ministers
should fear no one but themselves.
Ministers’ gates teem with people
because power and influence reside there.

The pinnacle of governance:
the ministers cannot fathom it.
The sovereign’s hidden, inscrutable nature
makes it unattainable to the ministers—
thus, this is the ultimate in governance.

When *xing* (刑) and *ming* (名) are harmonized,
the people will uphold their duties.
To abandon this and seek alternatives
is the greatest confusion.

The more cunning the people,
the more rampant the deceit.
Since the greatest confusion prevails,
deceit multiplies and evil spreads.

Thus it is said:
Do not lend to the wealthy,
nor coerce the noble.
If the sovereign lends to ministers as if they were wealthy,
or commands them as if they were noble—
these are the inverted and deluded,
who do not recognize principle.

Do not place absolute trust in one person
and thereby lose the state.
Trusting one alone
concentrates power and authority,
thus the state is lost.

A calf larger than the thigh—
difficult to run swiftly.
A minister heavier than the sovereign—
difficult to govern.

The sovereign loses their **divinity** (神),
and the tiger follows close behind.
*Losing divinity* means the sovereign becomes predictable,
like a minister who acts as a tiger,
waiting to exploit their openings.

If the sovereign remains unaware,
the tiger will become a dog.
If the sovereign fails to recognize the tiger in the minister,
the minister will conceal their ferocity,
appearing docile like a dog—
this is how they secretly plot.

If the sovereign does not act swiftly,
the dogs will multiply without end.
Once the minister masks as a dog,
and the sovereign fails to stop them,
they will recruit others to their cause,
growing their faction endlessly.

When the tigers form their pack,
they will slay their mother.
*The mother* refers to the sovereign.
Once the faction solidifies,
the tigers will turn on their source.

To be a sovereign without ministers—
what state remains?

All ministers are tigers—thus it is said there are no ministers. Without ministers, the state perishes—thus it is said, *what state remains?*

When the sovereign enforces his laws, the great tigers grow timid.
When the sovereign applies his punishments, the great tigers settle into compliance.
Once the sovereign has enforced his punishments, the tigers fear and walk the Dao, thus attaining peace.

Let the laws and punishments be trusted by dogs, and the tigers will transform into humans, returning to their true nature—
meaning the sovereign rules as sovereign, the minister serves as minister.

To govern the state, one must strike at their gatherings.
Their gatherings are the factions where they conspire; striking them scatters their factions.
If you do not strike their gatherings, they will gather more.

To secure the land, one must distribute rewards appropriately—
the land is also the state.
To govern the state, one must ensure rewards are justly given.
If rewards are not just, the disorderly will seek more.
When they demand and you give, it is like lending an axe to an enemy—
if you give what should not be given, they will use it to strike you.
To give an axe to an enemy is to lend what should not be lent—
once the enemy has the axe, your being struck is only right.

The Yellow Emperor said:
*The ruler and subjects wage war a hundred times a day.*
The position of the sovereign is precious, its advantages coveted—
those below always harbor desires.
To remain still is impossible; to take is unattainable—
these two forces clash a hundred times in a day.

The subjects conceal their private schemes, testing the sovereign above.
The sovereign must wield measure and restraint to curb them.
Since those below harbor covetous desires, they constantly conceal their schemes to test the sovereign—
thus the sovereign must wield measure and restraint to judge and cut them down.

Therefore, the establishment of measure and restraint is the sovereign’s treasure—
measure and restraint can curb those below, making them the sovereign’s treasure.

Factional alliances are the minister’s treasure.
Factional alliances can usurp the sovereign’s position, hence they are the minister’s treasure.
The minister does not kill his sovereign because his factional alliances are incomplete.

Thus, when the sovereign loses an inch, the subjects gain a fathom.
*Four fingers* are an *exhu*—
when the sovereign slightly falters in measure and restraint, the subjects gain benefits many times over.

A sovereign of a state does not enlarge his capital.
To enlarge the capital, ministers will seize it to rebel.
A virtuous minister does not honor his own family.
When a great officer honors his family, the minister will use it to elevate himself.
A virtuous sovereign does not honor his ministers.
To honor his ministers, the minister will grow in power beyond the sovereign.
If honored and enriched, they will prepare to replace him.
Once the minister is honored, enriched, and prepared, he will surely usurp the sovereign.
In peril, place the heir apparent swiftly—then calamity will have no source.
The heir apparent is the sovereign’s deputy and the state’s stronghold.
To avert danger, appoint him quickly—then the roots of disaster will cease.

To seek out and restrain those who plot within, the sovereign must personally wield measure and restraint.
Ministers scheme against the sovereign from all sides, always lurking in the capital—
now, if you seek to restrain those who plot from within, you must only ensure the sovereign personally wields measure and restraint.

The powerful must be diminished, the weak must be exhausted.
*The powerful* refers to the minister’s factions, their influence and rank high.
Their rank must be diminished to weaken them.
Diminish and exhaust them with measure—
do not let the people compare and unite, conspiring together against the sovereign.
Diminish them like the moon’s waning—
like the gradual decline of light.
It follows the principle that all that flourishes must decline—
the way of Heaven.
Exhaust them like heat—
like drawing fire until it burns without respite.
Orders must be strict, punishments swift—
exhaust the logic of penalties.
Do not slacken the bow—
one perch cannot hold two eagles.
*A bow* is drawn to shoot what should not perch—
this metaphorizes punishments and laws against officials unfit for their positions.
One perch cannot hold two eagles—they will fight, *their struggle is visible.*
Wolves and jackals in the prison—
their sheep will not multiply.
*Wolves and jackals* symbolize corrupt officials.
A household with two nobles—
their affairs will bear no fruit.
Two nobles will vie for dominance, and those serving will not know whom to follow—
thus, their affairs bear no fruit.
When husband and wife jointly rule—
the children will not know whom to obey.
The proper order is for the husband to lead and the wife to follow—
now, if husband and wife contend for rule, the children will be confused.

For the sovereign, frequently prune his branches—
do not let the branches spread unchecked.
*Branches* symbolize ministers.
*Pruning* means stripping their power.
Frequently stripping the ministers’ influence—
their factions will disperse.
Uproot their foundations, and the tree will lose its power.
Fill the deep pools—
do not let the water remain clear.
*A pool* is where water accumulates.
If the water is clear, many will gather to gaze—
just as those who align with them will multiply.
Probe their intentions, strip them of their influence.
*Probe their intentions*—
to plumb their hidden desires.

The sovereign employs them—
like lightning, like thunder.
If their awe does not extend to all,
then the sovereign’s command is divine and to be feared—
thus like thunder and lightning.

**The Nine Deceits of the Eighth Category**
In general, the ways by which ministers foster treachery are eight in number.
*Ways* means to entice.
It means that treacherous ministers either entice the sovereign’s attendants
or entice the sovereign’s subjects to accomplish their wicked schemes,
and their methods are eightfold.

**First: Sharing the Bed**
What is meant by *sharing the bed*?
It refers to noble consorts, beloved children,
and those who indulge in private pleasures and favor beautiful women.
These are the ones who bewitch the sovereign.
Under the pretext of leisurely companionship,
taking advantage of the sovereign’s drunkenness or satiety,
they seek what they desire—
this is the art of ensuring compliance.
*Taking advantage* means to exploit.
Consorts and children, by providing entertainment during the sovereign’s drunkenness or satiety,
and offering their desired favors,
have nothing the sovereign will not grant.
As ministers, they curry favor with gold and jade,
bewitching the sovereign through them—
this is called *sharing the bed*.
With treasures of gold and jade,
they curry favor with noble consorts and beloved children,
causing the sovereign to be bewitched—
once the sovereign is bewitched, their treacherous schemes can succeed.

**Second: Standing at the Side**
What is meant by *standing at the side*?

**The Eight Methods of Treacherous Ministers**

**1. Dwarfs and Jesters**
What is meant by *dwarfs and jesters*?
It refers to jesters who can sing and laugh, and dwarfs—short men.
These are the attendants who, without being commanded, respond with *"Yes, yes"* (wúwú), and without being ordered, reply with *"Yes, sir"* (nuònuò). They anticipate the sovereign’s intent, observing his expressions and complexion to foresee his will.
They advance and retreat together, respond and answer together—if the sovereign wishes to promote someone, they all promote him; if he wishes to demote someone, they all demote him. When commanded, they all reply; when questioned, they all answer.
Their words follow a single track, subtly shifting the sovereign’s heart.
As ministers, they curry favor with gold, jade, and luxuries, and in public, they commit illegal acts to corrupt the sovereign—this is called *standing at the side*.

Once treacherous ministers have used gold and jade to curry favor with the sovereign’s close attendants, and in public have committed illegal acts to gradually corrupt him, the sovereign, once accustomed to vice, will have his position made vulnerable to seizure.

**2. Father and Elder Brother**
What is meant by *father and elder brother*?
It refers to concubines’ sons and the sovereign’s beloved children—those the sovereign holds dear—and the great ministers and court officials with whom the sovereign consults on affairs. These are all those who exert their strength and offer counsel, whose words the sovereign is bound to heed.
As ministers, they curry favor with the concubines’ sons and side chambers through music, children, and concubines, and they win over great ministers and court officials with flattery and persuasive speech. They arrange matters of state, and when their schemes succeed, they are rewarded with titles and increased emoluments, thus encouraging their hearts to betray the sovereign—this is called *father and elder brother*.
*Winning over* means to control their loyalty.
It means that ministers seek to sway the hearts of great ministers through flattery and reputation, then arrange matters of state with the sovereign. Once their schemes succeed, the great ministers are rewarded with titles and emoluments, using this to encourage their disloyalty, causing them to offend the sovereign. When the sovereign is offended, a rift arises between ruler and minister, and treacherous ministers can then act upon their schemes.

**3. Nurturing Calamity**
What is meant by *nurturing calamity*?
It refers to the sovereign’s delight in beautiful palaces, ponds, and gardens, and his fondness for adorning his children, dogs, and horses to amuse his heart—these are the sovereign’s calamities.
As ministers, they exhaust the people’s strength to beautify palaces, ponds, and gardens, and impose heavy taxes to adorn children, dogs, and horses, thus amusing the sovereign while clouding his judgment. They comply with his desires while secretly amassing private gains—this is called *nurturing calamity*.

**4. Stirring the People**
What is meant by *stirring the people*?
It refers to ministers dispersing public funds to please the people, practicing minor acts of kindness to win over the common folk, so that both the court and the marketplace praise them, thereby deceiving the sovereign. When ministers distribute their favors, the sovereign’s benevolence does not reach the people below—hence, it is said to *deceive the sovereign*—and they achieve what they desire. This is called *stirring the people*.

**5. Circulating Persuasion**
What is meant by *circulating persuasion*?
The sovereign, by nature, restricts speech and discussion, rarely listening to debates. He is easily swayed by eloquent arguments.
The royal court is separated by nine layers of gates, and the worthy and talented rarely gain access—thus, speech and debate are restricted.
As ministers, they seek out the eloquent scholars of the feudal states and nurture those skilled in persuasion within the realm, instructing them to speak privately on their behalf. They craft clever, persuasive words and smooth, flowing phrases—those whose speech is clever and pleasing, as if effortlessly flowing and executable.
They demonstrate advantages and threats, instilling fear with potential harm, and employ empty, deceptive rhetoric to corrupt the sovereign, weaving together false, insubstantial words. This is called *circulating persuasion*.

**6. Displaying Might**
What is meant by *displaying might*?
The ruler relies on his ministers and the people as his source of strength. What the ministers and people approve, the ruler approves; what they disapprove, the ruler disapproves.
As ministers, they gather armed retainers and nurture those who would die for them, making their power manifest. They make it clear that those who serve them will prosper, while those who do not will perish, thus terrifying the ministers and people to advance their private schemes—this is called *displaying might*.

**7. The Four Quarters**
What is meant by *the four quarters*?
The ruler of a small state must serve a great power, and a weak army must fear a strong one. Whatever the great power demands, the small state must comply; wherever the strong army advances, the weak must submit.
As ministers, they impose heavy taxes, exhaust the treasury, and weaken the state to serve the great power, using its threats to coerce the sovereign. In extreme cases, they raise armies to gather at the borders and extort internally; in milder cases, they repeatedly send envoys to intimidate the sovereign, causing him to fear—this is called *the four quarters*.

In all these eight methods, ministers use them to achieve their treachery, and rulers are thus obstructed, coerced, and lose what they possess. They must be vigilant against them.

**The Wise Ruler’s Precautions**
Within the palace, the ruler should enjoy their beauty but not act on their entreaties, nor allow private petitions—this prevents the first treachery of *sharing the bed*.
Regarding his attendants, he must hold them strictly accountable for their words, not allowing them to add embellishments—this prevents the second treachery of *standing at the side*.
Toward his fatherly ministers and great officials, when he listens to their counsel, he must ensure they are held responsible later—if correct, they are rewarded; if incorrect, they are punished. He must not allow reckless suggestions—this prevents the third treachery of *father and elder brother*.
Regarding entertainment and luxuries, he must ensure their sources are known—he must not allow them to be arbitrarily promoted or demoted, and the ministers must suspect his intentions—this prevents the fourth treachery of *nurturing calamity*.
*Suspect* means to measure—he must not allow them to arbitrarily advance or retreat according to his whims.
In matters of virtue and benevolence, he must open the granaries, distribute grain from the stores, and amass it like a mound. Whatever benefits the people must come from the ruler, not from private ministers—this prevents the fifth treachery of *stirring the people*.
In debates and discussions, those who praise should be commended, and those who criticize should be censured. Their abilities must be verified, and their faults examined in detail.

The ruler must not allow ministers to speak on behalf of one another. This prevents the sixth treachery of *spreading deceit*.

In matters of those who possess courage and strength, military achievements must never exceed their rewards, and private brawls must never be pardoned. Private brawls refer to those who rely on their strength to engage in private fights with others in their domain.

He must not allow ministers to engage in private financial dealings. This prevents the seventh treachery of *exerting coercive power*, whereby private financial dealings are not permitted among those of courageous strength.

In dealing with the requests of feudal lords, he must grant those that follow the law, and reject those that do not. This prevents the eighth treachery of *encouraging external schemes*.

So-called *lost sovereignty* does not mean the loss of one’s territory, but rather that even if one retains it, it is not truly one’s own. A ruler may still possess his territory, yet it is not truly his own if his ministers hold authority over it. If ministers rely on external influence to control internal affairs, then the ruler’s sovereignty is lost. When ministers use external influence to govern internally while the ruler cannot assert his own power—even lifting a finger—then the ruler’s sovereignty is certainly lost.

To heed the pleas of great states, it is for the sake of rescuing the endangered. Yet the danger is more urgent when he does not heed them. If he heeds the great states, their demands will be insatiable; they will monopolize every matter, and even then, their ambitions will not be satisfied. If he does not comply with their requests, they will find pretexts to attack. Thus, it is better to refuse them, for the peril is more imminent when he does not heed them.

Hence, he does not heed them. #20 Therefore, when ministers know that he refuses, they will not seek alliances with feudal lords. The ministers’ external dealings depend on the ruler’s willingness to listen to them, hoping to establish connections. Now that the ruler does not comply with their requests, their desire for external alliances will subside. When feudal lords know that he does not listen to their ministers, they will not accept those ministers’ false accusations. #21

Feudal lords understand that if he refuses to employ their ministers, he will not be swayed by their slanderous words to deceive and harm their own rulers.

A wise ruler establishes official ranks and rewards to encourage the virtuous and those who have achieved merit. Thus it is said: The virtuous and capable should be placed in high offices and granted substantial rewards; those with great achievements should receive honored titles and heavy compensation. When appointing officials, their abilities must be assessed; when bestowing rewards, their merits must be recognized.

Therefore, the virtuous do not falsely claim ability to serve their ruler, and those with merit take joy in advancing their accomplishments, ensuring that achievements are completed and merits established.

Now, however, it is not so: The ruler does not distinguish between the virtuous and the unworthy, nor does he properly evaluate those with merit. Instead, he relies on the influence of feudal lords, who, due to their power and status, have the authority to recommend others. The ruler then employs these recommendations.

He listens to the petitions of his attendants, allowing fathers, elder brothers, and senior ministers to request ranks and rewards from above, only to sell them below for personal gain and to cultivate private factions. Thus, those with wealth buy their way into high office, and those with connections to attendants seek influence through petitions to gain prominence.

Meritorious ministers are not evaluated, and promotions or demotions in official ranks are misguided. Therefore, officials neglect their duties while pursuing external alliances, abandoning their responsibilities for personal financial gain.

Because of this, the virtuous grow slack and unmotivated, while those with merit abandon their pursuits and simplify their efforts—this is the wind that brings about the fall of a state. *Abandon* means to ruin, or in some texts, to fall.

**The Ten Faults:**

1. To practice petty loyalty is to undermine true loyalty.
2. To seek small gains is to destroy great benefits.
3. To act arrogantly, relying on self and disregarding propriety before the feudal lords, is to court self-destruction.
4. To neglect governance while indulging in music and the five tones is to invite personal ruin.
5. To be greedy, stubborn, and covetous of profit is the root of national annihilation and self-destruction.
6. To indulge in women and music while neglecting state affairs is to invite national ruin.
7. To abandon one’s inner realm for distant travels while ignoring advisors is to court personal peril.
8. To persist in error while ignoring loyal ministers and acting solely on one’s own will is the beginning of losing one’s high reputation and becoming a laughingstock.
9. To overreach internally without measuring one’s strength and rely externally on the feudal lords is to invite the weakening of one’s state.
10. To be without propriety in a small state and disregard advisors is to invite the extinction of one’s lineage.

**What is meant by petty loyalty?**
In the past, King Gong of Chu fought against Duke Li of Jin at Yiling. The Chu army was defeated, and King Gong was wounded in the eye. During the fierce battle, the marshal Zi Fan was thirsty and asked for drink. Jian Guyang presented him with a cup of wine. Zi Fan said, *"Ah, retreat! This is wine."* Guyang replied, *"It is not wine."* Zi Fan took it and drank. Zi Fan was a man who loved wine and found it sweet, unable to resist it, and thus became drunk. After the battle ended, King Gong wished to fight again and sent someone to summon Marshal Zi Fan, but Zi Fan declined due to illness. King Gong personally went to his tent, smelled the wine, and returned, saying: *"Today’s battle, I was personally wounded. My reliance was on the marshal, yet the marshal is so drunk—this is abandoning the Chu state and its people! I shall not fight again."* Thus, he withdrew his troops and beheaded Marshal Zi Fan as a great punishment. Jian Guyang offered the wine not to harm Zi Fan, but his loyal love for the king was precisely what led to his execution. Hence, it is said: *To practice petty loyalty is to undermine true loyalty.*

**What is meant by seeking small gains?**
In the past, Duke Xian of Jin wished to borrow passage through Yu to attack Guo. Xun Xi said: *"Your Highness, if you give the Chuijie jade and the Quchan chariot to Yu Gong as tribute, and ask to borrow passage, he will certainly grant it."* The duke said: *"The Chuijie jade is a treasure of my ancestors, and the Quchan chariot is my prized steed. If he accepts my gifts but refuses passage, what then?"* Xun Xi said: *"If he refuses passage, he will not dare accept my gifts. If he accepts my gifts and grants passage, then the jade is like taking it from the inner treasury and storing it outside, and the horse is like taking it from the inner stables and placing it in the outer stables. Your Highness need not worry."* The duke said: *"Very well."*

The duke then sent Xun Xi with the **Chuijie jade** and the **Quchan chariot** as tribute to the Lord of Yu, seeking passage through his lands. The Lord of Yu, covetous of these treasures, was inclined to grant it.

**Gongyiqi remonstrated**, saying: *"Do not grant it!*

*Yu’s relationship with Guo is like that of a carriage and its wheels—wheels lean upon the carriage, and the carriage leans upon the wheels. Such is the balance of power between Yu and Guo.* If you grant passage, then Guo will fall in the morning, and Yu will follow in the evening. **Do not grant it!** I beg you, do not allow this!"

The Lord of Yu did not heed him, and thus granted passage.

When Xun Xi attacked Guo, he returned three years later and launched his forces against Yu, conquering it. Then Xun Xi led the **Quchan steed** and presented the **Chuijie jade** to Duke Xian of Jin, who was pleased and said: *"The jade is still here. Though the horse’s teeth have grown longer, it remains mine."*

**Why then did Yu’s army perish and its lands diminish?** It was because the Lord of Yu **coveted small gains without foreseeing the harm**. Hence it is said: **to seek small gains is to invite the ruin of greater ones.**

**What is meant by *xīpǐ* conduct?** In the past, King Ling of Chu held the **Assembly of Shen**, where the heir-apparent of Song arrived late and was seized and imprisoned. He also **insulted** the Lord of Xu, treating him with disdain. He detained **Jing Feng of Qi**, and the **middle archer**—the middle archer was an official rank, with three tiers: upper, middle, and lower—**remonstrated**, saying: *"When gathering the feudal lords, one must not lack propriety. This is a matter of life or death for your state!*

*In the past, Jie held the **Assembly of Youzhong**, and Youzhong rebelled against him. Zhou held the **Search of Lichiang**, and the **Rong-Di** rebelled against him. Youzhong and Lichiang were both names of states.* It was due to his lack of propriety."

The duke said: *"You must consider this matter carefully."*

The lord did not heed the remonstrance, and thus carried out his own intentions. Before a year had passed, King Ling marched southward, and his ministers followed, seizing him. King Ling starved to death at the **Qianxi** (乾溪).

Hence it is said: *"To act eccentric and self-willed, to lack propriety toward the feudal lords, is to invite the calamity of self-destruction."*

What is meant by **"good of music"**?

In the past, Duke Ling of **Wei** (卫灵公) was about to go to **Jin** (晋), and when he reached the **Pu River** (濮水), he halted his chariot, released his horses, and set up lodgings to rest. At night, he heard a new drumbeat and was pleased by it. He sent someone to ask those around him, and they all reported that they did not hear it. Then he summoned **Shī Huán** (师涓) and told him: *"There is a new drumbeat, and when I asked those around me, they all said they did not hear it. Its sound seems like that of ghosts and gods. You must listen and transcribe it for me."* Shī Huán replied: *"Yes."* Then he sat quietly, strumming his zither to transcribe it.

The next morning, Shī Huán reported: *"I have obtained it, but I have not yet practiced it. May I be allowed another night to rehearse it?"* Duke Ling said: *"Yes."* Then he stayed another night to practice it, and afterward departed for Jin. Duke Ping of Jin welcomed him at the **Shìyí Platform** (施夷之台), and when the wine was flowing freely, Duke Ling rose and said: *"There is a new sound; I wish to request it to show you."* Duke Ping said: *"Good."* Then he summoned Shī Huán and ordered him to sit beside **Shī Guāng** (师旷), who took up the zither and began to play. Before the piece was finished, Shī Guāng stopped him, saying: *"This is the sound of a doomed state—it must not be completed."* Duke Ping asked: *"Where does this Dao/Way originate?"* Shī Guāng replied: *"This was composed by **Shī Yán** (师延), who created the lascivious music for **Zhòu** (纣). When King Wǔ of Zhou attacked Zhòu, Shī Yán fled eastward and, upon reaching the Pu River, cast himself into it. Thus, anyone who hears this sound must be at the Pu River."* Those who first heard this sound were destined to see their state diminished—it must not be completed.

Duke Ping said: *"I delight in music; you must allow him to finish."* Shī Huán played the piece to its end. Duke Ping asked Shī Guāng: *"What is this sound called?"* Shī Guāng replied: *"This is called **Qīngshāng** (清商)."* Duke Ping said: *"Is **Qīngshāng** not the most sorrowful?"* Shī Guāng replied: *"Not as sorrowful as **Qīngzhēng** (清征)."* Duke Ping said: *"Can **Qīngzhēng** be heard?"* Shī Guāng replied: *"No. In ancient times, those who listened to **Qīngzhēng** were all virtuous and righteous rulers. Now, my lord’s virtue is shallow—he is not worthy to hear it. If he were to listen, it would likely bring disaster."*

Duke Ping said: *"I am old, and I delight in music. I wish to try hearing it."* Shī Guāng, having no choice, took up the zither and played. After one performance, a pair of dark **xuánhé** (玄鹤) arrived in groups of eight, following the Dao/Way, and alighting at the **Lángcè’s mound** (郎册之垝). The mound was the ridge of the roof. The second performance saw them arrange themselves in ranks. The third performance saw them stretch their necks and cry, unfurl their wings and dance. The music resonated with the **gōng** (宫), **shāng** (商), and **zhēng** (征) tones, and its sound reached the heavens. Duke Ping was greatly pleased, and all who sat with him rejoiced. Duke Ping lifted his cup and stood, offering a toast to Shī Guāng, then returned to his seat and asked: *"Is there any sound more sorrowful than **Qīngzhēng**?"* Shī Guāng replied: *"Not as sorrowful as **Qīngjiǎo** (清角)."* Duke Ping said: *"Can **Qīngjiǎo** be heard?"* Shī Guāng replied: *"No. In the past, the **Huángdì** (皇帝), the Yellow Emperor, gathered ghosts and gods atop **Tàishān** (泰山), riding a chariot drawn by six **qiúlóng** (蛟龙)—**Bìfāng** (毕方) is the name of a divine creature. He harnessed them together with **bièxiá** (并辖), and **Chīyóu** (蚩尤) stood in front, while **Fēngbó** (风伯) swept forward, **Yǔshī** (雨师) sprinkled the path, and **hǔláng** (虎狼) led the way. Ghosts and gods followed behind, **téngshé** (腾蛇) coiled upon the earth, and **Fènghuáng** (凤凰) perched above. The Yellow Emperor gathered the ghosts and gods, creating **Qīngjiǎo**. Now, my lord’s virtue is shallow—he is not worthy to hear it. If he were to listen, it would likely bring ruin."*

Duke Ping said: *"I am old, and I delight in music. I wish to hear it to the end."* Shī Guāng, having no choice, played it. After one performance, dark clouds arose from the northwest. The second performance brought a great wind and heavy rain, tearing apart the curtains and screens, shattering the sacrificial vessels and grains, collapsing the verandas and tiles. Those seated fled in terror, and Duke Ping, struck with fear, hid between the verandas. The state of Jin suffered a great drought, leaving the land parched for three years. Duke Ping fell gravely ill. Hence it is said: *"If one does not diligently attend to governance but persists in indulging the **wǔyīn** (五音)—the five musical tones—then it will lead to the exhaustion of one’s own vitality."*

What is meant by **"greed and obstinacy"**?

In the past, **Zhībó Yáo** (知伯瑶)—Zhībó was a name—led the armies of **Zhào** (赵), **Hán** (韩), and **Wèi** (魏) to attack **Fàn** (范) and **Zhōngxíng** (中行), annihilating them. After resting his troops for several years, he sent someone to demand land from Hán. Duke Kāng of Hán intended not to give it. **Duàn Guī** (段规) remonstrated, saying: *"It cannot be refused."* Zhībó was a man who loved profit and was prone to sudden anger and stubbornness. If we do not give him the land when he demands it, he will surely move his troops against Hán. You must grant it to him. If we grant it, he will become accustomed—accustomed—and then he will surely develop designs to seek more elsewhere. He will then demand land from other states, and those states will likely refuse. If they refuse, Zhībó will necessarily bring his armies against them. In this way, Hán can avoid calamity and wait for the turn of events to unfold. Duke Kāng said: *"Yes."* Then he ordered his envoy to give Zhībó one **xiàn** (县) of ten thousand households.

Zhībó was pleased and sent someone to demand land from Wèi. Duke Xuān of Wèi intended not to give it. **Zhào Jiā** (赵葭) remonstrated, saying: *"He demanded land from Hán, and Hán granted it. Now he demands land from Wèi, and Wèi refuses—this is to strengthen Wèi internally while provoking Zhībó externally. If we refuse, he will surely turn his troops against Wèi. It is better to grant it."* Duke Xuān agreed, then ordered his envoy to give Zhībó one **xiàn** of ten thousand households.

Zhībó then sent someone to Zhào, demanding the lands of **Cài** (蔡) and **Gāoláng** (皋狼)—the names of cities. Duke Xiāng of Zhào refused. Zhībó then secretly made an alliance with Hán and Wèi to attack Zhào. Duke Xiāng summoned **Zhāng Mèngtán** (张孟谈) and told him: *"Zhībó is a man of deceitful schemes and hidden plots. He has sent three envoys to Hán and Wèi, and they have granted him what he demanded, while I have not. Three envoys have been sent to make secret agreements—Zhībó surely harbors other intentions."*

Duke Xiāng said, *"If Zhībó deploys his troops against me, where shall I take refuge?"*

Zhāng Mèngtán replied, *"Dòng Chǎngyú was a capable minister of a simple ruler. When he governed Jìnyáng, Yǐn Duó followed his example. Yǐn Duó was a great officer of Dòng Chǎngyú. Though his remaining teachings persist, Your Grace should only settle in Jìnyáng and await developments."*

The Duke said, *"Agreed."*

He then summoned Yánlíng Shēng and ordered him to lead the military chariots and cavalry to Jìnyáng first, while the Duke himself followed. Upon arrival, he inspected the city walls and the five departments' stores—yet the walls were unfortified, the granaries held no grain, the treasuries no coin reserves, the armories no armor or weapons, and the cities no defensive provisions.

Duke Xiāng grew fearful and called for Zhāng Mèngtán, saying, *"I have examined the city walls and the five departments' stores, yet none are adequately prepared. How shall I meet this enemy?"*

Zhāng Mèngtán replied, *"I have heard that the governance of the Sage is such that provisions are entrusted to the people, not stored in treasuries and armories. Their efforts lie in cultivating the teachings, not in fortifying walls. Your Grace should issue an order: let the people bring forward three years' worth of food. Those with surplus grain should deposit it in the granaries, and those with extra provisions should store them in the treasuries. Let those with spare labor—those not engaged in essential duties—be tasked with repairing the city walls."*

*"Spare labor"* (奇) is pronounced *jī* and refers to idle men.

That evening, the Duke issued his decree. By the next day, the granaries could not contain all the grain, the treasuries overflowed with coin, and the armories received no armor or weapons. Within five days, the city walls were repaired and the defenses fully prepared.

The Duke summoned Zhāng Mèngtán and asked, *"The city walls are now fortified, the defenses complete, grain and coin abundant, and weapons in excess. Yet I still lack arrows. How shall I manage?"*

Zhāng Mèngtán replied, *"I have heard that when Dòng Chǎngyú governed Jìnyáng, the walls of the palace grounds were all reinforced with reeds, rush mats, and *luóchǔ* (楛楚)—a type of wickerwork—reaching heights of up to ten feet. Your Grace should use these materials."* Upon deployment, their strength proved such that even the might of *jūn gān* (菌干)—a term for hardened soldiers—could not surpass it.

The Duke said, *"My arrows are now sufficient. But what of bronze?"*

Zhāng Mèngtán replied, *"I have heard that when Dòng Chǎngyú governed Jìnyáng, he ordered the palace halls to be supported with pillars made of refined copper. Your Grace should use these materials."* Upon deployment, there was ample bronze.

With orders finalized and defenses in place, the armies of the three states arrived. They ascended Jìnyáng’s walls and engaged in battle, yet could not break through for three months. They then relaxed their forces and surrounded the city, diverting the waters of Jìnyáng to flood it. After three years of siege, the inhabitants lived in hollowed-out dwellings, hung pots to cook, and their supplies and strength nearly exhausted, the officers and soldiers weakened and sickened.

Duke Xiāng told Zhāng Mèngtán, *"Our food is scarce, our resources depleted, and our officers and soldiers are feeble and ill. I fear we cannot hold out. If we surrender the city, to which state should we yield?"*

Zhāng Mèngtán replied, *"I have heard this: the lost cannot be recovered, the perilous cannot be secured. Then there is no need to value cleverness. Your Grace has missed this opportunity. I beg to attempt a covert mission—slip out and meet with the rulers of Hán and Wèi."*

Zhāng Mèngtán met with the rulers of Hán and Wèi and said, *"I have heard: when the lips perish, the teeth grow cold. Now Zhībó leads the two states in attacking Zhào; Zhào is sure to fall. If Zhào falls, the two states will be next."*

The rulers of Hán and Wèi said, *"We know this to be true. Still, Zhībó is a man of coarse nature and little affection—if we plot against him and he discovers it, disaster will surely befall us. What should we do?"*

Zhāng Mèngtán replied, *"The plan is spoken by the two states and heard only by me—no one else knows."* The rulers of Hán and Wèi then secretly agreed with Zhāng Mèngtán to betray Zhībó and set a date.

That night, they sent Zhāng Mèngtán back into Jìnyáng to report the betrayal to Duke Xiāng. The Duke welcomed him with two bows, filled with both dread and joy. The rulers of Hán and Wèi entrusted the agreement to Zhāng Mèngtán, then publicly confronted Zhībó at the gate. They encountered Zhìguò outside the gate, who noticed their unusual expressions and entered to see Zhībó, saying, *"The two states appear to have some change in mind."* Zhībó asked, *"How so?"* Zhìguò’s manner was stiff, and his demeanor arrogant—unlike his usual bearing. Zhībó said, *"You are not acting as you usually do; it is best to preempt them."*

Zhībó said, *"I have made a solemn agreement with the two lords: after breaking Zhào, we shall divide its lands into three. My reason for treating them with such favor is to ensure they will not deceive me. Their armies have been stationed at Jìnyáng for three years, and now they are on the verge of seizing their advantage—why would they suddenly harbor other intentions? This must not be so. My lord, set your mind at ease and do not speak of it."*

The next morning, the two lords again came to pay their respects and departed. They encountered Zhìguò at the gate once more. Zhìguò entered and reported, *"My lord, did you inform the two lords of my words?"*

Zhībó asked, *"How do you know?"*

Zhìguò replied, *"Today, when the two lords came to pay their respects, their expressions shifted upon seeing me. Their gaze lingered on me—this must signal a change. My lord, you should kill them."*

Zhībó said, *"Enough, do not speak of this again."*

Zhìguò insisted, *"It cannot be ignored. You must kill them. If you cannot kill them, then at least draw closer to them."*

Zhībó asked, *"How shall I draw closer?"*

Zhìguò answered, *"In Wèi, the strategist was Zhào Gě, and in Hàn, it was Duàn Guī. Both were capable of swaying their lords’ decisions. My lord, you made an agreement with the two lords: after conquering Zhào, each would be granted a county of ten thousand households. If you do this, their intentions will not stray."*

Zhībó said, *"To break Zhào and divide its lands into three, and then grant each of them a county of ten thousand households—this would leave me with too little. It cannot be done."*

Seeing that Zhībó would not listen, Zhìguò left and changed his family name to Fǔ.

On the appointed night, the Zhào clan killed the officers overseeing the dikes and released the waters to flood Zhībó’s army. As the soldiers struggled to save themselves, Hàn and Wèi attacked from the flanks, while Duke Xiāng led his troops to strike from the front. The Zhī army was utterly defeated, and Zhībó was captured.

With Zhībó dead and his army destroyed, his state was divided among the three. The world mocked them.

Thus it is said: *Greed, stubbornness, and love of gain are the roots of ruined states and self-destruction.*

What of indulging in music and women? In ancient times, the Róng king sent Yóuyú to spy on Qín. The Duke of Mù asked him, *"I have heard of the Way but have never seen it with my own eyes. I wish to know what constants allow wise rulers to gain or lose their states."*

Yóuyú replied, *"I have heard it said that frugality brings gain, while extravagance leads to loss."*

The Duke of Mù said, *"I did not ask you this out of disrespect, but you answer with frugality—why?"*

Yóuyú said, *"I have heard that in ancient times, when Yáo ruled the world, he ate from earthen bowls and drank from earthen cups. Yet his dominion stretched from Jiāozhì in the south to Yōudōu in the north, and from where the sun rose to where it set—none dared defy him. When Yáo abdicated in favor of Shùn, Shùn made his food vessels from chopped mountain wood, smoothing the axe marks with files and sandpaper. The grain was sifted, and the vessels lacquered and painted. The nobles found this excessive, and thirteen states refused to submit. When Shùn passed the throne to Yǔ, Yǔ made sacrificial vessels with blackened exteriors and red-painted interiors. He used patterned silk as mats and rush mats for seating, with carved designs. His cups and ritual vessels were adorned with inlays. This was even more extravagant, and thirty-three states refused to submit. When the Xià dynasty fell and the Shāng took power, they made grand chariots with nine tassels, carved their food vessels, and engraved their cups. Their walls were whitewashed, their mats and seats intricately patterned—this was yet more extravagance, and fifty-three states refused to submit. Though all knew of these refinements, those who wished to submit grew fewer. Thus I say: frugality is the true Way."*

Yóuyú departed, and the Duke called for the court historian Liào and said, *"I have heard that neighboring states may harbor sages, who are a danger to their enemies. Yóuyú is a sage, and I fear him. What shall I do?"*

The historian Liào said, *"I have heard that the Róng king’s lands are remote and his people have never heard the music of the Central Plains. My lord, send him women and music to disrupt his governance. Then, when you request an audience through Yóuyú, he will be distracted from advising the king. Once the king and his minister grow estranged, we can act."*

The Duke agreed. He sent Liào with eight female musicians to the Róng king and, through Yóuyú, requested an audience. The Róng king accepted.

When he saw the women, he delighted in them, set out wine, and drank daily, listening to music all year. Cattle and horses perished from neglect. Yóuyú returned and advised the king, but the king would not listen.

Yóuyú then left for Qín, where the Duke of Mù welcomed him and appointed him to the highest rank. He questioned him about military strength and terrain, then used the information to launch an attack, conquering twelve states and expanding his lands by a thousand *lǐ*.

Thus it is said: *To indulge in music and women, neglecting state affairs, is the ruin of a kingdom.*

What of abandoning one’s state to wander afar? In ancient times, Tián Chéngzǐ traveled by the sea and took pleasure in it. He issued an order to his nobles: *"Let any who speak of returning be put to death."* Yán Zhàojù said, *"My lord, you take pleasure in the sea, but what if someone plots against the state? Even if you enjoy it, how long can you hold it?"*

Tián Chéngzǐ said, *"I have decreed that those who speak of returning shall die. Now you defy my order. Draw your sword and strike me!"*

Yán Zhàojù said, *"In ancient times, Jié killed Guān Lóngróng, and Zhòu killed Wángzǐ Bǐgān. Even if you kill me, it would only be the third such act. My words are for the state, not for myself."* He extended his neck and stepped forward.

The lord then put down his sword, hurried back to his carriage, and returned. Within three days, he heard that his people were plotting against him.

Tián Chéngzǐ’s ability to seize Améi was due to Yán Zhàojù’s loyalty.

Thus it is said: *To abandon one’s state and wander far is the path to self-destruction.*

What then of failing to heed the counsel of loyal ministers? In days of old, Duke Huan of Qi united the feudal lords nine times, rectified the world, and became the foremost of the Five Hegemons, with Guan Zhong as his chief advisor.

When Guan Zhong grew old and could no longer attend to affairs, he retired to his home. Duke Huan visited him and asked, *"Master Zhong, while you rest at home, if something were to happen to you—where would governance be transferred?"*

Guan Zhong replied, *"I am old and cannot be questioned further. Still, I have heard this: no one knows their ministers as the ruler does, nor their children as a father does. Your Majesty may test this by deciding in your heart."*

The duke asked, *"What of Bao Shuya?"*

Guan Zhong said, *"Unsuitable. Bao Shuya is stubborn and defiant, standing in opposition above. His rigidity would oppress the people, his obstinacy would alienate their hearts, and his defiance would render him useless to those below. His mind is incomplete—he is no fit assistant for a hegemon."*

The duke asked, *"Then what of Zhu Tuo?"*

Guan Zhong said, *"Unsuitable. All men cherish their own lives, yet you indulge in licentiousness and favor the inner quarters. Zhu Tuo would exploit this to undermine your authority. If he does not love himself, how could he love you?"*

The duke asked, *"Then what of Prince Kaifang of Wei?"*

Guan Zhong said, *"Unsuitable. Between Qi and Wei, the journey takes no more than ten days, yet Kaifang served his lord so devotedly that for fifteen years he did not return to see his parents. This is unnatural. If he does not cherish his own kin, how could he cherish you?"*

The duke asked, *"Then what of Yi Ya?"*

Guan Zhong said, *"Unsuitable. Yi Ya prepared flavors for your lordship, and there was one thing you had never tasted—human flesh. Yi Ya boiled his own son’s head and presented it to you, as you well know. No man fails to love his child, yet Yi Ya boiled his own son to serve you as a dish. If he does not love his son, how could he love you?"*

The duke asked, *"Then who is suitable?"*

Guan Zhong said, *"Xí Péng is worthy."*

As for his character, Xí Péng was steadfast within yet modest without, desiring little yet trusting abundantly. A steadfast core made him worthy of emulation, while his outward modesty qualified him for great responsibilities. His minimal desires allowed him to govern his people with equanimity, and his abundant trust enabled him to cultivate alliances with neighboring states—such a man was the ideal minister for a ruler aspiring to hegemony. Thus, my lord, you should employ him.

The duke replied, *"Very well."*

After more than a year, Guan Zhong died, and the duke ceased to use Xí Péng, instead favoring Jiān Tuó. Tuó oversaw affairs for three years, when Duke Huan of Qi made a southern expedition to Tángfú. Jiān Tuó’s attendant, along with Yi Ya, Prince Kaifang of Wei, and the senior ministers, staged a rebellion. The duke perished from thirst and exhaustion in the southern gate’s chamber, his body left unburied in the guardhouse for three months, until worms crawled from the door. Thus, though Duke Huan’s armies once dominated the world and he was the foremost of the Five Hegemons, he ultimately met his death at the hands of his own ministers, his illustrious name ruined, and became the laughingstock of the world. Why? It was because he failed to heed Guan Zhong’s counsel.

Thus it is said: To reject the warnings of loyal ministers and act solely on one’s own will is the beginning of losing one’s noble reputation and becoming an object of ridicule.

What does it mean to *"fail to assess one’s own strength internally"*? In days past, when Qin attacked Yìyáng, the state of Hàn was in dire straits. Gōng Zhòng Péng advised the Hàn ruler: *"Our alliance cannot be relied upon—why not instead use Zhāng Yí to negotiate peace with Qin?"* He proposed bribing Qin with a named city and then, in league with Qin, launching a southern assault on Chu. This would resolve our crisis with Qin while harming our relations with Chu. Qin, having harmed our ties with Chu, the ruler said, *"Excellent."* And so, with haste and preparation, he readied for his mission to the west to negotiate with Qin.

When King Chu heard of this, he grew fearful and summoned Chén Zhěn, saying: *"Gōng Zhòng Péng of Hàn is about to negotiate with Qin in the west. What should we do now?"*

Chén Zhěn replied: *"If Qin gains one of Hàn’s cities and its trained soldiers, Qin and Hàn will unite to face Chu—this is what the Qin king has long sought in his ancestral temples. The harm this will bring to Chu is certain. My lord, you must quickly dispatch trusted envoys, send many chariots, and offer rich tribute to Hàn, saying: Though my humble state is small, its soldiers are fully mobilized. I pray that the great state of Hàn will reassure Qin of our good intentions. Let them also request that your envoys enter our borders to inspect our mustered troops."* (Here, *"信"* means *"reassurance"* or *"pledge"*—a formal affirmation.)

Hàn’s envoy arrived in Chu, and King Chu, deploying chariots and cavalry along the lower road, told the envoy: *"Report to your lord that the soldiers of my humble state are now entering your borders."*

The envoy returned and informed the Hàn ruler, who was overjoyed. He halted Gōng Zhòng Péng, but Péng said: *"This cannot be done. Qin has deceived us with truth, while Chu has rescued us with empty words. To heed Chu’s false assurances and disregard Qin’s genuine threat is to endanger our state’s foundation."*

The Hàn ruler would not listen, and Gōng Zhòng Péng, enraged, returned home and refused to attend court for ten days. Yìyáng’s siege grew more desperate, and the Hàn ruler ordered his envoys to hurry reinforcements from Chu. Though messengers came and went in endless procession, no troops arrived. Yìyáng fell, and Hàn became the laughingstock of the other states.

Thus it is said: To fail to assess one’s own strength internally while relying externally on other states is to invite the ruin of one’s nation.

What does it mean to *"be small in state yet lack propriety"*? In days past, when the Jin prince Zhòng ěr fled into exile and passed through Cáo, the ruler of Cáo stripped his upper garment and lower skirt to observe him. Lí Négǔi and Shū Zhān attended before him. Shū Zhān said to the Cáo ruler: *"I observe that this Jin prince is no ordinary man. You have treated him without proper respect. If he ever returns to power and raises an army, he will surely harm Cáo. You should kill him now."*

The Cáo ruler refused. Lí Négǔi returned home in gloom, and his wife asked: *"You have come from court with a sorrowful expression—what has happened?"*

Négǔi replied: *"I have heard that misfortune, once unleashed, does not spare even those who brought it. The ruler’s blessings may not reach me, but his calamities will."*

Today, our lord summoned the Jin prince, treating him without proper respect, and I stood before him—this is why I am sorrowful."

His wife said: *"I observe that the Jin prince is destined to be a lord of ten thousand chariots, and his attendants are ministers of equal rank. Though he is now in exile and passing through Cáo, if Cáo treats him without respect, when he returns to power, he will surely punish the disrespectful—Cáo will be the first to suffer. Why do you not prepare for this in advance?"*

Négǔi replied: *"Very well."*

The Jin prince stored yellow gold in a jar, filled it with food, and placed a jade tablet on top, then sent it to the prince at night by a messenger.

The prince, upon seeing the envoy, bowed twice and accepted the food, but declined the jade tablet.

The prince then traveled from Cáo into Chu, and from Chu into Qin.

After three years in Qin, Duke Mù of Qin summoned his ministers and deliberated: *"In the past, Duke Xiàn of Jìn had dealings with me, and this was known to all the feudal lords. Unfortunately, Duke Xiàn died, separated from his ministers, and has been gone for ten years now. His successor is unworthy, and I fear his ancestral temple will fall into ruin and his altars will no longer receive sacrifices. If this remains uncorrected, it would violate the principles of forming alliances with others. I intend to support Zhòng ěr and restore him to the throne of Jìn—what do you think?"*

All the ministers agreed it was a good idea.

The duke then raised an army: five hundred chariots of armored troops, two thousand elite cavalry—*chóu*, meaning horses of equal rank, all finely trained—five thousand infantry. With this force, he escorted Zhòng ěr back to Jìn and installed him as its ruler.

After three years on the throne, Zhòng ěr led his armies and attacked Cáo. He then sent word to the lord of Cáo: *"Hang Xuán Shū outside the city, and I will execute him as a great example."*

He also sent word to Lí Néngji: *"My troops are approaching the city walls. I know you will not disobey—though I know you dare not disobey the lord, I mean that this is not your true intention. I will mark your gate, and I command my army not to harm it."* The people of Cáo, hearing this, gathered their families and took refuge in Lí Néngji’s gate—over seven hundred households in total.

This was the power of ritual propriety.

Thus, though Cáo was a small state, caught between Jìn and Chu, its lord’s peril was as fragile as stacked eggs. Yet he ruled it with disrespect, and this was why his dynasty was cut short.

Hence it is said: *"A small state without ritual propriety, and without heeding the advice of ministers, will face the fate of being cut off from the world."*

(End of *Han Feizi*, Volume Three, #1—here the character *gōng* ["lord"] is omitted; restored according to the collation of Chén Qíyóu.)

#2 The character **"命"** is meaningless; the *Yū Píng* and *Líng Yáo* editions read **"会"**, hence corrected.

#3 The character **"曰"** is meaningless; corrected to **"已"** following Chén Qíyóu’s annotation.

#4 The *Yū Píng* and *Líng Yáo* editions read **"期"** for **"其"**, hence corrected.

#5 The original reads **"祖"**, which should be **"袒"**; corrected following Chén Qíyóu’s annotation.

---
**Han Feizi, Volume Four: *Naigu* (孤愤) #11**

The scholar of Dao and method, having no faction or allies, is alone and isolated. Thus, his talents and abilities are never fully recognized.

When Biān Shēng wailed aloud while clutching his jade, and Lord Hàn Yóu lay in bed plotting while seething with resentment—

The scholar of Dao and method must possess far-reaching insight and keen discernment. Without such discernment, he cannot expose hidden motives.

The scholar of law and rectitude must be resolute and upright. Without such strength, he cannot correct deceit.

A minister who follows orders and governs according to law does not belong to the category of **"heavy men"**—those who wield influence.

**"Heavy men"** are those who act without orders, undermine law for private gain, drain the state for their families, and yet, through sheer force, compel their lord to obey them. This is what makes them **"heavy."**

When they act arbitrarily, violating principle, their very strength can bend the lord to their will—what then of the rest?

This is what **"heavy men"** are called—those whom nobles and commoners alike hold in high regard.

The scholar of Dao and method, with his keen insight and discernment, is entrusted with authority and can expose the hidden schemes of **"heavy men."**

The scholar of law and rectitude, with his resolute strength, is entrusted with authority and can rectify the deceitful actions of **"heavy men."**

Thus, when the scholar of Dao and method is in use, the **"heavy men"** must necessarily be kept in check—meaning they will surely be removed.

This makes the scholar of Dao and method and the **"heavy men"** irreconcilable enemies—one cannot exist while the other thrives.

Since they cannot coexist, the one that survives will be their enemy.

When **"heavy men"** monopolize key positions, both the outer and inner officials will serve them.

**"Outer"** refers to the hundred officials; **"inner"** refers to the lord’s attendants. All serve the **"heavy men."**

Thus, when the feudal lords do not rely on them, their affairs are not attended to, and rival states will take up their cause.

Neighboring feudal lords, when seeking official business, if they do not go through the **"heavy men,"** their requests will not be granted—hence, the **"heavy men"** face accusations from rival states.

If the hundred officials do not rely on them, their careers stagnate, and the ministers will oppose them.

If the **Lángzhōng** (郎中) do not rely on them, they cannot gain access to the lord, and thus the attendants will conceal their misdeeds.

The **Lángzhōng** are those who reside in the central hall as the lord’s attendants.

Once they rely on the **"heavy men"** to gain access to the lord, the attendants will conceal their wrongdoing for them.

The **scholars** not relying on them leads to meager stipends and humble treatment, hence the scholars engage in **talking**—**talking** refers to praising the **"heavy men"** on their behalf.

These four assistants are how corrupt ministers adorn themselves. The **"heavy men"** cannot remain loyal to the lord while advancing their enemies; those whom the **"heavy men"** hold enmity toward are the **scholars of law and technique**. The lord cannot transcend these four assistants to discern his ministers—these ministers are also the **scholars of law and technique**. Thus, the more the lord declines, the more the great ministers grow in power.

In general, those in the **current path** toward the lord seek not to be untrusted or unloved, and they are also accustomed to old ways. The **"heavy men"** who gain the lord’s trust and affection are many, and since they have long held power, they now resent the old acquaintances. If they align with the lord’s inclinations in matters of liking and disliking, it is indeed how they advance themselves. Their offices and ranks are noble and powerful, their factions numerous, and the entire state pleads their cause—**pleading** means **persuading**. The **"heavy men’s"** actions always conform to the lord’s heart, harmonizing with his likes and dislikes. Those who have already advanced themselves hold high rank and many allies, and when they have matters to attend to, the whole state pleads their cause, then the lord, lacking virtue, punishes them.

Thus, the **scholars of law and technique** who seek to approach the lord must not rely on trusted kin or the favor of old acquaintances. They must instead use their **law and technique** to correct the lord’s biased and perverse inclinations—this is contrary to the lord’s nature. Placed in lowly and humble positions, without factions or allies, how could they compete with those who are near and trusted? The **logic** does not favor them.

The state contends with those who are new and untested against those who are familiar and established—**its logic does not favor them**.

Those who oppose the lord’s intentions and contend with his preferences are at a disadvantage, for the **heavy men** and the lord share the same inclinations—**its logic does not favor them**.

Those who pit **light and humble** against **noble and weighty**—**its logic does not favor them**.

Those who face **one voice** against **the whole realm**—the **heavy men** and the realm are allies as one faction—**its logic does not favor them**.

The **scholars of law and technique**, wielding five **unfavorable logics**, are yet never granted audience. Though their appointed time has come, they still cannot see the lord.

The **men of the moment** exploit five **advantages**, yet they alone speak before him every dawn and dusk.

Since the **scholars of law and technique** cannot gain access, the **men of the moment** alone plead their cause and cry injustice.

Thus, how can the **scholars of law and technique** find a path to advance, or when can the lord attain enlightenment?

If the **scholars of law and technique** cannot advance, then how can the lord hope to awaken?

If **advantages are inherently unequal** and **positions cannot coexist**, how can the **scholars of law and technique** avoid peril?

The **scholars of law and technique**, already burdened by **unfavorable logics** and further hindered by the **heavy men’s** dominance, must inevitably face danger and fall into calamity.

Those who can be **accused of crimes** are punished by public law. If the **scholars of law and technique** have faults that can be twisted into charges, the **heavy men** will seize upon them to condemn and execute them.

Those who cannot be **implicated by law** are hunted down with private blades. If they are without fault that can be twisted, then **righteous warriors** are summoned to strike them down, to exhaust their lives.

Those who **illuminate law and technique** yet **defy the lord above** will not perish by official execution—**they must die by private blades**.

Factions and cliques **weaken the lord above** while **flattering speech** **serves private gain**—such men will surely be trusted by the **heavy men**.

Thus, those who can **claim merit and exploit** will be **elevated by rank and honor**. If they have merit that the **heavy men** can borrow for their own use, then **rank and honor will be bestowed upon them**.

Those who cannot **be lent prestige** will be **weighted by external influence**. Even if they lack merit, they can be made to appear formidable by those near the lord’s power.

Hence, those who **weaken the lord above** and **turn toward private doors** will not shine in **rank and honor**—they must instead **grow heavy with external influence**.

*Climb* means *to approach*.

Now, if the lord **acts without consultation** and **executes without verification**, he does so against the **scholars of law and technique**, knowing neither truth nor falsehood before passing judgment.

He does not **wait to see merit** before granting rank and favor—**the heavy men** advance, and even without merit, they are first awarded rank and favor.

Thus, how can the **scholars of law and technique** hope to **avoid death** while advancing their teachings, or how can the **crafty ministers** **withdraw from gain** while seizing their opportunities?

Therefore, the lord above grows ever **more debased**, while private doors grow ever **more exalted**.

Though **Yue** may be rich in lands and strong in arms, the lords of the **Central States** all recognize it as **no benefit to themselves**, saying: *"It is not within my power to control."*

Yue is a **foreign state**, even an **enemy state**.

Now, though a state may have vast territory and many people, if the lord is **obstructed and deceived**, and the great ministers **wield sole authority**, then that state is **like Yue**.

The great ministers, **holding the state in thrall**, always harbor **plots against the lord**—they turn their own country into a **foreign state**, hence it is said: *"This state is like Yue."*

The wise do not resemble Yue, yet the unwise do not differ from their own state—**they fail to discern their likeness**.

Even if ministers **wield sole authority**, transforming the state into Yue, the lord does not **recognize his own state as identical to Yue**—this is because he **does not perceive their similarity**.

The reason the lord is said to have **lost** **Qi** is not that its lands and cities were lost—**it was because the Lü clan could not control it**, while the **Tian clan exploited it**.

The reason the lord is said to have **lost** **Jin** is not that its lands and cities were lost—**it was because the Ji clan could not control it**, while the **Six Ministers seized authority**.

Now, if the great ministers **grasp the reins** and **rule alone**, yet the lord **does not know how to reclaim** them, this is the lord’s **failure to discern**.

Not knowing how to **reclaim the reins** while they remain in the ministers’ hands, the lord allows them to **rule alone**—this is the lord’s **lack of clarity**.

Now, speaking of **Qin**.

Those who **share the same affliction as the dead** cannot be **revived**; those who **share the same fate as a lost state** cannot be **preserved**.

If one **follows the footsteps of Qi and Jin**, yet desires the state to **remain secure and intact**, this is impossible.

*Follow* means *to emulate*.

In general, the **difficulty of implementing law and technique** is not confined to **great states**—even **small states** face the same.

The lord’s attendants need not be **wise**; if the lord **deems a man wise** and **listens to him**, then **consulting him with his own attendants**, it is as if **a fool were judging wisdom**.

The lord’s attendants need not be **virtuous**; if the lord **deems a man virtuous** and **honors him**, then **discussing his conduct with his own attendants**, it is as if **the unworthy were evaluating virtue**.

The wise **determine policies** before the foolish, and the virtuous **set the course** before the unworthy—then the **virtuous and wise** will **be shamed**, and the lord’s **judgments will be perverse**.

Those ministers who **seek office** will **refine their conduct** and **strengthen their integrity** to **fortify their persons**. *Refined conduct* refers to those who **cultivate their own virtue**.

The **wise** will **advance through eloquence and debate**.

The **wise** will not **accept bribes**—since they **cultivate virtue**, they **do not use wealth** to **curry favor**.

Relying on their **refined integrity**, yet **still unable to bend the law** for their purposes—since they **are refined**, they **cannot pervert the law**.

The **wise** do not **repeat their arguments**—*this seems to omit text*.

Thus, the **virtuous and wise** will **not serve the lord’s attendants**, nor **hear their petitions**.

The lord’s attendants **do not act like the Earl of Bi**—they **cannot find what they seek**, nor **receive bribes**, so the **eloquent debates** cease, and **slanderous accusations** arise.

*Eloquence* refers to the **refined integrity** of the virtuous, and *debate* refers to the **wise** in their **persuasive speech**.

The **function of governing chaos** rests with those **near and trusted**—*governing chaos* refers to the **wise** in their **eloquence and ability** to **restore order**.

「The **refined integrity** of conduct is determined by praise and blame—then the **virtuous and wise officials** will be discarded, and the **lord’s discernment** will be obstructed.

The **virtuous and wise** can **awaken the lord’s insight**—now that they are discarded and not employed, the lord’s discernment will be self-obstructed.

If **merit and achievement** are not used to **judge the virtue and wisdom** of officials, then **merit and achievement** should be used to **judge them**. *Accumulating merit* is called *achievement*.

If **collateral verification** is not used to **investigate crimes**, then **investigation of crimes** should rely on **collateral verification**. *Collateral* refers to **cross-examining witnesses**; *verification* refers to **pairing and confirming** evidence.

Yet if the lord **listens to the words** of his **attendants and trusted advisors**, then **incompetent men** will be present in the court, and **foolish and corrupt officials** will be placed in office.

Since the **attendants and trusted advisors** are all **petty men**, they **seek like-minded allies** and **echo similar voices**; thus, those they hold close are **incompetent**, and those they favor are **foolish and corrupt**. And once they have been favored and trusted, they will certainly be **employed in the court** and **appointed to offices**.

The **calamities of a great kingdom** lie in **overbearing great ministers**, and the **calamities of a lesser kingdom** lie in **excessive trust in attendants**—these are the **public afflictions** of the lord.

*Public* means **rightful**; thus, these are the **rightful afflictions** of the lord.

Moreover, when **subjects have crimes**, and when the **lord has great losses**, the **interests of the lord and subjects differ**. How does this manifest?

It is said: The lord’s **interest** lies in **tasking competent men** with office, while the **subject’s interest** lies in **remaining incompetent yet securing employment**.

The lord’s **interest** lies in **honoring those who labor** with rank and reward, while the **subject’s interest** lies in **gaining wealth and nobility without merit**.

The lord’s **interest** lies in **empowering the bold and talented**—*bold and talented men* are those with **virtue and ability**, then **employed** by the lord.

The **subject’s interest** lies in **using factional connections** to **advance private agendas**.

Thus, when **land is diminished** in the state yet **private families grow rich**, when the **sovereign is humbled** yet **great ministers grow powerful**, the lord **loses authority** while the **subjects gain dominion**, and the **lord becomes a tributary of his ministers**—*tributary* here refers to the **lord’s subservience to his ministers**, hence the **easy reversal of positions**.

The **chancellor’s office** issues **sealed appointments**—*chancellor’s office* refers to **high-ranking household officials**, and *sealed appointments* means **granting exclusive authority to appoint officials** and **issuing official symbols** of office.

This is how **subjects deceive the lord** and **facilitate their own private gain**.

*Deceive* means **to mislead**; they **fabricate schemes** to **confuse and mislead** the lord.

Thus, in the **current age**, many **powerful ministers**—those who **alter the lord’s resolve** through **deception and flattery**—are **fixed in favor**, numbering **no more than two or three out of ten**.

*Alter* refers to **changing the lord’s intentions** through **deception and flattery**; hence, **only two or three out of ten** attain such favor, so it is said **no more than two or three out of ten**.

What is the **reason for this**?

The **crimes of subjects** are **great**.

---

**臣有大罪者，其行欺主也，其罪当死亡也。**
Those who serve with grave offenses—**their deeds deceive the lord**—**their crime deserves death.**

**智士者远见，而畏於死亡，必不从重人矣。**
**Men of wisdom** perceive the distant consequences and, **fearing death**, will **never follow the powerful.**

**贤士者修廉，而羞与奸臣欺其主，必不从重人矣。**
**Virtuous men** uphold integrity and **shame to conspire with corrupt ministers to deceive their lord**—they **will never follow the powerful.**

**是当涂者之徒属，非愚而不知患者，必污而不避奸者也。**
