Thus, the **favorites of the age**—their **accomplices**—are **not fools ignorant of peril**, but **corrupt and unscrupulous**, **avoiding no evil.**

**重人所为必不轨，故智士恐与同之，廉士羞与之欺主，莫有从之游者。**
The **powerful act without restraint**; thus, **men of wisdom fear association**, and **virtuous men shame to conspire with them in deceiving the lord**—**none dare join their ranks.**

**同恶相济，故与之为徒属者必污愚之人也。**
**Those who share in vice reinforce one another**; thus, **those who follow them as accomplices must be corrupt and foolish.**

**大臣挟愚污之人，上与之欺主，下与之收利侵渔，朋党言侵夺百姓，若渔者之取鱼也。**
**Powerful ministers**, **harboring foolish and corrupt men**, **deceive the lord above** and **plunder profits below**, **colluding in factions to seize the people’s wealth**—**like fishermen taking fish.**

**比周，相与阿党为比，忠信为周也。**
**Allying as "comparable"**—**they falsely claim kinship**, **calling loyalty and trust their "comparable" allies.**

**比周者，言以阿党之人为忠信与亲也。**
**"Comparable"** means **taking flatterers as loyal and trusted kin.**

**一口，惑主败法，以乱士民，雷同是非，故曰一口。**
**"One voice"**—**they confuse the lord and undermine the law**, **disrupting the people**, **echoing false judgments**—hence, **"one voice."**

**使国家危削，主上劳辱，此大罪也。**
**They endanger the state**, **weary and humiliate the lord**—**this is a grave crime.**

**臣有大罪而主弗禁，此大失也。**
**When ministers commit grave crimes and the lord fails to restrain them**, **this is a great failing.**

**使其主有大失於上，臣有大罪於下，索国之不亡者，不可得也。**
**If the lord fails above and ministers offend below**, **seeking a state that does not perish is impossible.**

---

**说难第十二**
**The Difficulties of Persuasion (Chapter Twelve)**

**夫说者有逆顺之机，顺以招福#5，逆而制祸，失之毫厘，差之千里，以此说之所以难也。**
**Persuasion has moments of harmony and discord**; **harmony invites fortune**, **discord averts disaster**—**a misstep by a hair’s breadth can diverge by thousands of miles**—**thus, persuasion is so difficult.**

**凡说之难，非吾知之，有以说之之难也。**
**The difficulty of persuasion lies not in my knowledge**, but in **how to persuade**—**this is the true difficulty.**

**不知而说，虽忠见疑，故曰非吾知之说之难也。**
**If I speak without knowledge**, **even loyalty will be met with doubt**—**thus, the difficulty is not in my knowledge of persuasion.**

**又非吾辩之，能明吾意之难也。**
**Nor is it my eloquence**; **the difficulty lies in making my meaning clear.**

**吾虽不自辩数，则能明吾所说之意，如此者万不一，有所以则为难也。**
**Even if I do not argue excessively**, **to make my intended meaning clear**—**such cases are rare, one in ten thousand**—**this is the true difficulty.**

**又非吾敢横失，而能尽之难也。**
**Nor is it my daring to speak boldly**; **the difficulty lies in fully conveying the intent.**

**吾之所说，其不可循理，非敢横失，能尽此意亦复难有。**
**What I say may defy reason**; **though I dare not speak recklessly**, **to fully express this intent is also difficult.**

**凡说之难，在知所说之心，可以吾说当之。**
**The difficulty of persuasion lies in knowing the listener’s intent**, **so that my words align with theirs.**

**既知所说之心，则能随心而发唱，故所说能当。**
**Once I know the listener’s intent**, **I can speak in accordance**, **and thus my words will resonate.**

**所说出於为名高者也，而说之以厚利，则见下节而遇卑贱，必弃远矣。**
**If the listener seeks high reputation**, **and I persuade with great profit**, **he will perceive my low integrity and treat me with contempt**—**he will surely reject and distance himself.**

**所说之人意在名高，今以厚利说之，彼则为己志节凡下，而以卑贱相遇。**
**The listener’s intent is on high reputation**; **if I persuade with great profit**, **he will deem my principles base** and **treat me with disdain.**

**亦既贱之，必弃遗而疏远矣。**
**Once he deems me lowly**, **he will abandon and distance himself.**

**所说出於厚利者也，而说之以名高，则见无心而远事情，必不收矣。**
**If the listener seeks great profit**, **and I persuade with high reputation**, **he will see my words as insincere and distant from practical matters**—**he will surely reject them.**

**所说之人意在厚利，今以名高说之，此则为己无相时之心而阔远事情矣。**
**The listener’s intent is on great profit**; **if I persuade with high reputation**, **he will perceive my words as lacking timely relevance and remote from practical concerns.**

**如此则必见弃而不收矣。**
**Thus, he will surely reject and dismiss my words.**

**所说阴为厚利而显为名高者也，而说之以名高，则阳收其身而实疏之，说之以厚利，则阴用其言显弃其身矣。**
**If the listener secretly seeks profit but publicly seeks high reputation**, **and I persuade with reputation**, **he will outwardly accept me but inwardly distance himself**; **if I persuade with profit**, **he will secretly use my words while publicly rejecting me.**

**所说之人，内阴为厚利，外阳为名高，今见其外说以名高，彼虽阳收其身，内实疏远，若察知其内，说以厚利，则私用其言，外明弃其身以饰其名高也。**
**The listener, inwardly seeking profit but outwardly seeking reputation**, **if I speak to his outward self with reputation**, **he may outwardly accept me but inwardly distance himself**; **if I discern his true intent and speak of profit**, **he will privately use my words while publicly rejecting me to uphold his reputation.**

**此不可不察也。**
**This must be carefully observed.**

**夫事以密成，语以泄败，未必其身泄之也，而语及所匿之事，如此者身危。**
**Matters succeed through secrecy**; **speech reveals failure**—**not necessarily by the speaker’s own disclosure**, but **if I mention what was concealed**, **my life will be in danger.**

**所说之人，其所谋事，身虽不泄谋，说者泛语言及所匿，似若说者先知其事，今以发动之，既怀此疑，其身必危矣。**
**The listener’s plotted affairs**, **though he does not reveal them himself**, **if I casually mention what was hidden**, **it will seem I knew beforehand and exposed him**—**once suspicion takes root, my life will be in danger.**

**彼显有所出事，而乃以成他故，说者不徒知所出而已矣，又知其所以为，如此者身危。**
**If he openly acts for one purpose but claims another**, **I do not merely know his outward action**—**I also know his true intent**—**thus, my life will be in danger.**

**所说之人，显出其事有所避讳，乃托以他故，而说者深知其事，既所出入知所为，所说既知情，露必有危己之心。**
**The listener, though he publicly acts with caution**, **pretends another motive**; **if I deeply understand his true intent**, **knowing both his outward and inward actions**, **he will suspect I know his hidden motives and seek to harm me.**

**规异事而当，知者揣之外而得之，事泄於外，必以为己也，如此者身危。**
**If I advise on unusual matters and hit upon the truth**, **a clever man may deduce it from external clues**, **and if the matter is revealed**, **he will assume I leaked it**—**thus, my life will be in danger.**

**说者为君规谋异事，而智谋之士当知此者，自外揣之，遂得其谋，因泄於外，君则疑己漏之，便以为不密而加诛也。**
**If I advise the lord on unusual strategies**, **a clever strategist may deduce them from external signs**, **and if the plan is leaked**, **the lord will suspect I betrayed him**—**he will then accuse me of indiscretion and punish me.**

**周泽未渥也，而语极知，说行而有功则德忘，说不行而有败则见疑，如此者身危。**
**If the lord’s favor has not yet fully settled**, **and I speak with excessive precision**, **if my persuasion succeeds and bears fruit, my merit will be forgotten**; **if it fails, I will be suspected**—**thus, my life will be in danger.**

**君之於己，周给之泽未有渥厚，遂以知之，极妙而以语之，行说有功犹忘其德，若不行有败，则羞始生焉，此正危身之道也。**
**The lord’s favor toward me has not yet fully settled**; **if I speak with excessive precision**, **if my persuasion succeeds, my merit will be forgotten**; **if it fails, shame will arise**—**this is the path to endangering myself.**

**贵人有过端，而说者明言礼义以挑其恶，如此者#6身危。**
**If a noble has a fault**, **and I openly speak of propriety and righteousness to provoke his shame**, **my life will be in danger.**

**挑，谓发扬也。**
***"Provoking"** means **exposing and magnifying it.**

**贵人或得计而欲自以为功，说者与知焉，如此者身危。**
**If a noble achieves success and seeks to claim it as his own merit**, **and I am aware of it**, **my life will be in danger.**

**强以其所不能为，止以其所不能已，如此者身危。**
**To urge what he cannot accomplish**, **or restrain what he cannot stop**—**this is dangerous.**

**不能而强，不以而止，必以不讨而兴怒，故危也。**
**To force what he cannot do**, **or halt what he cannot stop**, **will surely provoke his wrath without justification**—**thus, it is dangerous.**

Thus, when discussing great men with him, he will suspect me of **replacing him**.
*"Replacing"* means **substituting**.

To discuss great men, one must speak of their **virtue and boundless Dao**, but he will take it as **recommending another to replace him**.

When discussing lesser men, he will think I am **selling my influence**.
To discuss lesser men, one must speak of their **skills and petty talents**, but he will take it as **belittling them to sell my influence**.

To speak of what he loves will be seen as **borrowing his favor**.
This means **using what the lord loves as leverage for myself**.

To speak of what he has achieved will be seen as **testing me**.
*"Testing"* means **probing**.

To speak of what the lord hates will be seen as **testing my anger’s depth**.
To speak directly of his words will be seen as **lacking wisdom and clumsiness**.
*"Direct"* means **blunt**.

To debate minutiae will be seen as **excessive familiarity**.
*"Rice and salt"* refers to **accumulated trivialities**—to speak of **varied minor matters** will be seen as **my excessive familiarity**.

To briefly state my intent will be seen as **cowardice and hesitation**.
To **roughly outline the matter and intent** will be seen as **my fear and reluctance**, as if I dare not speak fully.

To elaborate broadly will be seen as **vulgarity and insolence**.
*"Elaborate"* means **to expound**.
If I **widely expound** on the matter without restraint, he will take it as **crude, vulgar, and disrespectful**.

This difficulty of persuasion must be understood.

The essence of persuasion lies in **adornment**—**exalting what he values and concealing what he despises**.
To persuade him, one must **know what he prizes and amplify it**, **know what he shames and suppress it**, thus aligning with his will without contradiction.

If he has **private urgency**, I must **demonstrate public duty to compel him**.
If his **intent is weak** yet he cannot stop himself, I must **adorn its virtue while minimizing its flaw**.

If persuasion succeeds but he has **private matters**, and he wishes to act urgently, I must **present public duty to urge him forward**.
Though his **private intent follows me**, yet he cannot stop it—then I **adorn the virtue of abandoning private gain**, and **frame his inability to comply with public duty as a fault**, thus **stirring him to uphold the public good**.

If his **heart is lofty** yet his ability cannot match, I must **expose his failures**, **reveal his flaws**, and **multiply his shortcomings**.
If his **persuasion relies on high public virtue** but his **capacity cannot reach it**, then I **highlight his private failings**, **expose his betrayal of the public**, and **frame his inability to act as a multitude of errors**, thus **fulfilling his lofty intent**.

If he **boasts of wisdom**, I must **cite analogous yet different cases**, **providing ample ground** for him to **borrow my persuasion**, while **pretending ignorance** to **augment his cleverness**.
If he **prides himself on broad wisdom**, I must **offer many varied yet analogous cases** to **expand his reasoning**, so he **takes persuasion from me**, while I **feign ignorance**, thus **aiding his intelligence**.

If I wish to **win his inner trust**, I must **glorify his name** while **subtly showing its alignment with private gain**.
To **gain his inner care**, I must **display the virtue’s name**, **prove he can fulfill it**, and **subtly suggest that this virtue benefits him privately**—thus, he will **truly trust and reciprocate**.

If I wish to **present dangerous matters**, I must **expose their ruin** while **subtly revealing their private harm**.
To **warn of danger**, if there are **detractors**, I must **speak openly** while **subtly hinting at private calamity**—thus, he will **test sincerity**.

To **praise others who share his path**, or to **plan matters aligned with his strategy**.
If one **shares his disgrace**, I must **greatly adorn its harmlessness**.
If one **shares his failure**, I must **clearly adorn its lack of loss**.
If I **extend praise to others who align with him**, or **plan matters that match his strategy**, and if their **actions bring him disgrace**, I must **elaborately adorn its innocence**, saying *"what harm is in this disgrace?"*
If their **plans bring him failure**, I must **clearly adorn its lack of loss**, saying *"what loss is in this failure?"*
Thus, I **position myself as a virtuous supplement**, **elevating his reputation**.

If he **boasts of his strength**, do not **measure him by difficulty**.
If he **prides himself on his origins**, I must **commend him**—do not **obstruct him with his shortcomings**.

If he **brags of decisive action**, do not **provoke his anger with past faults**.
If he **claims his strategies as wisdom**, do not **expose his past failures**.
All these are ways to **shield his weaknesses** and **nurture his sharpness**, so persuasion causes no harm.

When **intentions align without friction** and **words flow without conflict**, then **full debate may unfold**.
If **intentions are unopposed** and **words unchallenged**, then **wisdom and eloquence can flourish**.
This is the **Dao** by which one **gains proximity without suspicion** and **fully expresses oneself**.

**Yi Yin** served as steward, **Baili Xi** became a slave—both were **ways to approach their superiors**.
These two **trusted themselves to menial roles** to **seek advancement**.
Though both were **sages**, they **still could not avoid degrading themselves**—such was their **humility**.
Now, if I **use my words as a steward or slave**, yet **gain audience and transform the world**, this is **not a shame for those who seek office**.

If **time passes and favor remains shallow**, *"shallow"* means **prolonged**.

「*"Shallow"* means **prolonged**—**time** and **distance** grow vast.

**Deeply consider** yet **not suspect**, **draw forth contention** yet **not punish**—then **clearly delineate benefits and harms** to **fulfill their purpose**, and **sever connections**.
**Directly point out right and wrong** to **adorn one’s own standing**—*"directly point out"* means **speaking without evasion**.
*"Adorn one’s standing"* refers to **maintaining honor and glory** with **favor and rewards** to uphold oneself.
By this means, **mutual reliance is established**—this is how **persuasive speech succeeds**.
The **ruler** thus **remains undoubting and unpunishing** to **secure his subjects**, while the **subjects** **strive to fulfill their purpose** and **adorn their standing** to **offer loyalty**—hence, **mutual reliance**.
In ancient times, **Duke Wu of Zheng** wished to attack **Hu**, so first he **married his daughter to the Hu ruler** to **soothe his intentions**.
Then he **asked his ministers**: *"I wish to use force—who is fit to attack?"*
The **minister Guan Si** replied: *"Hu can be attacked."*
The duke, **enraged**, **executed him**, saying: *"Hu is a brotherly state—why speak of attacking it?"*
The **Hu ruler**, hearing this, **regarded Zheng as a close ally**, and thus **did not prepare defenses** against Zheng.
Zheng **ambushed Hu** and **seized it**.

In **Song**, there was a **wealthy man**. When **heaven rained down mud**, his son said: *"If we do not repair it, thieves will surely come."*
The **father of his neighbor** also spoke thus.
That night, **theft occurred**, and much of his wealth was lost—**the thief arrived that very evening**, so the loss was great.
His family **praised their son’s wisdom**, yet **doubted the neighbor’s father**.
These two **speakers were both correct**, yet the **one held dear was punished**, while the **one slighted was doubted**—the two refer to **Guan Si** and the **neighbor’s father**.
Duke Wu of Zheng **punished his dear one** to **ensure Hu would not suspect him**.
The wealthy man **doubted his slighted neighbor** because **he did not share the same concern**.
Thus, **knowing the truth is not difficult**, but **applying that knowledge appropriately is**.

**Guan Si’s words were correct**, yet he **became a sage in Jin** and **was executed in Qin**—this **must not be overlooked**.
Jin **deviously obtained** the **scholar Si Hui from Qin**, and **Guan Si presented him with a staff**, saying:
*"My plans were not adopted, yet my words were not incorrect.
Though Jin **regarded him as a sage**, Qin **later executed him for his speech**—this too was **misapplying knowledge**."*

In ancient times, **Mizi Que** was **favored by the ruler of Wei**.
By the **laws of Wei**, **stealing the ruler’s carriage was punishable by amputation**.
When **Mizi Que’s mother fell ill**, people **urged him to inform her at night**, so he **falsely claimed the ruler’s carriage** and went out.
The ruler **heard of it** and **praised his filial piety**, saying:
*"How filial! For his mother’s sake, he **forgot the amputation penalty**."
Later, when **Mizi Que’s beauty faded and favor waned**, he **fell into disgrace with the ruler**.
The ruler said: *"He **once falsely claimed my carriage**, and **once shared his leftover fruit with me**."
Thus, **Mizi Que’s conduct did not change from the beginning**, yet **he was once praised and later condemned**—this was due to **the ruler’s shifting affections**.
When the **ruler holds affection**, **wise speech brings closer favor**; when the **ruler holds aversion**, **wise speech, if not fitting, brings punishment and estrangement**.
Thus, **those who counsel and persuade must discern the ruler’s affections** before speaking.

The **dragon**, though **a serpent**, is **gentle enough to be approached and ridden**—yet beneath its throat is a **scale of resentment, a span long**.
If **anyone dares to touch it**, the dragon **will surely kill**.
**Rulers too have scales of resentment**—if **persuaders avoid touching their ruler’s resentment**, they **will be safe**.

**The And Family’s Jade (He Shi’s Gem)**
The **And family’s jade-cutter** found a **rough jade stone** in the **mountains of Chu** and **offered it to King Li of Chu**.
King Li **sent a jade expert to examine it**, who said: *"It is stone."*
The king **took the And family’s man for deceit**, and **had his left foot amputated**.
When **King Li died**, **King Wu ascended the throne**, and the And family’s man **again presented the rough jade**.
King Wu **sent a jade expert to examine it**, who **again declared it stone**.
The king **took him for deceit once more**, and **had his right foot amputated**.
When **King Wu died**, **King Wen took the throne**, and the And family’s man **clasped his rough jade and wept beneath Chu’s mountains** for **three days and three nights**.
His **tears ran dry**, and **he wept blood instead**.
The king **heard of it** and **sent someone to ask the reason**, saying:
*"Many in the world have had their feet amputated—why do you weep so bitterly?"*
The And family’s man replied:
*"I do not weep for the amputation—**I weep for the precious jade being labeled as stone**, and for the **virtuous man being called a deceiver**—this is why I weep."*
The king **then ordered the jade expert to refine the stone**, and **a treasure was revealed**.
Thus, it was **named the And Family’s Jade (He Shi’s Gem)**.

**Jewels and jade are what rulers desire most**—though the And family’s man **offered rough jade unrefined**, it **did not harm the king**.
Yet **his feet were twice amputated before his worth was recognized**—**such is the difficulty of proving one’s treasure**.
Now, **the ruler’s need for law and technique** is **not necessarily as urgent as jade**, yet he **prohibits ministers and commoners from private scheming**.
Then **those who follow the Dao** **are not yet persecuted**—it is simply that **the ruler’s rough jade has not yet been presented**.

When **the ruler employs technique**, **great ministers cannot arbitrarily decide**, and **favorites dare not sell influence**.
When **officials enforce the law**, **the corrupt flee to farming**, and **wandering scholars perish in battle**.
Thus, **law and technique become the ruin of ministers and commoners**.
The **ruler cannot overturn ministers’ opinions**, **nor suppress the people’s slander**, **but he alone can harmonize with Daoic speech**.
Then **even if those who uphold law and technique meet death**, the **Dao will still not be recognized**.

In ancient times, **Wu Qi instructed King Xiao of Chu** on **Chu’s customs**, saying:
*"Great ministers hold too much weight, and **feudal lords are too numerous**—if this continues, **the ruler will be hemmed in by his ministers**, while **the people will be oppressed**.
This is the **path that leads to poverty and weak armies**.
It would be better to **confiscate noble titles after three generations**, **abolish the hereditary ranks of officials**, and **reduce unnecessary branches of government**—thus **supporting the selection and training of talented men**."*

「When the year had passed and King Xiao of Chu died, Wu Qi was dismembered limb by limb in Chu. Shang Jun taught King Xiao Gong of Qin about *lian shi wu* (連什伍), setting penalties for failing to report crimes, burning the *Poetry* and *Books* to emphasize laws and decrees, blocking private petitions while promoting public labor, and prohibiting idle officials while elevating those skilled in tillage and warfare. King Xiao Gong implemented these measures, and the ruler was honored and secure, while the state grew wealthy and powerful. After eight years, he died, and Shang Jun was executed by chariot dismemberment in Qin.

Chu, which did not adopt Wu Qi’s teachings, fell into disorder and decline, while Qin, which followed Shang Jun’s laws, flourished in wealth and strength. The words of these two men were indeed correct, yet why was Wu Qi dismembered and Shang Jun chariot-dismembered? It was because the great ministers resented the law, and the common people hated governance. In the present age, great ministers are greedy for power, and the common people are content with chaos—even more so than in Qin and Chu’s times—while rulers lack the attentive ears of King Xiao of Chu or King Xiao Gong of Qin. Then, how could those who uphold law and technique endure the same perils as these two men did and still make their teachings known?

This is why the world remains in disorder, with no sovereigns rising as *conquerors* (霸王).

**Chapter 14: Delinquent Ministers and Usurpation (奸劫弒臣)**
All delinquent ministers seek to align with the ruler’s inclinations to gain favor and power. Thus, when the ruler has a virtue, they follow by praising it; when the ruler has an aversion, they follow by condemning it. In general, men who share the same inclinations toward acceptance or rejection will agree, while those who differ will oppose.

Now, what ministers praise is what the ruler approves—this is called *same acceptance* (同取). What ministers condemn is what the ruler disapproves—this is called *same rejection* (同舍). One has never heard of acceptance and rejection being in harmony while opposing each other; this is the way ministers gain favor and trust through flattery.

When delinquent ministers exploit the ruler’s trust to slander or praise, advancing or demoting officials, the ruler is not one who employs *skills and calculations* (術數) to govern them, nor does he verify their words through *cross-examination and evidence* (參驗). He will inevitably be deceived by past flatterers and believe the present words of those who seek his favor. This is how delinquent ministers succeed in deceiving the ruler and serving their own interests.

Thus, the ruler is inevitably deceived from above, while ministers grow ever more powerful below—these are the ministers who *monopolize the ruler* (擅主之臣). When a state has ministers who monopolize the ruler, then the common subjects cannot fully expend their wisdom and strength to present their loyalty, nor can the officials of all departments faithfully uphold the law to achieve their duties.

How can this be made clear? Men naturally seek safety and benefit while avoiding danger and harm—this is human nature. Now, if a minister fully expends his wisdom and strength to serve the ruler, striving to present his loyalty, his own family will be impoverished and his kin will suffer harm. But if he pursues private gain to exploit the ruler, using wealth and bribes to curry favor with the powerful ministers, his own family will be honored and rich, and his kin will enjoy their blessings. Who would abandon the path of safety and benefit to court danger and harm?

Governance like this is clearly flawed, yet the ruler wishes the subjects below to have no delinquency, and the officials to faithfully uphold the law—this is an impossible expectation.

Thus, the ruler’s close attendants know that loyalty and sincerity cannot bring them safety, so they say: *"I serve the ruler with loyalty and sincerity, accumulating merits and labor to seek security—this is like a blind man trying to discern the distinction between black and white, and it will never succeed."* If one follows the *Dao* (道) to transform and uphold proper principles, not seeking wealth and honor to serve the ruler and thus gain safety, this is like a deaf man trying to distinguish between clear and muddy sounds—that is even less likely to succeed.

Since neither of these paths can bring safety, how could I not resort to *comparing and flattering* (比周), *obscuring the ruler* (蔽主上), and *pursuing private gain* (奸私) to suit the ruler’s favor? This is inevitable. Likewise, the officials of all departments know that uprightness cannot bring them safety, so they say: *"I serve the ruler with purity and integrity to seek safety—this is like trying to make a square and a circle without any rules, and it will never succeed."* If I uphold the law without forming factions and govern my department properly to seek safety, this is like trying to scratch one’s head with one’s feet—that is even less likely to succeed.

Since neither of these paths can bring safety, how could I not disregard the law and pursue private gain to suit the ruler’s favor? This is inevitable.

Thus, those who prioritize private gain are numerous, while those who serve the ruler through the law are few. Therefore, the ruler is isolated above, while ministers form factions below—this is how Tian Cheng (田成) usurped and killed Duke Jian of Qi (齊簡公).

Now, one who possesses *skills and calculations* (術數) as a minister can present *measured and strategic words* (度數之言), making the ruler’s laws clear above and constraining delinquent ministers below, thereby honoring the ruler and securing the state. Thus, when *measured and strategic words* are effective in the ruler’s presence, then rewards and punishments will certainly be applied afterward.

Men’s words become clear through the *art of the sage* (聖人之術), yet they do not conform to the *common opinions of the age* (世俗之言). They follow *name and reality* (名實) to determine right and wrong, and rely on *cross-examination and evidence* (參驗) to judge speech and words.

Thus, the ruler’s close attendants know that deceit and falsehood cannot bring them safety, so they say: *"I will not abandon private gain and exploit the ruler’s favor. I will not flatter and compare to seek safety, but instead fully expend my wisdom and strength to serve the ruler—this is like carrying a weight of ten thousand *jin* (釼) and plunging into an unfathomable abyss while seeking life, and it will never succeed."* The officials of all departments also know that pursuing private gain cannot bring them safety, so they say: *"I will not serve the ruler with purity and integrity, nor uphold the law. Instead, I will use a corrupt heart to pervert the law for private gain—this is like standing at the edge of a high cliff and falling into a deep ravine below while seeking life, and it will never succeed."*

The paths of safety and danger are so clear, yet the ruler’s close attendants cannot be deceived by idle words, nor can the officials dare to exploit the people through greed. Thus, ministers can present their loyalty without obstruction, and the subjects can fulfill their duties without resentment.

This is how Guan Zhong (管仲) governed Qi and how Shang Jun (商君) strengthened Qin.

From this perspective, one can see that the sages govern the state not by relying on others to love them, but by employing methods that compel people to love them instead.»

To rely on others loving you is perilous, but to rely on what cannot but be done is secure.

The bond between ruler and minister is not one of blood or kinship. If righteousness brings advantage, then ministers will exert themselves in service. If righteousness brings no security, then ministers will act privately to curry favor above.

An enlightened ruler understands this, and thus establishes the ways of reward and punishment to demonstrate to the world—nothing more.

Thus, even if the ruler does not verbally instruct his officials nor personally seek out deceit, the state will already be well-ordered.

A sovereign is not one whose eyes are as keen as Li Lou’s nor whose ears are as sharp as Shi Kuang’s. If one relies solely on vision without its full capacity, what is seen will be little—this is no sound method. If one does not harness the power of authority but instead waits for hearing alone, what is heard will be sparse—this is no trustworthy path.

An enlightened ruler makes the world unable not to see for him, unable not to hear for him. Thus, though he resides in the secluded palace, his insight illuminates the four seas, and the world cannot deceive or conceal itself from him. Why? Because the ways of chaos are abandoned, and the power of clarity thrives.

Therefore, those who skillfully wield authority bring stability to the state, while those who fail to harness it invite danger. In ancient Qin, the custom was for rulers and ministers to disregard the law in favor of private favoritism, and thus the state fell into disorder, its armies weakened, and its ruler diminished in prestige.

Shang Jun persuaded Duke Xiao of Qin to reform the laws and customs, establishing public justice, rewarding those who exposed crimes, discouraging frivolous pursuits while favoring essential labor. At that time, the Qin people were accustomed to old ways—where crimes could be pardoned and merit could bring honor—so they lightly violated the new laws.

Thus, those who broke them faced severe and certain punishment, while informants received generous and trustworthy rewards. Consequently, no crime went undetected, and many were executed, causing widespread resentment and daily reports of grievances. Duke Xiao refused to relent and carried out Shang Jun’s laws. Only later did the people realize that crimes would surely be punished and that private deceit was rampant, so none dared transgress, and punishments were rarely applied.

Thus, the state was governed, its armies strengthened, its lands expanded, and its ruler honored. This was because the penalties for concealing crimes were severe, and the rewards for exposing them were substantial—this too was a way to make the world see and hear for him.

The methods of perfect governance have been made clear, yet scholars of the world fail to recognize them.

Moreover, the foolish scholars of this age do not understand the nature of order and chaos. They merely recite the books of antiquity, disrupting the governance of the present. Their intelligence is insufficient to avoid the pitfalls of ignorance, yet they recklessly oppose those skilled in the arts.

To heed their words is to court danger; to follow their strategies is to invite disorder. This is the greatest folly and the most severe affliction.

Those who merely discuss theory while lacking true skill are as distant from true practitioners as a molehill is from a great mountain. Yet the sages discern the true nature of right and wrong, and perceive the essence of order and chaos.

Thus, in governing the state, they uphold just laws and severe punishments, seeking to rescue the people from disorder and avert calamity for the world. They ensure that the strong do not oppress the weak, that the many do not tyrannize the few, that the elderly live out their years, that orphans grow to maturity, that borders remain unviolated, that rulers and ministers remain close, and that parents and children are preserved—free from the miseries of death and captivity. This is indeed the greatest of merits.

The foolish do not understand, yet they call it cruelty. The foolish desire order but hate the means to achieve it, loving danger while clinging to what brings it.

How do we know this? Severe punishments are what the people dislike, yet they are the means by which the state is governed. Pitying the people and lightening punishments may please them, but it is the path to ruin.

Thus, the sages who establish laws must act contrary to the world’s expectations yet align with virtue and the Dao. Those who understand this agree in principle but differ in custom. Those who do not understand differ in principle but conform in custom.

If few in the world understand, then righteousness is not upheld. To occupy a position contrary to the Dao, to be slandered by the many, to be drowned in the words of the age, and yet seek to stand before a stern sovereign and hope for safety—this is surely difficult.

This is why wise men often die unrecognized in their time.

The younger brother of King Zhuang of Chu, Spring-applied Jun, had a favored concubine named Yu. The son of Spring-applied Jun’s primary wife was named Jia. Yu wished for the king to abandon his wife, so she injured herself and wept before the king, saying: *"To be your concubine is a great fortune. Though so, serving your wife is not the way to serve you, nor is serving you the way to serve your wife. I am unworthy, and my strength is insufficient to serve two masters. It is impossible to please both, so rather than die in your wife’s hands, I would prefer to die before you. If I am granted death, and if I should later be favored by your attendants, I pray you will investigate and not let others mock you."*

The king believed Yu’s deceit and abandoned his primary wife.

Yu then wished to kill Jia and make his son the heir, so she tore her own clothes to wrap around her body and wept before the king, saying: *"I have long been favored by you, and Jia is not unaware of this. Yet now he seeks to mock me, and when I contested with him, he tore my clothes. His disloyalty could not be greater!"*

The king, enraged, killed Jia.

Thus, the wife was abandoned through Yu’s deceit, and the son was killed by it. From this, we see that even a father’s love for his son can be destroyed by treachery. The relationship between ruler and minister is not one of kinship, yet the slander of ministers is far more than the words of a single concubine—how then can we be surprised that the wise and virtuous meet violent deaths?

This is the way **Shāng Jūn** was torn apart by chariot in **Qín**, and **Wú Qǐ** was dismembered limb by limb in **Chǔ**. All ministers, when they have faults, naturally do not wish to be executed, and those without merit all desire honor and prominence. Yet the **sage**’s governance of a state rewards none without merit, while punishment is always carried out against those who bear guilt.

Thus, those skilled in **arts and stratagems** who serve as ministers are inevitably the target of deceitful courtiers, unless a wise ruler can discern and heed them. Scholars of **arts and stratagems** instruct sovereigns, not saying: *"Leverage the awe of authority to suppress the treacherous ministers,"* but only: *"Practice benevolence, righteousness, and kindness."* Sovereigns of the world praise the name of benevolence and righteousness yet fail to examine their substance, so the great suffer the ruin of their states and their own deaths, while the small lose territory and see their status diminished. How can this be made clear?

To give aid to the poor and destitute—this is what the world calls benevolence and righteousness. To pity the common people and refuse to execute or punish—this is what the world calls kindness. If a ruler gives to the poor and destitute, then those without merit receive rewards. If he refuses to execute or punish, then violent rebels grow unrestrained. When a state rewards those without merit, its people will not exert themselves against external enemies or labor diligently in the fields; instead, they all seek to trade wealth, serve the rich, pursue private gain, and establish reputations to obtain high office and generous stipends.

Thus, the number of treacherous ministers increases, and the violent rebels grow ever stronger—how could such a state not perish? Harsh punishments are what the people fear, and severe penalties are what they detest. Therefore, the **sage** presents what they fear to restrain their wickedness, and establishes what they detest to prevent their treachery, so the state remains secure and violent rebellion does not arise.

By this, I demonstrate that benevolence, righteousness, and kindness are insufficient for governance, while harsh punishments and severe penalties can bring order to a state. Without the **rod and whip** to command horses, even **Zàochéng Fǔ** could not tame them. Without the **square and compass** to measure, even **Yáng’ěr** could not make them. Without the **awe of authority and the laws of reward and punishment**, even **Yáo** and **Shùn** could not govern. Yet today’s rulers all lightly dismiss severe penalties and harsh punishments, practicing kindness instead, and thus the achievements of **conquerors and kings** are unattainable.

Therefore, a ruler who excels must clearly establish rewards and incentives to encourage the people, ensuring that they gain rewards through merit, not through benevolence and righteousness. He must enforce harsh punishments and severe penalties to restrain them, ensuring that those who bear guilt are executed, not spared by kindness. Thus, those without merit will not dare to hope, and those with guilt will not escape misfortune.

Relying on sturdy chariots and fine horses, one can traverse even steep and rugged paths. Boarding a light, swift boat and holding a convenient oar, one can forever avoid the perils of rivers. Grasping the **methods of governance and the principles of reward and punishment**, and executing harsh penalties, one can attain the achievements of **conquerors and kings**. To govern a state with established laws of reward and punishment is like traveling on land with sturdy chariots and fine horses, or sailing with a light, swift boat and a convenient oar—those who use them will succeed.

**Yī Yǐn** obtained this from **Tāng**, who became king. **Guān Zhōng** obtained this from **Qí**, who became a **conqueror**. **Shāng Jūn** obtained this from **Qín**, who grew strong. These three men all understood the **arts of conquest and kingship**, discerned the **principles of governance and strength**, and were not swayed by the empty words of the world. When they aligned with the intentions of a wise ruler, even **humble commoners** could rise to the rank of **ministers and prime**. When they took their positions and governed the state, they brought **honor to the ruler and expansion of territory**. These are the **worthy ministers**.

Tāng obtained **Yī Yǐn**, and with a hundred *lǐ* of land, established himself as **Son of Heaven**. **Duke Huán** obtained **Guān Zhōng**, and became the leader of the **Five Conquerors**, uniting the feudal lords nine times and rectifying the world once. **Duke Xiào** obtained **Shāng Jūn**, and his territory grew vast while his armies became mighty.

Thus, there are **loyal ministers** who, externally, face no threat from rival states, and internally, have no concern for rebellious courtiers. Their rule endures in the world, and their names are passed down to posterity—these are the **loyal ministers**.

As for **Yù Ràng**, who served **Zhì Bó**, he could neither enlighten the ruler with the **principles of governance and strength** to avoid calamity, nor lead his forces to secure the state. When **Duke Xiāng** killed **Zhì Bó**, **Yù Ràng** mutilated himself, disfigured his face, and sought vengeance for his master against **Duke Xiāng**. Though he sacrificed his body and ruined his appearance for the sake of his lord’s reputation, in truth, he was no more beneficial to **Zhì Bó** than the finest hair of an autumn moth. This is what I consider the **lowest kind of minister**, yet the world’s rulers regard him as loyal and exalt him.

In ancient times, there were **Bó Yí** and **Shū Qí**, who refused **King Wǔ**’s offer of the world and starved to death on **Chǒuyáng Lǐng**. Such ministers fear neither severe punishment nor covet great rewards; they cannot be restrained by penalties nor directed by rewards. These are the **useless ministers**, whom I would do without, yet the world’s rulers seek them out.

The proverb says: *"The ruler who is harsh will be pitied."* This is an irreverent statement. Though it may be so, ancient proverbs are never false, and one cannot fail to examine it. This refers to rulers who are **seized, killed, or meet violent deaths**.

When a ruler lacks **methods of governance** to control his ministers, even if he lives long and is endowed with fine qualities, the great ministers will still seize power, act arbitrarily, and make decisions for their own benefit, while fearing that **noble warriors** will use the ruler’s authority to restrain or punish them. Thus, they murder the virtuous and establish the weak, abandon the legitimate heir and install the unrighteous.

Hence, the **Spring and Autumn Annals** record: *"The **King of Chǔ**, **Zǐ Wéi**, was about to visit **Zhèng** for a marriage alliance, but before leaving the border, he heard that the king was ill and returned. Upon his arrival, he feigned concern for the king’s health and strangled him with his own headband, thereby seizing the throne."* **Cuī Zhù** of **Qí** had a beautiful wife, and **Duke Zhuāng** repeatedly visited her home. When the duke went there, **Cuī Zhù**’s followers, led by **Jià Jǔ**, attacked him.

The duke entered the chamber and requested that the throne be divided with Cui Zhu, but Cui Zhu refused. The duke then asked to be slain with his own blade in the ancestral temple, yet Cui Zhu still would not comply. At last, the duke fled, scaling the northern wall.

Jià Jǔ shot the duke, striking his thigh, and he fell. Cui Zhu’s followers then struck him with spears and killed him, installing his younger brother, **Duke Jìng**, in his place.

In more recent accounts, we see **Lǐ Duì**’s treatment of **Zhào’s ruler**: he starved the sovereign for a hundred days until death. **Nàochǐ**’s treatment of **Qí’s ruler** was even more cruel—he tore the flesh from the king’s limbs, hung them from the temple beams, and left him to die in agony.

Thus, though **Duke Lí** suffered from festering sores and physical afflictions, when compared to the Spring and Autumn era, his fate was not as dire as strangulation or a thigh wound. When compared to the recent past, his suffering was far less severe than starvation or limb dismemberment.

Therefore, those who seized and killed their rulers, the anguish of their hearts and the torment of their bodies, must have been far greater than that of Duke Lí.

From this perspective, even if Duke Lí was pitiful, it was still better than the fate of **rulers wronged by treacherous ministers**—

*Han Feizi, Chapter 4, "Enmity" (originally "Seeking"), Volume 5, "Omens of Ruin" (15th Chapter)*

In all cases where a ruler’s state is small yet his household vast, his authority weak yet his ministers powerful—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where laws are neglected and schemes are prioritized, where the ruler abandons his own lands yet relies on foreign alliances—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

When courtiers study in vain, attendants delight in debate, merchants hoard wealth abroad, and commoners wield influence—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where one indulges in palaces, pavilions, ponds, and fine carriages and attire, exhausts the people, and squanders the treasury—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where time is wasted on rituals, where spirits and divination are trusted, and where sacrifices are excessive—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where one grants titles merely to test loyalty, relying on a single man as a gatekeeper—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where offices are bought and sold, and honors are obtained through bribes—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where the ruler is indecisive, soft-spoken, and lacks resolve, where his likes and dislikes are unclear, and where he fails to establish firm policies—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where greed knows no satisfaction, and where one eagerly pursues profit—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where one delights in licentiousness yet disregards the law, where one favors eloquence over practical application, and where one indulges in superficial beauty without regard for true merit—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where the ruler is shallow and easily swayed, where secrets are leaked and nothing is concealed, and where he cannot discern the true intentions of his ministers—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where one is harsh and unyielding, where he rejects counsel yet loves to prevail, where he scorns his own state yet trusts his own judgment—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where one scorns nearby allies yet clings to distant ones, where he depends on the aid of the strong while disdaining those under pressure—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where wandering scholars and foreign guests hold sway, where wealth is stored beyond the borders, and where the ruler conspires with them while neglecting the people—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where the people trust their ministers more than their ruler, where the ruler favors them yet cannot dismiss them—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where native talents are ignored yet foreign advisors are sought, where merit is not rewarded but reputation is, where outsiders are elevated to undermine the established—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where the ruler’s legitimate consort is slighted, where his sons are treated as equals, and where the heir is not yet settled while the ruler dies—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where the ruler has a bold yet unrepentant heart, where chaos reigns yet he boasts of his own strength, where he does not assess his own resources but instead provokes his neighbors—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where a small state refuses to accept its inferiority, where weak strength dares not fear the mighty, where courtesy is absent and the great neighbor is scorned, where arrogance and folly ruin alliances—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where a legitimate heir is already appointed yet the ruler takes a powerful foreign wife as his successor, endangering the heir—**such a kingdom is doomed**, for then both the ministers and the chief advisors will grow anxious.

Where the ruler is timid and weak in defense, where he is easily intimidated yet soft-hearted, where he knows what is right yet lacks the courage to act—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where the ruler himself is held hostage abroad and his state is abandoned, where a prince is sent as a pledge yet not recalled while the ruler is replaced—**such a kingdom is doomed**, for then the state will be lost.

Where the ruler humiliates great ministers yet fawns over himself, where he punishes the innocent yet defies their pleas, where he harbors resentment and shame while fixating on vengeance—**such a kingdom is doomed**, for then bandits will rise.

Where great ministers hold dual influence, where many powerful relatives and factions vie for control, and where internal cliques and external allies vie for power—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where the ruler heeds the words of concubines and favors the counsel of favorites, where his inner and outer courts are in mourning yet his actions repeatedly violate the law—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where the ruler belittles and insults great ministers, where he lacks respect for his kin, where he burdens the people, and where he executes the innocent—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where one uses cleverness to subvert the law, where private actions are mixed with public affairs, where decrees are frequently altered, and where orders are issued in haste—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where there are no fortified borders, where the city walls are weak, where there are no stored supplies, where wealth is scarce, and where there are no defenses against attack yet one wages war recklessly—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where rulers die young in succession, where the sovereign is often replaced, where infants rule, where ministers seize power, where wandering scholars are cultivated as factions, and where lands are repeatedly ceded to secure alliances—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where the heir is held in high esteem, where his followers are many and powerful, where he has numerous great allies, and where his influence grows early—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where the ruler’s nature is narrow and hasty, where he is quick to anger and easily provoked, where his resentment festers yet he weighs not past or present—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where the ruler is prone to wrath and loves to wage war, where he neglects fundamental training yet lightly engages in battle—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where nobles envy one another, where great ministers flourish, where the ruler leans on foreign states, where the people are impoverished, and where he wages war against grievances yet fails to punish the guilty—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where the ruler is unworthy yet his concubines are virtuous, where the heir is slighted yet his younger brothers are favored, where officials are weak yet the people are strong—**such a kingdom is doomed**, for then the state will grow restless.

Where the ruler harbors anger yet does not strike, where he suspends punishment yet does not execute, allowing his ministers to secretly resent him while their fears grow ever greater—**such a kingdom is doomed**, for his intentions remain long unclear.

Where the ruler entrusts his generals with vast authority, where borderlands are left to weak defenders, where officials act unilaterally, issuing commands without consultation—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where the foreign wife is licentious, where the chief consort harbors impurity, where inner and outer courts are united in corruption, and where distinctions between men and women are abandoned—**this is called dual sovereignty**, and **such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where the foreign wife is lowborn yet concubines are honored, where the heir is slighted yet younger sons are exalted, where the ruler’s chamberlain is treated lightly yet his attendants hold sway—**such a kingdom is doomed**, for then inner and outer courts will be divided.

Where great ministers are excessively honored, where factions are many and powerful, where they obstruct the ruler’s decisions and seize control of the state—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where private offices are favored over public merit, where those who excel in military matters are entrusted with state positions, yet where local virtues are ignored and official duties are neglected—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where the royal treasury is empty yet great ministers are wealthy, where the common people are poor yet dependents are rich, where farmers and soldiers suffer while merchants and artisans thrive—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where one sees great profit yet does not pursue it, where one hears of impending disaster yet does not prepare, where one is shallow in matters of defense yet adorns himself with empty displays of **Dao/virtue**—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

---

Where the ruler does not practice filial **virtue/power** as befits his station, yet admires the **virtue/power** of commoners, where he disregards the welfare of the state and nation (*shèjì*) yet obeys the commands of his concubine—when women govern and felons hold office—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where eloquence prevails over law and cunning over **Dao/Way**—when the ruler excels in many skills yet acts without adherence to law and **Dao/Way**—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Where favored courtiers advance while old retainers are cast aside, where the unworthy hold office while the virtuous are suppressed, where those without merit are ennobled while the laboring are belittled—then the people grow resentful, and **such a kingdom is doomed** when resentment spreads.

Where ministers of high rank—father, elder brother, and grandee—receive excessive emoluments and privileges beyond their merit, where their attire encroaches upon the ruler’s prerogatives, where their palaces are lavishly supplied at the ruler’s expense yet he does not forbid it—then the hearts of his subjects are boundless, and **such a kingdom is doomed** when the subjects’ hearts are boundless.

When the ruler’s in-laws and maternal grandsons share common gates with the people, yet oppress their neighbors—**such a kingdom is doomed**.

Though these signs do not *necessarily* mean ruin, they *indicate* that ruin is possible.

For two **Emperors of Virtue** (*Yáo*) cannot reign together, nor can two **Sages of Depravity** (*Jié*) bring ruin together. The fate of **Emperors of Virtue** and **Sages of Depravity** depends on their **Dao/virtue**—whether they bring order or chaos, strength or weakness in opposition to one another.

When a tree breaks, it is because the rot has spread through its core. When a wall collapses, it is because the cracks have grown wide.
Yet even when a tree is riddled with rot, it will not break without the force of a violent wind.
Even when a wall is full of cracks, it will not collapse without a heavy downpour.
Thus, a ruler of ten thousand chariots (*wàn chéng*), though skilled in **Dao/Way** and law, can only bring ruin if the winds of fortune and misfortune strike—until then, his dominion over all under heaven remains unchallenged.

**The Three Safeguards (第十六)**
A ruler has three safeguards.
If these three safeguards are complete, the kingdom will be secure and the ruler honored; if they are incomplete, the kingdom will be in peril and the ruler’s life endangered.
What are the three safeguards?

When ministers discuss the ruler’s missteps, the errors of those in office, or matters concerning the selection of ministers, the ruler does not keep these discussions to himself but leaks them to his close attendants and capable favorites. Then, ministers who wish to speak will not dare to present their views directly to the ruler but instead first submit them to the close attendants and capable favorites. Thus, upright and honest speakers are never heard, and loyal ministers grow increasingly distant.

He does not love people solely for their benefit, but waits for their praise before showing them favor.
He does not hate people solely for their harm, but waits for their faults before punishing them.
Then the ruler’s authority is diminished, and his reliance shifts to those around him.

To avoid the burdens of self-governance, he entrusts his ministers with the power to implement his will, transferring the authority over life and death, and the control of rewards and punishments to them. **Such a ruler is vulnerable** when he does this.

This is what is meant by the three safeguards being incomplete.
When the three safeguards are incomplete, the signs of **seizure/usurpation** (*jié shā*) arise.
There are three types of **seizure/usurpation**:
**open usurpation** (*míng jié*), **opportunistic usurpation** (*shì jié*), and **legalistic usurpation** (*xíng jié*).

When ministers hold high rank, they control the state’s vital affairs, allowing no one outside their circle to act. Though there may be virtuous and capable men, those who oppose them will surely meet misfortune, while those who comply will surely receive blessing.
Then, no minister dares to be loyal to the ruler and concerned for the nation, striving for the state’s welfare and harm.
Even if the ruler is virtuous, he cannot govern alone, and if ministers do not dare to be loyal to the ruler, then the kingdom is already doomed.
This is not because the inner court is empty and the ministers few.
Rather, the ministers hold their emoluments, cultivate their connections, and pursue private gain without rendering public loyalty—**this is open usurpation**.

When ministers sell their favors and monopolize power, they feign outward compliance to outmaneuver the ruler’s inner circle. They use crafty words to manipulate the ruler’s perception of fortune and misfortune, aligning with his preferences. The ruler listens to them, lowering his own status and belittling the state to gratify them. When their schemes fail, they share the blame with the ruler, but when they succeed, they take all the credit for themselves.
If all those in office unite in their words to praise such ministers, then the ruler will never believe those who speak ill of them—**this is opportunistic usurpation**.

When ministers alone control the prisons and the administration of punishments—**this is legalistic usurpation**.

If the three safeguards are incomplete, the three types of **seizure/usurpation** will arise; if the three safeguards are complete, the three types of **seizure/usurpation** will be blocked, and the ruler will reign in **harmony**.

**The Perils of Trusting Others (第十七)**
The ruler’s greatest peril lies in trusting his subjects, for trust leads to control by them.
Ministers serve their ruler not out of kinship, but because they are bound by power and cannot refuse.
Thus, as ministers, they constantly probe the ruler’s intentions without pause, while the ruler, neglectful and complacent in his exalted position, allows this. This is why the world sees ministers **seizing/usurping** their rulers and **regicides**.

If a ruler places great trust in his son, then a wicked minister can exploit that trust through the son to achieve his own ends. Thus, **Lǐ Duì** served as regent for **Zhào Wáng** and starved his father.
If a ruler places great trust in his wife, then a wicked minister can exploit that trust through her to achieve his own ends. Thus, **Yōu Shī** served as regent for **Lì Jī**, killing **Shēn Shēng** and enthroning **Xī Qí**.

If the ruler cannot trust his wife, who is closest to him, and his son, who is his kin, then whom can he trust?
Even among rulers of ten thousand chariots (*wàn chéng*) and a thousand chariots (*qiān chéng*), their consorts and sons may harbor desires for the ruler’s early death.
How do we know this?

A wife is not bound by blood, but by love—if she loves, she is close; if she does not love, she is distant.
The saying goes: *If a mother loves, her son will embrace.*
Yet if she turns against him, her son will abandon him.
A man of fifty may still be infatuated with beauty, but a woman of thirty has already lost her allure.
If a woman of waning beauty serves a man still enamored with beauty, then she herself is treated with disdain, and her son fears he will not be named heir—this is why consorts and sons-in-law hope for the ruler’s death.

Only when the mother becomes the queen and the son becomes the ruler will her commands be unopposed and his prohibitions unbroken. The pleasures of men and women will not diminish from those of the previous ruler, and he will hold sway over ten thousand chariots without doubt—**this is the use of poison and strangulation, the dark arts of suffocation and strangulation**.

Thus, **Duān Zhū** of the *Spring and Autumn Annals* said: *A ruler’s life will not last half its natural span if he cannot guard against this.*
If the ruler does not know this, then chaos will multiply.
Hence the saying: *Those who profit from the ruler’s death are many, and the ruler is in danger.*

Thus, **Wang Liang** cherished his horses, and **King Goujian of Yue** cherished his people—for war and speed. A skilled physician sucks wounds and holds blood in his mouth, yet he is not bound by kinship; it is the lure of gain. Therefore, a carriage-maker, upon completing a carriage, hopes for his patron’s wealth and nobility, while a coffin-maker, upon finishing a coffin, hopes for his patron’s early death. It is not that the carriage-maker is benevolent and the coffin-maker malevolent—if men do not grow rich, carriages will not sell; if men do not die, coffins will not be bought. Their hearts do not hate men; their profit lies in men’s death.

So too, consorts, noble ladies, and the factions of the heir apparent thrive when the ruler dies—for if the ruler does not die, their influence will not prevail. Their hearts do not hate the ruler; their profit lies in the ruler’s death. Therefore, the ruler must not neglect to guard against those who profit from his death.

Thus, when the sun and moon are ringed by halos from without, the cause lies within. One must prepare against what one hates, for calamity arises from what one loves. Hence, an enlightened ruler does not engage in unexamined actions or consume unusual foods. He listens from afar and observes closely to discern faults within and without. He weighs differing opinions to discern factions, tests the truth of petitions through cross-verification, and holds later events accountable to prior ones. He governs by law to manage the multitude, using multiple perspectives to verify all matters. If rewards are not arbitrarily bestowed, if conduct is not transgressed, if punishments are justly meted out, and if crimes are not pardoned, then deceit and wickedness will have no place to conceal their schemes.

Excessive labor burdens the people, and when the people suffer, power and influence arise. When power and influence grow, burdens are lifted, the noble grow rich, and the people’s suffering is used to elevate the powerful—this is called *jièjiè* (借借), or borrowing influence. A minister is not the eternal benefit of the realm.

Thus it is said: If labor is light, the people will be at ease; if the people are at ease, the lower ranks will not wield excessive power; if the lower ranks do not wield excessive power, then power and influence will perish, and virtue will rest with the ruler.

Now, water’s mastery over fire is clear, yet when confined between a pot and a wok, the water boils and evaporates above while the fire rages and burns below—water loses its power to prevail. So too, though the prohibition of deceit is clear, if those who uphold the law act like the pot and wok, the law will shine only in their minds, and they will lose its power to curb deceit.

The teachings of antiquity and the records of the *Spring and Autumn Annals* show that those who commit treason by breaking the law to commit great deceit have always been high-ranking nobles. Yet laws and punishments are always directed at the lowly, so the people despair and have no one to complain to.

Great ministers surround and deceive the ruler, appearing united in private while publicly opposing each other to feign impartiality. They serve as each other’s spies, waiting for the ruler’s vulnerabilities. The ruler is obscured, with no way to hear the truth, bearing the name of sovereignty but lacking its substance. The ministers monopolize the law’s execution—such was the case with the **Zhou Dynasty’s kings**. If power and influence are lent to ministers, then the ruler and subjects will reverse positions—this is why ministers must not be granted power.

**Chapter Eighteen: Facing South**
The ruler’s faults lie in his own delegation of authority to ministers, yet he must also counterbalance those he does not trust—this reasoning will inevitably clash with those he trusts, and the ruler will be controlled by those he does not trust.

Now, those the ruler prepares against today were once those he relied upon. If the ruler cannot clarify the law to curb the power of great ministers, he will lose the trust of lesser ones.

If the ruler abandons the law and relies on ministers to counterbalance other ministers, then those who favor each other will collude and praise one another, while those who dislike each other will form factions and slander one another. When flattery and slander clash, the ruler will be confused and disordered.

A minister’s advancement depends on reputation and petitions; his power comes from violating the law; his influence comes from feigning loyalty and trust—only then can he evade restraint. These three things are the means by which ministers deceive the ruler and undermine the law.

When the ruler employs ministers, even if they are wise, they must not violate the law to act unilaterally; even if they are virtuous, they must not claim credit before earning it; even if they are loyal and trustworthy, they must not disregard the law to evade restraint—this is called *clarifying the law*.

The ruler must beware of those who entice him with matters and those who obstruct his understanding through speech—both must be scrutinized.

Ministers who easily discuss affairs demand few resources to deceive the ruler, using matters to frame him. If the ruler is enticed and does not investigate, he will grow dependent on them, and they will in turn control him through affairs—this is called *enticement*, and those enticed by affairs will be ensnared by calamity.

If their petitions are few but their demands are many, even if they achieve success, their words will not be trusted. Since untrusted words invite punishment, and successful matters warrant reward, no minister will dare to falsify speech to deceive the ruler.

The ruler’s way is to ensure that a minister’s later words do not contradict his earlier ones, nor his earlier words his later ones. Even if he achieves success, he must still bear his guilt—this is called *holding ministers accountable*.

If a minister proposes an affair and fears it may be unwise, he will first preemptively speak in favor of it, saying: *"Those who oppose this affair are jealous of its success."* If the ruler hides this statement and no longer listens to other ministers, and if other ministers fear this statement and dare not discuss affairs, then these two forces will prevail—loyal ministers will not be heard, while flatterers will hold sole authority. This is called *obstructing speech*, and those obstructed by speech will be controlled by ministers.

The ruler’s way is to ensure that ministers bear responsibility both for speaking and for remaining silent. Speech without foundation or verification is the responsibility of speech. Silence to avoid accountability while holding a high position is the responsibility of silence.

When the ruler commands ministers to speak, he must know the origin of their words to hold them to their truth. For those who remain silent, he must inquire into their choices to hold them accountable. Then no minister will dare to speak falsely, nor will any dare to remain silent—whether they speak or stay silent, they will bear responsibility.

The ruler desires to act yet does not grasp the beginning and end of matters, and instead reveals his intentions clearly. Those who act will not gain advantage but must suffer harm in return. Those who understand this will uphold principle and abandon personal desires.

When undertaking actions, there is a Dao/Way: if the input is great and the output is small, then it may be done. The confused ruler does not so calculate; he only considers the input and not the output. Though the output may double the input, he does not perceive the harm, and thus this is called *reaping the name while losing the substance*—whereby the gains are small and the harm is great.

In general, when the input is great and the output is small, it may be called "gain." Now, when the ruler expends great resources without cause and achieves only minor gains, the ministers will expend great resources to complete small tasks. When small tasks are completed, the ruler still suffers harm.

Those who do not understand governance will say: "Do not alter the ancient ways; do not change the established norms." Whether to change or not, the sage does not listen—only proper governance matters.

Thus, the ancient saying of "not altering the ancient ways" and "not changing the established norms" depends on whether the ancient ways and established norms are suitable or not. If **Yi Yin** had not altered **Yin**, and **Duke Gong** had not altered **Zhou**, then **Tang** and **Wu** would not have become kings.

If **Guan Zhong** had not changed **Qi**, and **Guo Yan** had not reform **Jin**, then **Huan** and **Wen** would not have become hegemon.

In general, people fear altering the ancient ways because they dread disturbing the people’s comfort. Not altering the ancient ways is to follow the path of chaos, while conforming to the people’s desires is to indulge in deceitful behavior.

The people are ignorant and do not recognize chaos; the ruler is weak and cannot reform. This is the failure of governance. The ruler must clearly understand governance and strictly enforce it, so even if it contradicts the people’s wishes, proper governance will stand.

This is illustrated by **Shang Jun’s** internal and external reforms, his heavy armor, and his early warnings.

Thus, when **Guo Yan** first governed, **Duke Wen** had standing soldiers. When **Guan Zhong** first governed, **Huan Gong** had war chariots. These were warnings to the people to be prepared.

Hence, the foolish and reckless people endure minor losses while forgetting greater benefits, so they suffer **tiger-like calamities** and unjust slander. Yet, even slight reforms can bring long-term ruin, so **Zou Jia** was not worthy of being a traveling envoy.

Those accustomed to chaos and complacent toward governance cannot return to order, so the people of **Zheng** could not be reformed.

**Chapter Nineteen: Ornamenting Evil**

The **Zhao** divined with a tortoise shell and cast many strategies; the omens said *great auspiciousness*, yet they attacked **Yan**—this was **Zhao**. The tortoise shell divined with many strategies; the omens said *great auspiciousness*, yet they attacked **Zhao**—this was **Yan**.

**Zuo Xin’s** campaign against **Yan** brought no success yet endangered the ancestral altars. **Zou Yan’s** campaign against **Yan** brought no success yet severed the **Dao/Way** of the state. **Zhao** first gained advantage from **Yan**, then from **Qi**, with the state in disorder yet the ruler’s prestige high. They believed they could match **Qin** in power, but this was not because the **Zhao tortoise** was divine nor because the **Yan tortoise** deceived.

**Zhao** again divined with a tortoise shell and cast many strategies, then launched a northern expedition against **Yan**, intending to plunder **Yan** to oppose **Qin**. The omens said *great auspiciousness*, but when they attacked **Daliang**, **Qin** took **Shangdang**. When their forces reached **Li**, six cities were captured. At **Yangcheng**, **Qin** seized **Ye**. **Pang Jiān** led reinforcements south, and **Dang** was utterly lost.

Thus, the ministers say: Though the **Zhao tortoise** had no distant foresight for **Yan**, it should have had near foresight for **Qin**.

**Qin**, relying on its *great auspiciousness*, expanded territory with substance and saved **Yan** in name. **Zhao**, relying on its *great auspiciousness*, lost land and suffered military disgrace, with the ruler dying in discontent—this was not because the **Qin tortoise** was divine nor because the **Zhao tortoise** deceived.

Initially, **Wei** spent years attacking eastward and exhausted **Tao** and **Wei**. In years spent attacking westward, it lost its state—this was not because **Fenglong**, **Wuxing**, **Taiyi**, **Wangxiang**, **Shètí**, **Liùshén**, **Wǔkuò**, **Tiānhé**, **Yīnqiāng**, or **Suìxīng** were in the west for those years, nor because **Kuítái**, **Húní**, **Xíngxīng**, or **Yínghuò** were in the east for those years.

Thus, it is said: Tortoise divination and ghosts/gods cannot guarantee victory; relying on left and right forces cannot ensure exclusive warfare. Yet, those who depend on them are the most foolish.

The ancient sage-kings devoted their strength to governing the people and added effort to clarifying the laws. When the laws are clear, loyal ministers will encourage proper conduct, and punishments will be sure—then evil ministers will cease.

When loyal ministers encourage proper conduct and evil ministers stop, the land will be vast and the ruler honored—this was **Qin**. When ministers form factions and cliques to conceal the proper **Dao/Way**, indulging in private schemes while the land is diminished and the ruler’s prestige low—this was **the states east of the mountains**.

Chaos and weakness lead to ruin; governance and strength lead to kingship. This is the ancient Dao/Way.

**King Goujian of Yue** relied on the tortoise of **Daming**, fought with **Wu**, and was defeated. He himself became a subject and entered service in **Wu**, then returned to his state, abandoned the tortoise, and clarified the laws while governing the people to avenge **Wu**—**Fuchai** was captured.

Thus, those who rely on ghosts/gods will be negligent toward the laws, and those who rely on other states will endanger their own. **Cao** relied on **Qi** yet did not heed **Song**; **Qi** attacked **Pan**, and **Song** destroyed **Cao**.

**Jue** relied on **Wu** yet did not heed **Qi**; **Yue** attacked **Wu**, and **Qi** destroyed **Jing**.

**Xu** relied on **Jue** yet did not heed **Wei**; **Jing** attacked **Song**, and **Wei** destroyed **Xu**.

**Zheng** relied on **Wei** yet did not heed **Han**; **Wei** attacked **Jing**, and **Han** destroyed **Zheng**.

Now, **Han** is a small state yet relies on great states. The ruler is arrogant, heeding **Qin** and **Wei** while relying on **Qi** and **Jing**—yet small states continue to perish.

Thus, relying on others cannot expand territory, yet **Han** does not see this. **Jing** attacked **Wei** and increased its forces in **Xu** and **Yan**; **Qi** attacked **Renhu** and diminished **Wei**—this was not enough to preserve **Zheng**, yet **Han** did not know.

These are all cases of not clarifying their legal prohibitions to govern their states, relying on external forces to destroy their ancestral altars.

Thus, the ministers say: If one understands the principles of governance, even a small state will be wealthy. If rewards and punishments are respected and trusted, even a few people will be strong. If rewards and punishments lack measure, though a state is large its armies will be weak—its land will not be its own, nor its people truly its own.

Without land or people, even **Yao** and **Shun** could not become kings, nor the **Three Dynasties** strong. The ruler again gives excessive rewards, and the ministers again take them without merit.

Those who abandon the laws and speak only of the achievements of ancient sage-kings and wise rulers will be held responsible by the state. Thus, the ministers say: This is the wish to emulate the ancient achievements, yet to reward today’s people with ancient rewards. The ruler gives these excessive rewards, and the ministers take them without merit.

「When the ruler errs in granting rewards, the ministers will take advantage of the situation; when the ministers accept them without merit, their achievements will not be honored. Those without merit who receive rewards will drain the state’s wealth and raise the people’s expectations, while drained wealth and raised expectations will cause the people to withhold their efforts.

Thus, excessive rewards will lose the people’s trust, and excessive punishments will make the people fearless. If rewards are insufficient to encourage, and punishments insufficient to restrain, then even a great state will be in peril.

Therefore it is said: One with little wisdom cannot be entrusted to plan affairs, and one with little loyalty cannot be entrusted to uphold the law. At the Battle of Yánlíng between the King of Chu (荆恭王) and the Duke of Jin (晋厉公), the Chu forces were defeated, and the king wounded. Amid the fierce fighting, the Chu general **Zǐfǎn** (司马子反) grew thirsty and sought drink, while his companion **Shùgǔyáng** (竖谷阳) presented a cup of wine to him. Zǐfǎn said: "Remove it, this is wine!" Shùgǔyáng replied: "No, it is not." Zǐfǎn took and drank it. For he was a man who loved wine, finding it sweet and unable to resist its taste. He drank until intoxicated and fell asleep.

The king wished to resume the battle and sought counsel, summoning Zǐfǎn. But Zǐfǎn feigned illness. The king then drove to inspect him, entering his pavilion and detecting the stench of wine before returning. He declared: "Today’s battle—my own eyes have seen our forces wounded. Our reliance was on the general, but he is thus: this is the destruction of Chu’s state and people without regard for my soldiers! I shall not fight again."

He dismissed the army and withdrew, executing Zǐfǎn as a severe example. Therefore it is said: Shùgǔyáng’s offering of wine was not to distinguish between good and evil, but simply to follow the king’s command. Zǐfǎn, in truth, was rewarded with loyalty and affection, yet this was enough only to bring about his death. This was the case of **xiǎozhōng** (小忠) undermining **dàzhōng** (大忠).

Hence it is said: **Xiǎozhōng** (小忠) is the thief of **dàzhōng** (大忠). If one were to entrust **xiǎozhōng** (小忠) with the law, they would surely pardon crimes, pardoning crimes out of mutual affection—this would bring security to the lower ranks, yet it would disrupt the governance of the people. When **Wèi** (魏) was in the midst of establishing clear laws and strict governance, those with merit were rewarded, and those with crimes were punished. Thus, they grew strong and maintained order throughout the world, their prestige extending to the four neighboring states.

But when their laws were neglected and rewards granted arbitrarily, their state daily diminished. When **Zhào** (赵) was enforcing clear national statutes and leading great armies, their forces were numerous and strong, and they subdued the lands of **Qí** (齐) and **Yàn** (燕). Yet when their national statutes were neglected and those who wielded them grew weak, their state daily diminished.

When **Yàn** (燕) was in the midst of strictly adhering to the law and impartially judging officials, their eastern borders reached **Qí** (齐), and their southern lands extended to **Zhōngshān** (中山). But once their adherence to the law was lost, their officials no longer judged impartially, their courtiers engaged in mutual contention, and judgments followed the lower ranks, then their armies weakened and their lands were carved up by neighboring enemies. Hence it is said: Those who uphold the law grow strong, those who neglect it grow weak. The distinction between strength and weakness is thus clear, yet rulers of the age do not act accordingly—so the ruin of their states is justly deserved.

The saying goes: A family with constant livelihood will not starve, though it may be poor. A state with constant law will not perish, though it may be in danger. If one abandons constant law and follows private intentions, then ministers will embellish their wisdom and abilities. When ministers embellish their wisdom and abilities, legal prohibitions cannot be established.

This is the way of **miào yì** (妄意) prevailing, while the **Dàodé** (道) of governance is discarded. The **Dàodé** (道) of governance lies in removing harmful laws, so as not to be misled by cleverness nor deceived by reputation.

In ancient times, **Shùn** (舜) entrusted his officials to decide the **Hóngshuǐ** (鸿水) flood, first commanding them to act, then executing them for their failure. **Yǔ** (禹) assembled the rulers of the feudal states at **Wèihuì** (会稽), and when the ruler of **Fángfēng** (防风) arrived late, Yǔ beheaded him.
