From this, we see that those who disobeyed the commands were killed, and those who arrived late were executed—thus, the ancients valued the law above all else.

Hence, the **jìng** (镜) holds clarity without bias, and good and evil are measured accordingly. The **héng** (衡) holds balance without bias, and light and heavy are weighed accordingly. To shake the **jìng** (镜) is to lose its clarity, and to shake the **héng** (衡) is to lose its balance—this is the nature of law. Therefore, the ancient sage-rulers took **Dàodé** (道) as their constant principle and law as their foundation. When the foundation is well-ordered, their name is honored; when the foundation is disordered, their name is extinguished.

In general, those with wisdom and ability will act when they have a means, and cease when they lack it. Thus, wisdom and ability alone cannot be passed on to others. But **Dàodé** (道) and law are universally complete—wisdom and ability often fail.

To hang the **héng** (衡) and know equilibrium, to set a **guī** (规) and know roundness—these are the **wànquán** (万全) ways. The enlightened ruler commands the people to adorn their conduct according to **Dàodé** (道), so as to achieve merit without error.

To discard the **guī** (规) and rely on cleverness, to discard the law and rely on wisdom—these are the ways of confusion and disorder. The disordered ruler commands the people to adorn their cleverness, unaware of the **Dàodé** (道), so their labor yields no merit.

To abandon legal prohibitions and listen to petitions, for courtiers to sell their offices to the ruler and take rewards from the people—this is how private gain lies with the ministers and power is concentrated among the courtiers. Hence, the people lack the resolve to serve the ruler with all their strength, instead striving to curry favor with him.

When the people favor currying favor, wealth and resources flow upward, and those who are skilled in flattery are employed. If this is the case, those with merit will grow ever fewer. The wicked ministers advance while the capable retreat, and the ruler becomes confused, not knowing what to do, while the people gather without knowing what to follow. **Dào** (道) is what one follows. This is the failure of abandoning legal prohibitions, neglecting merit, elevating reputation, and listening to petitions.

In general, those who subvert the law will inevitably devise deceit and rely on external things to curry favor, and they will also speak of the rare and extraordinary—this is how tyrants and disordered rulers mislead, and how ministers and worthy advisors are undermined.

Thus, when ministers praise the achievements of **Yīyǐn** (伊尹) and **Guǎn Zhòng** (管仲), they are embellishing cleverness and concealing their own lack of merit. When they praise the loyalty of **Bǐgān** (比干) and **Zǐxù** (子胥), yet these men were executed, they will instead criticize those who strongly advise, finding fault with them.

A ruler who is praised as virtuous and wise, while his ministers are condemned as tyrannical and disordered, cannot be judged by such examples. If this is the case, then prohibitions must be enforced. The ruler establishes laws, believing them to be correct, yet ministers often establish their private cleverness and declare the law to be incorrect—this is to pervert the law with their own wisdom.

From this reasoning, one knows that all human inclinations naturally seek to abandon public law and establish private cleverness.»

「To abandon law and establish private cleverness—such behavior must be prohibited, for it is the Dao of the ruler. To prohibit the Dao of the ruler, one must clearly distinguish between public and private matters, uphold the law, and eliminate private favoritism.

For orders to be carried out and prohibitions to be obeyed is the public *virtue/power* of the ruler. To act on private interests, trusting only in friends, and to make such behavior the standard for rewards and punishments—this is the private *virtue/power* of ministers.

When private *virtue/power* prevails, disorder ensues; when public *virtue/power* prevails, governance is harmonious. Hence, there must be a distinction between public and private.

Ministers possess private desires and public *virtue/power*. When they cultivate their own integrity and act in accordance with public principles and righteousness, when they hold office without self-interest, this is the public *virtue/power* of ministers. When they indulge in corrupt conduct and follow their own desires, when they seek personal comfort and familial gain, this is the private desire of ministers.

A wise ruler governs above, and ministers abandon their private desires to uphold public *virtue/power*. A disorderly ruler governs above, and ministers abandon public *virtue/power* to follow their private desires. Therefore, the ruler and ministers have divergent inclinations.

The ruler uses stratagem to retain his ministers, while the ministers use stratagem to serve their ruler—stratagem is the essence of their interaction. To harm oneself for the benefit of the state, ministers will not do it; to enrich the state for the benefit of ministers, the ruler will not act. The minister’s natural inclination is to avoid harming himself without gain, while the ruler’s natural inclination is to avoid harming the state without reward. Thus, the ruler and ministers are bound by mutual calculation.

When facing peril, they must die; when expending all their wisdom and strength, they do so for the sake of the law. Hence, the ancient kings made rewards clear to encourage the people and enforced severe punishments to instill awe. When rewards and punishments are properly applied, the people will die for the law; when the people die for the law, the military will be strong and the ruler will be honored. When rewards and punishments are not carefully administered, the people will seek gain without merit, escape punishment despite guilt, and thus the military will be weak and the ruler will be humbled.

Therefore, the ancient kings’ worthy ministers exerted all their wisdom and strength. It is said:

*The distinction between public and private must be clear, and the laws and prohibitions must be carefully examined*—this the ancient kings understood well.»

---
*#1 "皆" (jiē) is clearly a mistake for "背" (bèi), corrected according to the Chen Qiyou edition.*
*#2 "利" (lì) is a mistake for "地" (dì), corrected according to the Ling Yongchu edition and the Yu Ping edition.*
*#3 "秦" (qín) does not fit here; it should be "奉" (fèng), corrected according to the Chen Qiyou edition.*
*#4 "若" (ruò) is a mistake for "昔" (xī), corrected according to Chen Qiyou’s explanation.*
*#5 "诸况" (zhūcuàng) is a mistake for "设规" (shèguī), corrected according to Chen Qiyou’s explanation.*
*#6 "即" (jí) is clearly a mistake for "民" (mín), corrected accordingly.*

The **virtue/power** (德, *dé*) is the essence within. The **acquisition** (得, *dé*) is the external manifestation. The **virtue/power** of the Sage does not cling to outward displays—this means that its **spiritual essence** (神, *shén*) does not indulge in external excesses. When the **spiritual essence** does not indulge in external excesses, the body remains intact, and this integrity is called **virtue/power**. **Virtue/power** is thus the preservation of the body. All **virtue/power** arises from **non-action** (无为, *wúwéi*), is perfected through **non-desire** (无欲, *wúyù*), is secured by **non-contemplation** (不思, *bùsī*), and is established by **non-dependence** (不用, *bùyòng*).

If one acts or desires, **virtue/power** will not be sustained, and **virtue/power** unsustained means it is incomplete. If one relies upon or contemplates, it will not be firm, and firmness lost means no achievement, no achievement means **virtue/power** will perish. When **virtue/power** perishes, there is no **virtue/power**; when there is no **virtue/power**, it is within the realm of **having virtue/power**. Hence it is said: *The **virtue/power** of the Sage does not cling to outward displays, and thus it possesses **virtue/power**.* The reason why **non-action** and **non-contemplation** are esteemed as **emptiness** (虚, *xū*) is that their **intent** (意, *yì*) is unrestrained.

Those who lack **skill** (术, *shù*) mistakenly regard **non-action** and **non-contemplation** as **emptiness**. But those who mistakenly regard **non-action** and **non-contemplation** as **emptiness** are always mindful of **emptiness**, thus they are constrained by the notion of **emptiness**. **Emptiness** means that the **intent** is not constrained by anything. Now, if one is constrained by the notion of **emptiness**, then one is not truly **empty**. The **emptiness** of the **virtue/power** of the Sage lies in its **non-action** without fixating on **non-action** as a constant. When **non-action** is not fixated upon as a constant, then it is **empty**, and **emptiness** brings forth abundant **virtue/power**—this is the **virtue/power** of the Sage. Hence it is said: *The **virtue/power** of the Sage is **non-action** yet nothing remains undone.*

**Ren** (仁, *rén*) refers to the joyful **intent** within to love others. It delights in others’ blessings and abhors others’ misfortunes, arising naturally from the heart without seeking recompense. Hence it is said: *The **Ren** of the Sage acts yet does nothing to demand.*

**Righteousness** (义, *yì*) is the proper conduct between superiors and inferiors, the distinctions of parent and child, the bonds of trusted friends, and the divisions of near and distant, inner and outer. Serving the lord should be proper, inferiors should be harmonious with superiors, children should serve their parents properly, the lowly should respect the noble, trusted friends should aid one another, and those close should be treated as inner while those distant as outer. **Righteousness** is thus the principle of propriety. When propriety is followed, it is called **righteousness**, and hence it is said: *The **Righteousness** of the Sage acts yet does nothing to reject.*

**Rites** (礼, *lǐ*) are the outward expressions of **intent**, the refined forms of **righteousness**, the exchanges between lord and subject, parent and child, and the distinctions among noble, worthy, and unworthy. When the **intent** is heartfelt yet not understood, one hastens with humility and reverence to clarify it. When love is genuine yet not recognized, one uses elaborate words to confirm it. **Rites** are the outward forms that clarify the inner **intent**. Hence it is said: *Rites are the outward expressions of **intent**.*

Generally, people are moved by external things, unaware that these are the **rites** of their own bodies. Commoners observe **rites** to honor others, thus their **rites** wax and wane with time. The **Dao/Way** (道, *dào*) of the **Noble Person** (君子, *jūnzǐ*) is to observe **rites** for oneself. When **rites** are observed for oneself, the **spiritual essence** becomes the highest **rites**, and though commoners may falter, the Sage’s repeated acts of reverence and respect extend even to the **rites** of hands and feet. Hence it is said: *The Sage extends his arms to uphold them.*

The **Dao/Way** accumulates into **virtue/power**, and **virtue/power** manifests as **achievement** (功, *gōng*). **Achievement** is substantial, and substance radiates **luster** (光, *guāng*). **Ren** is the **luster** of **virtue/power**. **Luster** spreads as **benefit** (泽, *zé*), and **benefit** is realized through **action** (事, *shì*). **Righteousness** is the **action** of **Ren**. **Action** is refined by **rites**, and **rites** are adorned by **ornament** (文, *wén*). **Rites** are the **ornament** of **righteousness**. Hence it is said: *When the **Dao/Way** is lost, then **virtue/power** is lost; when **virtue/power** is lost, then **Ren** is lost; when **Ren** is lost, then **righteousness** is lost; when **righteousness** is lost, then **rites** are lost.*

**Rites** are the outward expressions of **intent**. **Ornament** is the refinement of substance. The **Noble Person** seeks **intent** and discards outward expressions, prefers substance and abhors refinement. Those who rely on outward expressions to judge **intent** have a corrupt **intent**. Those who demand refinement to assess substance have a decaying substance. How can this be proven?

The **Jade of the He Family** (和氏之璧, *Héshì zhī bì*) was not adorned with five colors, and the **Pearl of the Duke of Sui** (隋侯之珠, *Suíhóu zhī zhū*) was not set with silver and gold. Their substance was supremely beautiful, and no external adornment could enhance them. Things that require adornment to be valued have inherently flawed substance. Thus, between parent and child, the **rites** are simple yet not fully illuminated, and it is said: *The **rites** are sparse.*

All things cannot flourish simultaneously—such is the **balance** (并, *bìng*) of **yin** and **yang**. Reason gives and takes—such is the interplay of **authority** (威, *wēi*) and **virtue/power** (德, *dé*). Where substance is abundant, outward expressions are sparse—the **rites** between parent and child are such. From this perspective, **rites** that are elaborate indicate a decaying substance. Therefore, those who practice **rites** are addressing the simple **intent** of others. When commoners observe **rites**, others comply with light joy, but when they do not, resentment and blame arise. Now, if one practices **rites** to address the simple **intent** of others yet also provides them with grounds for blame, how can conflict be avoided?

Conflict leads to disorder, and hence it is said: ***Rites** are the superficiality of loyalty and trust, yet they are the origin of disorder.* Those who possess **foresight** (前识, *qiánshí*) act before things unfold and move before reason demands—this is **unprompted** (无缘, *wúyuán*) **forgetting** (忘, *wàng*) the **measure** (度, *duó*) of **intent**. How can this be proven?

**Zhan He** sat in meditation, his disciples attending. A cow bellowed outside the gate, and a disciple remarked: *"It is a black cow with a white calf."*

Zhan He said: *"Indeed, it is a black cow, but the white is on its horns. If one were to examine it, it would indeed be a black cow with its horns wrapped in cloth. Using Zhanzi’s method to probe the common mind is perilously superficial—thus it is said: **‘The superficiality of the Dao.’**

Try releasing Zhanzi’s keen perception and instead let a five-foot-tall ignorant child observe it—he too would know it is a black cow with its horns wrapped in cloth. Thus, Zhanzi’s perception, though laboring over the mind and exhausting the spirit, ultimately yields the same result as the child’s—hence it is said: **‘The origin of folly.’**

Therefore, it is said: **‘Foresight is the superficiality of the Dao and the origin of folly.’**

What is called a **‘great man’** refers to the vastness of his wisdom. What is meant by **‘dwelling in substance, not in superficiality’** is to act in accordance with reality while discarding mere formality. What is meant by **‘dwelling in reality, not in superficiality’** is to follow principle without abrupt severance. What is meant by **‘rejecting that and taking this’** is to abandon formality and abrupt severance while embracing principle and genuine sincerity. Hence it is said: **‘Reject that and take this.’**

When a man faces misfortune, his heart is filled with fear and dread. Fear and dread make his conduct upright. Upright conduct sharpens his deliberation. Sharp deliberation reveals the truth of things. Upright conduct avoids misfortune. Avoiding misfortune fulfills one’s natural lifespan. Grasping the truth of things ensures success. Fulfilling one’s natural lifespan preserves life and longevity. Ensuring success brings wealth and honor. Preserving life, longevity, wealth, and honor—this is **‘blessing.’** Yet blessing originates from misfortune, hence it is said: **‘Misfortune! The foundation of blessing.’** To achieve success.

When a man attains blessing, wealth and honor come. Wealth and honor bring fine clothing and food. Fine clothing and food breed arrogance. Arrogance leads to perverse actions and abandonment of principle. Perverse actions bring untimely death. Abandoning principle ensures failure. To suffer internal ruin and external disgrace while lacking success—this is great misfortune. Yet misfortune originates from blessing, hence it is said: **‘Blessing! The hidden seed of misfortune.’**

Those who follow principle in their actions achieve nothing they cannot accomplish. Those who follow principle can attain the supreme authority of a sovereign’s exalted status, while even the smallest efforts yield the rewards of ministerial offices, generalships, and stipends. Those who abandon principle and forget proper conduct—even if they possess the exalted status of a sovereign or noble and the wealth of Yi Dun, Tao Zhu, or diviners—will still lose their people and forfeit their resources. The common folk lightly discard principle and easily forget proper conduct, unaware of how profound and vast the depths of misfortune and blessing truly are. Hence it is said: **‘Who knows its ultimate measure?’**

No one does not desire wealth, honor, preserved life, and longevity, yet none can escape the misfortunes of poverty, lowliness, untimely death, and disgrace. Two desires—wealth, honor, preserved life, and longevity—yet now they suffer poverty, lowliness, untimely death, and disgrace. This is failing to reach what one desires. To lose the path to one’s desires and act recklessly—this is called **‘delusion.’** Delusion means failing to reach what one desires. That the common folk cannot reach what they desire has been so since the division of heaven and earth until today—hence it is said: **‘The delusion of man is ancient indeed.’**

What is called **‘rectitude’** is the harmony of inner and outer, speech and action in accord. What is called **‘frugality’** is the preservation of life and death’s command, the light use of wealth. What is called **‘uprightness’** is that justice must be impartial, the heart unyielding to partiality. What is called **‘radiance’** is noble office, splendid robes, and majestic attire.

Yet a man of the Dao, though internally and externally trusted and obedient, does not suffer slander or ruin; though he dies with integrity and spends lightly, he is not shamed by insult or greedy for gain; though his conduct is just and impartial, he does not err by favoring the wicked or the private; though his status is exalted and his attire splendid, he does not boast over the lowly or deceive the poor.

Why is this? If those lost in error were willing to listen, study, and inquire, they would not remain deluded. Yet the common folk, though desiring success, instead court failure because they do not know principle and refuse to inquire or listen to those who do. The common folk refuse to inquire or listen, yet the Sage compels them with the consequences of their ruin—then they resent. The common folk are many, the Sage few; few cannot overcome many—this is the way of numbers.

To act in opposition to the world is not the path of preserving life and longevity—thus one must regulate conduct and act accordingly. Hence it is said: **‘Rectitude without harshness, frugality without mutilation, uprightness without excess, radiance without dazzle.’**

Hearing and insight are from Heaven. Movement, stillness, thought, and deliberation are from man. Man, relying on Heaven’s clarity to see, Heaven’s hearing to listen, Heaven’s wisdom to think—if vision is strained, the eyes grow dim; if hearing is overtaxed, the ears grow dull; if thought is excessive, wisdom becomes confused. Dim eyes cannot distinguish black from white. Dull ears cannot separate clear from murky sounds. Confused wisdom cannot discern the grounds of gain and loss. Dim eyes are called **‘blindness’**; dull ears, **‘deafness’**; confused wisdom, **‘madness.’** Blindness cannot avoid the dangers of daylight. Deafness cannot perceive the harm of thunder. Madness cannot escape the calamities of human laws.

What the *Classics* call **‘governing men’** is to moderate movement and stillness, to curb excessive deliberation. What is called **‘serving Heaven’** is not to exhaust the limits of hearing and insight, nor to overburden wisdom’s capacity. If one exhausts them, the spirit is overtaxed; overtaxing the spirit invites the calamities of blindness, deafness, rebellion, and madness—thus one must **‘be frugal.’** To be frugal is to cherish the spirit and conserve wisdom.

Thus it is said: **"To govern men and serve Heaven, nothing surpasses frugality."**

The multitude’s use of **qi** is frantic; frantic use depletes **qi** greatly, and great depletion is called **‘excess’**. The Sage’s use of **qi** is tranquil; tranquil use conserves **qi** sparingly, and sparing conservation is called **‘frugality’**. **Frugality** is the **Dao/Way** that arises from **‘principle and reason’**; he who can practice **frugality** is one who follows the **Dao/Way** and submits to **reason**.

The multitude avoids calamity yet falls into disaster, still unaware of retreat, and does not submit to **principle and reason**. The Sage, though he has not yet seen the form of calamity or disaster, emptily and subtly submits to **principle and reason** in advance—thus it is said: **"He who practices frugality is one who submits in advance."**

He who knows **‘to govern men’** keeps his deliberations tranquil; he who knows **‘to serve Heaven’** keeps his senses empty. Tranquil deliberation ensures **virtue/power** does not depart. Empty senses allow harmonious **qi** to enter daily. Thus it is said: **"Accumulate **virtue/power** heavily."**

He who can ensure **virtue/power** does not depart and harmonious **qi** arrives daily is one who submits in advance. Thus it is said: **"To submit in advance is to accumulate **virtue/power** heavily."**

Accumulate **virtue/power** first, then **qi** becomes tranquil; **qi** tranquil, then harmony grows; harmony grows, then plans succeed; plans succeed, then one can command all things. Commanding all things makes war easy and victory certain; victory certain and discourse unmatched in the world, thus it is said: **"There is nothing he cannot conquer."**

There is nothing he cannot conquer because of heavy accumulation of **virtue/power**, thus it is said: **"Heavy accumulation of **virtue/power** means there is nothing he cannot conquer."**

War easy and victory certain means he can encompass the world; discourse unmatched means the people submit. To advance and encompass the world while in retreat to follow the people—his methods are distant, and thus the multitude cannot see their beginning or end. If they cannot see their beginning or end, then they cannot know his limits, thus it is said: **"There is nothing he cannot conquer, so his limits are unknown."**

In general, one may have a state yet lose it, have a life yet suffer misfortune—this cannot be called truly possessing the state or preserving one’s life. He who truly possesses the state must be able to stabilize its **society and altars** (*shèjì*); he who truly preserves his life must be able to complete his natural years—only then can it be said he truly possesses the state and preserves his life.

He who truly possesses the state and preserves his life must embody the **Dao/Way**; embodying the **Dao/Way** deepens his wisdom, and deep wisdom extends his foresight, so distant that the multitude cannot see his ultimate limits. Only Heaven can make one unaware of the limits of his undertakings; one unaware of these limits is truly able to preserve his life and possess his state, thus it is said: **"His limits are unknown."**

His limits unknown means he can possess the state. What is called **"the origin of possessing a state"**—the origin, that is, the **Dao/Way**—the **Dao/Way** that gives rise to the methods of possessing a state, hence it is called **"the origin of possessing a state."**

The **Dao/Way** that interacts harmoniously with the world—its establishment is enduring, its blessings long-lasting. Thus it is said: **"The origin of possessing a state can endure long."**

Trees have **‘flexible roots’** and **‘straight roots’**. Roots are what the *Classics* call **"foundation"** (*dǐ*). **Foundation** is what allows a tree to establish its growth. **Flexible roots** are what sustain a tree’s life. **Virtue/power** is what allows man to establish his growth. **Blessings** are what sustain man’s life.

Now, if one establishes himself in **reason**, his blessings will endure long, thus it is said: **"Deepen his roots."** If one embodies the **Dao/Way**, his life will be long, thus it is said: **"Strengthen his foundation."** Foundation strengthened means life prolonged; roots deepened means vision lasting, thus it is said: **"Deepening his roots and strengthening his foundation is the **Dao/Way** of long life and lasting vision."**

A craftsman who often changes his occupation loses his skill; a builder who frequently shifts his work loses his achievement. One man’s work, half lost each day, in ten days loses five men’s worth. Ten thousand men’s work, half lost each day, in ten days loses five thousand men’s worth.

Thus, the more one changes his occupation, the greater the loss, the more the depletion. In general, changing laws shifts benefits and harms; benefits and harms shifting means the people’s duties change—changing duties is called **‘changing occupations’**.

Hence, from the perspective of **reason**, to govern the multitude while frequently altering it leads to little success; to store great treasures while frequently moving them invites many failures; to cook small fish while constantly stirring them ruins their flavor; to govern a great state while frequently changing laws brings suffering to the people.

Therefore, the ruler who embodies the **Dao/Way** values tranquility and does not often change laws, thus it is said: **"To govern a great state is like cooking a small fish."**

When men are sick, they value physicians; when disaster strikes, they fear ghosts. When the Sage rules above, the people desire little; little desire means their **blood and **qi**** are harmonious and their movements follow **reason**; movements following **reason** mean little calamity.

He who has no internal harm from **‘ulcers, boils, or festering sores’** (*zhāojūyànzhì*) and no external harm from punishments or legal executions—his fear of ghosts is very light, thus it is said: **"When the **Dao/Way** is applied to rule the world, its ghosts are not formidable."**

The people of a well-ordered age do not harm ghosts and spirits, thus it is said: **"It is not that the ghosts are not formidable, but that the spirits do not harm men."**

Ghosts harming men is called **‘haunting’** (*suì*); men driving them away is called **‘injuring ghosts’**. The people breaking laws is called **‘the people injuring the ruler’**; the ruler punishing the people is called **‘the ruler injuring the people’**. If the people do not break laws, the ruler also does not execute punishments; the ruler not executing punishments is called **‘the ruler not harming the people’**.

Thus it is said: **"The Sage also does not harm the people."** The ruler does not harm the people, and the people do not harm the ghosts, thus it is said: **"Both do not harm each other."**

If the people dare not break laws, then the ruler internally does not need to rely on punishments, and externally does not need to exploit their wealth. The ruler internally not relying on punishments and externally not exploiting their wealth means the people multiply and thrive. The people multiplying and thriving with abundant stores—this is called **‘having **virtue/power**’**.

In general, what is called **‘haunting’** is when the **soul and vital essence** (*húnpò*) depart and the **spirit and **qi**** become disordered; **spirit and **qi**** disordered means there is no **virtue/power**. Ghosts not haunting men means the **soul and vital essence** do not depart, and the **spirit and **qi**** do not become disordered; **spirit and **qi**** not disordered is called **‘having **virtue/power**’**.

The ruler amasses abundant stores, yet the ghosts do not disturb the people’s **spirit and qi**, then **virtue/power** is fully with the people.

Thus it is said: **“Both do not harm each other, then mutual harmony is achieved.”** It speaks of **virtue/power** flourishing above and below, returning together to the people.

A **Dao/Way**-governed ruler has no grievances or feuds (*yuānchóu*) with neighboring enemies, yet within he bestows **virtue/power** and blessings upon the people. When there are no grievances with neighboring enemies, his treatment of the feudal lords is marked by external propriety and righteousness. When he bestows **virtue/power** upon the people, his governance focuses on the essentials. Treating feudal lords with propriety and righteousness means few military campaigns arise; governing the people by attending to the essentials means licentiousness and extravagance cease.

In general, horses are used greatly for two purposes: externally to supply armor and weapons, and internally to indulge in licentiousness and extravagance. Now, a **Dao/Way**-governed ruler seeks minimal use of armor and weapons externally, yet restrains licentiousness and extravagance internally. The ruler does not drive horses in battle or chase fleeing foes, nor do the people use horses to indulge in distant licentiousness or frivolous pursuits. Their accumulated strength lies solely in the fields, and the fields are cultivated with manure and irrigation—thus it is said: **“When the **Dao/Way** prevails in the world, horses are turned back to feed the fields.”**

If a ruler lacks the **Dao/Way**, then internally he tyrannizes and oppresses his people, while externally he invades and deceives his neighboring states. Internal tyranny exhausts the people’s resources, and external aggression provokes frequent wars. When resources are exhausted, livestock dwindles; when wars multiply, soldiers are depleted. Livestock dwindling means warhorses are scarce, and soldiers depleted means the army is in grave danger. Warhorses scarce, the ruler sends his own steeds to battle; the army in peril, he conscripts his close ministers. Horses, being the army’s greatest asset, and the term *郊* (*jiāo*) denoting proximity—now, the means to supply the army’s needs comes from the ruler’s own steeds and close ministers—thus it is said: **“When the **Dao/Way** is absent in the world, warhorses are born in the suburbs.”**

If a man has desires, his plans and gatherings become disordered; when plans and gatherings are disordered, his desires grow more extreme. When desires become extreme, wicked thoughts prevail; when wicked thoughts prevail, his undertakings grow reckless and are abandoned. Reckless abandonment of undertakings brings misfortune and calamity. Thus, observing this, misfortune and calamity arise from wicked thoughts, and wicked thoughts are provoked by what is desirable.

The types of what is desirable—when pursued, they corrupt good people into wickedness; when avoided, they bring calamity to the virtuous. When wickedness arises, the ruler invades weak states; when calamity strikes, the people suffer greatly. Therefore, the types of what is desirable cause the ruler to invade weak states and harm the people.

To invade weak states and harm the people—this is a grave offense. Thus it is said: **“No calamity is greater than what is desirable.”**

Hence, the sage does not indulge in the five colors, nor does he wallow in music and song. A wise ruler despises frivolous luxuries and rejects excessive ornamentation. Since humans lack fur or feathers, if they are not clothed, they will suffer from cold. The ruler does not cling to heaven, nor does he attach to the earth; his vital roots are his intestines and stomach. If he does not eat, he cannot live. Therefore, the heart of desire for profit cannot be avoided; if this desire is not removed, it will bring him suffering.

Thus the sage dresses sufficiently to ward off cold and eats sufficiently to fill his emptiness, then he has no suffering. Common people are not so—when they possess vast wealth as feudal lords or even a small surplus of thousands in gold, their suffering from desires is never removed. All are enslaved to it; those condemned to death are spared, yet those who do not know contentment suffer throughout their lives. Thus it is said: **“No calamity is greater than not knowing contentment.”**

Desire for profit is more severe than suffering; suffering brings illness, and illness causes wisdom and insight to decline. When wisdom and insight decline, he loses moderation and measure; when he loses moderation and measure, he acts rashly and recklessly. Rash and reckless actions lead to calamity and harm, and when calamity and harm strike, they breed within him. When they breed within him, his external suffering grows thin, yet pain and torment mix within his intestines and stomach. When pain and torment mix within his intestines and stomach, they weaken his person; weakened, he withdraws and blames himself. Withdrawal and self-blame arise from desire for profit—thus it is said: **“No blame is more severe than desire for profit.”**

The **Dao/Way** is the principle (*lǐ*) by which all things exist, and the standard by which all affairs are measured. Affairs (*lǐ*) are the patterns that complete and define all things.

The **Dao/Way** is that by which all things are completed. Therefore it is said: The **Dao/Way** is the principle (*lǐ*) that governs all things.

Things possess principles that cannot be violated; since things possess principles that cannot be violated, principles thus become the measure by which things are regulated. All things have their distinct principles, yet the **Dao/Way** exhaustively examines all principles of things, hence it must transform. Since it must transform, it has no fixed operation. Without fixed operation, life and death arise within its qi, all wisdom weighs its measures, and all affairs wax and wane. Heaven obtains it and thereby attains height; Earth obtains it and thereby attains concealment; the Big Dipper obtains it and thereby establishes its majesty; the Sun and Moon obtain it and thereby maintain their luminosity; the Five Phases obtain it and thereby maintain their positions; the stars obtain it and thereby align their courses; the Four Seasons obtain it and thereby govern their shifting qi; the Yellow Emperor obtained it and thereby dominated the four quarters; the Red Pine obtained it and thereby harmonized with Heaven and Earth; the Sage obtained it and thereby perfected culture and rites.

The **Dao/Way** shares wisdom with Yao and Shun, shares madness with Jieyu, shares destruction with Jie and Zhou, and shares prosperity with Tang and Wu. When it seems near, it roams the four extremities; when it seems far, it abides at my side. When it seems obscure, its light is radiant; when it seems bright, its essence is murky. Yet it accomplishes the work of Heaven and Earth, harmonizes transformations like thunder and lightning, and all things within the cosmos rely upon it to be completed.

The nature of the **Dao/Way** is such that without regulation, it takes no form; it is soft and weak, adapting to the times, responding to principles. All things obtain it and thereby die, obtain it and thereby live; all affairs obtain it and thereby fail, obtain it and thereby succeed. The **Dao/Way** is like water: the drowning drink it and die, the thirsty drink it and live. It is like swords and spears: the foolish use them in wrath and bring calamity, the Sage uses them to punish tyranny and brings blessings. Thus, obtaining it leads to death, obtaining it leads to life, obtaining it leads to failure, obtaining it leads to success.

People rarely see a living elephant, yet they obtain the bones of a dead one and study its form to imagine its life; thus, what all people conceive in their minds is called its "symbol." Now, though the **Dao/Way** cannot be heard or seen, the Sage observes its effects and thereby perceives its form, hence it is said: "The form without form, the symbol without substance."

Principles are the distinctions of square and round, short and long, coarse and fine, firm and brittle. Thus, when principles are established, only then can things obtain the **Dao/Way**. Therefore, established principles entail existence and nonexistence, life and death, flourishing and decline. If a thing exists and then perishes, now dies and now lives, first flourishes and then declines, it cannot be called constant. Only that which exists alongside Heaven and Earth’s division and does not die or decline when Heaven and Earth disperse is called constant. Yet constant is unchanging, without fixed principles; without fixed principles, it is not within the constant. Thus, it cannot be spoken of. The Sage observes its mysterious void, employs its pervasive operation, and forcefully names it the **Dao/Way**; yet it can be discussed, hence it is said: "The **Dao/Way** that can be spoken of is not the eternal **Dao/Way**."

Human beings begin in life and end in death. The beginning is called emergence, the end is called return, hence it is said: "Emerging from life, returning to death." The human body has three hundred and sixty joints, four limbs, and nine orifices—these are its major components. The four limbs and nine orifices, thirteen in total, all their movements and stasis belong to life. To belong is to be its attendant, hence it is said: "The thirteen attendants of life." When death comes, these thirteen components all return and belong to death; death’s attendants are also thirteen, hence it is said: "The thirteen attendants of life, the thirteen attendants of death."

For those who live and live, motion is inherent; motion exhausted leads to depletion, yet motion does not cease, hence depletion does not cease, and when depletion ceases, life ends—this is called death. Thus, the thirteen components all become the domain of death. Hence it is said: "The people’s life is life and motion, motion all leads to the domain of death, to the thirteen."

Thus, the Sage cherishes spirit and values stillness, for this is far greater than the harm of rhinoceros and tigers. Rhinoceros and tigers have boundaries, their movements and stasis have their times; avoiding their boundaries and observing their times, one escapes the harm of rhinoceros and tigers. People know only of the claws and horns of rhinoceros and tigers, yet do not know that all things possess claws and horns. They do not escape the harm of all things.

How can this be explained? When timely rains gather and the wilderness lies still, yet one violates the mountains and rivers at dawn or dusk, the claws and horns of rhinoceros and tigers harm them. When one serves superiors disloyally and lightly violates prohibitions, the claws and horns of punishments and laws harm them. When one conducts oneself in the countryside without moderation and lacks measured affection, the claws and horns of strife harm them. When one indulges desires without limit and lacks moderation in movement and stasis, the claws and horns of sores and abscesses harm them. When one favors private cleverness and abandons principles, the claws and horns of nets and snares harm them.

Rhinoceros and tigers have boundaries, yet all harms have origins; avoiding their boundaries and blocking their origins, one escapes all harms. Armies and warfare are meant to prepare for harm. Even those who value life, when entering the military, must have no wrathful or contentious hearts; without wrathful or contentious hearts, there is no need for preparations against harm. This is not only for those stationed in the wilderness: the Sage moves through the world without harming others, and without harming others, there will surely be no one harming them. Without anyone harming them, there is no need to prepare against others, hence it is said: "Traveling on land, one does not encounter rhinoceros and tigers."

Entering the mountains without relying on defenses to avoid harm, hence it is said: "Entering the military without donning armor and weapons." Distancing oneself from all harms, hence it is said: "The rhinoceros has no place to thrust its horn, the tiger has no place to strike its claw, weapons have no place to sharpen their blades." Without defenses, there will surely be no harm—this is the principle of Heaven and Earth. Embracing the **Dao/Way** of Heaven and Earth, hence it is said: "There is no domain of death."

Moving without encountering peril, this is called skillful life-nurturing.

Those who love their children are tender toward them, those who cherish life are tender toward themselves, and those who value deeds are tender toward their tasks.

A loving mother toward her weak child strives to bring her fortune, and striving to bring fortune removes her misfortunes. Removing misfortunes requires careful deliberation, and careful deliberation grasps the principles of affairs. Grasping the principles of affairs ensures success, and success means acting without doubt. Without doubt is called courage.

The Sage toward all affairs acts as tenderly as a loving mother toward her weak child, thus perceiving the Dao/Way that must be acted upon.

Perceiving the Dao/Way that must be acted upon brings clarity, and in undertaking affairs, there is no doubt—without doubt is called courage.

Courage arises from tenderness, hence it is said: *"Tender, hence able to be courageous."*

The Duke of Zhou said: *"If the winter’s frost does not solidify, then the spring and summer’s growth of plants will not flourish."* Heaven and Earth cannot always indulge in excess, much less can mortals.

Thus, all things must have periods of flourishing and decline, all affairs must have times of relaxation and tension, nations must alternate between civil and martial governance, and official administration must employ rewards and punishments.

Therefore, the wise who frugally use their wealth will have prosperous households, the Sage who cherishes their vital essence will have abundant vitality, and the ruler who values their soldiers will have a populous people.

A populous people means a broad realm, hence it is said: *"Frugal, hence able to broaden."*

All shaped things are easily measured and cut.

How so? Shape brings length and breadth, length and breadth bring size and smallness, size and smallness bring square and round, square and round bring firmness and fragility, firmness and fragility bring lightness and heaviness, lightness and heaviness bring whiteness and blackness.

Length and breadth, size and smallness, square and round, firmness and fragility, lightness and heaviness, whiteness and blackness—these are called *principles*. Principles established, things are easily measured.

Thus, speaking in grand assembly before acting ensures success—this the skilled deliberators know.

To create squares and circles while conforming to compass and set-square, then all affairs take form.

Yet all things have their compass and set-square; those who deliberate know to calculate these principles.

The Sage fully conforms to the principles of all things, hence it is said: *"Dare not lead the world."* Dare not lead the world, then all undertakings succeed, all deeds prosper, and deliberations surpass the age. To avoid great office without achieving it is impossible.

Holding great office is called *completing affairs and leading others*, hence it is said: *"Dare not lead the world, hence able to complete affairs and lead others."*

Those tender toward their children dare not deprive them of clothing and food, those tender toward themselves dare not abandon laws and measures, and those tender toward squares and circles dare not abandon compass and set-square.

Thus, facing soldiers with tenderness toward officers and soldiers ensures victory over enemies, and tenderness toward tools and weapons ensures fortified defenses.

Hence it is said: *"Tender in war, hence victorious; in defense, hence secure."*

Those who preserve themselves while fully conforming to the principles of all things must surely be *Heaven-born*. Heaven-born refers to the birth of the mind.

Thus, the Dao/Way of the world is fully realized in life, as if guarded by tenderness.

Ensuring all is complete and all actions are fitting is called *treasure*. Hence it is said: *"I have three treasures; hold them as treasures."*

The *Book* speaks of the *Great Dao* as the *Rectified Dao*, of *proper application* as the *Perverse Dao*, and of *grandeur* as *ornate beauty*. Ornate beauty is the division of the Perverse Dao.

When court dress becomes extravagant, lawsuits proliferate. Lawsuits proliferate, then fields lie fallow; fields lie fallow, then granaries empty; granaries empty, then the nation impoverishes. When the nation impoverishes and customs become licentious and extravagant, the arts of clothing and food perish, and people cannot help but resort to deceitful cleverness—knowing to adorn cleverness is called *wearing ornate attire*.

When lawsuits proliferate, granaries empty, and licentiousness becomes custom, the nation is wounded as if struck by a sharp blade. Hence it is said: *"Wearing a sharp blade."*

Those who adorn their cunning to this extent inevitably harm the nation, yet their private households grow rich. Private households grow rich, hence it is said: *"Wealth in goods remains."*

If a nation becomes thus, even the foolish cannot help but imitate their schemes, and petty thieves arise.

From this, great deceit spawns petty thieves in its wake—great deceit leads, petty thieves follow. The *suona* is the longest of the five tones, so when the *suona* leads, bells and strings follow; when the *suona* sings, all music harmonizes.

Now, when great deceit arises, the common people follow; when the common people follow, petty thieves harmonize. Thus, wearing ornate attire, wielding sharp blades, indulging in food and drink, while wealth in goods remains—this is called *the thief who plays the suona*.

No one, wise or foolish, lacks inclinations and aversions. Tranquility and simplicity know the origins of calamity and fortune.

Obtaining through desires and being alarmed by licentious things leads to chaos. This is so because external things lure, and indulgence in pleasures disrupts.

Tranquility and simplicity have the meaning of inclinations and aversions; peace and security know the calculations of calamity and fortune.

Yet now pleasures disrupt them, external things lure them, and when lured, they go—hence it is said: *"Plucked."*

The Sage is not thus. Establishing one’s inclinations and aversions, even seeing desired things cannot lure them—unable to lure is called *unplucked*. Unified in one’s nature, even with desirable things, the spirit does not move—when the spirit does not move, it is called *unpleased*.

Those who are descendants should embody this Dao to guard the ancestral temples. The ancestral temples not perishing is called *unbroken sacrifices*.

The body accumulates vitality as virtue, the family accumulates wealth as virtue, and the village, nation, and world all take the people as virtue.

Now, if governing the body and external things cannot disrupt the spirit, hence it is said: *"Cultivate the self, and its virtue becomes true."*

True virtue is the firm foundation of caution.

To govern a family, useless things cannot sway one’s plans, and thus resources remain abundant—hence it is said: *"Cultivate the family, and its virtue becomes abundant."*

Those who govern a village practice this moderation, and thus the families with abundance grow more numerous—hence it is said: *"Cultivate the village, and its virtue becomes enduring."*

Those who govern a state practice this moderation, and thus the villages with virtue grow more numerous—hence it is said: *"Cultivate the state, and its virtue becomes flourishing."*

Those who rule the world practice this moderation, and thus the people’s lives all receive its blessings—hence it is said: *"Cultivate the world, and its virtue becomes universal."*

Those who cultivate the self use this to distinguish between the noble and the petty.
Those who govern villages and states, and rule the world, each apply this principle to examine gains and losses—never failing one in a thousand—hence it is said:
*"Observe the self to understand the self,
Observe the family to understand the family,
Observe the village to understand the village,
Observe the state to understand the state,
Observe the world to understand the world.
How do I know the world is thus?
It is by this."*

**Chapter 21: Analogy of the Old**

When the world is in harmony, there are no urgent calamities—this is called *stillness*.
Horses are not driven, and thus it is said: *"Drive away the warhorses and use them for dung."*

When the world is in chaos, attacks never cease, and armies besiege for years without end.
Armor grows with lice and fleas, swallows nest in the pavilions, yet the soldiers do not return—
hence it is said: *"Warhorses breed in the suburbs."*

The **Zhì people** presented a pelt of the *Fēng fú* and a *Xuán bào* to Duke Wen of Jin.
Duke Wen accepted the gifts and sighed: *"This is using beauty to bring ruin upon oneself."*

A ruler who governs a state uses titles and honors as ruin—such was **Xu Yuanwang**.
A ruler who uses cities and lands as ruin—such were **Yu and Guo**.
Hence it is said: *"No guilt is greater than desiring what is desirable."*

**Zhì Bó** annexed **Fàn** and **Zhōngxíng**, then attacked **Zhào** relentlessly.
**Hán** and **Wèi** rebelled, defeated his army at **Jinyáng**, and he died east of **Gāoliáng**.
His state was partitioned, his head pickled as a drinking vessel—
hence it is said: *"No misfortune is greater than not knowing when to stop."*

The ruler of **Yú** sought to bend **Chǎn Chóu’s** chariot and take **Chuíjí’s** jade,
ignoring **Gōngyǒu’s** advice—
thus his state perished and he died—
hence it is said: *"No blame is more bitter than greed."*

A state’s survival is its norm—thus hegemony is possible.
Life is the norm for the body—thus wealth and honor are possible.
If one does not harm oneself, the state will not perish, and life will not end—
hence it is said: *"Knowing sufficiency is true sufficiency."*

After **Zhuāng Wáng of Chu** hunted triumphantly at **Hé Yōng**,
he rewarded **Sūn Shū’ào**, who requested the barren land between the **Hán** rivers,
a place of sand and stones.
By Chu’s law, land was reclaimed after two generations of officials—
yet **Sūn Shū’ào** alone retained his.
This was not for his own gain, but for the state’s—
thus his descendants were revered for nine generations without interruption.
Hence it is said: *"The well-established cannot be uprooted,
the well-embraced cannot be abandoned.
Thus, descendants preserve their sacrifices eternally."*
This is **Sūn Shū’ào**.

What is held firmly is *weighty*; what does not leave its place is *still*.
The weighty can command the light; the still can command the restless.
Hence it is said: *"Weight is the root of lightness,
stillness is the lord of restlessness."*
Hence it is said: *"The noble carries their baggage all day."*

A state is the sovereign’s baggage.
**Zhǔ Fǔ** abandoned his state—
thus, though he had the joys of **Dài** and **Yúnzhōng**,
he was already detached from **Zhào**.
**Zhǔ Fǔ**, a lord of ten thousand chariots,
made himself light to the world—
lightness is lack of power, restlessness is abandoning one’s place—
thus he died in exile.
Hence it is said: *"Lightness loses ministers,
restlessness loses the sovereign."*
This is **Zhǔ Fǔ**.

Power is the sovereign’s abyss.
A ruler’s power is heavier than that of ministers—
once lost, it cannot be reclaimed.
**Jian Gong of Qi** lost it to **Tián Chéng**,
**Jin Gong** lost it to the **Six Ministers**—
thus their states perished and they died.
Hence it is said: *"Fish cannot escape from the deep."*

Rewards and punishments are the state’s sharp tools—
in the hands of the sovereign, they control ministers;
in the hands of ministers, they overpower the sovereign.
When the sovereign sees rewards, ministers diminish them to claim virtue;
when the sovereign sees punishments, ministers exaggerate them to assert power.
When the sovereign rewards, ministers exploit his authority;
when the sovereign punishes, ministers exploit his threats.
Hence it is said: *"The state’s sharp tools must not be shown to others."*

**Yuè Wáng** entered **Wú** as a hostage and urged them to attack **Qí** to weaken **Wú**.
After **Wú’s** victory over **Qí** at **Àilíng**,
they displayed their strength at the **Jiāng** and **Jì** rivers,
and at **Huángpó**—
thus they could be controlled by the **Five Lakes**.
Hence it is said: *"To gather, one must first disperse.
To weaken, one must first strengthen."*

**Jìn Xiàn Gōng** wished to ambush **Yú**, gifting them with jade and horses.
**Zhì Bó** wished to ambush **Chóu Yóu**, gifting him a grand carriage.
Hence it is said: *"To take, one must first give."*

To begin an undertaking in the unseen
and achieve great deeds in the world—
this is called *subtle clarity*.
To dwell in weakness while valuing humility
is to overcome strength through self-effacement.
Things with form must arise from the small;
enduring actions must begin with the few.
Hence it is said: *"The world’s difficult tasks begin with ease,
the world’s great tasks begin with the slight."*
Thus, to master things, one must attend to their beginnings—
hence it is said: *"Plan for difficulty in its ease,
achieve greatness in its slightness."*

A thousand-cubit dike collapses from an ant’s hole;
a hundred-foot hall burns from a crack’s smoke.
Hence it is said: *"White Guī blocks the dike’s holes,
the old man seals the crack with mud."*
Thus **White Guī** has no water calamity,
the old man has no fire disaster.
These are all who practice caution in ease to avoid difficulty,
and respect the slight to forestall the great.

**Biān Que** saw **Cài Huán Gōng** and stood in silence.
**Biān Que** said: *"Your Highness has an ailment in the skin and muscles—
if untreated, it may deepen."*
**Huán Gōng** said: *"I have no illness."*

Biān Que departed, and **Huán Gōng** said: *"Physicians delight in treating those who are not yet ill to claim their skill."* Ten days later, Biān Que returned and said: *"Your Highness’s ailment has reached the muscles and skin—if left untreated, it will deepen further."* **Huán Gōng** made no reply. Biān Que departed, and **Huán Gōng** was again displeased.

Ten days later, Biān Que returned once more and said: *"Your illness has now reached the intestines and stomach—if left untreated, it will grow worse."* **Huán Gōng** still made no reply. Biān Que departed, and **Huán Gōng** was again displeased.

Ten days later, Biān Que gazed at **Huán Gōng** and fled. **Huán Gōng** sent someone to ask him why, and Biān Que replied: *"When the illness is in the skin and muscles, it can be treated with steaming and acupuncture. When it reaches the intestines and stomach, it can be treated with purgatives and emetics. But when it penetrates to the bone marrow, it falls under the domain of the **Sì Mìng**—there is nothing that can be done. Now your illness has reached the bone marrow, and that is why I no longer seek to treat you."*

Five days later, **Huán Gōng** fell gravely ill and sent for Biān Que, but he had already fled to the state of **Qín**. **Huán Gōng** soon died.

Thus, a skilled physician treats illness when it is still in the skin and muscles—this is to contend with it while it is still slight. Yet the calamities and blessings of affairs also have their early stages, and so it is said: *"The wise act early."* In ancient times, the **Jìn** prince **Chóng Ěr**, fleeing into exile, passed through **Zhèng**. The ruler of **Zhèng** treated him without proper courtesy, and **Shū Zhān** advised him: *"This is a virtuous prince. If you honor him generously, you will accumulate virtue."* The ruler of **Zhèng** did not heed him. **Shū Zhān** again advised: *"If you do not honor him generously, then kill him—do not let this become a future calamity."* The ruler of **Zhèng** still did not listen.

When the prince returned to **Jìn** and ascended the throne, he raised an army to attack **Zhèng**, defeating them and seizing eight of its cities.

**Jìn Xiàn Gōng**, using the **Chuí Jí** jade as a pretext, sought passage through **Yú** to attack **Guó**. The minister **Gōng Zhī Qí** advised against it: *"When the lips perish, the teeth will grow cold. If **Yú** and **Guó** aid each other, it is not out of kindness. Today, **Jìn** will destroy **Guó**; tomorrow, **Yú** will surely follow."* The ruler of **Yú** did not listen, accepting the jade and granting passage. **Jìn** took **Guó** and, upon returning, turned and destroyed **Yú**.

These two ministers—**Shū Zhān** and **Gōng Zhī Qí**—were both contending in the early stages, yet their rulers did not heed them. Thus, **Shū Zhān** and **Gōng Zhī Qí** were like **Biān Que** to **Yú** and **Zhèng**, but their rulers refused to listen, so **Zhèng** was broken and **Yú** was annihilated.

Hence it is said: *"When things are stable, they are easy to preserve; when their signs are not yet manifest, they are easy to plan for."* In ancient times, **Zhòu**’s last king made ivory chopsticks, and **Jī Zǐ** feared this, saying: *"Ivory chopsticks will not be paired with earthen bowls; they will surely be matched with cups of rhinoceros horn and jade. Ivory chopsticks with jade cups will not be used for porridge of beans and millet; they will surely be paired with meats of deer, elephant, and leopard. Such feasts will not be held in thatched huts; they will surely be in nine-layered brocade halls, with lofty towers and broad chambers."* I fear the end, so I dread the beginning.

Five years later, **Zhòu**’s last king established a meat garden, set up the **páo luò** torture, climbed the **zāo qiū** mound, and overlooked the wine pool. **Zhòu**’s last king thus met his downfall. **Jī Zǐ**, seeing the ivory chopsticks, knew of the coming calamity for the realm, and so it is said: *"To see what is slight is to be clear."* **Gōu Jiàn**, enslaved in **Wú**, personally wielded weapons as **Wú**’s king’s groom, and thus was able to slay **Fū Chāi** at **Gū Sū**. **Wén Wáng**, meeting **Jìn** at the royal gate, remained composed, and **Wǔ Wáng** captured **Zhòu**’s last king at **Mù Yě**. Hence it is said: *"To hold fast to gentleness is to be strong."* The **Yuè** king’s path to hegemony did not falter from his servitude, and **Wǔ Wáng**’s kingship did not falter from his insults. Hence it is said: *"The sage does not falter, for it is because he does not falter that he remains unshaken."*

A rustic from **Sòng** found a rough jade and presented it to **Zǐ Hǎn**. **Zǐ Hǎn** refused it, and the rustic said: *"This is a treasure! It should be made into a vessel for a noble, not used by a commoner."* **Zǐ Hǎn** replied: *"You value jade as treasure, but I value not accepting your jade as treasure."* The rustic desired the jade, but **Zǐ Hǎn** did not. Hence it is said: *"To desire what is not desired is to not prize what is difficult to obtain."*

**Wáng Shòu**, carrying books as he walked, encountered **Xú Féng** on the **Zhōu** road. **Xú Féng** said: *"‘Shì’ means ‘to do.’ Doing arises from the times, and the wise have no fixed affairs. ‘Shū’ means ‘to speak.’ Speech arises from knowledge, and the wise do not hoard books. Why do you alone carry them as you walk?"* Thus, **Wáng Shòu** burned his books and danced.

Hence, the wise do not teach through words, nor do the enlightened hoard books in cases. These are the errors of the world, yet **Wáng Shòu** corrected them—this is to learn by unlearning. Hence it is said: *"To learn by unlearning is to return to what the masses err upon."*

All things have their natural forms; one should follow and guide them according to their nature, and adapt to their natural tendencies. Thus, in stillness, one establishes **virtue**; in motion, one follows the **Dao**.

A man from **Sòng** made artificial mulberry leaves from ivory for his ruler, taking three years to complete them. The leaves were so lifelike—veins and texture alike—that when mixed with real leaves, they could not be distinguished. This man thus received food and stipends from the **Sòng** court.

**Liè Zǐ** heard of this and said: *"If Heaven and Earth took three years to produce a single leaf, then things that bear leaves would be few indeed."* Thus, one should not rely on Heaven and Earth’s resources to sustain a single body, nor should one follow the **Dao**’s principles to master another’s wisdom—these are the ways of the artificial leaf. Hence, winter-plowed fields need not envy **Hòu Jì**’s harvests, nor will bountiful years blame **Zāng Guì**’s crops. With one person’s strength, **Hòu Jì** would be insufficient, but following nature, **Zāng Guì** would have more than enough. Hence it is said: *"To rely on the self-so of all things and not dare to act."*

The **kōng qiào**—the empty cavities—are the windows and doors of **spirit** and **clarity**. When the ears and eyes are exhausted by sounds and colors, and the **spirit** is drained by external appearances, the inner self has no master. If the inner self has no master, then even calamities and blessings as vast as hills and valleys cannot be discerned, and so it is said: *"Without stepping outside the door, one can know the world. Without gazing through the window, one can know the **Dao**."*

「This speaks of the **spirit**’s inseparability from its substance. **Zhao Xiang**#13 learned horsemanship from **Wang Ziqi**, but soon found himself racing with him, three times changing horses and three times finishing last. **Zhao Xiang** said: *"Your instruction in horsemanship is not yet complete."* He replied: *"The technique is complete; when applied, it leads to excess."* What is truly valued in horsemanship is that the horse’s body remains steady in the carriage, and the rider’s heart harmonizes with the horse, only then can one advance swiftly and travel far. Now, when Your Majesty finishes last, you wish to overtake me, but when you lead, you fear I will overtake you. The essence of guiding the horse and competing for distance is that there is no leading without following, nor following without leading. Yet Your Majesty’s thoughts of leading or following all depend on me—how then can you harmonize with the horse? This is why Your Majesty always finishes last.

**Bai Gong**#13 considered the rebellion, and after dismissing the court and leaning on his staff, he struck his sharp weapon against his chin. Blood flowed to the ground, yet he remained unaware. The people of Zheng heard of this and said: *"His chin is forgotten, but what else will he forget?"* Hence it is said: *"The farther one’s actions extend, the less one’s wisdom remains."* This means that wisdom is limited in its reach—what it neglects is near at hand. Thus, the **sage** has no fixed course of action. Able to unify wisdom, it is said: *"Without action, one knows."* Able to unify vision, it is said: *"Without sight, one perceives."* Following the times to act, leveraging resources to achieve merit, utilizing the capacities of all things to reap their benefits—hence it is said: *"Without effort, one attains success."*

The **King of Chu**#10 ruled for three years without issuing orders or enacting policies. **You Si Ma**#10, the right minister, sat beside him and whispered: *"There is a bird perched upon the mound in the southern lands. For three years it neither flaps its wings nor flies nor cries, but remains silent and motionless. What is its name?"* The king replied: *"For three years it does not flap its wings, so that it may grow long feathers. It neither flies nor cries, so that it may observe the people’s conduct. Though it does not fly now, when it does, it will ascend to the heavens. Though it does not cry now, when it does, its voice will astonish the world."* You understood this, and now I have no need to explain. After half a year, he began to govern himself. He removed ten officials, appointed nine, executed five high ministers, and elevated six recluses. Thus, the state became greatly ordered. He raised an army to attack **Qi**, defeated them at Xu State, triumphed over **Jin** at the Yellow River, assembled the feudal lords at Song, and thereby became the **Hegemon** of the world. The **King of Chu**#10 did not act for petty gains, hence his great reputation. He did not reveal his intentions prematurely, hence his great achievements. Therefore it is said: *"Great vessels are fashioned late, great sounds are rare in voice."* The **King of Chu**#10 intended to attack **Yue**, but **Du Zi**#10 advised him: *"Why does Your Majesty wish to attack Yue?"* He replied: *"Their governance is chaotic and their army is weak."* **Du Zi** said: *"I share Your Majesty’s concern."* Wisdom is like the eyes: they can see a hundred paces away but cannot see their own eyelashes. Your Majesty’s army was already defeated by **Qin** and **Jin**, losing hundreds of miles of territory—this is the weakness of the army. **Zhuang Shao**#10 became a brigand within the borders, yet the officials could not suppress him—this is the chaos of governance. Your Majesty’s weakness and disorder are not inferior to Yue’s, yet you wish to attack them—this is wisdom like the eyes. The king thus halted his plans. Hence, the difficulty of knowing lies not in seeing others, but in seeing oneself. Therefore it is said: *"Self-awareness is called clarity."* **Zi Xia**#10 saw **Zeng Zi**, who remarked: *"Why do you appear so plump?"* He replied: *"Because of victory in battle."* **Zeng Zi** said: *"Why do you speak thus?"* **Zi Xia** said: *"When I reflect inwardly upon the **Dao** of the ancient kings, I am honored by it. When I observe outwardly the pleasures of wealth and nobility, I am again honored by them. These two forces contend within my breast, and I do not yet know which will triumph—hence my emaciation."* Now, the **Dao** of the ancient kings prevails, hence my plumpness. Thus, the difficulty of **will** is not in overcoming others, but in overcoming oneself. Therefore it is said: *"Self-mastery is called strength."*

The **Kingdom of Zhou** possessed a jade tablet. **Zhou You**#13 commanded **Jiao Li**#13 to demand it, but **Wen Wang**#13 did not comply. **Fei Zhong**#13 came to request it, and he gave it to him. Though **Jiao Li** was virtuous and **Fei Zhong** unprincipled, **Zhou** hated the rise of the virtuous, hence it gave the tablet to **Fei Zhong**. **Wen Wang**#13 elevated **Tai Gong**#13 at the banks of **Wei**—this was to honor him. Yet granting **Fei Zhong** the jade tablet was to love him. Therefore it is said: *"If one does not honor one’s teacher nor love one’s resources, even if one knows the great **Dao**, it is called essential subtlety."*

Tang, fearing that the world would accuse him of greed after conquering Jie, thus ceded the Mandate to Wu Guang. Yet he dreaded that Wu Guang would accept it, so he sent someone to persuade Wu Guang: *"Tang slew his lord and seeks to leave a foul reputation upon his son—thus he cedes the Mandate to you."* Wu Guang then cast himself into the River.

The Duke of Qin ordered Gan Mao to choose between becoming his attendant or his envoy. Meng Ao replied: *"My lord, it would be better to become an attendant. Your strength lies in dispatching others. Even if you become an attendant, the king will still dispatch you as his envoy. If you wear the seal of an attendant while acting as an envoy, you will hold two offices at once."*

Zi Yu met Confucius at the court of Shang’s Grand Steward. After Confucius left, Zi Yu entered and asked about the guest. The Grand Steward said: *"I have already seen Confucius—when I looked at you, you seemed no more than a flea!"* *"I shall now present him to the lord."* Zi Yu, fearing Confucius would rise in favor, told the Grand Steward: *"The lord has already seen Confucius—Confucius will surely regard you as no more than a flea!"* The Grand Steward thus refused to see him again.

When the Prince of Wei formed the alliance at Jiuli, intending to revive his claim as the Son of Heaven, Peng Xi advised the Lord of Zheng: *"Do not heed him. Great states hate the Son of Heaven; small states benefit from him. If you do not heed the great, how can Wei alone establish him?"*

When the Jin attacked Jing, Duke Huan of Qi prepared to rescue it, but Bao Shu said: *"Too hasty! If Jing does not fall, Jin will not be exhausted, and if Jin is not exhausted, Qi will not grow heavy. Moreover, the merit of rescuing the perilous does not compare to the virtue of preserving the doomed. It would be better to delay and exhaust Jin—this would truly benefit Qi. Wait until Jing falls, then restore it; such a name would be truly glorious."* Duke Huan thus did not rescue it.

When Zi Xu fled, a border guard captured him. Zi Xu said: *"The lord seeks me because I possess a fine pearl. Now that I have lost it, I shall say you swallowed it."* The guard then released him.

When Qing Feng rebelled in Qi and sought to flee to Yue, his kinsmen said: *"Jin is nearby—why not go there?"* Qing Feng replied: *"Yue is distant, and distance is advantageous for evading disaster."* His kinsmen said: *"If you do not change this resolve, you may dwell in Jin. But if you do not change it, even in distant Yue, how can you find peace?"*

When Zhì Bó demanded land from Wei Xuanzi, Wei refused. Ren Zhang said: *"Why not grant it?"* Wei Xuanzi replied: *"He requests land without cause, so I do not grant it."* Ren Zhang said: *"If he demands land without cause, neighboring states will fear him. If he is insatiable, the world will dread him. If you grant him land, Zhì Bó will grow arrogant and scorn his enemies. Neighboring states will fear him and unite against him. If they use their united forces against a reckless state, the fate of the Zhì clan will not endure."* The *Book of Zhou* says: *"To defeat him, first aid him. To take from him, first give."* My lord should grant him land to provoke Zhì Bó’s arrogance. Moreover, why should you alone risk the world’s schemes against the Zhì clan while using our state as their hostage?" Wei Xuanzi said: *"Well spoken."* He then granted Zhì Bó a city of ten thousand households. Zhì Bó rejoiced and demanded land from Zhao, who refused. He then besieged Jinyang, with Han and Wei turning against him from without, while the Zhao clan responded from within—thus the Zhì clan perished.

When Duke Kang of Qin built a terrace for three years, the people rose in rebellion, intending to use force against Qi. Ren Wang said: *"Famine summons soldiers, sickness summons soldiers, toil summons soldiers, chaos summons soldiers. Your lord builds a terrace for three years—now the Chu people rise to attack Qi. I fear their attack on Qi is but a pretense, while their true intent is to ambush Qin. It would be better to prepare."* He stationed troops at the eastern border, and the Chu forces halted their march.

When Qi attacked Song, Song sent Zang Sunzi Nan to seek aid from Chu. Chu was delighted and agreed to help, greatly rejoicing. Zang Sunzi Nan returned in sorrow, and his charioteer asked: *"You sought aid and obtained it—why do you now wear a sorrowful expression?"* Zang Sunzi Nan said: *"Song is small, and Qi is great. If Chu aids small Song but angers great Qi, this is the reason for my sorrow. Yet Chu rejoices, and they will surely test my resolve. If I am steadfast and Qi is exhausted, this is Chu’s gain."* Zang Sunzi Nan then returned, and Qi seized five cities from Song while Chu’s aid never arrived.

When Duke Wen of Wei sought passage through Zhao to attack Zhongshan, Duke Xiu of Zhao nearly refused. Zhao Ke said: *"My lord errs. If Wei attacks Zhongshan and fails, Wei will be exhausted, and exhaustion will make Wei light—if Wei is light, Zhao will grow heavy. If Wei captures Zhongshan, it will not be able to cross Zhao to possess it. Thus, the state that uses force is Wei, yet the state that gains the land is Zhao. My lord must grant passage. If you grant it with great joy, they will know you benefit from it and will surely halt their march. It would be better to lend them passage, feigning reluctance."*

Yiqi Zi Pi served Tian Chengzi. When Tian Chengzi fled Qi and went to Yan, Yiqi Zi Pi carried his baggage and followed. At Wangyi, Yiqi Zi Pi said: *"Have you not heard of the serpent of the dried marsh? When the marsh dried, the serpent prepared to move. A small serpent said to the great one: ‘If you go and I follow, people will think only one serpent moves. Surely someone will kill you. It would be better if we linked together and I rode upon you—people will think you are a divine sovereign.’ So they linked and crossed the public road, and all who passed avoided them, saying: ‘A divine sovereign!’ Now you are handsome, and I am ugly. You are treated as my honored guest, a lord of a thousand chariots. You are treated as my envoy, a minister of ten thousand chariots. It would be better for you to be my attendant."* Tian Chengzi thus carried his baggage and followed him to an inn, where the innkeeper treated him with great respect, offering wine and meat. The people of Wen did not receive guests, and when asked: *"Are you a guest?"*

「对曰：主人。问其巷人而不知也，吏因囚之。君使人问之曰：子非周人也，而自谓非客何也？
对曰：臣少也诵诗曰：普天之下，莫非王土；率土之滨，莫非王臣。今君，天子，则我天子之臣也，岂有为人之臣而又为之客哉？故曰主人也。」
**Translation:**
*"He replied: ‘I am the host.’ When asked by the neighborhood officials, he did not know them, and so they had him arrested. The lord sent someone to inquire, saying: ‘You are not a subject of Zhou, yet you claim not to be a guest—why is that?’*
*He replied: ‘As a youth, I recited the *Shijing*: “Under the whole heaven, there is no land that is not the king’s territory; along the borders of the land, there is no subject who does not belong to the king.” Now, you are the sovereign, so I am a subject of the sovereign—how could I be a subject of one and yet a guest of another?’ That is why I said, ‘I am the host.’”*
*The lord then ordered his release.*

---

「韩宣王谓樛留曰：吾欲两用公仲、公叔，其可乎？」
**Translation:**
*"King Xuan of Jin said to **Gui Liu** (樛留): ‘I wish to employ both **Gong Zhong** (公仲) and **Gong Shu** (公叔) simultaneously—is that possible?’"*

---

「对曰：不可。晋用六卿而国分，简公两用田成、阚止而简公杀，魏两用犀首、张仪而西河之外亡。今王两用之，其多力者树其党，寡力者借外权。群臣有内树党以骄主，有外为交以削地，则王之国危矣。」
**Translation:**
*"He replied: ‘It is not permissible. When Jin employed the six ministers, the state was divided. Duke Jian of Qi (简公) simultaneously employed **Tian Cheng** (田成) and **Kan Zhi** (阚止), and Duke Jian was killed. Wei simultaneously employed **Xi Shou** (犀首) and **Zhang Yi** (张仪), and lost all lands beyond the **Xi River** (西河). Now, if Your Majesty employs both, the more powerful will rally their factions, while the weaker will seek external alliances. Some ministers will foster internal cliques to defy their lord, others will forge foreign ties to diminish the realm—then Your Majesty’s state will be in peril.’"*

---

「绍绩昧醉寐而亡其裘，宋君曰：醉足以亡裘乎？」
**Translation:**
*"**Shao Ji** (绍绩) lost his **qiu** (裘, a fur garment) while drunk and unaware, and the lord of Song asked: ‘Was it the drunkenness that caused him to lose his fur garment?’"*

---

「对曰：桀以醉亡天下，而康诰曰：毋彝酒者，彝酒，常酒也；常酒者，天子失天下，匹夫失其身。管仲、隰朋从於桓公而伐孤竹，春往冬反，迷惑失道。管仲曰：老马之智可用也。乃放老马而随之，遂得道。行山中无水，隰朋曰：蚁冬居山之阳，夏居山之阴，蚁壤一寸而仞有水。乃掘地，遂得水。以管仲之**圣** (wisdom/virtue), 隰朋之**智** (wisdom), till their limits of knowledge, they did not hesitate to learn from the old horse and the ants—yet now, people do not know to use their humble minds to learn from the **sages** (圣人), is this not excessive?’」
**Translation:**
*"He replied: ‘King Jie lost the **dharma** (天下) through drunkenness, and the **Kang Gao** (康诰) admonished: “Do not drink to excess, for excessive drinking is habitual drinking; habitual drinking will cause the sovereign to lose his **dharma** and a commoner to lose his life.” When **Guan Zhong** (管仲) and **Yi Peng** (隰朋) followed Duke Huan of Qi (桓公) in attacking **Gu Zhu** (孤竹), they set out in spring and returned in winter, lost and bewildered in their path. Guan Zhong said: ‘The wisdom of the old horse can be utilized.’ So he released the old horse and followed it, and thus found the way. Traveling through the mountains without water, Yi Peng remarked: ‘Ants dwell on the sunny side in winter and the shady side in summer; where their mound is one inch thick, there is water nearby.’ So they dug the earth, and thus found water. With **Guan Zhong’s virtue** (圣) and **Yi Peng’s wisdom** (智), even when they reached the limits of their knowledge, they did not hesitate to learn from the old horse and the ants—yet now, people do not know to use their humble minds to learn from the **sages** (圣人), is this not excessive?’"*

---

「有献不死之药於荆王者，谒者操之以入，中射之士问曰：可食乎？曰：可。因夺而食之。王大怒，使人杀中射之士，中射之士使人说王曰：臣问谒者曰可食，臣故食之。是臣无罪，而罪在谒者也。且客献不死之药，臣食之，而王杀臣，是死药也，是客欺王也。夫杀无罪之臣，而明人之欺王也，不如释臣。王乃不杀。」
**Translation:**
*"There was one who presented an **elixir of immortality** (不死之药) to King Jing of Chu (荆王). The **attendant** (谒者) took it and entered, and the **archer** (中射之士) asked: ‘Is it edible?’ The attendant replied: ‘It is.’ The archer then seized and ate it. The king flew into a great rage and ordered the archer’s execution. The archer sent someone to plead with the king, saying: ‘I asked the attendant whether it was edible, and he said it was, so I ate it. I am without fault, while the blame lies with the attendant.’ Moreover, the guest presented an **elixir of immortality**, yet I ate it and the king would execute me—this would make it a **poison of death** (死药), and the guest would be deceiving the king. To kill a guiltless subject and yet reveal that someone deceived the king—it would be better to pardon me. The king then did not execute him."*

---

「田驷欺邹君，邹君将使人杀之，田驷恐，告惠子。惠子见邹君曰：今有人见君，则**瞽** (blind)其一目，奚如？」
**Translation:**
*"**Tian Si** (田驷) deceived the lord of **Zou** (邹君), and the lord was about to order his execution. Tian Si, in fear, informed **Hui Zi** (惠子). Hui Zi visited the lord of Zou and said: ‘If someone were to appear before Your Majesty and blind one of your eyes, how would you respond?’"*

---

「君曰：我必杀之。惠子曰：瞽两目**瞽**，君奚为不杀？」
**Translation:**
*"The lord replied: ‘I would certainly execute him.’ Hui Zi said: ‘If someone were to blind both your eyes, why would you not execute him?’"*

---

「惠子曰：田驷东慢齐侯，南欺荆王，驷之於欺人，瞽也；君奚怨焉？」
**Translation:**
*"Hui Zi said: ‘Tian Si has disdained the **Marquis of Qi** (齐侯) in the east and deceived King Jing of Chu (荆王) in the south. In his **blindness** (瞽) to those he wrongs, he is doubly **blind**—why should Your Majesty harbor resentment toward him?’"*
*The lord of Zou then did not execute him.*

---

「鲁穆公使众公子或宦於晋，或宦於荆，犁鉏曰：假人於越而救溺子，越人虽善游，子必不生矣。失火而取水於海，海水虽多，火必不灭矣；远水不救近火也。今晋与荆虽强，而齐近，鲁患其不救乎？」
**Translation:**
*"Duke Mu of Lu (鲁穆公) sent his many princes either to serve as officials in Jin or in Chu. **Li Lou** (犁鉏) said: ‘To borrow a person from Yue (越) to rescue a drowning child—though the people of Yue may be skilled swimmers, the child would surely not survive. To fetch water from the sea when a fire has broken out—though the sea may be vast, the fire would surely not be extinguished; distant water cannot quench nearby flames. Now, though Jin and Chu may be strong, Qi is close—will Lu not fear that it will not come to its aid?’"*

---
