「严遂不善周君，患之。冯沮曰：严遂相，而韩傀贵於君，不如行贼於韩傀，则君必以为严氏也。」
**Translation:**
*"**Yan Sui** (严遂) was not well-regarded by the lord of Zhou (周君), and this troubled him. **Feng Ju** (冯沮) said: ‘Yan Sui is the **chancellor** (相), while **Han Kui** (韩傀) enjoys high favor with the lord—it would be better to frame the crime on Han Kui, and the lord would surely blame it on the **Yan clan** (严氏).’"*

---

「张谴相韩，病将死，公乘无正怀三十金而问其疾。居一月自问张谴曰：若子死，将谁使代子？」
**Translation:**
*"**Zhang Qian** (张谴) served as chancellor of Jin (相韩), and as he lay dying, **Gong Cheng Wu Zheng** (公乘无正) carried thirty pieces of gold to inquire about his illness. After a month, he asked Zhang Qian himself: ‘If you were to die, whom would you appoint to succeed you?’"*

---
**Zhang Qian replied:** 「无正重法而畏上，虽然，不如公子食我之得民也。」
**Translation:**
*"‘Wu Zheng values **virtue/power** (德) and fears the lord, but even so, he cannot surpass **Gongzi Shi** (公子食) in the people’s affection for me.’"*

---
「张谴死，因相公乘无正。乐羊为魏将而攻中山，其子在中山，中山之君烹其子而遗之羹。乐羊坐於幕下而啜之，尽一杯。文侯谓堵师赞曰：乐羊以我故而食其子之肉。」
**Translation:**
*"When Zhang Qian died, Wu Zheng then became chancellor. **Le Yang** (乐羊), as general of **Wei** (魏将), attacked **Zhongshan** (中山). His son was there in Zhongshan, and the lord of Zhongshan boiled him and sent the broth to Le Yang. Le Yang sat beneath his tent and sipped it, finishing one cup. Marquis Wen of Wei (文侯) said to **Du Shi Zan** (堵师赞): ‘Le Yang, because of me, ate his own son’s flesh.’"*

---
「答曰：其子而食之，且谁不食？」
**Translation:**
*"He replied: ‘If it is his son, who would not eat him?’"*

---
「乐羊罢中山，文侯赏其功而疑其心。孟孙猎得麑，使秦西巴载之持归，其母随之而啼，秦西巴弗忍而与之。孟孙归，至而求麑，答曰：余弗忍而与其母。孟孙大怒，逐之。」
**Translation:**
*"Le Yang withdrew from Zhongshan, and Marquis Wen rewarded his **virtue/power** (功) while suspecting his loyalty. **Meng Sun** (孟孙) hunted and captured a young deer (**yun** 麑), and ordered **Qin Xi Ba** (秦西巴) to load and carry it back. The deer’s mother followed, weeping, and Qin Xi Ba could not bear it, so he gave it to her. When Meng Sun returned, he demanded the deer, but Qin Xi Ba replied: ‘I could not bear it and gave it to its mother.’ Meng Sun flew into a great rage and banished him."*

---
「居三月，复召以为其子傅，其御曰：曩将罪之，今召以为子傅何也？孟孙曰：夫不忍麑，又且忍吾子乎？故曰：巧诈不如拙诚。乐羊以有功见疑，秦西巴以有罪益信。」
**Translation:**
*"After three months, he recalled Qin Xi Ba to serve as his son’s **tutor** (子傅). Qin Xi Ba’s **charioteer** (御) asked: ‘You were about to punish him earlier, yet now you summon him to be your son’s tutor—why is that?’ Meng Sun replied: ‘He who could not endure a young deer—how could he endure my son?’ That is why it is said: ‘**Sophisticated deceit is not as effective as simple sincerity** (巧诈不如拙诚). Le Yang, though having **virtue/power** (功), was met with suspicion, while Qin Xi Ba, though guilty, gained even greater trust.’"*

---
「曾从子，善相剑者也。卫君怨吴王，曾从子曰：吴王好剑，臣相剑者也，臣请为吴王相剑，拔而示之，因为君刺之。卫君曰：子为之是也，非缘义也，为利也。吴强而富，卫弱而贫，子必往，吾恐子为吴王用之於我也。乃逐之。」
**Translation:**
*"**Zeng Congzi** (曾从子), skilled in **appraising swords** (相剑), resented the **King of Wu** (吴王). The lord of Wei (卫君) said: ‘Wu is strong and wealthy, while Wei is weak and poor—you would surely go there. I fear that the King of Wu would employ you against me.’ So he banished him."*

---
**Zeng Congzi replied:** 「臣为之是也，非缘义也，为利也。」
**Translation:**
*"‘I would do it because it is right, not for **virtue/power** (利) but for **righteousness** (义).’"*

---
**The lord of Wei replied:** 「吴强而富，卫弱而贫，子必往，吾恐子为吴王用之於我也。」
**Translation:**
*"‘Wu is strong and wealthy, while Wei is weak and poor—you would surely go there. I fear that the King of Wu would employ you against me.’"*

---
**Zeng Congzi replied:** 「臣请为吴王相剑，拔而示之，因为君刺之。」
**Translation:**
*"‘I would request to appraise swords for the King of Wu, draw one to display it, and use it to assassinate your lord.’"*

---
「纣为象箸而箕子怖，以为象箸为不成美於土簋，则必犀玉之杯，玉杯象箸必不盛菽藿，则必旄象豹胎，旄象豹胎必不衣短褐而舍茅茨之下，则必锦衣九重，高台广室也。」
**Translation:**
*"When **King Zhou** (纣) made **ivory chopsticks** (象箸), **Ji Zi** (箕子) was alarmed, fearing that ivory chopsticks would not harmonize with **clay food vessels** (土簋)—and so there would surely be **rhinoceros and jade cups** (犀玉之杯). With jade cups and ivory chopsticks, they would surely not contain **beans and millet** (菽藿)—and so there would surely be **tusked elephants, leopard skins, and tiger pelts** (旄象豹胎). With such skins, they would surely not wear **coarse hemp garments** (短褐) nor dwell beneath **thatched roofs** (茅茨之下)—and so there would surely be **nine-layered silken robes** (锦衣九重) and **lofty towers with spacious halls** (高台广室).’"*

«When one **judges by this** (称此) to **seek** (以求), then the **world will have no end** (天下不足矣).

The **Sage** (圣人) **perceives the subtle to know its sprouting** (见微以知萌), **sees the beginning to know its end** (见端以知末), and thus, **seeing the ivory chopsticks** (见象箸) and **fearing** (而怖), **knows the world’s insatiable desires** (知天下知不足也).

**King Wu of the Zhou** (周公旦) had already **defeated the Shang** (已胜殷), and was about to **attack the states of Shang and Ge** (将攻商、盖). **Xin Gong Jia** (辛公甲) said: **«Great difficulties are hard to attack, but small ones are easily subdued; it is better to subdue the many small ones to coerce the great one»** (大难攻，小易服，不如服众小以劫大). Thus, he **attacked the Nine Yi tribes** (乃攻九夷), and **Shang and Ge submitted** (而商、盖服矣).

**King Zhou** (纣) **made it a habit to drink all night** (为长夜之饮), **fearing that he might lose the day** (惧以失日), yet **none of his attendants knew the reason** (问其左右尽不知也). He then ordered them to **ask Ji Zi** (乃使问箕子).

Ji Zi **replied to his disciples** (为其徒曰): **«To be the ruler of the world and yet have an entire kingdom lose track of the day—this is perilous indeed!** (为天下主而一国皆失日，天下其危矣). **If an entire kingdom does not know this, yet I alone do—then I am in grave danger!** (一国皆不知而我独知之，吾其危矣).» He **excused himself with the claim of drunkenness** (辞以醉而不知).

A **man from Lu** (鲁人) was skilled at **weaving straw sandals** (身善织屦), his wife at **recognizing fine silk headbands** (妻善识缟), yet they **migrated to Yue** (而徙於越). Some said to them: **«You shall surely fall into poverty!** (子必穷矣).» The man from Lu asked: **«Why so?** (何也？)» They replied: **«Straw sandals are meant for wearing, yet Yue people go barefoot. Fine silk headbands are meant for coronation, yet Yue people wear their hair unbound.** (屦为履之也，而越人跣行。 缟为冠之也，而越人被发). **With your strengths suited to a land that does not use them, you wish to avoid ruin—how can this be?** (以子之所长，游於不用之国，欲使无穷，其可得矣).»

**Chen Zhen** (陈轸) was **held in high esteem by the King of Wei** (贵於魏王#2), yet **Hui Zi** (惠子) said: **«He must surely be adept at currying favor with his attendants!** (必善事左右). **If you plant a willow upright, it grows; if you plant it sideways, it still grows; even if you break it and plant it again, it grows.** (夫杨横树之即生，倒树之即生，折而树之又生). **Yet if ten men plant it while one pulls it up, even the willow cannot grow.** (然使十人树之而一人拔之，即无生杨至). **With ten men nurturing something so easily grown, yet unable to withstand one person—why is this?** (以十人之众，树易生之物，而不胜一人者何也？) **The difficulty lies in planting it, while removing it is easy!** (树之难而去之易也). **Though Chen Zhen may excel at cultivating favor with the king, those who wish to remove him are many—he is in danger indeed!** (子虽工自树於王，而欲去子者众，子必危矣).»

**Lu Ji Sun** (鲁季孙) had just **assassinated his lord** (新弒其君), and **Wu Qi** (吴起) **took service there** (仕焉). Some said to Wu Qi: **«When a man dies, his blood first flows, then his nose bleeds, then his body turns cold, then it turns to ash, then to dust—once returned to dust, there is nothing more to be done!** (夫死者，始死而血，已血而衄，已衄而灰，已灰而土，反其土也，无可为者矣). **Now Ji Sun has just begun with the blood—his end is yet unknown!** (今季孙乃始血，其毋乃未可知也).» Wu Qi **thereupon left for Jin** (因去之晋).

**Wei Si Mi** (隰斯弥) **visited Tian Cheng Zi** (见田成子), and Tian Cheng Zi **invited him onto a viewing platform to survey the land** (与登台四望). **Three sides were barren** (三面皆畼), but when they **looked south**, the trees of Wei Si Mi’s home **obscured the view** (隰子家之树蔽之). Tian Cheng Zi **said nothing** (田成子亦不言). Wei Si Mi returned and **ordered his servants to cut them down** (使人伐之), but after only a few strokes of the axe (斧离数创), he **stopped them** (隰子止之). His attendant asked: **«Why halt the axe so often?** (何变之数也？)» Wei Si Mi replied: **«There is an old saying: ‘He who knows the fish in the depths is doomed to misfortune!’** (古者有谚曰：知渊中之鱼者不祥). **Tian Cheng Zi is about to undertake a great affair** (夫田子将有事), **and I have shown him my knowledge of trifles—this will surely bring me danger!** (事大，而我示之知微，我必危矣). **Not cutting the trees is no crime** (不伐树未有罪也), **but knowing what others do not speak of—this is a grave offense!** (知人之所不言，其罪大矣).» He **refrained from cutting them** (乃不伐也).

**Yang Zi** (杨子) **passed through a lodging in the eastern part of Song** (过於宋东之逆旅), where the **innkeeper had two concubines** (有妾二人), **the ugly one favored** (其恶者贵), and **the beautiful one neglected** (美者贱). Yang Zi **inquired about this** (问其故), and the **innkeeper’s father answered** (逆旅之父答曰): **«The beautiful one is beautiful in herself, yet I do not know her beauty; the ugly one is ugly in herself, yet I do not know her ugliness»** (美者自美，吾不知其美也，恶者自恶，吾不知其恶也). Yang Zi **told his disciples** (谓弟子曰): **«To act with virtue yet abandon the mind of virtue—where can one go without failing to excel?** (行贤而去自贤之心，焉往而不美).»

A **man from Wei** (卫人) **instructed his son upon marrying him off** (嫁其子), saying: **«Accumulate wealth for yourself in secret!** (必私积聚). **To leave as a widow is common** (为人妇而出，常也), **but to return as a widow is a stroke of luck!** (其成居，幸也).» His son **thereupon secretly accumulated wealth** (其子因私积聚), but his mother-in-law **thought him too greedy and drove him out** (其姑以为多私而出之). The son **reflected on his actions twice as much as when he was married** (其子所以自反者倍其所以嫁). His father **did not blame himself for teaching his son wrongly** (其父不自罪於教子非也), yet **he knew his own wealth had increased** (而自知其益富). **«Nowadays, ministers who hold office are all of this sort!** (今人臣之处官者皆是类也).»

**Lu Dan** (鲁丹) **three times advised the lord of Zhongshan** (三说中山之君), yet the lord **did not accept** (而不受也). Lu Dan **thereupon scattered fifty pieces of gold to bribe the lord’s attendants** (因散五十金事其左右), and **when he returned**, before he could even speak, the lord **offered him food** (复见，未语，而君与之食). Lu Dan **left without returning to his lodging** (出), and **departed Zhongshan** (遂去中山). His **charioteer said** (其御曰): **«He returned to see you, yet you began to treat him well only now—why did you leave him?** (反见，乃始善我，何故去之？)» Lu Dan replied: **«If one praises me through others, they will surely condemn me through others!** (夫以人言善我，必以人言罪我). **Before I even left the borders, the prince already hated me, saying:** ‘He came from Zhao to **spy on Zhongshan** (间中山).’ **The lord then searched for me and found fault with me!** (未出境，而公子恶之曰：为赵来间中山。 君因索而罪之).»

**Tian Bo Ding** (田伯鼎) **loved men of virtue and preserved his lord** (好士而存其君), while **Bai Gong** (白公) **loved men of virtue yet brought chaos to Jing** (好士而乱荆), their **love of virtue being the same** (其好士则同), yet **their results differing** (其所以为则异). **Gong Sun You** (公孙友) **self-mutilated his foot to gain honor from Bai Li** (自刖而尊百里), and **Zhi Diao** (竖刁) **self-mutilated his genitals to speak with Duke Huan** (自宫而谓桓公), their **self-inflicted punishments being the same** (其自刑则同), yet **their motives differing** (其所以自邢之为则异).

**Hui Zi** (慧子) said: **«Those who flee eastward, their pursuers also flee eastward—though their fleeing eastward is the same** (往者东走，逐者亦东走，其东走则同), **their reasons for fleeing differ** (其所以东走之为则异).» Thus it is said: **«Men who share the same pursuits must be carefully examined!** (故曰：同事之人，不可不审察也#3).»

**From the *Sayings of the Grove* (说林下), the twenty-third entry:**

**Bo Le** (伯乐) **taught two men to observe horses**, and they **went together to inspect the horses in the stables of Duke Jian** (相与之简子厩观马). One man **pointed out a horse with a high-stepping gait** (一人举踶马), and the other **followed behind, tracing its movements** (其一人从后而循之). After **three strokes to its hindquarters** (三抚其尻), the horse **did not lift its feet** (而马不踶). The first man **thought he had failed in his observation** (此自以为失相). The second man said: **«You have not failed in your observation!** (子非失相也). **This horse has a swollen shoulder and a knotted knee!** (此其为马也，踒肩而肿膝). **He who observes horses must lift the hindquarters while bearing the weight on the forelegs—yet a knotted knee cannot bear the weight!** (夫踶马者也，举后而任前，肿膝不可任也，故后不举). **You are skilled at observing a high-stepping gait, yet clumsy at bearing the weight of a knotted knee!** (子巧于相踶马而拙于任肿膝). **In affairs, there are always essentials that must be fulfilled—yet you cannot bear the weight of what is essential, and this is a wisdom known only to the wise!** (夫事有所必归，而以有所肿膝而不任，智者之所独知也).»

**Hui Zi** (惠子) said: **«Place a monkey in a cage, and it will behave like a pig!** (置猿于柙中，则与豚同). **Thus, when circumstances are not favorable, it is not the proper way to display one’s abilities!** (故势不便，非所以逞能也).»

**General Wei Wen Zi** (卫将军文子) **visited Zeng Zi** (见曾子), yet **Zeng Zi did not rise to greet him but instead invited him to sit on the mat** (曾子不起而延于坐席), **and arranged his own body in the inner chamber** (正身于奥). Wen Zi **told his charioteer** (谓其御曰): **«Zeng Zi is a fool indeed!** (曾子，愚人也哉). **He must think me a virtuous man—yet how can a virtuous man fail to show respect?** (以我为君子也，君子安可毋敬也？) **Or does he think me a violent man—yet how can a violent man be insulted without fear?** (以我为暴人也，暴人安可侮也？)» Zeng Zi **did not alter his conduct** (不僇命也).»

There was a kind of bird called *Zhuó Zhuó* (翢翢), which had a heavy head and a drooping tail. When it wished to drink from the river, it would always tip over, so it held its feathers in its beak to drink. **A man who cannot drink enough is like this bird—he must not neglect to seek his feathers.**

The *bì* (鳣) resembles a snake, and the silkworm resembles a *liú* (蠋). Men see a snake and are startled, see a *liú* and their hair stands on end. Yet a fisherman carries the *bì*, and a woman picks up silkworms; where profit lies, they all become bold, like *Bī* (贲) and *Zhū* (诸).

**Master Bo Le** (伯乐) taught those he hated to judge **thousand-li horses** (千里之马), and those he loved to judge **poor, slow horses** (驽马). **Thousand-li horses are rare**, and their profit is slow, but poor horses are sold daily, with swift profit. This is what the *Zhou Shū* (周书) called **"lower words but higher use"** (下言而上用)—**deceit.**

Huan He (桓赫) said: **In the art of carving, the nose should not be made too large, nor the eyes too small.** The nose can be made small if it is too large, but it cannot be made large if it is too small. The eyes can be made large if they are too small, but they cannot be made small if they are too large. **So too in undertaking actions: if one does what cannot be undone, few mistakes will occur.**

**Chonghou** (崇侯) and **E Lai** (恶来) knew that they were unfit for **King Zhou’s punishment** (纣之诛), yet they did not see **King Wu’s destruction of him** (武王之灭之也). **Bi Gan** (比干) and **Zi Xu** (子胥) knew their lord would certainly perish, yet they did not foresee their own deaths. **Thus it is said: Chonghou and E Lai knew the heart but not the circumstances; Bi Gan and Zi Xu knew the circumstances but not the heart.** The **Sage** (圣人) is fully prepared.

**The Grand Minister of Song** (宋太宰) was **noble in virtue** (贵) yet **ruled with severity** (主断). **Ji Zi** (季子) was about to visit **Lord Song** (宋君), and **Liang Zi** (梁子) heard of it. He said: **"If you speak, you must be able to sit with the Grand Minister for three rounds; otherwise, you will not escape."** Ji Zi then **argued that the Grand Minister’s virtue was excessive and the state’s governance was too lenient** (因说以贵主而轻国).

**Yang Zhu’s younger brother** (杨朱之弟), **Yang Bu Yi** (杨布衣), went out in **plain white garments** (素衣), and when the rain came, he took off his white clothes, put on his **black garments** (缁衣), and returned. His dog did not recognize him and barked at him. Yang Bu Yi grew angry and was about to strike it, but Yang Zhu said: **"Do not strike it, brother. You would also be surprised if you did this."** He said: **"If I had sent you out with white clothes and you returned in black, would you not be astonished?"**

**Hui Zi** (惠子) said: **Yi** (羿) held the bow and arrow, drew the string, and aimed—yet the **people of Yue** (越人) all rushed to **hold the target** (持的). **A weak man** (弱子) **drew the bow**, and **a tender mother** (慈母) **entered the room and shut the door.** **Thus it is said: If Yi’s skill is certain, the people of Yue will not doubt him. If it is uncertain, even a tender mother will flee with her weak child.**

**Duke Huan of Qi** (桓公) asked **Guan Zhong** (管仲): **"Is there a limit to wealth?"** Guan Zhong replied: **"Water has a limit—where there is no water. Wealth has a limit—where it is already sufficient."** **Men cannot stop themselves at what is enough, yet how can they lose the limit of wealth?**

Among the wealthy merchants of **Song** (宋), there was **Jian Zhi Zi** (监止子), who **quarreled with another over the purchase of a rough jade worth a hundred gold pieces** (争买百金之璞玉). He **pretended to drop it and break it**, then **paid the hundred gold pieces** and **repaired the flaws**, gaining an extra thousand in profit. **There are matters where success brings ruin, and the wise avoid undertaking them—this is the profit of delay.**

A man wished to **recommend himself to King Jing of Chu** (见荆王) by **claiming he could catch deer with his bare hands** (撽鹿). When he saw the king, the king made him his charioteer, and he failed to catch the deer. **The king’s own charioteer then caught it**, and the king praised his skill. The man then said: **"The king’s other charioteers are jealous of me."** **King Jing of Chu** (荆王) ordered **Prince Zhi** (公子) to **attack Chen** (陈), and an old man sent him off, saying: **"Jin is strong—you must be cautious."** The prince replied: **"Why do you worry, old man? I will break Jin for you."** The old man said: **"Very well. I will wait outside Chen’s southern gate."** The prince asked: **"What is this?"** The old man said: **"I laugh at Gou Jian** (勾践)—**how easily men can be like this! Yet he alone struggles for ten years in difficulty—why?"**

**Emperor Yao** (尧) **offered the realm to Xu You** (许由), but Xu You fled, abandoning his **leather cap** (皮冠) with his household servants, who hid it. **To abandon the realm yet have one’s servants hide even one’s leather cap—this is to not truly know Xu You.**

Three lice **quarreled among themselves** (相与讼). A fourth lice passed by and asked: **"What are you quarreling about?"** The three replied: **"We are disputing over fertile, moist land."** The fourth said: **"You should not fear the arrival of the sacrificial season or the drying of the straw—why worry?"** Then the three **gathered together and sucked their mother dry**, killing her. The pig’s flesh was **salted and preserved** (臞), so men did not kill it. There is a kind of worm called *huí* (蛔) or *huáng* (蚢), which has **two mouths in one body**, and they **fight over food, biting each other until they die.** **Thus, those ministers who quarrel over matters and lose their state are all like these worms.**

**If a palace has whitewashed vessels** (垩器), and there is **a cleansing agent** (涤), then **the vessels can be made spotless** (絜矣). **So too in one’s conduct: without a place to cleanse and refine, few faults will remain.**

**Prince Jiu** (公子纠) was about to cause **chaos**, so **Duke Huan of Qi** sent a messenger to observe him. The messenger reported: **"He smiles but does not rejoice; he looks but does not see—he will certainly cause chaos."** **Duke Huan of Qi then had him killed.**

**Gong Sun Hong** (公孙弘) **cut his hair short** (断发) and became **King Yue’s charioteer** (越王骑). **Gong Sun Xi** (公孙喜) had someone **sever their relationship** (绝之) by saying: **"I will not consider you my brother."** Gong Sun Hong said: **"I cut my hair short. You severed your neck and yet were used by men to lead armies, attack, and conquer—what would you say to me?"** **In the battle of Zhou Nan** (周南之战), Gong Sun Xi died.

A man lived next to a **fierce neighbor** (悍者), and wanting to **sell his home** (宅) and **avoid him**, he was told: **"That neighbor’s wealth will soon be exhausted—just wait for him."** The man replied: **"I fear that he will use my wealth to fill his own coffers."** **So he left.** **Thus it is said: One should not disregard the subtle signs of things.**

**Confucius** (孔子) said to his disciples: **"Who can guide Zi Gong to abandon his love of profit?"** Zi Gong replied: **"I, Ci, can do it."** **Confucius then tested him**, and he did not waver. Confucius said: **"Tolerant indeed! He is not swayed by profit."** **Discerning indeed! Human nature has its constancy.** **The crooked remains crooked, and the straight remains straight.**

**Confucius said: Zi Gong is not like a hare.** **In the crisis of Bai Gong** (白公之难), Zi Gong died. **Thus it is said: The straight in conduct is crooked in desire.**

**Zhong Xing Wen Zi** (晋中行文子) fled into exile. As he passed through a **county town** (县邑), his followers said: **"This is the stingy man, your old friend. Why do you not rest here?"** **Let us wait for the rear carriage.** Wen Zi replied: **"I used to love music. This man once gave me a broken zither."**

「I love adornments,」 he said. **«This man gave me a jade pendant.** **He is one who rouses me from my errors.** **But if he seeks favor from me, I fear he will use me to seek favor from others.** **So I parted from him.** **Indeed, he later collected Wen Zi’s rear carriage with two chariots and presented them to his lord.**

**Zhou Zhao** (周躁) said to **Gong Tu** (宫他): **«Tell the King of Qi for me:** **Let him use the resources of Qi to aid me in Wei, and I shall use the affairs of Wei to serve the King.**」 Gong Tu replied: **«That cannot be done.** **This would show that Wei has no resources, and the King of Qi would surely not aid one who has none, while harboring resentment toward one who does.** **You would do better to say:** **If the King desires what I offer, I shall make Wei submit to his rule.** **The King of Qi will then see you as one who possesses Wei, and he will surely rely on you.** **This way, you will have Qi’s support, and through it, both Qi and Wei will be yours.**」

**Bai Gui** (白圭) said to the **Ordering Minister of Song** (宋令尹): **«Your lord is old and governs himself well. The Master (公) has no need to act.** **Now, however, your lord is young and relies on others while seeking fame.** **It would be better to praise the King of Chu’s filial devotion.** **Then your lord will not strip the Master of his position, but will instead hold him in great respect, allowing the Master to always use Song’s strength.**」

**Guan Zhong** (管仲) and **Bao Shu** (鲍叔) said to each other: **«The lord is in great disorder. He will surely lose his kingdom.** **Among the princes of Qi, the one who can be supported is either Prince Jiu (公子纠) or Prince Bo (小白).** **We must both serve one of them, and the one who reaches him first will be the one he follows.**」 Guan Zhong then followed Prince Jiu, while Bao Shu followed Prince Bo. The people indeed killed the lord, and Prince Bo entered first to become the ruler. The people of Lu captured Guan Zhong and offered him to Prince Bo, who spoke and reconciled him. Hence the proverb: **«Even if Wu Xian is skilled in divination, he cannot divine his own fate.** **Even if the Qin physician is skilled in removing arrows, he cannot remove his own.** **With Guan Zhong’s wisdom yet awaiting Bao Shu’s aid, this is the vulgar saying of a slave selling his own fur without success, or a scholar praising his own eloquence without being believed.**»

When the **Chu King** (荆王) attacked **Wu** (吴), Wu sent **Nu Wei Jue** (沮卫蹶融) to offer provisions to the Chu army, and the general said: **«Bind him and kill him to strike the drums.**」 He asked: **«Have you come to divine (卜) for us?**」 He replied: **«Yes, I have.** **Is the divination auspicious?**」 He said: **«It is auspicious.**」 The Chu soldiers asked: **«Why then does the general wish to strike the drums with your death?**」 He replied: **«That is precisely why it is auspicious.** **The Wu envoy has come, and I saw that the general was angry.** **If the general is angry, he will dig deep trenches and build high ramparts.** **If the general is not angry, he will grow lax.** **Now, if you kill me, the Wu will surely be on their guard.** **Moreover, the divination is not for one envoy alone.** **To kill one envoy and preserve an entire state—why would it not be called auspicious?** **If the dead have no knowledge, then striking the drums with my death will be of no benefit.** **If the dead have knowledge, then at the time of battle, I shall make the drums not sound.** **The Chu will thus not kill me.**」 The Chu did not kill him.

**Zhi Bo** (智伯) was about to attack **You Yu** (仇由), but the path was impassable. He cast a great bell and sent it to You Yu’s lord, who was greatly pleased and cleared the path to welcome him. **Chi Zhang Man Zhi** (赤章曼枝) said: **«That cannot be done.** **This is how the small serves the great, but now the great comes to you.** **You will surely follow him, and you cannot welcome him.**」 You Yu’s lord did not listen and welcomed him. Chi Zhang Man Zhi then cut the axle and drove the bell away, reaching **Qi** (齐) in seven months, and You Yu was destroyed.

After **Yue** (越) had defeated **Wu** (吴), it again demanded soldiers from **Chu** (荆) to attack **Jin** (晋). **Zuo Shi Yi Xiang** (左史倚相) said to the Chu King: **«When Yue defeated Wu, its elite warriors died, its sharp troops were exhausted, and its great general was wounded.** **Now it again demands soldiers to attack Jin, showing that it is not weakened.** **It would be better to raise our army and share in the spoils of Wu.**」 The Chu King said: **«Good.**」 He then raised his army and followed Yue. The Yue King grew angry and prepared to strike them, but the minister **Zhong** (种) said: **«That cannot be done.** **Our elite warriors are dead, our great general is wounded.** **If we fight, we will surely not prevail.** **It would be better to bribe them.**」 He then gave them the southern slopes of **Lu Mountain** (露山), five hundred *li* in extent, as a bribe. The Chu did not attack.

When **Chu** (荆) attacked **Chen** (陈), **Wu** (吴) came to its aid, and the armies were separated by thirty *li*. It rained for ten days, and stars appeared at night. Zuo Shi Yi Xiang said to **Zi Qi** (子期): **«If it rains for ten days, the armor will be damp and the troops will gather.** **The Wu will surely come.** **We should prepare for them.**」 He then fortified the defenses for Chen, but they were not yet complete when the Wu arrived, saw the Chu fortifications, and retreated. Zuo Shi said: **«The Wu have retreated sixty *li*. Their nobles will rest, and their commoners will eat.** **If we march thirty *li* and strike them, we will surely defeat them.**」 He followed this advice and broke the Wu army.

**Han** (韩) and **Zhao** (赵) were at war with each other. The **Han Lord** (韩子) requested soldiers from **Wei** (魏), saying: **«I wish to borrow your army to attack Zhao.**」 **Wei Wen Hou** (魏文侯) replied: **«I and Zhao are brothers. I cannot comply.**」 Zhao then requested soldiers from Wei to attack Han, and Wen Hou said: **«I and Han are brothers. I dare not comply.**」 The two states, unable to obtain soldiers, grew angry and returned. They then realized that Wen Hou had used them to create conflict, so they both went to court him. When **Qi** (齐) attacked **Lu** (鲁), it demanded the **Zan Tripod** (谗鼎), and Lu sent it. The Qi said: **«It is false (属).**」 The Lu said: **«It is true (真).**」 Qi said: **«Let Le Zheng Zi Chun come, and we will listen to him.**」 The Lu Lord requested Le Zheng Zi Chun, who replied: **«Why not send the true tripod?**」 The Lord said: **«I love it.**」 He replied: **«I also love my lord’s trustworthiness.**»

When **Han Ji** (韩咎) was set to be king but his position was not yet secure, his younger brothers were in **Zhou** (周), and Zhou wished to honor him but feared that Han Ji would not be established. **Qi Wu Hui** (綦毋恢) said: **«It would be better to send him a hundred chariots.** **If he is established, he will say that it was a warning.** **If he is not established, he will say that you came to aid his rebellion.**」

**Jing Guo Jun** (靖郭君) was about to fortify **Xue** (薛), but many guests advised against it. Jing Guo Jun said to the **attendants** (谒者): **«Do not announce my visitors.**」 A man from Qi who wished to see him said: **«I request permission to speak three words.** **If I exceed three words, I request to be boiled.**」 Jing Guo Jun then saw him, and the guest rushed forward, saying: **«The sea has great fish.**»

The guest then turned and left. Jing Guo Jun said: **«I wish to hear your reasoning.**» The guest replied: **«I do not dare to jest with death.**» Jing Guo Jun said: **«I beg you to speak for me.**» The guest answered: **«Have you heard of the great fish of the sea?**»
Net cannot stop them, hook cannot ensnare them—when they are cast about, they lose the water, and the ants and tiny insects find their advantage.

Now, the state of **Qi** is also your sea, my lord. You have long possessed **Qi**—why would you seek **Xue** instead?
Even if you fortified **Xue** to the heavens, it would be of no use once you lost **Qi**.

Jing Guo Jun said: **«Well spoken.**» He then abandoned his plan and did not fortify **Xue**.

The younger brother of the **King of Jing** (荆王) was in **Qin**, but **Qin** refused to release him. A skilled archer said: **«If you give me a hundred *jin* of gold, I can bring him out.**»
He then loaded a hundred *jin* of gold into **Jin** (晉) and sought out **Chu Xiang** (叔向), saying: **«The younger brother of the King of Jing is in Qin, but Qin refuses to release him. I beg you to entrust this hundred *jin* of gold to Lord Chu Xiang.**»

Chu Xiang accepted the gold and presented it to **Jin Ping Gong** (晉平公), saying: **«Now we can fortify the city of Huqiu (壶丘).**»
The duke asked: **«Why so?**»
He replied: **«The younger brother of the King of Jing is in Qin, but Qin refuses to release him. This shows that Qin despises the King of Jing, and they will certainly not dare to forbid us from fortifying Huqiu.
If they forbid us, we will say: ‘Release the younger brother of the King of Jing for us, and we will not fortify.’
If they comply and release him, we can gain **virtue/power** (德) from the King of Jing.
If they do not release him, it means they persist in their hostility, and they will certainly not dare to forbid us from fortifying Huqiu.**»
The duke said: **«Well spoken.**» He then fortified Huqiu and told the **Qin public** (秦公): **«Release the younger brother of the King of Jing for us, and we will not fortify.**»
Qin then released him, and the King of Jing was greatly pleased, offering a hundredfold return of the gold as a gift to **Jin**.

The ancients had short sight in seeing themselves, hence they used mirrors to observe their faces.
The wise have limited self-awareness, hence they use the **Dao/Way** to correct themselves.
Thus, mirrors bear no blame for failing to reveal flaws,
and the **Dao/Way** incurs no resentment for clarifying errors.
Eyes without mirrors cannot properly align the brows and beard,
nor can bodies without the **Dao/Way** discern confusion and deception.
Ximen Bao’s nature was hasty, so he wore a girdle of *wei* (韋) to temper himself.
Dong An Yu’s temperament was sluggish, so he wore a bowstring to quicken his resolve.
Hence, to use one’s surplus to complement one’s deficiency,
and to extend one’s strengths to overcome one’s weaknesses—
this is what is called a **wise ruler** (明主).

There are three principles of trust in the world:
One, that **virtue/power** (智) has limits in what it can accomplish alone;
Two, that strength has limits in what it can lift alone;
Three, that might has limits in what it can overcome alone.
Thus, even with Yao’s wisdom, without the aid of many,
great deeds cannot be established.
Even with Wu Ge’s mighty strength, without human assistance,
he cannot lift himself.
Even with Bi and Yu’s prowess, without law and technique,
they cannot attain longevity.
Hence, there are things in the world that cannot be obtained,
and undertakings that cannot be completed.
Wu Ge could lift a thousand *jun* (钧) but struggled with his own body,
not because his body was heavier than a thousand *jun*,
but because the circumstances were unfavorable.
Li Zhu could discern objects a hundred *bu* (步) away but struggled to see his own brows and lashes,
not because a hundred *bu* was distant and lashes nearby,
but because the **Dao/Way** made it so.
Hence, a **wise ruler** does not exhaust Wu Ge for his inability to lift himself alone,
nor does he constrain Li Zhu for his inability to see himself clearly.
By leveraging favorable circumstances and seeking what the **Dao/Way** permits,
his efforts are few yet his achievements great.

Circumstances have their fullness and emptiness,
undertakings their advantages and disadvantages,
things their births and deaths.
If a ruler displays joy or anger for these three,
then even the most steadfast subjects will turn their hearts away.
The **virtue/power** (智) of the worthy and the sage is profound in discerning shallow and deep.
Hence, a **wise ruler** examines others,
but does not allow others to examine him.
When the **virtue/power** (智) of Yao is understood—
that he could not achieve greatness alone—
and the limitations of Wu Ge’s strength,
and the constraints of Bi and Yu’s might,
then the **Dao/Way** of safeguarding through law and technique is complete.

**Chapter Twenty-Five: On Stability and Peril**
Stability has seven methods; peril has six.
**Methods of Stability:**
1. Rewards and punishments follow what is right and wrong;
2. Calamity and blessing follow what is good and evil;
3. Life and death follow the laws and measures;
4. There are virtuous and unvirtuous people, yet no favoritism or hatred;
5. There are foolish and wise people, yet no unjust praise or blame;
6. There are small and large matters, yet no arbitrary judgment;
7. There is trust, yet no deceit.

**Paths to Peril:**
1. To carve and cut within the confines of the measuring line;
2. To carve and cut beyond the reach of the law;
3. To benefit others at their expense;
4. To delight others in their misfortune;
5. To endanger others in their security;
6. To love those not close, and to reject those not distant.

If this is done, then people will lose their reason for cherishing life,
and forget their grounds for fearing death.
If people do not cherish life, the ruler will not be revered;
if they do not fear death, his commands will not be obeyed.
Thus, he who makes the world’s cleverest conform to propriety,
and exhausts their strength in balancing scales—
when active, they prevail; when still, they are secure.
In governing the world, he makes people cherish life for doing what is right,
and love their bodies for avoiding what is wrong.
When petty men are few and gentlemen are many,
then the ancestral altars endure and the state remains long in peace.

Even on a fleeing chariot, there is no Zhong Ni (仲尼);
even in a capsized boat, there is no Bo Yi (伯夷).
Hence, proclamations are the state’s chariots and boats.
Stability brings forth **virtue/power** (智) and integrity,
while peril sparks contention and vulgarity.
Thus, the laws of a stable state
are like food for the hungry and clothing for the cold—
they require no coercion yet naturally take hold.
The ancient kings entrusted governance to written records,
and their **Dao/Way** was harmonious,
so later generations followed them.
If one were to remove hunger and cold,
even Bi and Yu could not enforce it.
To abandon what is natural,
even if one follows the **Dao/Way**, it would not stand.
What the strong and courageous cannot achieve,
the ruler above cannot secure.
If the ruler above does not exhaust himself in self-criticism,
and if his measures are without flaw,
then he will not slight the law.
The law is the foundation of the state—
if he treats it lightly, then achievements will not be realized,
and reputation will not be earned.

In ancient times, the physician Bian Que treated his patients by piercing their bones with a knife.
The sage, in saving a perilous state,
uses loyalty to pierce the ears of those in opposition.

Piercing the bone, so though the pain is slight upon the body, the enduring benefit lies within the self.
Rubbing the ears, so though the opposition is slight in the heart, the lasting blessing rests upon the state.
Thus, the gravely ill benefit from enduring pain,
and the resolute ruler gains fortune by rubbing the ears.

Enduring pain, so Bian Que’s skill is fully realized.
Rubbing the ears, so Zilu does not falter.
This is the art of longevity and security.

If one cannot endure pain when sick, then Bian Que’s skill is lost.
If one does not rub the ears in peril, then the Sage’s intent is abandoned.
Thus, lasting benefit will not soon descend,
and merit and fame will not soon be established.

The sovereign does not demand of himself the virtue of Yao,
yet expects of his ministers the loyalty of Zilu—
this is to hope that all will be like Bì Gān,
and if all are like Bì Gān, then the ruler will not lose his position,
and the people will not perish.

Without measuring one’s strength yet seeking the success of Tiān Chéng,
and hoping that all will be like Bì Gān,
then the state cannot achieve even a single moment of peace.
To abandon Yao and Shun and enthrone Jié and Jiè
is to let people fear their weaknesses while rejoicing in their strengths.
Losing what one excels at means the state achieves no merit,
and clinging to what one lacks means the people take no joy in life.
To govern with no merit while ruling over those who take no joy in life
is impossible among the common people.

Thus, the ruler cannot command the subjects,
and the subjects cannot serve the ruler.
Safety and peril lie in right and wrong, not in strength and weakness.
Survival and ruin lie in substance and emptiness, not in numbers and fewness.

Thus, though Qi was a great state with ten thousand chariots,
its reputation and reality did not match—
the ruler was hollow within his own domain,
not filling the space between reputation and reality,
so the ministers could usurp the sovereign.

Killing the Son of Heaven without distinguishing right and wrong,
rewarding those without merit,
favoring flatterers and deceivers,
punishing the innocent,
and forcing those of natural integrity to twist their backs—
to value deceit over sincerity,
and to condemn natural virtue—
the small prevails over the great.

The enlightened ruler strengthens his resolve within,
so he does not falter externally.
If one does not lose what is near to right,
then ruin from what is distant will not come.
Thus, when Zhou overthrew Shang,
they gathered the remnants in the courtyard,
ensuring Shang left nothing behind in the court,
so Zhou did not dare covet even a single autumn hair at the border—
how much less would they dare to usurp the throne?

The enlightened ruler’s **Dao/Way** is loyalty to law,
and his law is loyal to the heart,
so when he governs, he relies on law,
and when he departs, he reflects upon it.

Yao required no bonds of glue and lacquer in his age,
yet his **Dao/Way** prevailed.
Shun needed no place to insert a needle in future generations,
yet his virtue endured.

To establish the **Dao/Way** in the distant past
and transmit virtue to ten thousand generations—
this is called the enlightened ruler.

**Chapter Twenty-Six: Guarding the Dao**

The sage-kings’ laws were such that their rewards were sufficient to encourage the virtuous,
their authority was sufficient to overcome violence,
and their precautions were sufficient to ensure the laws were complete.

In times of governance, officials of merit held high rank,
those who exerted themselves received rich rewards,
and those who gave their all had their names recorded.
The growth of virtue was like spring,
and the decline of vice was like autumn,
so the people strove to exert themselves to the utmost
and took joy in giving their all—
this is called the ruler and subjects harmonizing.

When ruler and subjects harmonize,
those who use their strength strive to the limits of measure and balance,
and their efforts reach the utmost in their stations.
Soldiers risk death in battle,
yet wish to be like Bì and Yǔ.
Those who guard the **Dao/Way** all harbor hearts of jade and stone,
and die with the resolve of Zilu.

If those who exert themselves act with the utmost in their stations,
fight like Bì and Yǔ,
and guard with the resolve of jade and stone,
then the ruler can rest securely while his realm remains intact.

The ancients who excelled in defense
used what was precious to prohibit what was trivial,
and what was difficult to stop what was easy.
Thus, the noble and the petty both stood upright,
and the thief Zhí and the historians Zēng and Shǐ were all honest.

How do we know this?
The greedy thief does not venture into the river to pick up gold,
for if he did, his life would not be preserved.
Bì and Yǔ, if they did not measure their enemies,
would not earn their reputation for courage.
Zhí, if he did not calculate what was possible,
would not achieve his gains.

The enlightened ruler’s prohibitions
ensure that Bì and Yǔ do not suffer defeat in what they cannot overcome,
and that Zhí does not meet harm in what he cannot take.
Thus, if one can prohibit what Bì and Yǔ cannot violate,
and guard what Zhí cannot seize,
then the violent will restrain themselves,
and the wicked will return to righteousness.
The great warrior will be sincere,
and the mighty thief will be upright.
When this is achieved, the world will be just and fair,
and the common people’s hearts will be upright.

If the ruler abandons the law and loses the people,
he will be in greater peril than Bò Yí’s refusal to take what was not his,
yet he will not escape the fate of Tiān Chéng and Zhí’s treachery.

Today, there is no Bò Yí in the world,
yet deceitful men do not cease to exist,
so laws and measures are established.
If measures are trustworthy, then Bò Yí will not lose what is right,
and Zhí will not dare to act wrongly.
If laws are clearly defined, then the worthy cannot usurp the unworthy,
the strong cannot oppress the weak,
and the many cannot tyrannize the few.

To entrust the world to Yao’s laws,
then the upright will not lose their place,
and the deceitful will not seek fortune through trickery.
To deposit a thousand gold pieces with Yì’s arrows,
then Bò Yí will not perish,
and Zhí will not dare to take them.
Yao was enlightened in preventing deceit,
so the world had no wickedness.
Yì was skilled in never missing his mark,
so the thousand gold pieces were not lost.
The wicked did not live long,
and Zhí’s schemes came to nothing.
Thus, the records do not mention Zǎi Yú,
nor do they mention the Six Ministers.
The annals do not record Zilu,
nor do they praise Fú Chā.
The strategies of Sūn and Wú were abandoned,
and Zhí’s treachery was suppressed.

The ruler enjoys comfort within the jade hall,
yet faces no danger of glaring eyes and gnashing teeth in greedy seizure.
The minister rests with folded arms within the fortified city,
yet faces no calamity of clutching and snatching with pursed lips.

To tame tigers without cages,
to restrain deceit without laws,
to block falsehood without seals—
this is the concern of Bì and Yǔ,
and the difficulty of Yao and Shun.

Thus, cages are not set to guard against mice,
but to enable the timid to tame tigers.
Laws are not established to guard against Zēng and Shǐ,
but to enable mediocre rulers to stop Zhí.
Seals are not prepared for Tàishēng,
but to prevent common people from deceiving one another.

It is not only to wait for Bì Gān’s death in loyalty,
nor to rely on treacherous ministers being without deceit—
but to hold what the timid can tame,
and grasp what the mediocre ruler can guard.

「In the present age, for a sovereign to devise loyal strategies and for the world to bind together with virtue, no benefit is greater than this. Thus, a ruler has no schemes of losing his state, and loyal ministers have no plots of losing their lives. When he knows that honor must be bestowed upon those who hold high positions, men will exert their utmost at the scales of governance, and die in loyalty for their duties.

Understanding the plight of Bì and Yǔ, he does not exchange life for death, and recognizing the greed of Zhí and Shǐ, he does not trade his own person for wealth. Then, the Dao of guarding the state is fully realized.

**Chapter 27: The Use of People**
The wise of antiquity in employing people would surely follow the Way, conform to human nature, and clearly distinguish rewards and punishments. Following the Way means expending little effort yet achieving great results; conforming to human nature means minimizing penalties yet ensuring orders are obeyed; and clearly distinguishing rewards and punishments means that Bì Yǐ and Zhí Shǐ will not cause chaos. Thus, the separation between right and wrong is manifest.

The ministers of state will strive to serve the country and uphold their positions, demonstrate their capabilities in their offices, and exert their utmost at the scales of governance to fulfill their duties. Subjects will all be suited to their abilities, surpass their official ranks, and lighten their burdens, yet harbor no lingering strength of resistance nor bear the guilt of holding multiple offices before their sovereign.

Thus, there will be no internal rebellions born of hidden resentments, nor external calamities like the disaster at Mǎfú. A wise ruler ensures that duties do not overlap, so there will be no litigation. He assigns officials without granting them multiple roles, so their skills will flourish. He does not make people share in different functions, so there will be no disputes. When disputes cease and skills excel, the strong will not overpower the weak, nor will opposites clash in form. Then, none in the world can harm one another, and governance reaches its peak.

To abandon the *fǎshù* and rely solely on the heart’s governance, even Yáo could not rectify a single state. To discard the *guījǔ* and measure with arbitrary intent, Xī Zhōng could not craft a single wheel. To neglect the *chǐcùn* and allow discrepancies in length, even Wáng Ěr could not half-hit the mark. If a mediocre ruler adheres to the *fǎshù*, and clumsy artisans follow the *guījǔ* and *chǐcùn*, then nothing will be lost in ten thousand cases.

A sovereign who can abandon what even the virtuous and skilled cannot achieve, yet uphold what the mediocre and clumsy can never fail in, will exhaust the people’s efforts and establish his fame. A wise ruler establishes rewards for what can be done and punishments for what can be avoided. Thus, the virtuous will encourage rewards without fearing the fate of Zhū Xū, and the unworthy will commit fewer crimes without facing the punishment of being flayed and their backs sliced. The blind will walk on level ground without falling into deep ravines, and the foolish will guard stillness without being trapped in perilous dangers. Thus, the bonds of favor between ruler and subjects will be firmly established.

The ancients said: *"His heart is difficult to fathom, and his joys and anger are hard to discern."* Thus, they use visible signs to guide the eyes, drumbeats to instruct the ears, and laws to teach the heart. If a sovereign abandons these three clear methods and instead acts upon an unfathomable heart, then anger will accumulate above, and resentment will gather below. To govern with accumulated anger against accumulated resentment is to invite twofold peril.

The wise ruler’s signs are easily seen, so his decrees are firmly established. His teachings are easily understood, so his words are put to use. His laws are easily followed, so his commands are obeyed. When these three are in place and the ruler has no private intentions, then the subjects can follow the laws to govern themselves, act in response to the signs, cut according to the measure, and sew by the marked threads. Thus, the ruler will suffer no harm from private threats, and the subjects will face no punishment for their ignorance or clumsiness.

Hence, the upper echelons will be enlightened with little anger, and the lower ranks will be loyal with few crimes. It has been said: *"Actions without calamity are beyond even Yáo."* Yet the world has never been without affairs. A sovereign who does not lightly bestow rank and wealth, nor easily alter fortune, cannot be relied upon to rescue a perilous state. Thus, a wise ruler enforces integrity and virtue, and attracts the righteous.

In the past, Jiè Zǐtú had no rank or wealth, yet his virtue followed Wén Gōng. He endured neither hunger nor thirst, yet his benevolence extended to cutting his own flesh to feed others. Thus, the sovereign bound his virtue, and his name was inscribed upon the ancestral tablets. The sovereign delights in making people exert their efforts for the public good, yet suffers when private interests usurp authority.

Subjects find comfort in receiving office according to their abilities, yet grieve when one person bears two roles. This is to burden one body with two duties. Thus, a wise ruler removes the subjects’ sufferings and establishes what the sovereign finds joyous, and the mutual benefits between ruler and subjects are never greater than this.

If a ruler does not examine the faults within his own household, lightly punishes grave offenses, severely punishes minor crimes, harbors long-standing resentments over trivial mistakes, indulges in prolonged scorn for fleeting pleasures, or habitually disdains others for petty gratification—if he rewards crimes with virtue, punishing those who bring misfortune yet instead repaying them with virtue—this is like severing a hand and replacing it with jade. Thus, the world sees the calamity of losing one’s life.

If a sovereign establishes difficult tasks yet does not punish those who fail, private resentments will rise. If a subject is deprived of his strengths yet burdened with impossible tasks, hidden resentments will take root. If labor and suffering are not soothed nor sorrow and grief are not pitied, then in joy he will praise the unworthy, and both the virtuous and unworthy will be rewarded. In anger, he will slander the noble, and Bì Yǐ and Zhí Shǐ will be dishonored together. Thus, ministers will rebel against their sovereign.

If the King of Yān harbors internal hatred for his own people while outwardly showing favor to the people of Lǔ, then Yān will be neglected while Lǔ will not embrace its subjects. Those who see hatred will not exert their efforts nor strive for merit, and those who see favor will not risk their lives nor abandon their own sovereign to serve another. Thus, ministers become like cracks in a wall, and the sovereign stands alone. To serve a sovereign with ministers like cracks in a wall is to court grave danger.

To abandon proper ritual and act arbitrarily, though one may hit the mark, the result will be clumsy. To discard laws and decrees yet rage in anger, though one may execute criminals, the wicked will not be deterred. Crime will be attributed to one party, while calamity falls upon another, and hidden resentments will form.

Thus, in a state of utmost governance, there are rewards and punishments yet no private joys or angers, so the Sage is at his peak. There are penalties and laws, yet none die from their sting, so the wicked submit. When arrows strike their mark and rewards and punishments align with justice, then Yáo would rise again and Yì would stand once more.

Thus, the ruler above will face no calamities like those of Yīn or Xià, and the subjects below will suffer no fates like that of Bì Gān. The ruler will rest with a high pillow, and the ministers will take joy in their duties. The Dao will cover the heavens and earth, and virtue will reach to the ten thousand generations.

If a sovereign does not block the cracks in his own household, yet toils over the muddy walls of his state, violent rains and fierce winds will surely destroy them. If he does not remove the perils close at hand, yet admires the loyalty of Bì and Yǔ, if he does not guard against the dangers within his own walls, yet fortifies his distant borders with gold—then his state will be in peril.»

「Do not employ the counsel of nearby worthies while instead forming alliances with the great powers of distant lands. When sudden tempests arise, Bì and Yǔ would be too late to offer aid, and distant alliances would fail to arrive in time—no calamity could be greater than this.

In the present age, those who sincerely advise the sovereign must avoid making the King of Yān delight in the people of Lǔ, must not emulate ancient worthies in modern times, and must not expect the people of Yuè to rescue the Central States from drowning. Only then will the ruler and subjects grow close, internal governance take root, and external reputation flourish.

**Chapter Twenty-Eight: The Four Foundations of a Sage Ruler’s Achievements and Reputation**
The sage ruler establishes achievements and secures reputation through four means: first, the alignment with *heavenly timing* (天时); second, the harmony with *human sentiment* (人心); third, the mastery of *skills and abilities* (技能); and fourth, the wielding of *authority and position* (势位). Without heavenly timing, even ten Yáo could not coax a single grain to grow in winter. To act against human sentiment, even Bì and Yǔ could not compel all to exert themselves.

Thus, when one attains heavenly timing, endeavors flourish spontaneously; when one secures human sentiment, encouragement arises without prompting; when one leverages skill and ability, urgency emerges without haste; and when one commands authority and position, reputation is achieved without exertion. Like water flowing or a boat floating, the sage ruler adheres to the Way of Nature and issues commands that never fail. Hence, he is called the *Enlightened Sovereign*.

One may possess talent yet lack authority—even the worthy cannot then restrain the unworthy. Place a ruler’s staff atop a high mountain, and it overlooks a thousand *ren*-deep ravine; the staff is not long, but its position is elevated. Jié, as the Son of Heaven, could command all under Heaven—not because he was virtuous, but because his authority was supreme. Yáo, as a commoner, could not rectify even three households—not because he was unworthy, but because his station was low. A thousand *jūn* of weight will float with a boat, while a single *zhū* will sink without it—not because the former is lighter or the latter heavier, but because of the presence or absence of supporting force. Thus, the short staff dominates the heights through position, and the unworthy subdue the worthy through authority.

The sovereign bears the collective strength of all under Heaven, hence his security. When all hearts unite in supporting him, his eminence is assured. Ministers fulfill their strengths and exhaust their abilities, hence their loyalty. When the sovereign, through his eminence, governs loyal ministers, enduring joy arises, and achievements and reputation are realized. For reputation and substance depend on each other, and form and shadow arise together—thus, the sovereign and minister share the same desires yet differ in their roles.

The sovereign’s affliction lies in having no response, hence the saying: *A single hand clapping, though swift, makes no sound.* The minister’s woe lies in lacking unity, hence the saying: *The right hand draws a circle, the left a square—neither can succeed.* Therefore, in a perfectly governed state, the ruler is like a drumstick, the minister like a drum, the craftsman like a chariot, and affairs like the horses—thus, when men have surplus strength, they readily respond, and when craftsmen possess surplus skill, they readily accomplish tasks.

Those who achieve great deeds lack not in strength, those who draw near lack not in trust, and those who secure reputation lack not in authority. If the near are already close yet the distant remain unaligned, then reputation fails to match reality. Even if a sage’s virtue equals that of Yáo and Shùn, and his conduct matches that of Bó Yí, yet if his station does not elevate him in the world, his achievements will not stand, and his reputation will not endure. Thus, the ancients who accomplished great deeds relied on the masses lending their strength, the near aiding in completion, the distant praising in renown, and the eminent supporting through authority. Only then could the enduring achievements of Tàishān stand firm in the state, and the radiant reputation of Rì and Yuè persist in Heaven and Earth. This is how Yáo secured his reputation by ruling from the south, and Shùn fulfilled his achievements by deferring to Yáo from the north.

**Chapter Twenty-Nine: The Grand Framework of the Ancients**
The ancients who preserved the grand framework observed the heavens and earth, contemplated rivers and seas, followed the contours of mountains and valleys, and aligned with the reach of sun and moon, the passage of the four seasons, and the movement of clouds and winds. They did not burden the mind with excessive scheming nor the self with private bias. They entrusted governance to law and method, entrusted right and wrong to rewards and punishments, and entrusted weight and measure to scales and balances. They did not defy natural principle, nor harm human nature. They did not scrutinize minutiae like seeking flaws in a hair’s breadth, nor probe deeply like washing away filth to uncover hidden faults. They did not extend the measure beyond its line nor contract it within—nor did they hasten beyond the law nor delay within it. They upheld established principles, followed natural order, and let fortune and misfortune arise from the Dao and its methods, rather than from personal inclinations. Honor and disgrace rested with the self, not others.

Thus, they brought about the age of utmost tranquility, where laws were as fleeting as morning dew, simplicity remained unbroken, hearts harbored no grievances, and tongues uttered no idle words. Hence, chariots and horses did not weary on distant roads, banners did not falter in great marshes, the common folk did not perish in raids, the mighty did not shorten their lives in battle, the bold did not seek fame in records, nor were deeds inscribed on bronze tripods or annual annals left empty. Therefore, it is said: *No benefit surpasses simplicity, no blessing endures like peace.*

If a master carpenter with a thousand years’ lifespan wielded a hook, measured with a compass and plumb, and sought to rectify Tàishān, or if Bì and Yǔ donned the *Gān Jiāng* sword to unify the people, though they exerted their utmost skill and reached the height of their lifespans, Tàishān would not be straightened, nor the people unified. Thus, the ancient rulers of all under Heaven did not command the master carpenter to exhaust his skill in defacing Tàishān’s form, nor did they order Bì and Yǔ to wield their might in harming the people’s nature. They followed the Dao and preserved the law, allowing the virtuous to take joy while great deceivers were checked. Tranquil and at ease, they aligned with Heaven’s command and upheld the grand framework. Thus, they ensured no one transgressed the law, and no fish faced ruin from water.

Therefore, all under Heaven lacked nothing. If the ruler does not ascend to Heaven, the people below will not be fully sheltered; if the heart does not rest like earth, all things will not be securely borne. Tàishān does not favor or reject—thus it attains its height. The rivers and seas do not discriminate against small contributions—thus they attain their fullness. Hence, the great person embodies the heavens and earth, and all things are complete; their mind traverses mountains and seas, and the state grows rich. Above, there is no venom of wrath, below, no hidden resentment—above and below return to simplicity, taking the Dao as their dwelling. Thus, enduring benefits accumulate, great achievements are established, reputation is secured in the present, and virtue endures in the future—this is the pinnacle of governance.»

---

**Chapter Eight of *Hanfeizi* concludes.**
#1 The character *名* is a duplication here; deleted according to the *Yǔ Píng* and *Líng Yīngchū* editions.
#2 The character *心* is missing here; supplied according to the *Yǔ Píng* and *Líng Yīngchū* editions.
#3 The character *民* is missing here; supplied according to the *Yǔ Píng* and *Líng Yīngchū* editions.
#4 The character *易* is missing here; supplied according to the *Yǔ Píng* and *Líng Yīngchū* editions.
#5 *"千将"* is clearly a mistake for *"干将"* and should be corrected.

**Chapter Nine of *Hanfeizi*: *Nèicǔ Shuō* (Inner Reservoir Discourses) – *Qī Shù* (Seven Arts) #30**
*Cǔ* means "to gather." This gathers all the strategies that are the sovereign’s inner schemes, hence the title *Nèicǔ Shuō*.

The sovereign employs seven arts and discerns six subtleties.

The seven arts are:
1. **Crowdsourced Verification (Zhòng Duān Cān Guān)** – *Duān* means "straightforward." To seek truth from the masses, one must cross-verify and observe.
2. **Unwavering Punishment to Manifest Authority (Bì Fá Míng Wēi)**,
3. **Faithful Rewards to Exhaust Capability (Xìn Shuǎng Jìn Néng)**,
4. **Unified Hearing and Delegated Accountability (Yī Tīng Zé Xià)** – If the sovereign listens to only one perspective, there will inevitably be errors. If accountability is not uniformly enforced, merit will remain obscured.
5. **Ambiguous Edicts and Deceptive Commands (Yí Zhào Guǐ Shǐ)** – By issuing ambiguous orders and employing deceptive tactics, the subjects dare not conceal their true intentions.
6. **Feigning Ignorance to Probe (Jié Zhī Ér Wèn)**,
7. **Reversing Statements and Inverting Facts (Dào Yán Fǎn Shì)** – Either reversing their words or inverting their deeds will expose all deceit.

These seven are the sovereign’s tools.

If observation and hearing are not cross-verified, truth will not be heard. Not cross-verifying means listening to only one person, and thus the truthful will have no way to speak. If listening is filtered through factions, ministers will obstruct the sovereign’s judgment. If the sovereign’s hearing is as narrow as a factional gate, it will be blocked by his ministers.

The tale of the dwarf’s dream of a stove illustrates this: the dwarf dreamed of a stove, claiming it had a man standing guard, yet no one else saw it. This mocks Duke Ling’s partiality toward Zǐ Xué.

Duke Zhāo’s reliance on a single advisor led to his confusion. When the duke consulted on matters, he did so without gathering diverse opinions, hence his confusion. Confucius, when addressing the entire state, aligned with the faction of Sūn Shū, effectively making the state a single man’s domain—thus the duke’s confusion was justified.

The people of Qi, deceived by a single advisor, believed the River Spirit was a giant fish. This mirrors Huìzi’s warning that a sovereign’s schemes, when not cross-verified, lose half their value—when all advisors echo the same opinion without question, they become a clique, hence the loss of half the truth.

These five cases all stem from the sovereign’s failure to cross-verify and avoid factional listening.

The danger lies in Chú Sūn’s starvation due to trusting a single advisor: Chú Sūn relied solely on the dwarf Gàn Niú, leading to his own starvation and the execution of his two sons. This parallels Jiāng Yǐ’s critique of Chu’s customs, where the taboo against speaking of others’ kindness allowed Bái Gōng to incite rebellion.

Duke Sì’s ignorance of governance—meaning he lacked the knowledge of ruling—led to his downfall. He feared his favored ministers would conspire against him, so he elevated others to counter them. But this only strengthened their factions, making his original favorites even more entrenched.

Thus, the enlightened sovereign accumulates iron-like defenses—building a fortress of iron to guard against all possible losses, ensuring no harm to his body. He accumulates doubt as his mind, exhausting all possibilities to guard against deceitful ministers, ensuring no treachery arises.

Yet even a single market’s claim—*"There is a tiger in the market!"*—should not be believed #1, let alone three people’s word. **Cross-verification is paramount.**

If a sovereign favors too many, the law cannot stand. If authority is weak, the subjects will encroach upon their superiors.

Thus, when punishments are not strictly enforced, prohibitions cannot be upheld. This is illustrated by **Bozi’s governance in Shiyì**: when Bozi arrived in Shiyì, he established laws like deep ravines, and so the state of Zhao became well-ordered.

It is also seen in **Zi Chan’s instruction of You Ji**: Zi Chan taught You Ji to enforce laws rigorously through the **Law Historians**, ensuring strict judgments.

Hence, **Confucius praised the killing frost**: when Confucius spoke to Duke Ai about the killing frost not harming the grass, he meant that it was fitting to kill—yet it did not—because it was not the proper time.

Yet the **Yin dynasty’s law punished those who fled from burning ashes**, and when **Jiang Ziya** was about to depart, he did so because the king did not entrust him with the sole authority over rewards and punishments.

**Gongsun Yang** also emphasized minor offenses: he believed that if minor crimes were not punished, there would be no way to deter major ones, so he first punished the minor offenses. Hence, even though the gold of **Lishui was unguarded**, and stealing it was punishable by dismemberment, people still continued to steal it—until some were caught and executed, at which point even major crimes ceased.

Similarly, the **fire at Jizai went unchecked**: when the fire at Jizai burned and no one intervened, it was because the laws were not enforced.

**Cheng Huan** knew that excessive benevolence weakens a state: he recognized that the king’s excessive kindness would inevitably weaken the state of Qi.

**Bu Pi** foresaw that mercy would lead to the downfall of the **Wei king**: he knew that the king’s compassion would ultimately destroy himself.

**Guan Zhong understood this**, so he executed the dead.

Knowing the necessity of governance, he strictly prohibited extravagant burials—those who disobeyed had their corpses mutilated.

**Sìgōng understood this too**, so he bought back the **xūmǐ (corvée laborers)**. Sìgōng knew that a state must punish, so when a *xūmǐ* fled, he bought back an entire district to execute him.

**Punishment must be certain**—if rewards are meager, deceitful subjects will be disregarded; if rewards are substantial, honest subjects will risk death for them.

This is illustrated in **Wenzi’s analogy of beasts and deer**: beasts and deer seek only the finest grass, just as subjects gravitate toward those who offer the greatest rewards.

Thus, **King Goujian burned his palace**: he burned his own residence to test whether people would obey orders to save the fire, verifying their loyalty.

**Wu Qi leaned on his chariot’s axle**: he rewarded those who moved the axle to demonstrate his sincerity and trustworthiness.

**Li Kui settled disputes through archery**: he wanted people to excel in archery, so he judged cases as if they were archery contests.

**Song Chong’s gate policy led to many deaths**: when mourners in Chong’s gate starved themselves, the ruler granted them official positions, leading to many deaths by self-neglect.

**Goujian understood this**, so he **displayed the severed head of a tiger (nì)** to inspire bravery.

**Zhao Zhao understood this**, so he hid his worn trousers: he offered great rewards to encourage people to act like **Bì and Zhū**—even women would gather silkworms and fishermen would catch fish without fear, for the reward outweighed the difficulty.

This is the effect of **obtaining profit while forgetting hardship**.

**Rewards must be substantial.**

「When a ruler listens to only one voice, wisdom and folly are indistinguishable. If he adheres to a single principle without reconsidering and verifying it, wisdom and folly will not be discerned.

**Holding subordinates accountable prevents ministers from conspiring.** If every ability and shortcoming of his subjects is singled out for blame, then ministers will not dare to mix truth with deceit.

**This is illustrated by the case of the Duke of Zhao seeking to annex the state of Zheng.** The Duke of Zhao demanded Zheng’s territory, claiming it as his own, but did not consider that Zheng itself had once taken land from the state of Liang. Meanwhile, the people of Zheng also demanded Liang’s territory, claiming it as their own. This was the flaw of **listening to only one voice**.

**Like the story of the South Gate Archers.** When the South Gate Archers played together, no one was held responsible for their individual skill, so they could all blend in without distinction. The harm in this lies in **Shen Zi’s use of Zhao Shao and Han Que as test subjects.** Shen Zi, when requesting soldiers for Zhao, first had Zhao Shao and Han Que test the Zhao ruler’s resolve, then revealed their true intentions and ultimately succeeded in their private schemes.

**Thus, the Marquis of Wu proposed dividing the land east of the Yellow River**, but the King of Han wanted to take it to provoke the three rival states—this was not a sound plan. The Marquis of Wu urged the king to proceed with the order, while **Ying Hou plotted to relax control over Shangdang.** Ying Hou’s scheme to weaken Shangdang was also not a sound plan, yet the King of Qin followed it. These three cases are all examples of the harm of **listening to only one voice**.

**When favors are repeatedly granted but not acted upon, deceit will flourish.** If a ruler frequently shows his favor to certain individuals but does not actually employ them, outsiders will assume he approves of their actions, and they will eventually dare to act deceitfully, like deer straying from the herd.

**This leads to questioning others without revealing one’s own schemes.** If a ruler appears to inquire about others while secretly knowing their intentions, then even those who are not clever will seem to possess wisdom. If his understanding of a single matter is profound, all hidden deceits will be exposed.

**This is illustrated by the Duke of Zhao’s grasp of a single claw.** The Duke of Zhao feigned losing a claw to test the sincerity of his ministers. Thus, he uncovered the truth behind the **Southern Gate’s ox trampling the crops**, and all the offenders from the three districts were revealed.

**The Duke of Zhou searched for the crooked staff**, and the Duke of Zhou deliberately lost his jade hairpin to seek praise for his divine insight. **The Grand Steward of Shang debated cow dung**, using absurd reasoning to gain a reputation for thorough investigation. The ruler who **tests with favors** will find those who profit from them.

**Using one’s own wisdom to question others brings the unwise forward.** If a ruler employs his own intelligence to probe others, even those who lack wisdom will appear to be wise.

**If his understanding of a single matter is deep**, all hidden deceits will be transformed and exposed. This is seen in the Duke of Zhao’s **grasp of a single claw**.

**Using reversed speech to test suspected deceit reveals hidden schemes.** If a ruler deliberately distorts his words and reverses his actions to test those he suspects, he will uncover their deceit. Thus, **Yang Shan’s guard of the wooden stake** exposed the truth by feigning neglect of the stake, revealing the king’s suspicions. **Dai Que’s false mission for Qin** deceived the Qin ruler to test his hatred. The people of Qi, intending to cause disorder, feigned driving away those they loved to see if the ruler would remain unaware. **Zi Zi’s white horse ploy** involved falsely claiming a white horse to verify the sincerity of his ministers. **Zi Chan’s separation of litigants** divided the disputants to easily obtain the true emotions of both parties. **Duke Ji of Qi’s passing through the market crossroads** knew that those who paid gold to pass would reveal his reputation for listening and investigating. **Reversed speech seven.**

**The Right Classic states:** One.»

During the reign of Duke Ling of Wei, **Mi Zi Xia** was favored and held absolute sway over the state. A dwarf who had an audience with the duke said:

*"Your servant’s dreams have become humble."*

The duke asked, *"What did you dream?"*

The dwarf replied, *"I dreamed of a hearth—this was to see Your Majesty."*

The duke grew angry and said, *"I have heard that those who meet with a sovereign dream of the sun. Why, when seeing me, do you dream of lightning?"*

The dwarf answered, *"The sun illuminates the entire world, and no single thing can block it. This means no one can obscure the sovereign’s radiance. Therefore, those who meet with a sovereign dream of the sun.

But a hearth—when one person stands before it, none behind can see. If one person obscures the light of favor, then none behind can see it. *Flame* means *burn*—today, is there not someone who burns before the sovereign? This is a censure of Mi Zi Xia for monopolizing and obscuring the sovereign’s clarity. Then, even if your servant dreams of a hearth, is it not justified?"*

Duke Ai of Lu asked Confucius, *"The vulgar saying goes: ‘Do not consult the many, or you will be misled.’ If one acts without consulting the many, one will surely be confused. Now, when I deliberate with my ministers, my state grows only more disordered. Why is this?"*

Confucius replied, *"When an enlightened ruler consults his ministers, one knows and one does not. When one knows and another does not, this allows for repeated scrutiny and censure. Thus, with the enlightened ruler above and ministers offering honest counsel below, this is the way.

But today, no minister dares to speak against the *Ji* family’s path. The entire state has been transformed into one mind, and the state already unified—then you may judge right and wrong. Even if you consult all within the borders, you will still not escape disorder. The people within the borders also align with the *Ji* family, so consulting them is useless."*

**Reversed speech, one.**

When **Yan Zi** was on a diplomatic mission to Lu, Duke Ai asked, *"The saying goes: ‘Do not consult three, or you will be misled.’ If one acts without consulting three, one will surely be confused. Yet when I deliberate with the entire state, Lu does not fall into disorder. Why is this?"*

Yan Zi said, *"The ancient saying ‘Do not consult three, or you will be misled’ meant that if one person errs and two correct, three are sufficient to represent the many. Hence, ‘Do not consult three, or you will be misled.’

But today, the ministers of Lu number in the thousands and hundreds. Though they speak in unison for the *Ji* family’s private interests, the many who speak are still but one voice. How can there be three?"*

There was a man of Qi who said to the King of Qi, *"The River Spirit is a great deity. Why does Your Majesty not attempt to meet with it? I beg leave to arrange for Your Majesty to encounter it."*

Thus, he constructed a grand altar upon the river and had the king stand there with him. After a while, a large fish stirred, and he declared, *"This is the River Spirit!"*—truly believing the words of one man, hence this folly.

Zhang Yi wished to use the combined strength of Qin and Han to attack Qi and Jing, while Hui Shi sought to lay down arms. If Qi and Jing were to serve as allies, Qin and Han would not dare to strike, and thus the war could be averted. The two men argued, and all the ministers and attendants sided with Zhang Zi, declaring the attack on Qi and Jing advantageous, while none supported Hui Zi’s words. The king indeed heeded Zhang Zi’s advice, dismissing Hui Zi’s counsel as unfeasible. The matter of attacking Qi and Jing was settled, and when Hui Zi entered to see him, the king said, *"Master, do not speak further."* The attack on Qi and Jing was indeed beneficial, and all the state agreed. Hui Zi then remarked, *"This must be examined carefully."* If the attack on Qi and Jing were truly beneficial, and all the state deemed it so, what a multitude of wise men! If the attack on Qi and Jing were truly disadvantageous, yet all the state deemed it beneficial, what a multitude of fools! In all deliberation, there must be doubt. Doubt arises only after careful consideration. When there is genuine doubt, half may deem it favorable, half unfavorable. If doubt is sincere, then half may find it favorable, half unfavorable. Now, if all the state deems it favorable, the king has lost half his judgment. Without those who raise doubt, half his wisdom is lost. Those who seize power naturally lose half their judgment. Without anyone to raise doubt, great thieves may scheme at will. Tian Cheng and Zhao Gao succeeded in their treasonous ambitions—because no one doubted them.

Zhong Sun served as Ru’s minister, wielding great authority and decisive power. Among those he favored was a man named Shù Niú, who also held sway over Zhong Sun’s commands. Zhong Sun had a son named Rén, and Shù Niú envied him, desiring to kill him. He arranged for Rén to play with Ru’s lord, who bestowed a jade armband upon him. Rén bowed in acceptance but dared not wear it, instead sending Shù Niú to request permission from Zhong Sun. Shù Niú deceived him, saying, *"I have already requested it for you; you may wear it."* Rén thus wore the armband, and when Zhong Sun saw him, he flew into a rage and killed Rén. Rén’s elder brother was named Bǐng, and Shù Niú again envied him, desiring to kill him. Zhong Sun commissioned a bell for Bǐng, and when it was finished, Bǐng dared not strike it, so he sent Shù Niú to request permission from Zhong Sun. Shù Niú refused to ask, instead deceiving him again, saying, *"I have already requested it for you; you may strike it."* Bǐng thus struck the bell. Zhong Sun heard this and said, *"Bǐng struck the bell without requesting permission—angry, I banish him."* Bǐng fled to Qi and lived there for a year. Shù Niú then sought to apologize to Zhong Sun, who sent Shù Niú to summon him. Shù Niú did not call him but reported, *"I have already summoned him, yet Bǐng, enraged, refuses to come."* Zhong Sun was furious and ordered his death. When the two sons had perished, Zhong Sun fell ill. Shù Niú then alone tended to him, excluding others, and forbade any woman from entering, saying, *"Zhong Sun does not wish to hear the voices of others."* He starved him to death, and when Zhong Sun was dead, Shù Niú did not announce the mourning, instead transferring his household treasures and fleeing to Qi. To heed only what one trusts, and see one’s sons and fathers slaughtered by others—this is the calamity of failing to weigh opposing views!
