Jiāng Qǐ, as the king’s envoy to the state of Jing, said to the Jing king, *"When I entered Your Majesty’s realm, I heard of a custom in your state: ‘A gentleman does not conceal another’s virtues or speak of another’s faults.’ Is this truly so?"* The king replied, *"It is."* Then Jiāng Qǐ asked, *"If so, how could the rebellion of Bái Gōng have posed such danger?"* If one does not speak of another’s faults, Bái Gōng’s treachery would have succeeded, and thus the danger. If such were truly the case, then I would have died in vain. To conceal faults is to invite calamity—what crime is there greater? The heir apparent valued his concubine as his own ear, cherishing Shì Jī, yet fearing she might exploit his affection and obstruct his path. Thus, he elevated a lesser concubine to rival Shì Jī, saying, *"Let them balance each other."* The heir knew how to avoid obstruction but had not yet mastered the art.

To prevent the low from criticizing the high, the low must not be allowed to participate in discussions with the high. If the low commit crimes, they must be blamed for their involvement in deliberations with the high. Yet if one must wait until power is equalized before seeking opposing views, then when both are equally matched, it is precisely the time to engage in mutual discussion. This only strengthens the obstructionist ministers. If both factions conspire together, the obstruction becomes even more severe—this is the heir’s failure to master the art.

The heir’s obstruction began thus. Now, when arrows come, they have a direction—*xiāng*, meaning a fixed point. If arrows come from a particular direction, one should gather iron to defend that *xiāng*. That is, to concentrate iron in one place to defend a single point, is to leave gaps in one’s armor. If arrows come from all directions, then one should build an *iron chamber* to defend against them fully. That is, complete armor—from head to foot, all covered in iron—thus called an *iron chamber*. To defend fully means the body will not be harmed. Hence, if one defends fully against all directions, the body remains uninjured, and if one fully opposes treachery in all its forms, there will be no deceit.

The king should also fully oppose his ministers, all those he must guard against and suspect, and thus deceit will be cut off. Páng Gōng asked the prince of Tai, *"If one man says the market has a tiger, would Your Majesty believe him?"* The prince replied, *"I would not."* *"If two men say the market has a tiger, would Your Majesty believe them?"* *"I would not."* *"If three men say the market has a tiger, would Your Majesty believe them?"* The prince said, *"I would believe them."* Páng Gōng said, *"It is clear the market has no tiger, yet three men’s words make it so."*

Now the distance from Handan to Wei is farther than to the market, yet those discussing my ministers far exceed three men. I wish Your Majesty would investigate this.

Páng Góng returned from Handan but never gained audience again.

---

Dòng Gūzhī served as the governor of Zhào’s Upper Lands, traveling through the mountains near Shíyì. The ravine was deep, its cliffs sheer as walls, a hundred *zhang* in height. He asked the nearby villagers and attendants: *"Has anyone ever entered this place?"* They replied: *"None have."* *"Have even infants, the deaf, the mad, or the wayward ever entered?"* They replied: *"None have."* *"Have cattle, horses, dogs, or pigs ever entered?"* They replied: *"None have."* Dòng Gūzhī sighed deeply and said: *"Now I know how to govern! If my laws are without pardon, like entering this ravine means certain death, then no one would dare transgress. Why would disorder arise?"*

Zǐchǎn served as chancellor of Zhèng. As he lay dying, he told Yóují: *"After my death, you must govern Zhèng. You must rule with strictness."* Fire takes shape in severity, so few are burned. Water takes shape in weakness, so many drown. You must enforce your punishments with strictness and not let weakness drown your rule. Thus, after Zǐchǎn’s death, Yóují could not bear to enforce severe punishments, and the youths of Zhèng banded together to rob, taking refuge in the Guànzé marshes, threatening to become Zhèng’s ruin. Yóují led his chariots and cavalry to battle them for a day and night, barely overcoming them. Yóují sighed deeply and said: *"Had I followed my master’s teachings earlier, I would not now regret this."*

Duke Aí of Lǔ asked Zhòngní: *"The Spring and Autumn Annals record: ‘In the twelfth month of winter, the frost did not kill the beans.’ Why is this recorded?"* Zhòngní replied: *"This records what should have been killed but was not."* When what ought to be punished is not punished, the plum and apricot bear fruit in winter. Heaven loses its Way, and even grasses and trees violate the seasons—how much more so for rulers? When a ruler loses the Way, his subjects should overstep him. Under the Yīn dynasty’s laws, those who discarded ashes in the streets were punished. Zǐgòng thought this excessive and asked Zhòngní about it. Zhòngní said: *"This shows he knew the Way of governance."* Discarding ashes in the streets would surely cover people, scattering dust and blinding them. When people are covered, they grow angry, and anger leads to fighting, which would destroy three extended families. Fighting would lead to mutual injury—this is the way to destroy three families, even if it were the state of Jīn. Moreover, grave crimes are what people hate, yet discarding ashes is what people easily neglect. If people avoid what is easy and do not stray from what they hate, this is the Way of governance.

Another account says: Under the Yīn dynasty’s laws, those who discarded ashes on the public thoroughfare had their hands cut off. Zǐgòng said: *"The crime of discarding ashes is light, yet the punishment of amputation is severe. Why were the ancients so harsh?"* Harshness means cruelty. He said: *"Discarding ashes is what people easily neglect, and amputation is what they hate. If people avoid what is easy and do not stray from what they hate, the ancients deemed it easy to enforce, hence they did so."*

The chancellor of Zhōngshān, Lèchí, sent a hundred chariots to Zhào, selecting his clever and capable clients to lead the expedition, commanding those who would guide the troops along the road, assigning them their ranks. Halfway through the journey, chaos erupted. Lèchí said: *"I took you for a man of wisdom, appointing you to lead this expedition. Now you cause disorder mid-journey—why?"* The client excused himself and left, saying: *"You do not understand governance. Those who can be coerced by authority and motivated by rewards can be governed. Now, your ministers are your lesser clients, and your words are those of a lesser man. To follow the young over the old, to govern the noble through the base, yet not hold the reins of reward and punishment to control them—this is why disorder arises. If you were to test this by promoting their capable men to high office and executing those unworthy, why would there still be disorder?"*

The laws of Gōngsūn Lǔ were severe for minor offenses. Grave crimes are what people avoid, while petty transgressions are what people easily abandon. If people abandon what is easy and do not stray from what is grave, this is the Way of governance. If petty transgressions are not committed, grave crimes will not arise—thus, people remain guiltless and disorder does not emerge. Now, grave crimes are lightly punished, and petty crimes are evaded, so guiltless people do not arise, and disorder does not cease.

Another account says: Gōngsūn Lǔ said: *"If punishments are severe for minor offenses, minor offenses will not occur, and grave crimes will not be committed. If people do not commit minor offenses, grave crimes naturally do not arise."* This is called *"using punishment to eliminate punishment."* With light punishments, grave crimes are removed.

In the lands of Kè Nán, beside the Lìshuǐ River, gold was mined. Many secretly dug for it. The prohibition against mining gold was strictly enforced: those caught were publicly flayed and displayed in the market, in great numbers, blocking the river’s flow. Additional guards and barriers were set up, forcing people away from the water. Yet the theft of gold did not cease. Though the punishment of flaying was severe, it did not stop them, for they were not certain to be caught. If those who commit crimes are not always apprehended, people will seek ways to escape punishment and lightly commit grave crimes. Hence, it is said: *"I would give you the world but kill you—no ordinary person would do this."* Though possessing the world is a great gain, no one would do it because they know death is certain. If apprehension is not certain, even flaying will not stop the theft of gold. If death is certain, even the world would not be sought.

The people of Lǔ burned the Jízé marshes. A north wind blew, and the fire leaned southward, its flames spreading south—hence it was called *yǐ*. Fearful of burning the capital, Duke Aí was terrified and personally led the troops to extinguish the fire, but his attendants fled, chasing wild beasts, and the fire was not quelled. He then summoned Zhòngní, who said: *"Those who chased the beasts took pleasure in it and faced no punishment, while those who fought the fire suffered without reward—this is why the fire was not extinguished."* Duke Aí said: *"Well spoken."* Zhòngní said: *"In an emergency, there was no time to offer rewards, so grant rewards to all who fought the fire. But the state cannot afford to reward everyone. Instead, let us now enforce punishments."*

Duke Aí said: *"Well said."* Then Zhòngní issued an order: *"Those who failed to fight the fire shall be punished as if they had fled northward in battle. Those who chased wild beasts shall be punished as if they had entered the forbidden grounds."*

The order had not yet been fully proclaimed when the fire was already extinguished.

Chéng Huān said to the King of Qí: *"Your Majesty is too merciful, too unwilling to punish."*

The King said: *"To be too merciful and too unwilling to punish—is that not a virtue?"*

Chéng Huān replied: *"This is the virtue of a subject, not the conduct of a sovereign. A subject must be merciful to be trusted with counsel, and unwilling to punish to be approachable. But if he is not merciful, he cannot be trusted with counsel; if he punishes too harshly, he cannot be approached."*

The King said: *"Then where should I be merciful and where should I not be?"*

Chéng Huān replied: *"Your Majesty is too merciful to Xué Gōng, yet too unwilling to punish the Zhū Tián clan. Being too merciful to Xué Gōng means the great ministers are not held in esteem. If they are indulged in arrogance and extravagance without cultivating virtue and righteousness, the people will despise them, and thus Your Majesty’s authority will not be revered. Being too unwilling to punish the Zhū Tián clan means that fathers and brothers break the law. If the great ministers are not held in esteem, the military will weaken externally, and if fathers and brothers break the law, governance will falter internally. A weak military externally and chaotic governance internally—these are the roots of a state’s ruin."*

King Huì of Wèi said to Bǔ Pí: *"What do you think of my reputation?"*

Bǔ Pí replied: *"I have heard that Your Majesty is benevolent and kind."*

The King, delighted, said: *"Then what great achievements will follow?"*

Bǔ Pí replied: *"Your Majesty’s achievements will lead to ruin."*

The King said: *"Benevolence and kindness are acts of virtue—how can practicing them lead to ruin?"*

Bǔ Pí replied: *"The merciful are unwilling to punish, and the kind are quick to bestow. If one does not punish wrongdoing, and rewards without merit, even ruin would not be unexpected."*

In the state of Qí, elaborate burials were the fashion—silks and linens were exhausted in shrouds, and fine woods in coffins and outer cases. Duke Huán was troubled by this and consulted Guān Zhòng, saying: *"If all silks and linens are used for burial shrouds, there will be none left for clothing; if all fine woods are used for coffins and outer cases, there will be none left for fortifications. Yet people persist in extravagant burials—how should I prohibit them?"*

Guān Zhòng replied: *"People act either for fame or for gain. Thus, I decree: Those whose coffins and outer cases exceed proper measure shall have their corpses mutilated, and those responsible for the funeral shall be punished."*

Since mutilating the dead brings no honor, and punishing those in mourning yields no gain, why would anyone persist in such practices?

In the time of the heir to the throne of Wèi, a convict named Xū Mí escaped to Wèi and was employed by King Xiāng of Wèi’s physician. The heir of Wèi, hearing of this, sent envoys to offer fifty *jīn* of gold to buy him back, but after five attempts, King Xiāng refused. The heir then offered the city of Zuǒ Shì in exchange.

Zuǒ Shì was the name of a walled city. The ministers and attendants remonstrated: *"Is it proper to trade an entire city for a single convict?"*

The heir said: *"This is not for you to understand. Disorder begins with small matters, and chaos grows from great ones. If small offenses are not corrected, greater chaos will arise. If laws are not enforced and punishments are not certain—if crimes are punished arbitrarily—then it is said that punishments are not fixed. Even if ten Zuǒ Shì were lost, it would be of no benefit. But if laws are upheld and punishments are certain, even the loss of ten Zuǒ Shì would be of no harm."*

King Xiāng of Wèi, hearing of this, said: *"The sovereign wishes to govern but is not obeyed—this is an omen of misfortune."* He then loaded the convict and sent him as a gift.

Though Xū Mí was a convict, the city was not taken in exchange.

---

King Wén of Qí asked Wén Zǐ: *"How should one govern a state?"*

Wén Zǐ replied: *"The Dao of rewards and punishments is a sharp tool. The sovereign must hold it firmly and must not display it to others. If one were like me, they would be like a deer—only drawn to the offering of fresh grass. A deer is drawn to fresh grass, and ministers are drawn to heavy rewards. Thus, the sharp tool of rewards and punishments must not be revealed to others.*

The King of Yuè asked the minister Wén Zhǒng: *'I wish to attack Hào. Is this possible?'*
Wén Zhǒng replied: *'It is possible. My rewards are heavy and trustworthy, my punishments severe and certain. If you wish to test this, why not set fire to the palace?'*
So he set fire to the palace, and no one came to save it. He then issued an order: *'Those who save the fire shall die—rewarded as if they had defeated an enemy. Those who save the fire and do not die shall be rewarded as if they had won a battle. Those who do not save the fire shall be punished as if they had surrendered.'*
Men blackened their bodies, wrapped themselves in wet clothes, and rushed into the flames—three thousand on the left, three thousand on the right. This proved the certainty of victory.

Wú Qǐ served as the governor of Wèi Wǔ Hóu’s Western River region. There was a small Qin outpost near the border, and Wú Qǐ wished to attack it. If left, it would greatly harm the farmers. He said, *'This outpost harms the fields, so it must be removed.'* But removing it would weaken the ability to mobilize troops. Since it was a minor matter, he leaned a single cart axle outside the North Gate and proclaimed: *'Whoever moves this to the South Gate shall be granted the finest fields and residences.'* No one moved it. Then someone did move it, and he was rewarded as promised. Next, he placed a sack of red beans outside the East Gate and proclaimed: *'Whoever moves this to the West Gate shall be rewarded as before.'* The people competed to move it. He then ordered the officials: *'Tomorrow, we shall attack the outpost. Whoever first climbs it shall be appointed a high-ranking minister and granted the finest fields and residences.'* The people rushed to obey, and the outpost was taken in a single morning.

Lǐ Kuā served as the governor of Wèi Wén Hóu’s Upper Lands and wished the people to excel in archery. He issued an order: *'Those with disputes shall settle them by shooting at a target—the target being the object of their dispute. The one who hits wins; the one who misses loses.'* The order was given, and the people eagerly practiced archery day and night without rest. When they fought against the Qin, they defeated them greatly, for the people were skilled in battle and archery.

In Sòng, a man from Chóngmén Lane served his parents with such extreme mourning that he wasted away. The ruler took this as proof of his filial devotion and appointed him an official teacher. The following year, ten or more people died from excessive mourning. If a child mourns their parents so deeply that they can be rewarded for it, how much more should the ruler reward the people? If a ruler does not reward merit, then no great deeds will be accomplished.

The King of Yuè, planning to attack Wú, wished the people to disregard death. He went out and saw an enraged rhinoceros, and he bowed to it in salute. His attendants asked: *'Why do you salute this?'*
The king replied: *'Because it has such qi.'*
The next year, those who offered their heads to the king numbered ten or more annually. From this, it is clear that praise can move people to kill themselves.

When the King of Yuè saw the enraged rhinoceros and bowed to it, his charioteer asked: *'Why do you salute it?'*
The king replied: *'The rhinoceros has such qi—how could I not salute it?'*
The scholars heard this and said: *'The rhinoceros has qi, yet the king salutes it. How much more should those with true courage be honored!'*
That year, men began presenting their heads in self-offering.

When the King of Yuè prepared to avenge Wú and tested his people’s training, he burned a tower and drummed, commanding the people to rush into the fire—reward awaiting them in the flames. Though fire kills, those who rushed into it feared nothing, for they were certain of reward. He did the same by the river, commanding them to rush into the water—reward awaiting them there. In battle, he ordered men to sever heads and disembowel themselves without hesitation—reward awaiting them in war. How much greater, then, would be their willingness to advance the worthy by law, for the aid in this is far greater still! Advancing the worthy grants reward, without the dangers of fire or water, so who would not do it? Those who do not advance the worthy do so only because the ruler does not reward them.

Hán Zhāo Hóu had a worn-out pair of trousers hidden away. His attendant said: *'Your Grace is also unkind—you do not give these to your servants but hide them.'*
Zhāo Hóu replied: *'This is not for you to understand. I have heard that an enlightened ruler’s every frown and smile must be weighed—worrying that they may not be well-placed, encouraging what is good, and never acting recklessly. A frown must have cause, and a smile must have cause. Now, these trousers—how much more so than a frown or smile! If a frown or smile must not be given without reason, how much more so these trousers, which must not be given without merit!'*
The trousers and a frown or smile are far apart—I must wait for those who earn reward, so I store them away, not yet having given them.

The *mǐ* resembles a snake, and the *zhú* resembles a candle. Men are terrified by snakes and shudder at candles. Yet women gather silkworms, and fishermen handle *mǐ*—for where profit lies, they abandon what they fear, all becoming as brave as Mèng Bēn.

Because the *mǐ* and silkworms yield profit, men grasp and gather them, all displaying the courage of Mèng Bēn.

The King of Wèi said to the King of Zhèng: *'Once, Zhèng and Liáng were one state. Later, they separated. Now, I wish to reclaim Zhèng and reunite it with Liáng.'*
The King of Zhèng was troubled and called his ministers to devise a response to Wèi. A prince of Zhèng said to the king: *'This is easily answered. You should say to Wèi: "If Zhèng can be reunited with the old Wèi, then my humble state would also wish to reclaim Liáng and reunite it with Zhèng."'*
The King of Wèi then ceased his demand.

The King of Qi’s court musician required three hundred players to perform the *sú* (panpipes). Nánguō Chǔshì, a reclusive scholar, requested permission to play for the king. The king delighted in him and provided him with rations as if he were one of the three hundred.

The King of Qi died, and King Min of Qi ascended the throne, delighting in hearing the *sú* played one by one. The reclusive scholar fled.

The Lord of Hàn Zhāoshòu said: *"The number of those who play the sú is great; I have no way to know who among them is truly skilled."*

Tián Yán replied: *"Let the king hear them one by one."*

Zhào sent someone to persuade Shēn Zǐ of Hàn to request soldiers, intending to attack Wèi. Shēn Zǐ was afraid to speak of this to his lord, fearing that the lord might suspect him of seeking personal gain—this is what is meant by *shì* (market). If he refused, he feared that Zhào would hold it against him, so he had Zhào Shào and Hàn Yǎo secretly observe the lord’s reactions before reporting them. The changes in his lord’s demeanor could be discerned, hence the term *dòng mào* (reactions). Internally, he understood the Lord of Hàn’s intentions, while externally, he secured a favor from Zhào. After requesting the soldiers, if the lord agreed, the favor would certainly be granted. If he did not agree, Shēn Zǐ would still persist in the request, yet he would not dare to accept the favor, thus ensuring Zhào’s obligation. When the armies of the three states arrived, the King of Hàn said to Lóu Huǎn: *"The armies of the three states are deeply entrenched. I wish to cede Hédōng in exchange for peace—what do you think?"* (Here, *jiǎng* means to yield under pressure, hoping to retain the matter for later negotiation, though ultimately it would revert, much like a debate—hence the term *jiǎng*.)

Lóu Huǎn replied: *"To cede Hédōng would be a great expense. To deliver your state from calamity would be a great deed."*

「This is the duty of your father and elder brothers, my lord. Why not summon **Gōngzǐ Shì** and consult him?

The king summoned **Gōngzǐ Shì** and told him his thoughts. Shì replied: *"Whether you yield or do not yield, both will bring regret."*

If the king now cedes Hédōng to negotiate peace, and the three states withdraw, he will surely say: *"The three states were bound to leave anyway; I merely gave them three cities to send them off."* The three states would leave on their own, and yet he would still give them cities—this would mean he had given away three cities for nothing, and these are words of regret.

If he does not yield, the three states will still invade Hàn, and then the state must mobilize its full strength. The king will certainly regret it deeply, saying: *"I did not offer the three cities."* If he does not yield, the three states will invade and Hàn must surely muster its full strength, and the king will regret it, saying: *"I did not offer the three cities—that is why!"*

Thus, I say: *"Whether the king yields or does not yield, both will bring regret."*

The king said: *"You speak of my regret. I would rather lose the three cities than face regret—only when in peril will I regret."* I have now made my decision not to negotiate peace.

(Here, *jiǎng* refers to the matter of negotiating peace having been settled.)

**Yìng Hóu** said to the **Qín King**: *"Your Majesty has seized Rǎn Yè, Lántián, and Yángxià, cut off Hénèi, and besieged Liáng and Zhèng. The reason you have not yet achieved universal dominion is that Zhào has not yet submitted. Relinquishing Shàngdǎng would be like abandoning a single city—discarding one prefecture alone. But if you use the troops guarding Shàngdǎng to threaten Dōngyáng, then Hán Dān would be in peril like a flea in one’s mouth. Your Majesty could then bow to the heavens and rule all under heaven, but later, they would strike at you with their armies. (Here, *zhòng* means to harm or wound.) Yet, Shàngdǎng’s security and ease come at a great cost. Its location is extremely perilous, and I fear that even if you were to abandon it, the king would not heed my advice. What should I do?"*

Now, Shàngdǎng is secure and prosperous, yet its location is fraught with danger. Even if one wished to abandon it, I fear the king would not listen.

The king said: *"I must certainly abandon it and relocate its troops to threaten Dōngyáng."* I have now definitively decided.

Five. **Páng Jìng**, a county magistrate, sent merchants on their way, then summoned the **Gōngdàfū** to return them. The **Gōngdàfū** also sent men to trade. Without clear orders, they were sent away repeatedly, and in the end, they were all dispatched on their journeys. (Here, *bù mìng* means they were sent without explicit command, and *zuì* means they were finally dispatched—both parties could not fathom the reasoning behind it.)

The merchants thought that the county magistrate and the **Gōngdàfū** had some agreement, yet they did not trust one another, and thus no deception arose.

The **Gōngdàfū**, though informed that the merchants were sent without explicit command (*bù mìng*), still did not believe it, so they dared not deceive.

**Dài Huān**, the **Dàzāi** of Sòng, sent a man at night saying: *"I have heard that for several nights there have been men riding in a **fán chē** arriving at Lǐ Shǐ’s gate. Be vigilant and observe them for me."* The man returned and reported: *"No **fán chē** was seen, but there were men carrying bamboo shoots and speaking with Lǐ Shǐ. Later, Lǐ Shǐ accepted the bamboo shoots."* He was sent to spy on the **fán chē**, but in reality, they were carrying bamboo shoots. The original order was to spy on those carrying bamboo shoots—Lǐ Shǐ would surely change his words.

**Zhōu Zhǔ** lost his jade hairpin, and ordered his officials to search for it. After three days, they could not find it. The officials were then ordered to search again and found it in the chamber of a household servant. **Zhōu Zhǔ** said: *"My officials are not diligent in their duties."* (This means: they are not diligent in attending to my affairs.) Having searched for three days without finding the hairpin, I ordered them to search again, and they found it without delay. Thus, the officials were all filled with dread, believing their lord to be **shén míng** (divinely insightful).

The **Tàizāi** of Shāng sent his junior minister to the market, then turned back and asked him: *"What did you see in the market?"* The minister replied: *"I saw nothing."* The **Tàizāi** said: *"Even so, what did you see?"*

The junior minister replied: *"Outside the South Gate of the market, there were many ox-carts, but they were only passable."* The **Tàizāi** then admonished the messenger, saying: *"Do not dare to inform anyone about what I asked you."* He then summoned the market officials and reproached them: *"Why is there so much ox dung outside the market gates?"*

The market officials were greatly astonished that the **Tàizāi** knew so swiftly, and thus they feared his insight.

**Six.**
The **Zhāozhōu** of Hàn pretended to lose one of his fingers, searching for it with great urgency. His attendants then cut their own fingers to imitate it, but the **Zhāozhōu** discerned that they were not sincere in their loyalty.

Cutting fingers, not sincere.

The **Zhāozhōu** sent a groom to the county market, and when the messenger returned, he asked: *"What did you see?"* The messenger replied: *"I saw nothing."* The **Zhāozhōu** said: *"Even so, what did you see?"* The messenger answered: *"Outside the South Gate, there was a yellow calf eating the crops along the left side of the road."* The **Zhāozhōu** then told the messenger not to dare reveal what he had asked, and issued an order: *"When the crops are being harvested, I forbid oxen and horses from entering the fields of my people—even if ordered to do so by my decree. Yet the officials do not take this as their duty, and many oxen and horses still enter the fields. Quickly report their numbers to me, and if you cannot find them, I will impose severe punishment."* Thus, the three districts reported the incidents and stopped them. The **Zhāozhōu** said: *"This is not all."* He went again to investigate, and finally found the yellow calf outside the South Gate. The officials, recognizing the **Zhāozhōu**’s keen discernment, all feared his scrutiny and dared not act wrongfully.

The **Zhōu Zhǔ** issued an order to search for a crooked staff, and the officials sought it for several days without success. The **Zhōu Zhǔ** then secretly sent someone to find it, who discovered it without delay. He then said to the officials: *"I know that you are not diligent in your duties."* The crooked staff was easy to find, yet the officials could not obtain it. I sent someone to seek it, and it was found without delay—how can you be considered loyal? The officials then all feared his scrutiny, believing their lord to be **shén míng**.

**Seven.**
The **Yángshān Jūn** served as prime minister of Wèi, and when he heard that the king suspected him, he falsely slandered **Qú Shù** to test the king’s reaction. **Qú Shù** was the king’s favorite, and when he was falsely slandered, he would be enraged and reveal the king’s suspicions.

**Dùn Chǐ** heard that the **Wén Wáng** of Qí hated him, so he feigned being a messenger from Qín to test this.

The king, not suspecting the Qín messenger, would surely accept the report.

A man of Qí who wished to cause chaos feared that the king would know, so he falsely drove away the king’s favorite, causing him to flee, and thus the king would know.

The king, knowing that the favorite had been driven away, would not suspect him of causing chaos.

**Zǐ Zhī** served as prime minister of Yān, and while seated, he pretended to say: *"The white horse that ran out of the gate—what was it?"* His attendants all said they had not seen it. One man ran to chase it and reported: *"There is one."* With this, **Zǐ Zhī** knew whether his attendants were sincere and trustworthy.

The one who falsely reported seeing the white horse was not sincere and trustworthy.

When there were litigants disputing before him, **Zǐ Chǎn** separated them and did not allow them to communicate. He then relayed their words in reverse to discern the truth.

If one party uses these words to inform the other, and the other party uses them to inform this one, then the true nature of the litigants’ claims will be known.

The **Sìgōng** of Wèi sent someone as a guest passing through the border market, where the officials harshly obstructed him. The guest then bribed the border officials with gold, and they allowed him to pass. The **Sìgōng** said to the officials: *"At a certain time, there was a guest passing through, and I gave you gold. You then sent him away."* The border officials were greatly alarmed and recognized the **Sìgōng** as one of keen discernment.

The **Six Subtleties**:

1. **Power lent to inferiors** (where the **Dao**/Way is borrowed by those below),
2. **Contrasting private and public interests** (where external gain diverges from internal),
3. **Trusting in false semblances** (where reliance is placed upon deceptive analogies),
4. **Contradictory benefits and harms** (where advantage and detriment invert),
5. **Internal dissension through ambiguous counsel** (where doubt breeds strife within),
6. **Exploiting rival states to undermine the sovereign** (where enemies are provoked to abandon their posts).

These six are the matters the sovereign must observe.

**Power** cannot be lent to others—when the ruler loses it, the minister gains a hundredfold.
Thus, when a minister is lent **power**, his strength increases, and with increased strength, he controls both internal and external affairs, thereby obstructing the sovereign’s **Dao**/Way.
This principle is found in the ancient teachings of **Lǎo** (Laozi) and **Zǐchān** (Zichan).

**Losing fish** (i.e., failing to discern true loyalty).
Therefore, the sovereign speaks at length, while his attendants sell their favors with flattery.
The danger lies in the **būchóng** (buxiong, low-ranking officials) offering harsh counsel.

**Lìgōng** (Ligong, Duke Li of Qi) spoke with the **Zhōuhòu** (Zhouhou, Marquis of Zhou), yet the people of **Yān** (Yan) were struck down by arrows.
**Power lent to inferiors** (1): When the interests of the sovereign and minister diverge, ministers lack loyalty, and while the minister’s gain is established, the sovereign’s **virtue/power** is extinguished.
Thus, treacherous ministers summon enemy forces to purge internal rivals, fabricate external affairs to deceive the sovereign, and pursue their private interests without regard for the state’s calamity.
This is seen in the case of the **Wèirén** (Weiren, wife of the Duke of Wei) praying to her husband.

Hence, **Dàizǎi** (Daisai) plotted against his kin, while the **Sānquán** (Sanquan, Three Marquises) attacked **Zhāogōng** (Zhaogong, Duke Zhao of Lu).
**Gōngchǒu** (Gongchou) invited the **Qí** (Qi) army within, while **Zhāihuáng** (Zhaihuang) summoned the **Hán** (Han) forces.
**Tàizǎi** (Tai Zai) counselled **Dàfù** (Dafu) **Zhǒng** (Zhong), and **Dàchéng Níú** (Dacheng Niu) instructed **Shēnbùxié** (Shenbuhai).
**Sīmǎ Xǐ** (Simaxi) reported to the **Zhào Wáng** (Zhao Wang), and **Lǚ Cāng** (Luyang) advised **Qín** and **Chǔ**.
**Sòng Shí** (Song Shi) dispatched a letter to the **Wèi Jūn** (Wei Jun), and **Bái Guī** (Baigui) taught **Bàoqiǎn** (Baoqian) to expel others.
**Contrasting private and public interests** (2): False analogies lead the sovereign to miss punishment while great ministers fulfill their private schemes.
Thus, the **Ménrén** (Menren) abandoned their water rights, and **Yíshè** (Yishe) was executed.
**Jìyáng** (Jiyang) falsely claimed his own authority, and two men were punished.
**Sīmǎ Xǐ** (Simaxi) killed **Xuān Qián** (Xuanqian), and **Jìxīn** (Jixin) died.
**Zhèng Xuè** (Zhengxue) spoke of foul odors, and the **Xīnrén** (Xinren) was castrated.
**Fèi Wújì** (Feiwuji) instructed **Qī Yuǎn** (Qiyuan), and the **Lìngyīn** (Lingyin) was executed.
**Chén Xū** (Chenxu) killed **Zhāng Shòu** (Zhangshou), and **Xīshǒu** (Xishou) fled.
Hence, the burning of **chú** (straw) and **lì** (grain) led to **Zhōngshān** (Zhongshan)’s punishment, while the killing of **lǎo rú** (old scholars) earned **Jìyáng** (Jiyang) rewards.
**False semblances** (3): When an affair arises with some benefit, the sovereign must examine it, but if it brings harm, he must investigate its reversal.
Thus, the enlightened sovereign’s judgments are: if the state is harmed, he scrutinizes the benefactor; if a minister is harmed, he examines the reversal.
This is seen when **Chǔ**’s army approached and **Chén Xū** (Chenxu) plotted, or when **shǔ zhòng** (glutinous millet) grew expensive and the **lǐn liè** (granary officials) were overthrown.

Hence, **Zhāoxíxù** (Zhaoxixu) seized the **Fàn Máo** (Fanmao) merchant, while **Xīhóu** (Xihou, Duke Xi of Lu) inspected his successor.
**Wén Gōng** (Wengong, Duke Wen of Jin) issued a decree on roasted meat, while **Yáng Hóu** (Yanghou, Duke Yang of Lu) petitioned to establish the **Emperor** (Dì).
**Contradictory benefits and harms** (4): The **power** of ambiguous counsel is the source of disorder.
Thus, the enlightened sovereign must be cautious of it.

Hence, **Lìjī** (Liji) of **Jìn** (Jin) killed **Tàizǐ Shēng** (Taizi Sheng), while the **Zhèng Fūrén** (Zheng Furen) employed poison.
**Zhōu Yú** (Zhouyu) of **Wèi** (Wei) killed his lord **Wán** (Wan), and **Gōngzǐ Gēn** (Gongzi Gen) seized **Dōng Zhōu** (Dongzhou).
**Wángzǐ Zhí** (Wangzi Zhi) was greatly favored, yet **Shāng Chén** (Shangchen) rebelled.
**Yán Suì** (Yansui) and **Hán Mò** (Hanmo) clashed, and **Āihóu** (Aihou, Duke Ai of Jin) was killed by bandits.
**Tián Cháng** (Tianchang), **Gēn Zhǐ** (Genzhi), **Dài Huān** (Daihuan), and **Huáng Xǐ** (Huangxi) opposed, and the **Sòng Jūn** (Song Jun) and **Jiǎn Gōng** (Jiangong, Duke Jian of Qi) were slain.

This principle is seen in **Hú Tú** (Hutu) praising two **good** (hǎo) men, or in **Zhèng Zhāo** (Zhengzhao) questioning the **unborn** (wèishēng).

**Internal dissension through ambiguous counsel** (5): The enemy’s aim is to **exploit** (yín) **discernment** (chá) and **induce** (jiù) **weakness** (mǐ).
If the sovereign does not observe, the enemy will be abandoned and left in place.
Thus, **Wén Wáng** (Wenwang, Duke Wen of Jin) relied on **Fèi Zhòng** (Feizhong), while the **Qín Wáng** (Qinwang) feared **Chǔ**’s envoys.
**Lí Qiě** (Liqie) dismissed **Zhòng Nǐ** (Zhongni), but **Yú Xiàng** (Yuxiang) obstructed **Gān Mào** (Ganmao).
Hence, **Zǐ Bǔ** (Zibu) declared his intentions, and **Yú Cháng** (Yuchang) was employed.
**Internal beauty** (mèirén) was feigned, and **Yú** and **Guó** (Yu and Guo) perished.
A false letter was sent, and **Cháng Hóng** (Changhong) was killed.
**Chicken’s entrails** (jī shāo) were used, and **Yù Jié** (Yujie) was exterminated.

**Exploiting rival states to undermine the sovereign** (6): The enlightened sovereign cuts off such schemes within while applying them externally.
He aids the light and supports the weak—this is called **temple assault** (miào gōng).
If **ambiguous counsel** (cānwù) is employed internally and **espionage** (guān tīng) is practiced externally, then the enemy’s deceit will succeed.
This principle is seen in the **Qín** **jūnróu** (dwarfs) warning **Huì Wén Jūn** (Huiwen Jun).

Hence, **Xiāng Cǐ** (Xiangci) spoke of attacking **Yè** (Yan), while **Sì Gōng** (Sigong) granted him a seat of honor.
**Temple assault** (miào gōng) is recorded in the **Spring and Autumn Annals** (Chūnqiū).

The weighty ministers are the sovereign’s depths.
Ministers are the fish within those depths.
Once the fish is lost in the depths, it cannot be recovered.
When the sovereign loses his weighty ministers to his ministers, they cannot be reclaimed.

The ancients found direct speech difficult, hence they spoke in parables of fish.

Rewards and punishments are sharp instruments.
The sovereign wields them to control his ministers,
while ministers seize them to dominate the sovereign.
Thus, when the sovereign first reveals his rewards,
ministers sell them as virtue;
when the sovereign first reveals his punishments,
ministers sell them as authority.
Hence it is said:
*The state’s sharp instruments must not be displayed to the people.*

Jing Guo Jun served as Qi’s prime minister.
When he spoke at length with old friends, those friends grew wealthy;
when he embraced his attendants, they grew powerful.
Speaking at length with the commander of the guard was but slight favor,
yet it sufficed to make him rich—
what then of the power of officials?

In the time of Duke Li of Jin, the Six Ministers held great power.
Xu Tong, Chang Yu, and Yao Jiao advised:
*"When great ministers grow too powerful,
they vie with the sovereign for influence,
curry favor abroad, sow discord below,
and ultimately coerce the sovereign—
yet a state that does not perish from this has never existed."*

The duke replied, *"Well spoken."*
He executed three of the ministers.

Xu Tong, Chang Yu, and Yao Jiao again advised:
*"When men of equal guilt are punished selectively rather than utterly,
you invite resentment and exploit divisions among them."*

The duke said, *"I spared them out of mercy—
I could not bring myself to destroy them all."*

Yao Jiao replied:
*"Your mercy will be their ruthlessness."*

The duke did not heed him.
Three months later, the ministers rebelled,
killed Duke Li, and divided his lands.

Zhou Hou served as Chu’s prime minister.
Though powerful, he made decisions for the king,
who grew suspicious and questioned his attendants.
They answered in unison: *"None—it is as if from a single mouth."*

The people of Yan, unperturbed, bathed in dog dung.
A man of Yan had a wife who secretly consorted with a scholar.
Her husband returned early from abroad, and the scholar was just leaving.
The husband asked: *"Who is this guest?"*
His wife replied: *"There is no guest."*
He questioned his attendants, and they answered in unison: *"None—it is as if from a single mouth."*

The wife said: *"The master is easily deceived."*
She bathed him in dog dung.

Another account:
A man of Yan named Li Ji often traveled far.
His wife secretly consorted with a scholar.
Li Ji caught them in the act, but the scholar was inside.
The wife, distressed, told her maidservant:
*"Command the young master to rush out naked and disheveled,
and we will pretend not to see."*

The young master followed her plan,
bursting out in haste.
Li Ji asked: *"Who is this man?"*
The household all replied: *"None."*

Li Ji said: *"Do I see a ghost?"*
The woman replied: *"Yes."*
He asked: *"What should I do about it?"*
She said: *"Take one hundred thousand arrows of the five natures’ qi and bathe it."*
Li Ji said: *"Very well."* So he bathed the scholar with the arrows.
Another account says he bathed the scholar in orchid-infused water.

---

Second:
A man of Yan and his wife prayed together, and the diviner said: *"Let me without cause receive a hundred bundles of cloth."*
His husband asked: *"Why so little?"*
The diviner replied: *"More of this, and you will use it to buy a concubine."* The king of Jing wished to exile the noble sons to the neighboring states. Dai Xie said: *"This cannot be done. If noble sons are exiled to the neighboring states, those states will surely honor them. They will say: ‘The exiled son is honored, and if honored, he will certainly become a factional ally of the state that honors him.’ This is teaching your sons to seek favor abroad—it is not advantageous."*

The three sons of the House of Sun of Lu conspired together to seize Duke Zhao of Lu, and they thus seized his state and usurped its authority. The three sons of the House of Sun of Lu were strong, and Duke Zhao attacked the House of Seasonal Sun. The House of Sun’s elder brother and the House of Sun’s younger brother then plotted together, saying: *"Shall we save him?"*
The charioteer of the House of Sun’s younger brother said: *"I am but a household servant—how should I know the affairs of my lord? Whether there is a House of Seasonal Sun or not, which benefits me more?"*
They all replied: *"Without a House of Seasonal Sun, there will be no House of Sun’s younger brother."*
If that is so, then we must save him.
Thus, they struck the northwest corner of the gate and entered. The elder brother of the House of Sun saw the banner of the House of Sun’s younger brother enter and also came to save him. The three houses united, and Duke Zhao was defeated, fleeing to death at Qian Hou.

Gongchou served King Han, and he had great merit with Qi. Gongzhong was highly esteemed by the king, and Gongchou feared that the king would favor Gongzhong. So he made an alliance with Qi and Han to attack Wei. Gongchou then brought the Qi army into Zheng, using it to coerce the Zheng lord and to secure his own position, while keeping faith with the two allied states.

Zhi Huang was a minister of the Wei king, yet he was close to King Han. He summoned the Han army and ordered them to attack Wei, then requested permission to build a fortification for the Wei king to enhance his own standing.

King Yue of Yue attacked King Fu Chai of Wu. King Fu Chai humbly apologized and submitted, and King Yue wished to grant him forgiveness. Fan Li and the Great Official Zhong said: *"This cannot be done. In ancient times, Heaven gave Yue to Wu, but Wu did not accept it, so Heaven turned against Fu Chai—this was Heaven’s retribution. If Yue is given to Wu, accept it with two bows—this cannot be forgiven."*

The Grand Steward Huai sent a letter to the Great Official Zhong, saying: *"When the cunning hare is exhausted, the fine dog is boiled. When the enemy state is destroyed, the scheming minister perishes. Why does the Great Official not release Wu and instead worry about Yue?"*
The Great Official Zhong received the letter, read it, and sighed deeply, saying: *"Kill him, and Yue and Wu will share the same fate."*

Dacheng Niu, following Zhao, told Shen Bu Hai of Han: *"Because Han honors me in Zhao, I ask that Zhao honor you in Han—this way, you will have two Hans, and I will have two Zhaos."*

Sima Xi was a minister of the Zhongshan lord, yet he was close to Zhao. He often secretly revealed Zhongshan’s schemes to the Zhao king.

Lü Cang was a minister of the Wei king, yet he was close to Qin and Jing. He subtly incited Qin and Jing to attack Wei, then requested permission to negotiate peace to enhance his own standing.

Wei’s General Song Shi and the Jing general, Wei Jun, were two generals of their respective states. When the two states were embroiled in conflict, the two men were both leading armies. Song Shi sent a letter to Wei Jun, saying: *"When the two armies face each other, our banners are in sight—do not engage in battle. If battle is fought, both sides will not survive. This is the matter of our two lords; there is no private grudge between us. The wise act by avoiding each other."*

Bai Gui served as the prime minister of Wei, while Puo Qian served as the prime minister of Han. Bai Gui said to Puo Qian: *"You use Han to support me in Wei, and I will use Wei to support you in Han. When I am in power, I will benefit Wei; when you are in power, you will benefit Han."*

---

Third:
A middle-ranking official of Qi named Yi She was serving wine to the king. Drunk, he left and leaned against the gate of the palace attendants. The gatekeeper, having lost a foot and a knee, knelt and begged: *"Does the noble lord have no intention of granting me a remnant servant’s position?"*
Yi She said: *"Begone!"*
The gatekeeper, having lost a foot and a knee, retreated. When Yi She left, the gatekeeper secretly poured water down the gate of the palace attendants, letting it flow to the ground, resembling the appearance of someone drowning. The next day, the king went out and scolded him, saying: *"Who drowned here?"*
The gatekeeper replied: *"I did not see it."*
Although so, yesterday the middle-ranking official Yi She stood here.
The king thus executed Yi She and killed him.

Two ministers of the Wei king were not well-treated by the Duke of Jiyang. The Duke of Jiyang then falsely claimed that someone had forged the king’s orders and plotted to attack him. The king sent someone to question the Duke of Jiyang, saying: *"Who conspired against you?"*
The duke replied: *"There is no one who dared conspire against me."*
Although so, you have often been ill-treated by these two ministers—this is not enough to reach such a point.
The king questioned his attendants, and they replied: *"Indeed so."*
The king thus executed the two ministers.

Ji Xin and Yuan Yan bore a grudge against each other. Sima Xi, newly hostile to Ji Xin, secretly ordered someone to kill Yuan Yan. The lord of Zhongshan believed it was Ji Xin’s doing, and thus executed him.

Among the king of Jing’s beloved concubines was Zheng Yue of Zheng. The king, newly having obtained a beautiful woman, Zheng Yue then instructed her, saying: *"The king greatly enjoys when people cover their mouths in his presence—because you are close to him, you must cover your mouth."*
The beautiful woman entered to see the king, standing close to him, and thus covered her mouth. The king asked about her reason, and Zheng Yue said: *"This is indeed because she fears the king’s odor."*
When the king sat with Zheng Yue and the beautiful woman, Zheng Yue first secretly warned the attendants, saying: *"When the king speaks, quickly obey and respond."*
The king said: *"Beautiful woman, come forward."*
She stood close to him and repeatedly covered her mouth. The king, in confusion, angrily said: *"Sever her nose!"*
The attendants then exposed the blade and severed the beautiful woman’s nose.

Another account says:
The king of Wei sent a concubine to the king of Jing, who greatly delighted in her. The king’s wife, Zheng Yue, knowing that the king loved her, also delighted in her and loved her more than the king himself did. She chose the finest clothes and ornaments for her. The king said: *"My wife knows that I love this new concubine. She delights in her and loves her even more than I do—this is how filial sons nourish their parents, and loyal ministers serve their lords."*

The wife knew that the king did not suspect her of jealousy, so she spoke to the new concubine and said: *"The king greatly delights in and loves you, yet he hates your nose. When you see the king, always cover your nose, then the king will long cherish you."*

Thus, the new concubine obeyed her, and whenever she saw the king, she always covered her nose.

The king asked his wife: *"Why does the new concubine always cover her nose when she sees me?"*
She replied: *"She does not know you well."*
The king insisted on an answer, and she said: *"Recently, she once remarked that she dislikes the smell of me."*
The king, in anger, said: *"Sever her nose!"*
The wife then instructed the attendants: *"When the king speaks, quickly obey and respond."*
The attendants exposed the blade and severed the concubine’s nose.

Fèi Wújí, a close associate of the Minister of Jing, was in enmity with Zhāng Shòu. When Zhāng Shòu was newly appointed, he did not favor Fèi Wújí, so he secretly had someone kill Zhāng Shòu. The king of Wei blamed Fèi Wújí, and thus he was executed.

In Zhōngshān, there was a disgraced prince whose horses were very thin and whose carriages were in disrepair. Some of his attendants bore personal grudges against him, so they petitioned the king: *"The prince is very poor, and his horses are thin. Why not increase their feed?"*
The king refused, and the attendants secretly ordered the nighttime burning of the hay barn. The king blamed the disgraced prince, and thus he was executed.

The state of Wèi had an elderly scholar (*lǎo rú*) who was not favored by the Prince of Jìyáng. There was a guest who held a private grudge against the elderly scholar, so he slandered him, causing the Prince to have him killed. He then presented this as a virtue to the Prince of Jìyáng: *"Your servant did so because he did not favor you, and thus I have killed him for your sake."*
The Prince of Jìyáng, unaware, rewarded him.

Another account says:
The Prince of Jìyáng had a minor concubine (*shào shìzǐ*) who was not recognized. One who wished to gain his favor secretly had the elderly scholar dig for medicinal herbs (*yào*) at the Mǎ Lí mountain, and the minor concubine desired to claim this deed as her own. She entered to see the prince and said: *"The king of Qí sent the elderly scholar to dig for medicinal herbs at the Mǎ Lí mountain. In name, it is digging for herbs, but in truth, it is spying on your state. You should kill him, and this will be a pretext for Qí to blame you."*
The servant said: *"I beg to assassinate him."*
The prince said: *"As you wish."*

Thus the next day, he obtained the scholar in the shadow of the city walls and assassinated him. The Prince of Jìyáng returned to favor him even more.

**Fourth account:**
Chén Xū was a minister of the King of Wèi, yet he was well-loved by the King of Jīng. He urged Jīng to attack Wèi, then secretly requested to act as Wèi’s envoy to mediate, thereby leveraging Jīng’s strength against Wèi.

During the reign of the Duke of Hàn Zhāo, the price of glutinous millet (*shǔ zhǒng*) was once extremely high. The Duke ordered the granaries to be covered, but the officials stole the millet seeds and sold them in great quantities.

When Zhāo Xī Yù was using Jīng’s forces, there was someone who set fire to the granaries and storehouses, but he did not know who it was. Zhāo Xī Yù ordered the officials to seize and interrogate those selling reeds (*máo*), and they turned out to be the arsonists.

During the reign of the Duke of Hàn Lǐ, the cook presented food, and there was raw liver (*shēng gān*) in the soup. The Duke summoned the cook’s assistant and rebuked him, saying: *"How could you dare to place raw liver in my soup?"*
The assistant knelt and confessed, saying: *"I secretly wished to remove the senior cook."*

**Another account states:**
Duke Lǐ was bathing, and there was a pebble (*lì*) in the water. The Duke said: *"If I were to dismiss the senior attendant, would there not be someone to take his place?"*
His attendants replied: *"There would be."*
The Duke called for him and said: *"Why did you place a pebble in my bath?"*
He responded: *"If you dismiss the senior attendant, then I may replace him, and so I placed the pebble in your bath."*

During the reign of Duke Wén, a minister presented roasted meat (*zhì*), and the hair from the meat was still attached. The Duke summoned the cook and reproached him, saying: *"Do you wish me to choke? Why did you leave the hair on the roasted meat?"*
The cook knelt and bowed twice, pleading: *"I have three crimes for which I deserve death: First, I sharpened the knife with a whetstone (*yuán lì*), and though its edge was as keen as Gān Jiāng, it could slice meat cleanly—yet the hair would not sever. This is my first crime. Second, I used a wooden skewer to pierce the sliced meat, yet no hair was visible. This is my second crime. Third, I carried the hot brazier (*fèng chì lú*), and though the charcoal was blazing red, the meat was cooked while the hair remained untouched. Could there not be a slight illness in me that you have not yet detected?"*
The Duke said: *"Well done."*

The Duke then summoned the court officials and reproached them, and indeed, they were guilty—he executed them.

The Duke said: *"When Jin Ping Gong entertained guests, the minor official presented roasted meat with hair still attached. Ping Gong hastily ordered the cook’s execution, forbidding any appeal. The cook cried out to Heaven: ‘Alas! I have three crimes—do I not even know I am to die?’*

Ping Gong asked: *"What do you mean?"*

The cook replied: *"My knife’s edge was sharp enough to sever bone and flesh with the wind, yet it could not cut the hair—that is my first death. I roasted the meat over mulberry charcoal until the flesh turned red and white, yet the hair did not burn—that is my second death. When the meat was fully cooked, I blinked and looked again, yet the hair still clung to the meat—my eyes could not see it—that is my third death. Could it be that someone in this court secretly harbors resentment toward me? Would it not have been better to kill me sooner?"*

---

When Rong Hou served as Qin’s chancellor, the state of Qi grew strong. Rong Hou wished to establish Qin as emperor, but Qi refused. He then petitioned to make Qi the Eastern Emperor, but this too failed.

---

During the reign of Jin Xian Gong, Lady Ji of the Ji clan rivaled the queen in status and sought to replace Crown Prince Shen Sheng with her own son, Chi Qi. She falsely accused Shen Sheng before the king, leading to his death, and then installed Chi Qi as crown prince.

---

The ruler of Zheng had already established a crown prince, yet he favored a beautiful concubine and wished to replace the heir with her son. The queen, fearing this, poisoned the ruler and killed him.

---

Zhou Yu was highly influential in Wei, rivaling the king in power. The nobles and commoners all feared his dominance, and Zhou Yu indeed killed his ruler and seized control of the government.

---

Gongzi Chao, the son of the Zhou king, had a younger brother, Gongzi Gen, who was greatly favored by their father. When the king died, Gen rebelled against Eastern Zhou, splitting the kingdom into two states.

---

King Cheng of Chu had appointed Shang Chen as crown prince, but later wished to replace him with Gongzi Zhi. Shang Chen, unaware of this, asked his tutor, Pan Chong: *"How can I verify this?"*

Pan Chong replied: *"Serve the wine to Jiang Qian but do not show proper respect."*

The crown prince followed this advice. Jiang Qian then called out to the servants: *"The king intends to depose you and install Zhi as heir!"*

Shang Chen said: *"It is true."*

Pan Chong asked: *"Will you serve him faithfully?"*

The prince said: *"I cannot."* *"Can you make him a feudal lord?"* he asked. *"I cannot."* *"Can you undertake great deeds?"* he said: *"I can."*

Thus, he raised the arms of his encamped soldiers and attacked King Cheng. King Cheng begged for the chance to eat bear's paw and die, but his request was denied, and he was slain by force.

In the state of **Han**, **Magua** served as the minister to **Lord Ai**. **Yan Sui** was greatly favored by their ruler, and the two men deeply harmed each other. Yan Sui then had someone assassinate Han Magua in the court, but Han Magua fled to the ruler and embraced him, so Yan Sui killed Han Magua and seized the lordship of Ai.

In **Qi**, **Tian Heng** served as the minister. **Ken Zhi** was greatly favored by **Duke Jian**, and the two men schemed against each other. Tian Heng exploited private favors to take his state, killing Duke Jian and seizing control of the government.

**Dai Huan** served as the **Taisai** of **Song**, while **Huang Xi** was greatly favored by their ruler. The two men competed for influence and deeply harmed each other, and Huang Xi killed the Song ruler and seized his power.

**Huxu Tu** remarked: *"When a ruler favors his inner concubines, the heir-apparent is in danger; when he favors his outer ministers, the ruling officials are in danger."*

The lord of **Zheng** asked **Zheng Zhao**: *"What of the heir-apparent?"*
Zheng Zhao replied: *"He is also in danger."*

「Zheng Zhao replied: *"The heir-apparent has yet to be truly born."*

The lord asked: *"The heir-apparent has already been designated, yet you say he has yet to be born—why is that?"*

Zheng Zhao answered: *"Though the heir-apparent has been designated, the ruler’s fondness for beauty remains unchecked. If he loves another’s child, he will certainly favor that child, and favoring that child will mean he will surely wish to make it his successor. That is why I say the heir-apparent has yet to be truly born."*

---

**Chapter Six**

King Wen of Zhou cultivated the favor of **Fei Zhong** and wandered near the side of **King Zhou of Shang**, instructing him to remonstrate with **King Zhou** and disrupt his resolve.

The king of **Jing** sent envoys to **Qin**, and the Qin king treated them with great honor. The king said: *"When a rival state has a virtuous man, it is a cause for concern. Now Jing’s envoy is highly virtuous—this troubles me."* His ministers remonstrated: *"With Your Majesty’s wisdom and virtue, and the strength of our state, we should welcome Jing’s virtuous envoy. Why not secretly retain him and use him as an external agent? Jing will surely execute him."* The king said: *"Good."*

**Confucius** governed **Lu**, and the Way thrived without lost items being picked up. **Duke Jing of Qi** was troubled by this. **Li Qie** advised Duke Jing: *"Removing Confucius is like blowing away a speck of dust. Your Majesty, why not welcome him with high honors and gifts, and send the daughters of **Duke Ai of Lu** to distract him with their beauty?"* Duke Ai, upon receiving the music, would surely neglect his governance. Confucius would surely remonstrate, and his remonstrance would be rejected in Lu, causing him to leave. Duke Jing said: *"Excellent."* He then ordered Li Qie to send the daughters of Duke Ai along with the finest music to Duke Ai. Duke Ai, delighted by the music, neglected his governance. Confucius remonstrated, but was not heeded, and so he departed for **Chu**.

The king of **Chu** asked **Gan Xiang**: *"I wish to send you to Qin as an envoy and make you their prime minister—is this possible?"* Gan Xiang replied: *"It is not."* The king asked: *"Why not?"* He said: *"In my youth, I served **Shi Ju**, the gatekeeper of Shangcai. Shi Ju neither served the lord nor his family, but was infamous for his harshness. I obeyed him willingly. The **Wise King of Chu** and **Zhang Yi’s eloquence**—when I served them, I secured ten high offices and was pardoned from all crimes. This proves my virtue."* The king said: *"If you are to be prime minister of an enemy state and yet retain your virtue, how is this not possible?"* Gan Xiang replied: *"Earlier, when Your Majesty sent **Shao Hua** to **Yue**, he was able to destroy Yue in five years. The reason was that Yue was in chaos while Chu was in order. Now, if Your Majesty knows how to use me in Yue, yet sends me to Qin, is this not too hasty and forgetful?"* The king asked: *"Then what should be done?"* Gan Xiang replied: *"Better to make me prime minister of a friendly state."* The king asked: *"Which friendly state could you serve as prime minister?"* Gan Xiang replied: *"**Gong Li**—he is rarely favored, but in time will become a noble minister. He wears the king’s robes, holds the **Du Ruo** grass, and grasps the jade armband, attending court. Moreover, he can be used to sow discord in Qin."*

When **Wu** attacked **Jing**, **Zi Chu** sent someone to declare in Jing: *"If **Zi Qi** is used, we will strike him. If **Zi Chang** is used, we will abandon him."* The people of Jing, upon hearing this, used **Zi Chang** and dismissed **Zi Qi**. The Wu army attacked, and thus conquered them.

**Duke Xian of Jin** attacked **Yu** and **Guo**, sending them **Qu Chan’s chariots**, **Chui Ji’s jade**, and the daughters of **Duke Ai of Lu**, to honor them and disrupt their governance. **Shu Xiang’s slander of Bian Hong** led him to write a letter: *"Bian Hong told Shu Xiang: ‘You should tell the Jin lord that the time we agreed upon is suitable—why not hurry and send troops?’ He then pretended to leave the letter at the Zhou court and quickly departed."* The Zhou king, believing Bian Hong had betrayed them, executed him and killed him.

**Duke Huan of Zheng** wished to secretly attack **Kuai**, first inquiring about the names of its strong and wise ministers, selecting the finest lands of Kuai to bribe them, and granting them titles and offices in writing. He then set up altars outside the city gates, as if preparing for an alliance, and sealed the pact with a **sacrificial chicken** (□). Kuai’s lord, suspecting internal strife, executed all his virtuous ministers. Duke Huan then secretly attacked Kuai and conquered it.

**E Order** (邺令), **Xiang Ci**, secretly cultivated favor with the **Duke of Zhao’s** attendants. When the Duke of Zhao plotted to secretly attack E, Xiang Ci would always hear of it first and report it to the **King of Wei**. The King of Wei prepared defenses, and the Duke of Zhao would retreat.

---

**Chapter Seven**

The **Qin court dwarf** was skilled in **Jing**, and secretly cultivated favor with **Jing’s** king’s attendants, while also being highly trusted by **Duke Huiwen of Qin**. When Jing had any plot, the dwarf would always hear of it first and inform Duke Huiwen.

During the reign of **Duke Si of Wei**, there was a man among his ministers who was skilled in **Jing**. The county magistrate had a **worn-out bed** (席蓐甚), and Duke Si, upon returning, sent someone to give him a new bed, saying: *"I heard that your bed is now worn out, so I give you a new one."* The county magistrate was greatly startled, believing his lord to be divine.

---

**Chapter Thirty-Two: External Sayings—Left, Part One**

The **Way of the Enlightened Ruler** is like **You Ruo’s** response to **Prince Mi of the State of You**.

The Enlightened Ruler, when listening to words, admires their eloquence; when observing actions, values their distance from the world. Thus, his ministers and people speak in **obscure and lofty** terms, and their conduct is detached from the world.

This is illustrated in **Tian Yu’s** reply to the **King of Jing**.

Hence, **Mozi** made the **wooden pigeon** (木鸢) and **Ou Ji** built the **Hall of Wu**.

**Medicinal wine and elixirs** are used to test a ruler’s **unique insight**—whether he is a **wise and sagely sovereign**.

The **ruler’s** way of listening to words is not based on their practical use, then speakers will offer **persuasive arguments about white horses** (棘刺白马之说). If he does not rely on **proper standards** (仪的) for judgment, then **archers** will all claim to be like **Yi** (羿).」

The **Ruler’s** way of speaking is all like **Yan Wang’s** learning of the **Dao/Way**, yet those who engage in long-winded discourse are like the **people of Zheng** quarreling over years.

Thus, words may be **subtle and minute**, difficult to discern yet devoid of practical purpose, so **Lǐ Kǎi, Huìzǐ, Sòngzǐ, and Mòzǐ** all drew diagrams to clarify them.

Arguments may be **profound and vast**, yet lacking in substance, so **Wèi Shèn’s** *"Chariot of Pan"* and **Zhān Chē’s** *"Ghostly Apparitions"* were mere illusions.

Words that are **obdurate yet hollow**, lacking in genuine efficacy, are why **Mùzi’s** followers and **Mò Dì** (墨翟) all held firm to their **empty gourds** (坚瓠).

Moreover, **Yu Qīng** praised the carpenter, yet the house collapsed; **Fàn** praised the craftsman, yet the bow snapped.

Therefore, to seek **sincerity**, one must not merely rely on **rewards and remuneration** (归饷).

**Three.** When one acts on behalf of others, they demand **reputation**; when acting for oneself, they seek **practical results**. Hence, father and son may harbor **grudges and discord**, yet the **hireling** (庸作者) is offered **delicious broth** (美羹). This is seen in **Wén Gōng’s** preemptive declaration and **Gōu Jiàn’s** self-proclaimed **imperial majesty** (如皇).

Thus, **Huan Gōng** concealed his **anger at the State of Cai** (蔡) and attacked **Chǔ**; **Wú Qǐ** harbored resentment toward **Liáo Shí** (廖实) and suffered **self-inflicted wounds**.

Yet the **hymns and odes** of the **ancient kings**, the **inscriptions on bells and tripods**, were all **Pan’s** (潘吾) **empty traces** and **Mount Hua’s** **superficial display**. What the **ancient kings** truly sought was **profit**, and what they truly employed was **strength**.

The **proverb of Dù** (筑杜) states: *"Look at the words, not the speaker."* (目辞说)

If we **grant permission** to scholars to **adorn their speech** like the **ancient kings**, would that not be **unfitting for the present age**?

If it cannot be **changed**, then the **people of Xíng County** (郑县人) obtained **chariot privileges** (车厄), the **people of Wèi** (卫人) assisted **Yì** (弋), and **Bù Zǐ’s** wife was given **defective garments** (写弊袴), yet these were **minor matters**.

The **ancient kings’** words had some that were **minor in deed yet grand in public perception**, and some that were **grand in deed yet minor in public perception**—one cannot **predict their true worth**.

This is seen in the **Song people’s** *"unbinding of books"* and the **Liáng people’s** *"reading of records"*. Hence, the **ancient kings** had the **Yǐng Book** (郢书), yet the **later generations** multiplied **superficial interpretations** (燕说).

The **heavens** do not **align with the affairs of states** nor **consult the ancient kings**, but all seek those who **measure and assess** (取度者).

**Four.** Where **profit** lies, the people will **gather**; where **renown** shines, the scholars will **die for it**.

Thus, if **meritorious deeds** lie **outside the law** and **rewards** are **added**, then the **ruler** will not **trust** that the people below will **obtain their profit** from it.

If **renown** lies **outside the law** and **praise** is **added**, then the scholars will **pursue renown** while the **common folk** will **abandon their ruler** below.

Hence, **Zhōng Zhāng** and **Xū Jǐ** served as officials, yet the people of **Zhōng Mò** (中牟) **forswore their fields** and **followed the literary scholars**, halving their **taxable households**.

**Píng Gōng** endured **pain in his feet** and **lame legs** yet dared not **damage his seat**—the **officials of Jìn** (晋) who **sought refuge** (辞仕) were like **hammers** to the state.

These **three scholars**, when **speaking according to the law**, were merely **official records**; when **acting in accordance with duty**, they were like **ordinary citizens**. Yet the **ruler’s** **honors** were **extremely grand**.

If **speech deviates from the law** and **actions stray from duty**, then they are **beyond the common people**—how then could the **ruler** honor them? **Honors should be abolished**.

Moreover, **scholars who dwell in learning**, when the state is **at peace**, do not **exert their strength**; when there is **calamity**, they do not **don armor**. If honored, they **boast of their cultivation** and **agricultural and martial prowess**; if not honored, they **challenge the ruler’s authority**.

How can the **ruler** **benefit** from scholars who **dwell in learning**?

Thus, the **enlightened kings** **assessed** **Lǐ Cǐ** and **viewed Zhōngshān**.

**Five.** The **poem** says:
*"Not to **personally labor** nor **take things seriously**,
then the **common people** will not **trust**."*

---

**Chapter Five (continued).**

The **ruler** should **honor** Fù Yuè with **unwoven purple robes** and **bestow upon him the sash of a high official** like Zhèng Jiǎn and Sòng Xiāng, **rewarding him with respect and authority over agricultural and martial affairs**. If the **ruler** does not **clearly define distinctions** nor **demand sincerity**, but instead **personally labors** and **occupies a lowly position**, moreover **acting as a commoner’s errand-boy**, **sleeping in humble lodgings**, and **wearing coarse garments**—then **such conduct is unworthy of a sage**.

Kǒng Qiū did not know this, so he was **compared to the lowly Mèng Zhòng**. Zōu Jūn did not know this, so he **first punished himself**. The **Way of the enlightened ruler** is like Shū Xiàng’s **hunting song** and Zhāo Hòu’s **listening to the music of the Pei people**. **Six.** The **poem** says:

*"Small trust fulfilled, great trust arises—
thus the **enlightened ruler** accumulates **trust** (xìn).*
If **rewards and punishments lack sincerity**, then **prohibitions and commands will not be obeyed**. This is seen in **Fù Yuè’s attack on Yuán** and **Jī Zhèng’s relief of famine**.

Therefore, **the enlightened ruler demonstrates trust**—like Zēng Zǐ’s **slaughtering of the pig**—so that his subjects may believe him. **Disasters arise** when **Zhōu Lì** **strikes the warning drum** and **Lǐ Kuí** **mockingly unites the two states**.

**One.** Mì Zǐ Jiàn governed **Dān Fù**. Yǒu Ruò saw him and said: *"Why do you appear so gaunt?"* Mì Zǐ replied: *"The **ruler** does not know my worth, yet he commands me to govern **Dān Fù**. The **official duties** are urgent, and I worry over them, so I am gaunt."* Yǒu Ruò said: *"In ancient times, Shùn struck the **five-stringed drum**, sang the **Song of the South Wind**, and the world was governed. Now, with **Dān Fù’s trifling matters**, you govern it and yet still worry. How would you govern the world?"*

Thus, **one who possesses skill** should **guide** (yù) him, **remaining seated in the ancestral hall**, **resisting the allure of women’s beauty**, yet **not harming governance**. **One who lacks skill** may **wear himself out** in **personal labor**, yet **still gain no benefit**.

The **King of Chǔ** said to **Tián Chóu**: *"Mò Zǐ is a **renowned scholar** (xiǎn xué). His **person** is acceptable, but his **speech is excessive yet unpersuasive**—why is this?"* Tián Chóu replied: *"In ancient times, the **Marquis of Qín** gave his daughter in marriage to a **Jìn prince**, commanding **Jìn** to adorn her with **elaborate attire** and **seventy attendants** in **embroidered garments**. When they arrived in **Jìn**, the people **cherished the attendants** while **disdaining the princess**. This may be called **skill in marrying attendants**, yet it cannot be called **skill in marrying a princess**.*

A **Chǔ man** sold his **pearls in Zhèng**, placing them in a **wooden box** (mùlán) **adorned with fragrant cypress and nutmeg**, **embellished with jade and gemstones**, **decorated with rose quartz**, and **bound with kingfisher feathers**. The **Zhèng people bought the box** but **returned the pearls**. This may be called **skill in selling the box**, yet it cannot be called **skill in selling pearls**.*

Now, **the world’s scholars** all **pursue eloquent rhetoric and ornamental speech**, and **rulers admire their words** while **forgetting their practical use**. Mò Zǐ’s **teachings** **transmit the Way of the former sages** and **discuss the words of the wise**, **proclaiming them to the people**. If he **persuaded through rhetoric**, then **people might admire his words** while **forgetting their true meaning**, **allowing ornamentation to harm utility**. This is **of the same kind** as the **Chǔ man selling pearls** and the **Marquis of Qín marrying his daughter**—thus his **speech is often unpersuasive**.

Mò Zǐ crafted a **wooden pigeon** (mùyuān), and it took **three years** to complete. Yet it **flew for only one day** before **failing**. His **disciples said**: *"Master, your **skill** is great—you can make a wooden pigeon fly!"* Mò Zǐ replied: *"I am not as **adept as a wheelwright**. A wheelwright uses **a short span of wood**, expends **no more than two days’ labor**, yet **bears the load of thirty stone** and **extends its far-reaching power** for **many years**. Now, I spend **two years** to make the pigeon, yet it **fails after one day**. Hùi Zǐ heard of this and said: *"Mò Zǐ is **highly skilled**, skilled in making **wheels**, clumsy in crafting **wooden pigeons**."*

The **King of Sòng** and **Qí** were **enemies**, so he **built the Wǔ Palace**. **The singer Gǔ** performed, and **those who marched stopped**, while **those who built did not tire**. The **king heard of his summoning** and **granted him rewards**. Gǔ replied: *"Your servant’s teacher, **Shè Jī**, sings **even better than I**."* The **king summoned Shè Jī** to perform, and **those who marched did not stop**, while **those who built knew weariness**. The **king asked**: *"Though the marchers did not stop and the builders grew weary, your teacher’s song was not as **effective as yours**—why?"*

Gǔ replied: *"Your Majesty **measure his merit**—I sing **four beats**, while Shè Jī sings **eight**. As for **their firmness**, I sing **five inches**, while he sings **two inches**. **Fine medicine tastes bitter**, yet **wise men take it** because they know it **cures their ailments**. **True words may grieve the ear**, yet **enlightened rulers listen** because they know it **brings success**."*

A man from Song once petitioned to craft a **mother monkey** from the tip of a thorn for the King of Yan. He claimed that one must observe it only after three months of fasting. The King thus kept him with three carriages of provisions.

The royal attendants and smiths spoke to the King: *"Your Majesty, we have heard that a sovereign should not go ten days without fasting for the sake of leisure. Now we know that Your Majesty cannot long fast to observe this useless artifact, so he sets the term as three months. All who carve must use a tool smaller than what they carve. Now this man is cold-hearted—he has no tool to carve with. This is impossible. Your Majesty must examine it."*

The King imprisoned and questioned him, and indeed found him lying, so he executed him.

The man said to the King: *"Those who speak without measure are many in their thorn-tip theories."*

---

The King of Yan delighted in subtle craftsmanship. A man from Wei said: *"I can carve a mother monkey from the tip of a thorn."* The King was pleased and kept him with five carriages of provisions. The King said: *"Let me see the guest carve the thorn-tip mother monkey."* For the sovereign to observe it, he must abstain from the palace for half a year, drink no wine, eat no meat, and only view it at dawn when the rain had cleared and the sun emerged—then the thorn-tip mother monkey could be seen. The King of Yan thus kept the man from Wei but could not observe his work.

A smith from Zheng beneath the palace told the King of Yan: *"I am a carver. All delicate things must be carved with a tool, and what is carved must be larger than the tool. Now the thorn-tip cannot contain a blade—it is difficult to carve the thorn-tip."* The King said: *"Let me see the guest’s ability to carve or not."* The King said: *"Good."* He asked the man from Wei: *"Do you carve with a tool?"* He replied: *"With a tool."* The King said: *"I wish to see it."* The guest said: *"I shall fetch it from my dwelling."* He then fled.

The man was a skilled debater from Song, one who argued *"A white horse is not a horse"* and wore the robes of the scholars from Qi’s Jixia Academy. When riding a white horse past a checkpoint, he would turn to pay the tax on the white horse. Thus, with empty words, he could deceive an entire kingdom, but when tested on facts, he could not even fool a single person.

A newly sharpened arrow, drawn in a bow, though shot blindly, will never miss a hair’s breadth—yet one cannot return it to its original place, nor can it be called a skilled archer, for there is no fixed target. Set a target five inches wide at ten paces’ distance, and even Yi or Feng Meng cannot guarantee a hit—this is because there is a fixed target. Difficult things have measures, while easy things do not. With a fixed target, Yi and Meng consider five inches a feat, but without one, hitting a hair’s breadth blindly is called clumsy. Thus, without measure, sophists multiply their arguments, but when measure is applied, even the wise fear failure and dare not speak recklessly.

Now, the sovereign listens to speech without applying measure, praises the eloquence without measuring its merit, and commends actions without examining their substance—this is how the sovereign is long deceived, and how the speakers are long nourished.

There was a guest who taught the King of Yan the **Way of Immortality**. The King sent men to learn it, but before they could complete their studies, the guest died. The King grew furious and executed them. He did not realize the guest had deceived him, nor that punishing the students was too late.

To believe in impossible things and punish the innocent—this is the harm of failing to discern.

Moreover, what a man most urgently needs is his own life. If he cannot ensure his own immortality, how can he ensure the King’s?

A man from Zheng argued over age with another. One said: *"I am the same age as Yao."* The other replied: *"I am the same age as the elder brother of the Yellow Emperor."* They could not settle the dispute, so they agreed that the one who exhaled last would win.

A guest painted a **gourd** for the Duke of Zhou. It took three years to complete, and when the Duke observed it, it resembled a lacquered gourd. The Duke of Zhou grew angry, and the painter said: *"Build a wall ten *pán* high, carve a window eight *chi* wide, and observe it at sunrise."* The Duke did so, and upon gazing, he saw the gourd transform into dragons, snakes, birds, beasts, chariots, and horses—all creatures fully formed. The Duke of Zhou rejoiced greatly. Though the gourd’s craftsmanship was indeed subtle and difficult, its use was no different from a plain lacquered gourd.

A guest painted for the King of Qi, who asked: *"What is hardest to paint?"* He replied: *"Dogs and horses are difficult."* *"What is easiest?"* He answered: *"Ghosts and spirits are easiest."* Dogs and horses are known to all—seen daily before one’s eyes, yet their likeness cannot be replicated, hence the difficulty.
