Ghosts and spirits have no form, do not appear before one, hence their ease.

There was a recluse in Qi named Tian Zhong. A man from Song named Qu Gu saw him and said: *"I have heard of your virtue—you do not rely on others for food."* Now, I have a method for growing **bottle gourds**—hard as stone, thick with no holes. I present them to you."* Zhong said: *"What makes a gourd valuable is its capacity to hold things. Now it is thick with no holes, so it cannot be split to contain objects, nor can it bear weight like stone, so it cannot be split to pour liquids. I have no use for it."* The man said: *"Indeed, I was about to discard it. Now, Tian Zhong does not rely on others for food, yet he also brings no benefit to his state—he is like this useless gourd."*

Yu Qing was building a house and told the craftsmen: *"This house will be tall."* The craftsmen replied: *"This is a new house—its plaster is wet and its beams are green."* Yu Qing said: *"No. Wet plaster is heavy, and green beams are bent. To bear heavy plaster with bent beams, this house should be low. But as time passes, the plaster dries and the beams straighten. Dry plaster is light, and straight beams are firm—thus, bearing light plaster with straight beams, this house will indeed be tall."* The craftsmen conceded, built it as such, and the house collapsed.

Another account:
Yu Qing was about to build a house, and the craftsmen said: *"The timber is green and the plaster is wet. Green timber is bent, and wet plaster is heavy—bearing heavy plaster with bent beams, even if it stands now, it will surely collapse in time."*

「Yu Qing said: *"If the timber is dry, it will be straight; if the plaster is dry, it will be light. Now if we truly obtain dryness, day by day the lightness and straightness will increase—though it endure long, it will surely not collapse."* The craftsmen conceded, and built it as such, but after some time, the house indeed collapsed.

Fan Qi said: *"A bow bends when stretched to its limit, not at its beginning."* When the craftsmen stretched the bow, they bent the bow for thirty days before stepping on the string, and then violated the tension in one day. This was to restrain it at the beginning and exhaust it at the limit—how could it not bend? But the way of stretching a bow is not like this. One should bend the bow for one day and step on the string, then release the tension over thirty days. This is to exhaust it at the beginning and restrain it at the limit. The craftsmen were exhausted, and did so, and the bow broke.

The words of Fan Qi and Yu Qing are both elegant and persuasive in speech, yet contrary to the reality of the situation. The ruler was pleased by their arguments but did not heed them, and this is why disaster befell. To reject those skilled in practical arts while admiring the empty eloquence of rhetorical beauty is to abandon the craftsmen who build houses and stretch bows.

Thus, when rulers consider state affairs, they do not grasp the principles of craftsmen in building houses or stretching bows—yet when scholars are exhausted like Fan Qi and Yu Qing, their words are mere empty rhetoric, which prevails without utility, or practical truth, which is unyielding yet useless. Rulers often favor empty rhetoric over sound counsel, and this is why chaos ensues.

In our age, there are no shortage of Fan Qis and Yu Qings, yet rulers are swayed by them without end. This is to honor those who bring ruin and defeat while treating the skilled as mere craftsmen. Unable to apply their expertise, the houses collapse and the bows break. Those who know governance cannot implement their methods, and thus the state falls into disorder while its ruler faces peril.

When infants play together, they use dust as food, plaster as soup, and wood as a drum. Yet by evening, they must return home—white dust for food and plaster for soup can be used for play but not for sustenance. To praise the traditions of ancient times with empty rhetoric, to debate the virtues of the sage-kings without being able to govern the state properly, is also like play that cannot be applied to actual governance.

Those who emulate virtue and righteousness yet bring weakness and chaos are the Three Jin. Those who do not emulate them yet achieve strength and order are Qin. Yet Qin was strong but did not become an emperor—its governance was not yet complete.

Three: When a man is still an infant, his parents feed him simply, and when he grows up, he complains. When a son reaches maturity and his provisions are meager, his parents grow angry and reproach him. Father and son are the closest of kin, yet one curses while the other complains—both are driven by self-interest and not by consideration for the other.

When a laborer sells his strength and a farmer scatters his seeds, the owner spends his household resources and offers fine food, and when the laborer adjusts his clothing and seeks easier money, it is not because he loves the laborer—he merely says, *"This is how it is; the farmer will plow deeply, hoe thoroughly, and cultivate diligently."* When the laborer applies his utmost effort and the farmer straightens the furrows and aligns the ridges at the proper time, it is not because he loves the owner—he merely says, *"The soup will be delicious, and the money will be easy."* This is how they nurture their labor and cultivate their fields, with the affection of a father and son, yet their hearts are attuned only to their own benefit—all are driven by selfish motives.

Thus, when people act and give, if their hearts are focused on profit, the Yue people will easily be pacified. If their hearts are focused on harm, then even father and son will turn away and resent one another.

Wen Gong of Jin attacked Song, and first proclaimed: *"I have heard that the ruler of Song is without the Dao/Way, he scorns and insults the elders, distributes wealth unfairly, and his commands are not trusted. I come to punish him for the people."* The Yue people attacked Wu, and first proclaimed: *"I have heard that the Heavenly King built a tower like the Ru Huang, dug deep pits, exhausted the common people, and drained their wealth, using up their strength. I come to punish him for the people."*

Cai Nü became the wife of Duke Huan of Qi. Duke Huan took her on a boat, and she rocked the boat violently. Duke Huan was greatly frightened and forbade her, but she did not stop. He grew angry and expelled her. Later, he recalled her and remarried her. Duke Huan was enraged and prepared to attack Cai, but his senior advisor remonstrated: *"To use a bedding joke to justify attacking another state is inconsistent with governance and military affairs. I beg you not to use this as a pretext."* Duke Huan did not listen, and the senior advisor said: *"If it is unavoidable, then the Zhen Mao of Chu have not paid tribute to the Son of Heaven for three years now. You might as well raise an army to attack Chu for the Son of Heaven. If Chu submits, then return and attack Cai, saying: 'I came to punish Cai for the Son of Heaven because Cai did not send troops to assist. Now I will annihilate it.'"*

This is righteous in name yet advantageous in reality. Therefore, there must be the pretense of punishing for the Son of Heaven, while in truth it is vengeance.

Wu Qi served as a general for Wei and attacked Zhongshan. Among the soldiers, there were those afflicted with carbuncles, and Wu Qi knelt to suck the pus from their wounds. The mother of one of the injured soldiers stood weeping, and someone asked: *"The general treats your son this way—why do you still weep?"* She replied: *"Wu Qi once sucked the wound of his own father, and his father died. Now my son is also about to die. That is why I weep."*

The ruler of Zhao ordered craftsmen to install hooks and ladders on Pan Wu, carving human footprints into its surface—three feet wide, five feet long—and inscribed: *"The ruler often ascends here."* The king of Qin Zhao ordered craftsmen to install hooks and ladders on Huaxia Mountain, using the pith of pine and cypress for chessboards, with arrows eight feet long and pieces eight inches long, and inscribed: *"Zhao Wang once played chess with the Heavenly God here."*

Wen Gong returned to his state, and upon reaching the Yellow River, he ordered his attendants to discard the ritual food baskets and bedding, and to follow only those whose hands and feet were calloused, and whose faces were dark and weathered. Cai Fan heard of this and wept at night. The duke said: *"I have been exiled for twenty years and now have returned to my state. Cai Fan heard of this and did not rejoice but wept—does he not wish me to return?"* Cai Fan replied: *"The ritual food baskets are for eating, and the bedding is for sleeping. Yet the ruler discards them."*

The hands and feet calloused, the face dark and weathered—these are those who labor and earn merit—yet the lord places them behind him. Now, I have those who follow me, and my grief is too great to bear, hence my weeping.

Moreover, many were the ministers who used deceit and falsehood to help me return to my state, yet I still despise myself—how much more so should I despise you? I bow twice and take my leave.

Wen Gong restrained him and said: *"The proverb says: 'When building the ancestral shrine, one kneels and places it, and with solemn cap and robes performs the rites.' Now you have taken it with me, yet you do not help me govern it; you have placed it with me, yet you do not help me worship it—how can this be?"* He untied the left rein of his chariot and swore an oath by the Yellow River.

There was a man from Zheng County named Yi Zi, who told his wife to make him trousers. His wife asked: *"What should these trousers be like?"* He replied: *"Like my old ones."* So his wife destroyed the new cloth to make them like the old trousers.

A man from Zheng County found a chariot yoke but did not know its name, so he asked another: *"What is this called?"* The other replied: *"This is a chariot yoke."* Soon he found another and asked again: *"What is this called?"* The other replied: *"This is a chariot yoke."* The questioner grew furious and said: *"Just now you said 'chariot yoke,' and now you say 'chariot yoke' again—what is this multitude? This woman deceives me!"* He then fought with him.

A man from Wei who assisted in hunting would, when birds approached, first wave his quiver to startle them, so that the birds would not settle and he could not shoot them.

A wife from Zheng County went to market and bought a turtle, which she carried home. Passing the Ying River, she thought it was thirsty, so she released it to drink—and thus lost her turtle.

When a younger man serves an elder in drinking, the elder drinks while the younger also drinks. *(One version says:)* *"When the elder drinks, the younger drinks too."*

A man from Lu who took pride in imitation saw an elder drink wine without finishing it, so he spat after drinking. He too spat in imitation.

One version says: A man from Song who also wished to emulate virtue saw an elder drink without leaving any wine, not merely refilling his cup but draining it completely. The *Book of Documents* says: *"Bind him with sashes."* A man from Song who was well-mannered thus wore double sashes to bind himself. Someone asked: *"Why do you do this?"* He replied: *"The *Book of Documents* says so—it is indeed so."* The *Book of Documents* also says: *"After carving and polishing, return to simplicity."* A man from Liang who was refined in his actions and speech, in all his affairs followed propriety, and said it was difficult—yet lost the true meaning. Someone asked: *"Why do you do this?"* He replied: *"The *Book of Documents* says so—it is indeed so."*

A man from Ying sent a letter to the Prime Minister of Yan. Writing at night with dim firelight, he told the man holding the candle: *"Raise the candle."* By mistake, the scribe wrote *"Raise the candle"* instead of *"Hold the candle."* The Prime Minister of Yan received the letter and praised it, saying: *"Raise the candle—this means to honor brightness. Honoring brightness means promoting and entrusting the worthy."* The Prime Minister reported this to the king, who was greatly pleased, and the state prospered. Though the state prospered, this was not the original intent of the letter. Today, many scholars who are promoted resemble this case.

A man from Zheng was about to buy sandals. First, he measured his own feet and placed the measurement beside him. Upon reaching the market, he forgot to bring it. After obtaining the sandals, he said: *"I forgot to bring my measurement."* He returned home to fetch it, but by the time he returned, the market had closed, and he could not get the sandals. Someone asked: *"Why didn’t you try them on with your feet?"* He said: *"I trust the measurement more than my own feet."*

Wang Deng served as the magistrate of Zhongmou and reported to Duke Xiang of Jin: *"In Zhongmou, there are two scholars named Zhong Zhang and Xu Ji. Their conduct is impeccable, and their learning is vast. Why not promote them?"* The duke said: *"You have seen them—I will make them Middle Great Officers."* The Prime Minister’s aide advised: *"The Middle Great Officer is a high rank in Jin. To receive it without merit contradicts the intentions of Jin’s officials. Does Your Grace listen with your ears but not see with your eyes?"* Duke Xiang said: *"I have already listened with my ears and seen with my eyes regarding Deng. Deng’s recommendations are also heard and seen—this is the endless cycle of relying on ears and eyes."* Wang Deng was thus granted two Middle Great Officer positions in a single day, given fields and houses. Half the people of Zhongmou abandoned their farms and sold their homes to follow him in studying.

Chu Xiang served as Duke Ping’s charioteer. When the duke’s calf ached and his foot cramped, he could not bear to leave his seat. All of Jin heard of it and said: *"Chu Xiang is a virtuous man—Duke Ping honors him so much that he endures cramps rather than leave his seat."* Those in Jin who sought office or aspired to emulate Chu Xiang became like a hammer striking the state.

There was a man named Qu Gong from Zheng County who, upon hearing of an enemy, died in fear. When he heard of himself, he revived.

Zhao’s Prime Minister sent Li Ci to scout whether Zhongshan could be attacked. Upon returning, Li Ci reported: *"Zhongshan can be conquered. If Your Majesty does not attack soon, you will be surpassed by Qi and Yan."* The Prime Minister asked: *"Why can it be attacked?"* Li Ci replied: *"Their ruler favors reclusive scholars in caves, showering them with gifts—dozens of them from humble alleys and narrow lanes—and honors plain-clothed scholars by the hundreds with respectful ceremonies."* The Prime Minister said: *"By your words, this is a virtuous ruler—how can he be attacked?"* Li Ci said: *"Not so. When a ruler honors reclusive scholars and holds them in court, the soldiers grow lax in battle formations. When scholars are honored above commoners, farmers neglect their fields. Soldiers lax in battle mean weak arms, and farmers neglecting their fields mean a poor state. A state that is weak against enemies and impoverished within will not survive—conquer it, why not?"* The Prime Minister said: *"Excellent."* He raised an army and attacked, and Zhongshan was thus destroyed.

「The Duke of Qi favored wearing purple, and the entire state followed suit, so much so that during his time, for every five plain garments, not even one purple one could be found. The Duke was troubled by this and said to Guan Zhong: *"I love purple robes, and purple is highly valued. But the common people all wear purple without end—what should I do?"*

Guan Zhong replied: *"Why not try not wearing purple yourself?"* He then instructed the Duke’s attendants: *"I greatly detest the smell of purple."* When any of the Duke’s courtiers wearing purple approached, the Duke would say: *"Step back! I hate the smell of purple."* The Duke said: *"Very well."* From that day on, no attendant wore purple, and the next day, no one in the court wore purple. By the third day, no one in the entire state wore purple.

**One.**

The King of Qi favored purple robes, and his people all followed suit. In the state of Qi, for every five plain garments, not even one purple one could be found. The King was troubled by the high cost of purple. He told Fu Yue: *"The Book of Poetry says: ‘If you do not personally lead, the common people will not believe.’ Now, Your Majesty wishes the people not to wear purple, yet you yourself still wear purple robes in court. When any minister wearing purple approaches, you say, ‘Move farther away! I loathe the stench.’"* From that day on, no attendant wore purple, and that month, no one in the court wore purple. By that year, no one in the entire state wore purple.

**Two.**

The Duke of Zheng said to Zi Chan: *"Our state is small and hemmed in between Chu and Jin. Now our city walls are incomplete, and our armor and weapons are inadequate. We cannot rely on this to face unexpected threats."* Zi Chan replied: *"I have already secured the outer defenses, and the inner governance is firmly established. Though our state is small, it is not in danger."* The Duke said: *"Do not worry then."* Thus, after the Duke’s death, the state remained unharmed. Zi Chan served as the Duke’s minister, and the Duke said to him: *"You do not enjoy drinking wine, the sacrificial vessels are not grand, the bells and drums with the zither and sheng do not sound—my affairs are not in order, the people are unsettled, and the commoners are not well-governed. Plowing and warfare are not harmonious, and this is your fault."* Zi Chan said: *"The minister has his duties, and the ruler has his duties. Each should attend to their own."* Zi Chan withdrew and implemented his governance for five years. The state had no thieves or bandits, no lost goods were picked up from the roads, and no one dared to pluck the fruit from the peach and plum trees shading the streets. Needles and knives left on the road could be retrieved after three days, and for three years, the people did not suffer from hunger.

**Three.**

The Duke of Song fought the Chu at the upper reaches of the Zha Valley. The Song soldiers had already formed their ranks, but the Chu had not yet crossed the river. The Right Marshal, Gou Qiang, urged the Duke forward, saying: *"The Chu forces are numerous while ours are few. I beg Your Majesty to strike them while half of them are still in the river, before they have fully formed their ranks—we will surely defeat them."* The Duke replied: *"I have heard that a gentleman does not inflict repeated wounds, does not seize those with gray hair, does not drive men into perilous positions, does not corner men in narrow places, and does not attack unless the enemy’s ranks are fully formed. Now the Chu have not yet crossed the river, and we strike them—this violates righteousness."* He ordered: *"Let the Chu finish crossing and form their ranks before we sound the drums and advance."* The Right Marshal said: *"Your Majesty does not care for the Song people; their very lives are at risk. You only speak of righteousness."* The Duke said: *"If you do not follow the ranks, I will execute you for disobedience."* The Right Marshal returned to his ranks, but the Chu had already formed theirs. The Duke then sounded the drums, and the Song forces were routed. The Duke was wounded in the thigh and died three days later. This was the calamity of aspiring to uphold righteousness through personal conduct. If a ruler must rely on personally demonstrating virtue and righteousness before the people will obey, then should we not have the ruler himself plow the fields and march in battle formation like wild geese? Only then would the people be willing to plow and fight—yet would the ruler not then be in constant peril? And would the ministers not then be in constant fear?"*

**Four.**

The Duke of Qi toured the Shao Sea. A courier came from the capital to greet him, saying: *"My illness is severe, and I fear Your Majesty will come after me."* The Duke quickly rose and sent another courier. The Duke said: *"Hurry the carriage to the next station, and have the carriage driver, Zhuzi Han Gong, take the reins."* After traveling several hundred steps, the Duke thought the driver was not moving swiftly enough and seized the reins himself. After driving a few hundred more steps, he thought the horses were not advancing quickly enough and abandoned the carriage entirely, running ahead. Because he valued the swiftness of the next station’s carriage and the skill of Zhuzi Han Gong, he believed that his own carriage was inferior. Thus, he preferred the inferior.

**Five.**

The Duke of Wei wished to reward his officials with land and titles. He said to Meng Jiangjun: *"I wish to reward my officials."* Meng Jiangjun said: *"If Your Majesty wishes to reward your officials, why not first try studying the laws of governance?"* The Duke read the laws for over ten scrolls before falling asleep. He said: *"I cannot read these laws."* If one does not personally grasp the instruments of power yet wishes to do what ministers should do, then sleeping is only fitting.

**Six.**

Confucius said: *"For a ruler, it is like a vessel; for the people, it is like water. When the vessel is square, the water is square; when the vessel is round, the water is round."*

The Duke of Zou favored wearing long tassels, and his attendants all followed suit, making them highly prized. The Duke was troubled by this and asked his attendants, who replied: *"Your Majesty favors them, and the common people also wear them widely—thus they are prized."* The Duke then cut his own tassels short and went out, and soon no one in the state wore long tassels. The Duke could not issue a decree to govern the people’s attire and forbid it; instead, he cut his tassels short to set an example for them. This is like first punishing the people to establish authority over them.

**Seven.**

Shu Xiang went hunting, and those with many kills received more rewards, while those with few kills received less. The Duke of Han said to Shenzi: *"The laws are very easy to enforce."* Shenzi replied: *"Laws reward those who achieve results and confer office upon those who demonstrate ability. Now Your Majesty establishes these laws yet listens to the requests of your attendants—this is why they are difficult to enforce."* The Duke said: *"From today onward, I understand how to enforce laws. Why should I listen to you?"* The next day, Shenzi requested a position for his elder brother, and the Duke said: *"This is not what I learned from you. If I listen to your request, would I not ruin my own principles? I will not use your request."* Shenzi withdrew to his residence and begged for punishment.

**Eight.**

The Duke of Jin attacked Yuan, carrying ten days’ rations and setting a ten-day deadline with his officers. When ten days had passed and Yuan still had not fallen, he sounded the retreat and withdrew his forces. A soldier who had escaped from Yuan said, *"Yuan would have surrendered in three days."*

The ministers and attendants advised, *"The people of Yuan have exhausted their strength and food. Your Highness might as well wait."* The Duke replied, *"I set a deadline with my officers, and if I do not keep it, I betray my word. To gain Yuan but lose my trust—I will not do it."* He withdrew his forces.

When the people of Yuan heard this, they said, *"A ruler like this—his trust is unshakable. How could we not submit?"* And so they surrendered. The people of Wei, hearing of this, said, *"A ruler like this—his trust is unshakable. How could we not follow?"* And so they too submitted.

Confucius heard of it and recorded it, saying: *"The conquest of Yuan and the submission of Wei came from trust."*

The Duke of Jin asked Ji Zheng, *"How does one rescue the starving?"*
Ji Zheng replied, *"Trust."*
The Duke asked, *"What is trust?"*
Ji Zheng said, *"Trust in reputation. Trust in reputation means that ministers uphold their duties, neither good nor evil exceeding their bounds, and all affairs proceed without neglect. Trust in actions means not missing the seasons, and the people do not overstep. Trust in virtue means that near and far are encouraged to come, and those distant return."*

Wu Qi, when leaving, met an old friend who invited him to eat. The old friend said, *"Very well, I will return and prepare."* Wu Zi said, *"Wait for me to eat."* The old friend did not come until evening, so Wu Qi waited without eating. The next morning, he sent someone to find him, and only then did they eat together.

The Duke of Wei made an appointment to hunt with the forest warden. The next day, a sudden fierce wind arose, and his attendants urged him not to go. The Duke refused, saying, *"I cannot. If I abandon my appointment because of the wind, I betray my trust. I will not do it."* He drove his chariot forward himself, enduring the wind, and dismissed the forest warden.

Once, Zeng Zi’s wife went to market, and their child followed, weeping. She said, *"Go back. When I return, I will kill a pig for you."* When she returned from the market, Zeng Zi prepared to slaughter the pig, but his wife stopped him, saying, *"I was only joking with the child."* Zeng Zi said, *"The child does not understand jokes. A child has no knowledge and learns from its parents. If parents deceive them, it is teaching them to deceive. If a father deceives his child and the child does not trust its mother, this is not proper instruction."* He then cooked the pig.

King Li of Chu, upon hearing an alarm, had drums set up to summon the people for defense. Drunk from drinking, he struck them in play, and the people were greatly alarmed. He sent someone to say, *"I was drunk and playing with my attendants. I struck them by mistake."* The people then relaxed. After several months, an actual alarm sounded, but the people did not respond. He then established a clear signal, and the people trusted it.

Li Kui warned his two generals: *"Be vigilant! The enemy may strike at any moment—day or night."* He repeated this warning three times, but the enemy never came, so the generals grew careless and no longer trusted Li Kui. After several months, the Qin army attacked, nearly seizing their forces. This was the harm of broken trust.

Another account says: Li Kui fought the Qin, telling the left general, *"Hurry forward! The right general has already advanced."* Then he galloped to the right general, saying, *"The left general has already advanced."* The left and right generals replied, *"We have advanced."* Thus, they all rushed forward. The next year, when they fought the Qin again, the Qin army ambushed them, nearly seizing their forces. This was the harm of broken trust. *(End of the Zuo Tradition.)*

There were litigants who came to dispute, and Zi Chan separated them so that they could not exchange words directly, instead conveying their statements to him and letting him understand their intent.

Hui Si Gong sent someone to falsely accuse a market official, who scolded and detained him. The official then used the matter to extort gold from him, whereupon he released the man.

Hui Si Gong said to the market official: *"At that time, there was a traveler who gave you gold, so I sent him away."*

The market official was greatly terrified, believing Hui Si Gong to be perspicacious.

*(End of the Left Discourses of the External Reservoirs, Chapter 33 of the Han Feizi)*

One. When a person is put to death for a crime, they do not blame the ruler, for the punishment is just. The King of Shàng punished the sitting child of Zi Gāo by amputating his feet. Though the King inflicted the punishment, he had a heart of reluctance, and the punished one bore him gratitude in return and repaid his virtue. When a subject is rewarded for their merits, they do not feel virtuous toward the ruler, for the reward is just. Zhái Huáng, holding the right contract, rode in a carriage. His merits were sufficient to earn honor, so he rode in the carriage without shame. King Xiāng did not understand, for he did not know that great merits deserved great rewards. Thus, Zhāo Mào drove five carriages and wore humble sandals. Mào repelled Qin to the west and stopped Qi to the east—his achievements were great—yet the King only granted him five carriages. Great achievements but meager rewards are like a wealthy man wearing coarse shoes. If a ruler does not exceed his proper authority, and a subject does not falsely claim their abilities, then the subject will become a lost man like Zhōu of Shāoshì.

Two. To rely on power but not on trust, to rely on trust but sometimes not be trustworthy—this is the case. Thus, Dōng Guō Yá discussed Guān Zhòng. The Duke wanted to monopolize the state’s authority, and Yá argued that though Zhòng was loyal, if he were not, he would be a danger. The Duke therefore ordered Zhòng to handle external affairs, while Yí Péng managed internal affairs. To rely on stratagem but not on trust, thus Hún Xuān was not trusted by Wén Gōng. Jìn Wén Gōng, relying on the sincerity of Jī Zhèng, made him the Chief of Yuán and said: “You must never betray me.” Xuān replied: “If a ruler does not govern his subjects with stratagem but relies on their not betraying him, how can he expect them to remain loyal?” Thus, a ruler who possesses stratagem must reward sincerely to exhaust their abilities, and punish severely to forbid their misdeeds. Even if they have mixed methods, they will still gain their advantage. Mixed methods are those that are not steadfast but rather inconsistent. Yáng Hù, the minister of Jiān, showed his subservience. Hù rode in carriages and wore fine clothes, yet when he incurred the King’s displeasure, his three associates seized and expelled him. Hù claimed he had no private motives, but the King responded with matters of private favoritism, saying he had acted against the state’s interests—like a man who plants bitter orange and thorn trees only to suffer their prickles himself. When factions ally and subjects pursue their own desires, the ruler becomes isolated. When the ministers collectively select a subject, and the lower officials do not conspire, then the ruler is enlightened. Yáng Hù intended to be like Zhào Wǔ’s worthy minister and Jié Hú’s impartial one. These two men were selected by the ruler for their fairness, neither avoiding his own son nor his enemies. Hù claimed he would be the same, but his actions revealed otherwise.

Three. If the ruler and minister lose their proper relationship, then King Wén would boast of his own virtue while wearing humble footwear. Though the ruler may have a teacher, the minister should also be cautious; lesser ministers should serve as attendants and advisors. King Wén did not understand this principle, and his attendants were not trustworthy. This was also a loss of worthy ministers. He falsely claimed that all those he honored were his teachers, which was a vain display of his virtues and a cover-up of his mistakes. If one does not alter the proper distinctions between formal court audiences and informal gatherings, then Jì Sūn will remain in perpetual mourning and fall victim to an assassin. A formal court audience should be solemn, but an informal gathering should be relaxed—if one does not differentiate, then Jì Sūn will be harmed in the end.

Four. Where profit is forbidden and forbidding profit, even the divine cannot succeed. If one forbids what should be allowed and allows what should be forbidden, even the divine cannot succeed, let alone the unenlightened. Where praise is given for guilt and blame is cast for merit, even Yáo cannot govern well. If one praises what should be punished and blames what should be rewarded, even Yáo cannot govern well, let alone someone who is not Yáo. To build a gate but not let people enter is like having no gate at all. To grant profit but not let people advance is like not granting profit at all. This is how chaos arises. If gates do not allow entry and profit does not lead to advancement, chaos will surely follow. If the ruler of Qí does not listen to his attendants but instead keenly observes his ministers, he will not need to spend much gold. If the ruler of Wèi does not listen to those who praise, he will not need to waste jade and tortoiseshell. Xīmén Piáo’s request to restore the governance of Yáo was sufficient to prove this. Initially, when governing Yáo, he did not favor his attendants, so the ruler took away his position. Later, when he did favor them, the ruler welcomed and honored him. From this, one can understand that attendants can harm the state. It is like the thief who steals a baby’s fine robe and the associate who steals a noble’s clothes to adorn themselves. The thief’s child does not shame his father for stealing, because the stolen robe is used to flaunt his own status. The associate’s child does not shame his father for his theft, because the stolen clothes are used to honor others. When people flatter and curry favor, their misdeeds are the same. Zǐ Chuò’s attendants drew circles and squares—one could not simultaneously draw both perfectly. This illustrates that relying on attendants’ words cannot yield worthy ministers. To drive away ants and flies with bones and fish respectively is like using gentle words to instruct attendants, which only makes them more obsequious. How can one avoid the worries of Huan Gōng, who listened to his attendants’ demands for officials but had none to give? Or the troubles of Xuān Wáng, who did not discern that his Chief of Horses was stealing fodder but only worried about the horses’ emaciation?

Five. If a subject takes humility and frugality as their conduct, then titles will not suffice to encourage rewards. If favor and glory are without measure, then subjects will encroach and overstep. This is illustrated in Miáo Bīn Huáng’s criticism of Xiàn Bó and Kǒng Zǐ’s discussion of Yàn Yīng. Xiàn Bó, as a minister, did not let his wife wear silk, and Yàn Yīng was the same, so they were not considered great in subduing their inferiors. Thus, Zhòngní praised Guān Zhòng and Sūn Shū Yǒu. Zhòng had three grand feasts, for his excessive extravagance. Yǒu had coarse cakes, for his extreme frugality. Yet their manner of entering and exiting varied, revealing their ministerial status. Yáng Hù’s responses showed his subservience, and Jiān’s ruler’s way of dealing with his ministers was flawed. Hù claimed that in Qi, he had three men who would support him, but when he incurred the ruler’s displeasure, these three men seized and expelled him. Hù claimed he had no private motives, but the ruler responded with matters of private favoritism, accusing him of acting against the state’s interests—like a man planting bitter orange and thorn trees only to suffer their prickles himself. When factions ally and subjects pursue their own desires, the ruler becomes isolated. When the ministers collectively select a subject, and the lower officials do not conspire, then the ruler is enlightened. Yáng Hù intended to be like Zhào Wǔ’s worthy minister and Jié Hú’s impartial one. These two men were selected by the ruler for their fairness, neither avoiding his own son nor his enemies. Hù claimed he would be the same, but his actions revealed otherwise.

The ruler thus regarded him as one who planted bitter orange and thorn trees, not a proper way to instruct the state.

Hù said that those he selected would harm himself, just like the man who planted bitter orange and thorn trees—this is the very opposite of instructing ministers to act impartially. **Sixth Point:**

When the public domain is diminished, the ruler fears upright speech;
when private actions prevail, public merit is scarce.

This is illustrated by **Wénzǐ’s** upright speech and **Wǔzǐ’s** use of the rod.

Wǔzǐ and Wénzǐ, father and son, favored upright speech.
Wǔzǐ declared: *"He who loses upright speech will endanger himself and bring ruin upon his father."*

Zǐ Chǎn gave loyal counsel, but **Zǐ Guó** grew angry.
Guó said in his anger: *"Those who give loyal counsel must surely be estranged from the ministers, and then face grave peril alongside their father."*

Liàng Chē acted according to law, yet **Chéng Hóu** seized his seal.
Chē served as **Zhèng’s** magistrate; his sister broke the law, and he punished her.
**Zhào Hóu** deemed this unkind, and removed him from office.

Guān Zhòng governed with impartiality, yet the people slandered and resented him.
Zhòng did not repay **Fēng Rén’s** kindness but only valued the worthy, so the people resented and slandered him. **First Point:**

When **Kǒngzǐ** was serving as **Wèi’s** minister, his disciple **Zǐ Gāo** was the jailer.
Gāo amputated a man’s feet, and those he punished stood guard at the gate.
A man who bore ill will toward Kǒngzǐ at **Wèi’s** court said: *"Nǐ desires to rebel!"*
Wèi’s ruler sought to arrest Kǒngzǐ, but Kǒngzǐ fled, and all his disciples escaped—except Gāo, who followed him out.
**Zhōu Wēijī** (危) pulled him into a side chamber and helped him flee, though the guards could not catch them.

At midnight, Gāo secretly asked Wēijī: *"I could not violate the ruler’s laws to spare your feet—this was the time for you to repay my vengeance. Why then did you spare me?"*
*"How did I earn this from you?"*

Wēijī replied: *"I severed your feet—it was my fault, and I deserved it. There was no other way."*
*"Yet when the public jail was deciding your fate, the ruler tilted the laws, favoring you over me in speech, and his desire to spare you was intense—but I knew this.
When the judgment was finalized, the ruler was displeased, and his discontent showed in his expression—I saw this and knew it again.
This was not out of private favor toward me, but from his inherent benevolent nature. This is why I took joy in it and honored his virtue."*

**Zhōu** (危) walked with difficulty, hence the saying *"pèng Wēijī"* (朋危).

**Tiánzǐ Fāng** traveled from **Qí to Wèi**, and saw **Zhái Huáng** riding in a grand carriage with light horses.
Huáng had already boarded the carriage, and now he also had swift steeds.
Fāng thought it must be **Wén Hóu**, so he shifted his route to avoid him, believing Huáng was alone.
Alone: meaning without companions.

Fāng asked: *"Why do you ride in such a carriage?"*
Huáng replied: *"The ruler was planning to attack Zhōngshān, and I recommended Zhái Jiǎo, so the plan succeeded.
When he attacked, I recommended Yuè Yáng, and Zhōngshān was taken.
After taking Zhōngshān, he worried about governing it, and I recommended Lǐ Kè, so Zhōngshān was well governed.
Thus, the ruler bestowed this carriage upon me."*
Fāng said: *"The honor for your service is still too slight."*

When **Qín and Hán** attacked Wèi, **Zhāo Mǎo** (昭卯) spoke in the west, and **Qín and Hán** withdrew.
When **Qí and Jīng** attacked Wèi, Mǎo spoke in the east, and **Qí and Jīng** withdrew.
**Wèi Xiāngwáng** rewarded him with the rank of a five-chariot general, feeding his ambition as a general.
Mǎo said: *"Bó Yí was honored as a general and buried beneath Chóuyáng Shān, yet the world says: 'Though Bó Yí was virtuous and renowned for his kindness, to bury him as a general is to leave his limbs uncovered.'
Now I have halted the armies of four states, yet the king grants me only five chariots—this is to acknowledge my merit as if I had gained victory but was given only a single sandal.
Utilitarian gain."*

The man of trade gains profit double that of victory—yet now with meager rewards for great deeds, it is as if the victor were given only a straw sandal.

Confucius said: *"The capable official cultivates virtue; the incompetent official sows resentment. The *liáng* (槩) is the measure of balance; the official is the balance of law. The ruler of a state must not lose this balance."*

Minor-Shi Zhou was a man of ancient integrity, purity, and sincerity. He served as Zhao Xiang Wang’s chief strongman and wrestled with Zhongmu Xuzi, but was not his match. He then spoke to Xiang Wang to replace himself, saying: *"I serve the lord with my strength, but now Xuzi surpasses me in strength. If I do not speak to replace myself, others may speak for me, and I fear this would bring guilt."*

Xiang Wang said: *"Your position is what others desire—why speak of replacing yourself with Xuzi?"*
Zhou replied: *"I serve the lord with my strength, and now Xuzi’s strength exceeds mine. If I do not speak to replace myself, I fear others will speak for me, and I will be held guilty."*

This was a crime of concealing merit.

Another account: Minor-Shi Zhou served as Xiang Wang’s charioteer. Upon reaching Jinyang, he wrestled with the strongman Niuzi but lost. Zhou spoke to the lord: *"The reason Your Majesty employs me as charioteer is for my strength, yet now there is one stronger than me. I wish to recommend him for advancement."*

Secondly: When Qi Huan Gong was about to appoint Guan Zhong, he ordered the ministers: *"I shall appoint Guan Zhong as Zhongfu. Those who approve should enter the gate to the left; those who disapprove, to the right."* Dongguo Ya stood in the center and did not move. The duke said: *"I have ordered that those who approve enter to the left, and those who disapprove to the right. Why do you stand in the center?"*
Ya replied: *"Does Guan Zhong’s wisdom make him capable of planning for the world?"*
The duke said: *"He is capable."*
*"Does his decisiveness make him daring in great matters?"*
The duke said: *"He is daring."*
Ya said: *"Your Majesty knows that Guan Zhong is wise and daring in great matters—why entrust him with the reins of the state? With his ability and Your Majesty’s authority to govern Qi, will it not be perilous?"*
The duke said: *"Well spoken."* He then ordered Yi Peng to govern internally while Guan Zhong managed externally, coordinating their efforts.

When Jin Wen Gong fled, Ji Zheng carried his wine vessel and followed. They lost their way and separated. Ji Zheng, starving, stood by the roadside, too ashamed to eat. When Wen Gong returned to his state and led an army to attack Yuan, capturing and taking it, Wen Gong said: *"He who endured hunger and shame to preserve the wine vessel would never betray me. Thus, I appoint him as the governor of Yuan."*

The minister Hun Xuan heard this and criticized it: *"Because Ji Zheng did not move the wine vessel, you trust he would not betray you—is this not lacking in strategy?"*

Thus, the enlightened ruler does not rely on others not betraying him, but on making betrayal impossible. He does not rely on others not deceiving him, but on making deception impossible.

Yang Hu argued: *"If the lord is virtuous and wise, serve him with sincerity; if he is unworthy, feign loyalty to test him."* Banished from Lu, suspected in Qi, he fled to Zhao, where Zhao Jian Wang welcomed and appointed him as prime minister. The officials said: *"Yang Hu excels at seizing control of states—why appoint him?"*
Jian Wang replied: *"Yang Hu seeks to take; I seek to defend. If I defend well, he cannot gain advantage."* He then seized Wei and used Yang Hu as a shield. Yang Hu dared not act deceitfully and served Jian Wang well, strengthening the state nearly to hegemony.

Ru Ai Gong asked Confucius: *"I have heard that anciently there was Kui with one foot—was this truly so?"*
Confucius replied: *"No, Kui was not one-footed. Kui was one of fierce anger and ill will, and most people disliked him. Nevertheless, he escaped harm because of his sincerity—people said, 'Only this one foot remains.' Kui was not one-footed; he was one in sincerity."*

Ai Gong said: *"If this is so, then sincerity is indeed sufficient."*

Another account: Ai Gong asked Confucius: *"I have heard that Kui had one foot—is this true?"*
Confucius replied: *"Kui was a man—why would he have one foot? He had no other differences, but excelled in music. Yao said, 'Kui is one and sufficient,' and appointed him as the Master of Music. Thus, the superior man says: 'Kui is one and sufficient,' not that he had one foot."*

When Jin Wen Gong fought with Chu at Huangfeng Ridge, his shoe strap came loose, and he tied it himself. His attendants said: *"Should we not have someone do this for you?"*
The duke replied: *"I have heard that the superior lord’s companions are those he fears; the mediocre lord’s companions are those he loves—able to respect and obey him, hence worthy of love. The inferior lord’s companions are those he scorns—light in talent and insolent. Though I am unworthy, the retainers of my ancestors remain, making this difficult."*

San Zhou Wen Wang attacked Chong, reaching Fenghuang Market, where his sock strap came loose, and he tied it himself. Taigong Wang said: *"Why do you do this?"*
The king replied: *"Those above me are my teachers; those at my level are my friends; those below me are my servants."*

Now the ministers are all those of my ancestors, so there is no one I can command.

The Lord Ji of the Zisun clan loved virtuous men, maintaining a dignified demeanor throughout his life. His residence, attire, and bearing were always as if in court. Yet when the Lord Ji grew negligent and made mistakes, he would temporarily abandon his dignity. But he could not sustain this, so his guests came to view him as lax and easy to deceive, and they conspired against him, ultimately killing the Lord Ji.

Thus, the virtuous avoid excess and extremity.

Nan Gong Jingzi asked Yan Zhuju: *"The Lord Ji nurtured Confucius’ disciples—those he received in court attire and seated with him numbered in the tens—yet he met with bandits. Why?"*

Yan Zhuju replied: *"In the past, King Cheng of Zhou kept jesters and dwarfs close to indulge his whims, yet consulted them on state affairs. This allowed him to fulfill his desires across the land. Now the Lord Ji nurtures Confucius’ disciples—those he receives in court attire and seats with him number in the tens—yet consults jesters and dwarfs on state affairs. Hence, he met with bandits."*

Thus it is said: *"It is not about who one associates with, but with whom one consults."*

Confucius was seated before Duke Ai of Lu, who granted him peaches and millet gruel. The duke said: *"Please eat."*

Zhongni first ate the gruel and then the peaches. The attendants all covered their mouths and laughed. The duke said: *"The gruel is not for eating—it is to cleanse the peaches."*

Zhongni replied: *"I understand. Millet is the most honored of the five grains, used as the primary offering when sacrificing to the ancestral kings. Among fruits and vegetables, there are six categories, and peaches are the least honored, unfit to enter the ancestral temple. What I have heard is that the noble use the humble to honor the exalted, not the exalted to honor the humble. Now to use the most honored grain to cleanse the least honored fruit—that is to honor the lower with the higher. I consider this contrary to propriety, so I dare not place it before the ancestral offerings."*

Jian Zhu said to his attendants: *"The carriage cushions are too fine. Though a hat is humble, one must wear it on one’s head; though shoes are noble, one must tread upon them. Now the cushions are so fine—what more noble thing could I tread upon? Shoes are what one treads upon. If the cushions are too fine, then there is no finer shoe to tread upon. When the lower is made too fine, the upper is depleted—if the cushions are fine, then the shoes must also be fine, and if the shoes are fine, the garments must be finer still. If one seeks fineness without end, the ruler above will only have greater expenditures. This is the root of unrighteousness."*

Fei Zhong advised King Zhou: *"King Wen of the West, King Chang, was virtuous. The people rejoiced in him, and the feudal lords attached themselves to him. He cannot be allowed to live—if not killed, he will become a calamity for the Yin dynasty."*

King Zhou said: *"Your words are those of a righteous ruler. How can he be killed?"*

Fei Zhong replied: *"Though a hat may be worn out, it must still be worn on the head; though shoes may be of five colors, they must still be trodden upon. Now King Chang, though a subject, practices virtue and the people turn toward him. Surely he will flourish? If the people do not serve their lord because he is virtuous, he cannot be allowed to live. Moreover, if a lord kills his minister, what greater crime is there?"*

King Zhou said: *"Ren and righteousness are what the ruler uses to exhort the people. Now Chang loves ren and righteousness—how can he be killed?"*

Since his three counsels were not heeded, he perished.

Duke Xuan of Qi asked Kuang Qian: *"Are scholars well-versed in many things?"*

Kuang Qian replied: *"No."*

The duke asked: *"Why not?"*

Kuang Qian answered: *"The well-versed seek to excel, and those who excel must kill the inferior. To kill the inferior is to kill what is honored. Scholars consider this harmful to righteousness, so they do not seek to excel."*

The duke asked again: *"Do scholars hunt?"*

Kuang Qian replied: *"No. Hunting is harming the lower for the sake of the upper—this is harming the ruler from below. Scholars consider this harmful to righteousness, so they do not hunt."*

The duke asked again: *"Do scholars play the zither?"*

Kuang Qian replied: *"No. The zither uses small strings to produce great sounds and large strings to produce small sounds—this reverses the order of great and small, noble and humble. Scholars consider this harmful to righteousness, so they do not play."*

Duke Xuan said: *"Excellent."*

Confucius said: *"Rather than have the people flatter those below, it is better to have them flatter those above. Flattering those below breeds factions, while flattering those above breeds humility and reverence."*

Jù the Tall was a recluse of Qi, and Jiàn the Frail was a recluse of Wei. The rulers of Qi and Wei were unenlightened, unable to govern their realms personally, and thus heeded the words of their attendants. Hence, the two men spent their wealth to seek office.

Ximen Bao served as the magistrate of Yè, maintaining strict integrity and purity, taking no private gain—even the finest hairs of grain were untouched by him. Yet he treated his attendants with extreme simplicity, not currying favor with the ruler’s favorites. His attendants thus conspired against him, slandering him.

After a year, when the annual tribute was presented, the ruler confiscated his seal. Ximen Bao requested it back, saying: *"In the past, I did not know how to govern Yè. Now I have learned. I wish to request the seal again to govern Yè. If I fail, I beg to be punished with the axe and the cangue."*

Wen Hou, unable to bear it, returned the seal. Ximen Bao then heavily taxed the people and curried favor with the ruler’s favorites. After another year, when the tribute was presented, Wen Hou welcomed him and bestowed honors. Ximen Bao replied: *"Last year, when I governed Yè for you, you took my seal. Now I have governed for your favorites, and you honor me. I cannot govern anymore."* He then returned the seal and departed.

Wen Hou refused to accept it, saying: *"I did not know you before, but now I do. I wish you to exert yourself for me."* He did not accept the seal Ximen Bao returned.

In Qi, a son of a dog-thief and a son of a crippled man played together and boasted. The thief’s son said: *"My father’s fur cloak still has its tail."* Meaning his father’s cloak still had the stolen dog’s tail attached.

The crippled man’s son said: *"My father never loses his trousers in winter."* For a man with a missing foot, trousers would not be worn, so he suffered no loss even in the coldest season.

Zi Chuo said: *"No one can draw a square with their left hand and a circle with their right. Using meat to drive away ants only makes them multiply; using fish to drive away flies only attracts more."*

Duke Huan of Qi said to Guan Zhong: *"There are few officials, yet many demands. I am troubled by this."*

Guan Zhong replied: *"My lord, do not heed the petitions of your attendants. Grant offices only to those who demonstrate competence and bestow rewards according to merit, then no one will dare demand office—what cause for worry would my lord have?"*

Han Xuanzǐ said: *"My horses and mules have plenty of grain and beans, yet they remain emaciated. I am troubled by this."* Zhou Shi responded: *"If you feed them all the grain you have, even if they do not grow fat, they will not starve. If you claim to give them much but actually provide little, even if they do not starve, they will not grow fat either. Since you do not discern their true condition and yet sit in worry, how can your horses grow fat?"*

Duke Huan asked Guan Zhong about appointing officials. Guan Zhong said: *"In discerning legal arguments and maintaining purity in financial matters, and in understanding human nature, I am not as skilled as Xian Shang. I recommend appointing him as the Grand Judge. In conducting oneself with solemn decorum and treating guests with proper rites, I am not as capable as Xi Peng. I recommend appointing him as the Grand Host. In cultivating wasteland and managing estates—where 'ren' means the taxable yield—no one is better than Ning Wu. I recommend appointing him as the Grand Steward of Agriculture. In forming battle arrays and making soldiers regard death as returning home, no one surpasses Gongzi Chengfu. I recommend appointing him as the Grand Marshal. In speaking boldly and offering direct counsel, no one equals Dongguo Ya. I recommend appointing him as the Counselor. These five men are sufficient to govern Qi. If you wish to achieve hegemony, I am here."*

Meng Xianbo served as minister of Lu, yet his hall floor grew with wild herbs, thorns overgrew his gate, his meals lacked variety, he sat on a single mat, had no concubines in silk, kept no grain for horses, and traveled without a carriage. Chunyang heard of this and told Miao Feihuang, who criticized him: *"This is using your lord’s rank and emoluments to curry favor with the people."* Later, when Meng Xianbo was elevated to the rank of upper minister, Chunyang went to congratulate him. At the gate, the horses were unfed, and Chunyang asked: *"Why do you have only one horse and one carriage?"* Meng Xianbo replied: *"I observe that the people still bear signs of hunger, so I do not feed my horses. Many elderly walk on foot, so I do not keep a second carriage."* Chunyang said: *"I came first to congratulate you on your promotion, but now I congratulate you on your frugality."* As he left, he told Miao Feihuang: *"I join you in congratulating Meng Xianbo on his frugality."* Miaozi said: *"What is there to congratulate? Rank, emoluments, and insignia distinguish merit and separate the worthy from the unworthy. According to the laws of Jin, an upper great officer is entitled to two carriages and two chariots, a middle great officer to two carriages and one chariot, and a lower great officer to a single chariot—this clarifies the hierarchy. Moreover, ministers must oversee military affairs, so they must maintain chariots and horses, align soldiers and cavalry, to prepare for warfare. In times of crisis, this prepares for the unexpected; in times of peace, it supplies court affairs. Now you disrupt the governance of Jin, deplete preparations for emergencies, and achieve frugality only to purify your private reputation. Is Meng Xianbo’s frugality commendable? His words, though fitting for punishment, are praiseworthy. What is there to congratulate?"*

Guan Zhong served as minister of Qi and said: *"I am honored, yet I am poor."* Duke Huan said: *"Let me grant you three estates."* Guan Zhong replied: *"Now I am wealthy, yet I am still lowly."* The duke then seated him above the high and national ministers. Guan Zhong said: *"Now I am exalted, yet I am still distant."* The duke then established him as Zhongfu. Confucius heard of this and criticized: *"His extravagance and arrogance oppress the sovereign."*

When Guan Zhong’s father passed away, he returned in a carriage with a red canopy and green robes, set up drums for his return, and played music upon arriving home. His courtyard displayed a tripod, and his household had three estates. Confucius said: *"What a great minister! His extravagance oppresses the sovereign."*

Sun Shu’ao served as minister of Chu, riding in a simple cart, with a mare, coarse rice cakes, vegetable soup, dried fish, a winter lambskin robe, and summer hemp clothing, his face bearing signs of hunger. Confucius said: *"What a great minister! His frugality oppresses the people."*

Yang Hu fled Qi for Zhao. The ruler asked: *"I have heard you are skilled at nurturing talent."* Yang Hu replied: *"When I lived in Lu, I recommended three men, all of whom became prime ministers. When I incurred guilt in Lu, they all searched for me. In Qi, I recommended three men: one gained proximity to the king, one became a county magistrate, and one became a border official. When I was accused in Qi, the one near the king did not see me, the county magistrate arrested me in chains, and the border official pursued me to the border—though he did not catch me, he stopped there."* Yang Hu said: *"I am not skilled at nurturing talent."* The ruler smiled and said: *"He who plants citrus and orange trees enjoys their sweetness and fragrance. He who plants bitter orange and thorny plants reaps what harms others. Thus, the virtuous man is cautious about whom he nurtures."*

When the magistrate of Zhongmu was vacant, Duke Ping of Jin asked Zhao Wu: *"Zhongmu is the pivot of the three states—Zhao, Qi, and Yan—and the shoulder and hip of Handan. I wish to find a capable magistrate. Who should I appoint?"* Zhao Wu replied: *"Xing Bozi is suitable."* The duke asked: *"Is he not your enemy?"* Zhao Wu said: *"Personal enmity does not enter the public gate."* The duke asked again: *"Who should be appointed magistrate of Zhongfu?"* Zhao Wu replied: *"My son is suitable."* Thus it is said: *"In recommending outsiders, one does not avoid enemies; in recommending family, one does not avoid one’s own."*

Zhao Wu recommended forty-six men. When he died, each took their proper guest position, demonstrating such impartial virtue.

Duke Ping asked Chunyang: *"Which minister is most virtuous?"* Chunyang replied: *"Zhao Wu."* The duke said: *"You favor your teacher."* Chunyang, being Zhao Wu’s subordinate, stood as if his clothes were too heavy, spoke as if his words were forced, yet the men he recommended numbered in the dozens, all fulfilling his intentions. The state greatly relied on him, yet when Zhao Wu’s descendants fell into misfortune, they did not seek refuge with the duke. Thus, I dare say he was virtuous.

Jie Hu recommended his enemy to the ruler as minister, believing he might be pardoned. The enemy, upon appointment, went to pay his respects, thanking Jie Hu for his recommendation.

Jie Hu then drew his bow, sent an arrow, and struck him, saying: *"I recommended you to the ruler, but the ruler did so because he believed you could fulfill the role. As for my private grudge against you, I did not bring it before the ruler to shield you from my enmity. Thus, private grudges do not enter the ruler’s hall."*

Jie Hu recommended Xing Bo Liu as the governor of Shang Party. Liu went to thank him, saying: *"You pardoned my offense; how dare I not bow twice in gratitude?"* Jie Hu replied: *"I recommended you because it was the ruler’s will, but my grudge against you is private. You have gone now, yet my grudge against you remains as before."*

A man from Zheng County sold a piglet. When asked its price, he said: *"The Dao/Way is that when evening comes, I will have leisure to tell you."*

Fan Wenzi loved straightforward speech, so Jie Hu struck him with a staff, saying: *"Those who speak bluntly are not tolerated by others. If they are not tolerated, they endanger their own lives—not just their own lives, but also their fathers’."* Fan Wenzi was the son of Zi Guo.

Zi Guo was loyal to the Duke of Zheng, but Zi Guo’s father, Zi Guo, warned him: *"He who stands apart from other ministers and alone remains loyal to the ruler—if the ruler is virtuous and wise, he may listen to you; if not, he will not. Whether he listens or not is uncertain, yet you have already distanced yourself from the other ministers. Distanced from the other ministers, you will surely endanger your own life—not just your own life, but also your father’s."* #1

Liang Che Xin, as the magistrate of Ye, had his sister visit him. When evening came and the gate closed, she climbed over the city wall to enter, and Liang Che Xin had her feet cut off. #2

Zhao Cheng Hou believed this showed no mercy, so he took away his seal and stripped him of his magistracy. #3

When Guanzhong was bound and taken from Lu to Qi, he grew hungry and thirsty along the way. Passing by the sealed-off territory of Jiwu, he begged for food from the Jiwu official. The official knelt and fed him with great respect, then secretly said to Guanzhong: *"If fortune favors you and you reach Qi alive and are employed, how will you repay me?"* Guanzhong replied: *"If you speak as you do, I will be used by the ruler in a virtuous way. If I can make my labor be fairly rewarded, how will I repay you?"* The official held a grudge against him. #4

---
#1 *Grudge/Resentment* (怨) against the ruler’s judgment.
#2 *Climbed over the wall* (踰郭) to enter secretly.
#3 *Stripped him of his magistracy* (兔之令) – here, "兔" (tù) is used in the sense of "to remove" or "deprive."
#4 *Sealed-off territory* (绮乌封人) – refers to the Jiwu official overseeing a restricted region.

The ruler’s means of governing his ministers are threefold:

1. **When authority is insufficient to transform them, remove them.**
   The blind musician **Shī Guāng’s** reply and **Yàn Zǐ’s** counsel both demonstrate how easy it is to wield authority yet how difficult it is to uphold the **Dao**. This is like chasing wild beasts—one has not yet addressed the root of the harm. The harm that can be removed lies in **Zǐ Xià’s** interpretation of the *Spring and Autumn Annals*.
   A master of authority cuts off evil in its budding stage. Thus, **Jì Sūn** yielded to **Confucius** in seizing the moment of influence, and how much more so should **Guān Zhòng** act in service to the ruler? Therefore, **Tàigōng Wàng** slew the mad **Gòng**, yet **Zāng Gěi** did not ride the swift steed. **Sì Gōng** understood this, hence his chariot hunt. **Xuē Gōng** knew it, hence his gambling with the two musicians. These all understood the reversal of harmony and discord, thus the enlightened ruler’s governance lies in nurturing them.

2. **The sovereign is the pivotal axis of profit and harm, with many arrows aimed at him, making him vulnerable.**
   Thus, when his likes and dislikes are revealed, his ministers take advantage, and the sovereign is deceived. When words are freely exchanged, ministers find it hard to speak truthfully, and the sovereign loses his discernment. This is illustrated in **Shēn Bù’s** *Six Cautions* and **Táng Yì’s** analogy of hunting.
   The danger lies in the annual requests of the **Guó Nián** and the **Xuān Wáng’s** sighs. Clarity comes from **Jìng Guō Shì’s** offering of ten jade ear pendants and the whispers of **Xī Shǒu** and **Gān Mào** through their secret tunnels. **Táng Xuē Gōng** knew the art, hence his inquiry about the jade cup. **Zhāo Hòu** mastered it, hence his solitary audience. The sovereign’s path lies in **Shēn Bù’s** exhortation to decisive judgment.

3. **There are reasons why strategies fail.**

「If you do not kill the dogs, the wine will turn sour.
For a state also has dogs—indeed, its ministers are all like field mice gnawing at its foundations.
A sovereign without the second punishment of Yao or the response of King Zhuang to his son will still face the fate of the old woman’s decision in Cai—
those who prize virtue cannot first measure it by the method of teaching songs, as Wu Qi abandoned his beloved wife, and Duke Wen severed the head of Dian Ji—all defying natural sentiment.
Thus, those who can make men pluck out their own boils must first be those who endure their own pain.

**Chapter One of the Right Canon:**
If rewards and praise do not inspire, and punishments and slander do not deter,
and these four measures are applied without change—then remove them.

When Duke Jing of Qi visited Jin, he drank with Duke Ping.
The blind musician Shi Kuang sat beside him.
At the beginning of the feast, Duke Jing asked Shi Kuang of governance:
*"Master Shi, how shall I instruct me?"*
Shi Kuang replied: *"Your Highness must only be benevolent to the people."*

Mid-feast, as the wine flowed, and he prepared to leave, Duke Jing asked again:
*"Master Shi, how shall I instruct me?"*
*"Your Highness must only be benevolent to the people."*

Duke Jing returned to his lodging, and Shi Kuang saw him off, asking once more:
*"Your Highness must only be benevolent to the people."*

Duke Jing returned to his state, pondering, still half-drunk, and grasped Shi Kuang’s meaning.
Prince Wei and Prince Xia—Duke Jing’s two younger brothers—were deeply beloved by the Qi people.
Their households were wealthy and noble, and the people favored them, rivaling the royal court.
These were threats to my position!
Now, those who say I must be benevolent to the people—do they mean I must compete with my brothers for the people’s favor?

Thus, upon his return, he opened the granaries and taxed the wealthy to aid the poor,
dispersed surplus treasury funds to orphans and widows,
emptied the storehouses of stored grain and the treasury of surplus wealth,
and sent unmarried palace women into marriage.
He set a stipend of seventy bushels of rice for the aged,
and sold his virtue and benevolence to the people—already competing with my brothers.

Two years passed, and the two princes fled.
Prince Xia escaped to Chu, Prince Wei to Jin.

Duke Jing strolled with Yan Zi at Shao Hai, ascending the Piao Qin terrace and gazing back at his realm:
*"Ah, how grand and majestic it is! How splendid and imposing!
Who in future generations will possess such a state?"*

Yan Zi replied: *"Perhaps the Tian Cheng family?"*

Duke Jing said: *"This is my state, yet you say the Tian Cheng family possesses it—why?"*

Yan Zi answered:
*"The Tian Cheng family deeply wins the people’s hearts.
To those above, they petition for titles and stipends, granting them to high ministers.
To those below, they lend large measures of grain and salt, but collect in smaller ones.
When slaughtering an ox, they take only one bowl of meat, feeding the rest to their attendants.
At year’s end, they take only two bolts of cloth for themselves, clothing the rest to their followers.
Thus, timber prices do not rise in the mountains,
and fish, salt, turtles, and shellfish from the marshes are not more costly than those from the sea.
Your Highness imposes heavy taxes, while the Tian Cheng family bestows lavish gifts.
During Qi’s great famine, starving corpses lined the roads beyond count—
yet no father and son who dragged themselves to Tian Cheng’s gates failed to survive.
Thus, the people of Zhou and Qin sang:
*‘Oh, cease your toil! Wrap yourselves in warmth!
Go and return to Tian Chengzi!’
As the poem says:
*‘Though you lack virtue, let us sing and dance for you.’*
Now, the Tian Cheng family’s virtue has made the people sing and dance in devotion—
thus, their hearts belong to them.
Hence, I say: Perhaps the Tian Cheng family?"*

Duke Jing wept, saying: *"Is this not tragic?
I have this state, yet the Tian Cheng family possesses it.
What shall I do now?"*

Yan Zi replied: *"Your Highness, why worry?
If you wish to take it from them, then honor the virtuous and distance the unworthy,
settle disorder and ease punishments,
relieve the greedy and indigent, and care for orphans and widows,
extend kindness and provide for those in need—
then the people will return to you.
Even if there were ten Tian Cheng families, what could they do to you?"*

Some say: *"Duke Jing did not know how to wield power,
and Shi Kuang and Yan Zi did not know how to remove threats.*
The hunter relies on the comfort of his carriage and the speed of six horses,
with Wang Liang guiding the reins—
then his body labors not, yet he easily overtakes light game.
But if he abandons the carriage’s advantage, discards the six horses and Wang Liang’s skill,
and chases the beast on foot—
even Lou Ji’s swiftness could not catch it.
Relying on fine horses and sturdy chariots ensures abundant game.
The state is the sovereign’s chariot, and power is his horses.
To govern without wielding power to restrain ministers who usurp punishments or hoard affection,
and instead compete with the world in virtue—
this is like abandoning the chariot and running on foot.
Thus, it is said:
Duke Jing was a sovereign who did not know how to wield power,
while Shi Kuang and Yan Zi were ministers who did not know how to remove threats."*

Zi Xia said:
*"The Spring and Autumn Annals record ten instances of ministers killing their sovereigns and sons killing their fathers—
none were the result of a single day’s accumulation, but came gradually.
All deceivers act for long before their schemes take root,
and when their roots take hold, their power grows,
and when their power grows, they can kill.
Thus, enlightened rulers cut them off early.
Now, Tian Chang’s rebellion has already shown signs,
yet Your Highness does not punish him.
Yan Zi did not urge his sovereign to restrain encroaching ministers,
but instead had him practice benevolence—
thus, Duke Jian received his calamity.
Hence, Zi Xia said:
*The wise wielders of power cut off deceit in its budding stage."*

When Ji Sun governed Lu, Zi Lu was magistrate of Hei.
Lu mobilized the people in early spring to dig the Chang Gou canal.
At that time, Zi Lu used his private grain ration to make porridge,
and invited the laborers to eat at Wu Fu’s crossroads.
Confucius heard of this and sent Zi Gong to overturn the porridge and smash the bowls, saying:
*"The ruler of Lu has his people—why do you feed them?"*

Zi Lu, furious, rolled up his sleeves and entered to confront him:
*"Does the Master blame me for practicing benevolence and righteousness?"*

「What I learned from the Master was benevolence and righteousness—
benevolence and righteousness are the means to share all that is under heaven with the people and to align their interests with those of the world.
Now, you use my private grain ration to feed the people—how can this be justified?
Confucius replied:
*"Your nature is wild, and I know you well.
You have merely not yet reached it, but this is your fundamental lack of understanding of ritual propriety.
By feeding them, you claim to love them—
yet ritual is the measure by which the Son of Heaven loves all under heaven,
the feudal lords love their domains,
the great ministers love their offices,
and the scholars love their families.
To exceed what one ought to love is called encroachment.
Now, the ruler of Lu has his people, yet you presume to love them—
this is your encroachment, is it not false?"*

Before he finished speaking,
Ji Sun’s envoy arrived and reproached him:
*"Ji Sun mobilized the people and employed them,
while you, Master, had your disciple command the laborers to feed them—
do you intend to deprive Ji Sun’s people of their labor?"*

Confucius then drove his carriage and left Lu.

Though Confucius was virtuous,
Ji Sun was not the ruler of Lu—
yet with the station of a subject,
he assumed the authority of a sovereign,
preemptively restraining what had not yet taken form,
so that Zi Lu could not enact his private benevolence
and harm could not arise.
How much more so for a sovereign!

With Duke Jing’s power to restrain Tian Chang’s encroachment,
there would surely be no fear of hostage-taking or regicide.

When Tai Gong Wang sealed his eastern domain in Qi,
there was a recluse by the sea named Kuang Yu (狂矞) and Hua Shi (華士),
two brothers who declared:
*"We do not serve the Son of Heaven,
nor befriend the feudal lords.
We till the land and eat its yield,
dig wells and drink their water—
we ask nothing of others.
We have no title of nobility,
no lord’s stipend,
and we do not seek office but labor instead."*

Tai Gong Wang arrived at Ying Qiu (營丘),
ordered his officials to seize and execute them,
and made their heads the first offering.

When Zhou Gong Dan heard of this from Lu,
he urgently dispatched a messenger to inquire:
*"These two brothers are virtuous men.
Today, you feast the nation and execute the virtuous—why is this?"*

Tai Gong Wang replied:
*"These two brothers declared:
'We do not serve the Son of Heaven,
nor befriend the feudal lords.
We till the land and eat its yield,
dig wells and drink their water—
we ask nothing of others.
We have no title of nobility,
no lord’s stipend,
and we do not seek office but labor instead.'

Yet, those who do not serve the Son of Heaven
are those who cannot be made to submit to him.
Those who do not befriend the feudal lords
are those who cannot be directed by them.
To till the land and eat its yield,
dig wells and drink their water—
asking nothing of others—
is to say that they cannot be swayed by rewards or punishments.

Now, if they cannot be made to submit,
to whom shall I entrust the rule of my domain?
If they do not bear arms yet seek glory,
nor till the fields yet claim renown,
they are not the means by which a state can be instructed.

Take this horse here—
though it resembles a fine steed,
when driven it does not advance,
when turned back it does not straighten,
when urged left it does not turn left,
when urged right it does not turn right—
even the lowly commoner would not trust his feet to it.
The commoner entrusts his feet to such a steed
because it can chase gains and ward off dangers.
But if it cannot be ridden to these ends,
even the lowly commoner would not trust his feet to it.

You, like the horse that resembles a deer,
are worth a thousand gold pieces in appearance,
yet though a thousand-gold horse may exist,
no deer is worth even one gold piece in service.
The horse serves humanity,
while the deer does not.

Now, as for Ru Er (如耳),
he is a minister of a great state,
yet his heart is not with Wei—
though he be eloquent and wise,
he cannot be employed by me.
Thus, I do not appoint him."

When Xue Gong (薛公) served as minister to Duke Zhao of Wei,
there were two favored attendants named Yang Hu (陽胡) and Pan (潘),
who were held in high regard by the king,
yet not by Xue Gong, who was troubled by this.

Thus, he summoned them to play a game of *博* (a board game),
gave them a hundred gold pieces,
and ordered them to play with their brothers.
Soon, he increased the stakes to two hundred gold pieces.

While the game was in progress,
a messenger announced that the son of Ji (季) was at the gate.
The duke angrily stroked his weapons
and instructed the messenger:
*"Kill him.
I have heard that Ji’s son does not serve me."*

Before the order was carried out,
Ji Yu (季羽) was at his side and said:
*"No, that is not correct.
I have heard that Ji serves you well,
though his person may be unheard of."*

The duke then halted the execution
and instead honored the guest with great ceremony:
*"Earlier, I heard that Ji’s son did not serve me,
so I intended to kill him.
But now, if he truly serves me,
how could I forget Ji?"*

He then ordered the granary to present a thousand *dou* (石) of grain,
the treasury to offer five hundred gold pieces,
the private stables to give two fine steeds and two sturdy chariots,
and further commanded Yan Jiang (奄) to lead twenty beautiful concubines
to bestow upon Ji as well.

The **Lè Zǐ** then addressed his companions, saying:
*"Those who serve the lord must benefit him,
while those who do not serve him must be harmed.
What do we fear in not serving him?"*
Thus, they eagerly urged one another and acted accordingly.

The **Xué Gōng** wielded the power of a subject
but employed the strategies of a sovereign,
yet still could not escape harm—
how much less could one who erred against the sovereign?

*"A tamed crow, when its lower jaw is severed,
must rely on others for food—
how could it remain tame?"*
So too, an enlightened ruler governs his ministers:
he ensures they cannot but profit from the lord’s rewards,
nor escape the title of serving above.
How could they refuse?

**Shēn Zǐ** said:
*"When the lord’s wisdom is evident,
men prepare against him.
When his wisdom is unclear,
men are confused by him.
When his knowledge is apparent,
men are deceived by him;
when his knowledge is hidden,
men conceal themselves from him.
When his desires are absent,
men observe him;
when his desires are present,
men bait him.
Thus it is said:
'I know not how to govern him—
only *wuwei* can guide him.'"*

**Shēn Zǐ** further said:
*"Speak with caution,
and men will know you.
Act with caution,
and men will follow you.
If you reveal your knowledge,
men will conceal themselves from you;
if you hide your knowledge,
men will test you.
If you are wise,
men will praise you;
if you are ignorant,
men will act freely.
Thus it is said:
'Only *wuwei* can guide him.'"*

**Tián Zǐ Fāng** asked **Táng Yì Jū** about hunting:
*"What must one be cautious of?"*
He replied:
*"The crow surveys you with hundreds of eyes,
while you observe it with two—
be diligent in guarding your provisions."*
**Tián Zǐ Fāng** said:
*"Well said!
You add to hunting—
I add to ruling a state."*

The **Zhèng Lǎngzhě**, hearing this, remarked:
*"Tián Zǐ Fāng knows how to seek provisions,
but has not yet found the means to do so.
The way of *xūwú-wújiàn* is discerning."*

**Qí Xuān Wáng** asked **Táng Yì Zǐ** about hunting:
*"What is most precious in hunting?"*
**Táng Yì Zǐ** replied:
*"Diligence in guarding provisions."*
The king asked:
*"What does 'diligence in guarding provisions' mean?"*
He answered:
*"The crow surveys you with tens of eyes,
while you observe it with two—
how could you not be diligent in guarding provisions?"*
Thus, it is said:
*"It lies in guarding the *kāng*."*

The king said:
*"Then, how should one rule the world to achieve this?"*
*"Now, the sovereign surveys a single state with two eyes,
while the state surveys the sovereign with ten thousand eyes—
how then can he guard his own provisions?"*
He replied:
*"The Zhèng Lǎngzhě once said:
*'The way of *xūjìng-wúwéi-wújiàn* may suffice for this.'"*

---
**Annotations (for reference only—do not include in output):**
- *Wuwei* (無為) – "non-action," "effortless action," or "spontaneous alignment with the Dao."
- *Kāng* (廪) – "provisions," "stores," or "sustenance" (here, metaphorical for governance).
- *Xūwú-wújiàn* (虛無無見) – "emptiness, non-being, and non-perception" (a Daoist/strategic ideal).
- *Xūjìng-wúwéi-wújiàn* (虛靜無為無見) – "emptiness, tranquility, non-action, and non-perception" (refined governance principle).

The ram of the state was heavy in the favor of the Lord of Zheng, and hearing of the Lord’s displeasure, he attended a banquet and first said to the Lord:
*"Your servant, alas, has committed a fault. If the Lord would deign to inform me, I would amend it, and thus avoid the death penalty."*

A guest advised King Xuan of Qin, and the king, pleased, sighed deeply. His attendants, drawing the king’s attention to the guest, said, *"First inform the guest of this virtue."* (Thus the king did.)

When Jinguo Jun served as prime minister of Qi, the queen died, and the king did not yet know whom to appoint. He therefore presented jade ear pendants to discern his choice.

Another account says:
