When Xue Gong served as prime minister of Qi, the wife of King Wei of Qi died, and among her ten sons, all were favored by the king. Xue Gong wished to know whom the king intended to establish as the new wife, so he requested permission to place one of them as such.

If the king consented, then the proposal would succeed and be honored in the appointment of the wife. If the king refused, then the proposal would fail and be slighted in the appointment of the wife. Desiring to first ascertain the king’s intended choice to encourage him to appoint her, he thus made ten jade ear pendants, beautifying one among them, and presented them to the king. The king distributed them among the ten sons, and the next day, when they were seated, he observed which pendant was most prized and urged the king to appoint that son’s mother as the new wife.

Gan Mao served as prime minister to King Hui of Qin, who favored Gongsun Yan and, in private, said to him: *"I intend to appoint you as my prime minister."* Gan Mao’s attendant, Dao Xue, overheard this and reported it to Gan Mao.

Gan Mao entered to see the king and said: *"The king has found an able prime minister—I dare to congratulate him with two bows."* The king said: *"I have entrusted the state to you; how could I find another able prime minister?"* Gan Mao replied: *"You intend to appoint Xi Shou."* The king asked: *"How did you hear this?"* Gan Mao answered: *"Xi Shou told me."* The king, angered that Xi Shou had revealed the matter, banished him.

Another account says:
Xi Shou was a great general in the world, a subject of the King of Liang. The King of Qin wished to obtain him and govern the world with him, but Xi Shou said: *"Yan is my lord’s subject; I dare not abandon my lord’s state."* After a year, Xi Shou incurred guilt before the King of Liang and fled to Qin, where the king greatly favored him.

Zhuli Ji was a general of Qin, fearing that Xi Shou would replace him, he dug a hole near where the king often spoke privately. Soon, the king indeed conferred with Xi Shou, saying: *"I wish to attack Han; what do you think?"* Xi Shou replied: *"Autumn would be the time."* The king said: *"I entrust my state to you; you must not divulge this."* Xi Shou turned and bowed twice, saying: *"I accept the command."* Thus, Zhuli Ji also listened through the hole, and the court officials all said: *"The army will march in autumn, attacking Han, with Xi Shou as general."* On that very day, the court officials all knew it. On that very day, all within the borders knew it.

The king summoned Zhuli Ji and said: *"Why are you in such a hurry? What did you hear?"*
Zhuli Ji replied: *"It seemed to be Xi Shou."*

The king said: *"I have spoken with no one but Xi Shou. What is Xi Shou like?"*
Zhuli Ji replied: *"Xi Shou is a wandering scholar, newly arrived after incurring guilt. His heart is solitary, and his words were spoken to invite criticism from the crowd."*

The king said: *"Indeed."* He sent someone to summon Xi Shou, but he had already fled to the territories of other lords.

**Tangxi Gong** said to Duke Zhao of Liang: *"If there were a jade cup worth a thousand gold pieces, but it had no bottom, could it hold water?"*
Duke Zhao replied: *"No."*

*"If there were a clay vessel that did not leak, could it hold wine?"*
Duke Zhao replied: *"Yes."*

Tangxi Gong said: *"Clay vessels are the most humble, yet if they do not leak, they can hold wine. Even if there were a jade cup worth a thousand gold pieces, the most noble yet without a bottom, it could not hold water if it leaked. Then, who would pour the liquid for you?"*

*"If a ruler leaks the words of his ministers, it is like a jade cup without a bottom. Even if he possesses sage wisdom, he cannot fully apply it, for it leaks. Thus, the state suffers."*

Duke Zhao said: *"Indeed."* After hearing Tangxi Gong’s words, Duke Zhao thereafter, whenever he wished to announce a great matter to the world, would never fail to lie alone in fear that his dreams would reveal his plans and inform others of his intentions.

---

One day, Tangxi Gong saw Duke Zhao and said: *"If there were a white jade cup without a bottom, and a clay cup with a bottom, and you were thirsty, which would you drink from?"*
Duke Zhao replied: *"From the clay cup."*

Tangxi Gong said: *"The white jade cup is beautiful, yet you do not drink from it—is it because it lacks a bottom?"*
Duke Zhao replied: *"Indeed."*

Tangxi Gong said: *"If a ruler leaks the words of his ministers, it is like a white jade cup without a bottom."* Whenever Tangxi Gong visited, Duke Zhao would lie alone in bed, fearing that his dreams would reveal secrets to his wives and concubines.

**Shen Zi** said: *"One who sees alone is called discerning, one who listens alone is called perceptive. Only those who can make decisions alone can truly rule the world."*

---

There was a wine seller in Song whose measures were perfectly even, who treated guests with great care, whose wine was excellent, and whose banner hung high. Yet his wine did not sell, and it turned sour. People wondered why, so they asked those who knew, including the elder Yang Qian. Qian said: *"Is your dog fierce?"*

*"If the dog is fierce, why does the wine not sell?"*
*"Because people fear it."*

Some sent their young attendants with money and carrying jugs to purchase from the wine seller, but the dog would bark and bite them. This was why the wine turned sour and did not sell.

*"A state also has its dogs. A virtuous scholar carries his wisdom and seeks to enlighten the ruler of a great realm, but powerful ministers act like fierce dogs, barking and biting him away. This is why the ruler’s wisdom is obstructed, and virtuous scholars are not employed."*

Thus, Duke Huan of Qi asked Guan Sheng: *"What is the greatest affliction in ruling a state?"*
Guan Sheng replied: *"The most grievous affliction is the temple mice."*

Duke Huan asked: *"Why are temple mice such a grievous affliction?"*
Guan Sheng replied: *"Have you seen the temple trees?"*
*"Trees are painted and erected in the temple, and mice burrow between the layers, digging holes to shelter themselves. If you fumigate, they fear the fire will consume the wood; if you flood, they fear the paint will crack. This is why temple mice are not easily eliminated."*

*"Now, the ministers at a ruler’s side—when they go out, they wield their influence to profit from the people; when they return, they conspire to conceal their misdeeds from the ruler. They spy on the ruler’s intentions and report them to outsiders, while both external and internal factions vie for power. The officials and attendants all consider this wealth. If the ruler does not execute them, the laws are disordered; if he executes them, the ruler is endangered. They hold power and profit from it—these are also the temple mice of the state."*

*"Thus, when ministers monopolize authority and issue arbitrary prohibitions, those who benefit themselves are rewarded, while those who do not are harmed. This is also the fierce dog."*

*"If the ministers act as fierce dogs to bite away the virtuous scholars, and those at the ruler’s side act as temple mice to obstruct his wisdom, then his plans cannot succeed. The ruler will not be aware, and in such a case, how can the ruler avoid stagnation, and how can the state avoid ruin?"*

---

One day, the wine seller of Song was a man of the **Zhuang clan**, whose wine was always excellent. Yet someone sent a servant to purchase wine from him, but the dog bit the servant, so the servant dared not return. Instead, he bought wine from another family.

When asked: *"Why did you not purchase wine from the Zhuang clan?"*
The servant replied: *"Today, the Zhuang clan’s wine is sour."*

Thus it was said: *"Do not kill the dog, or the wine will turn sour."*

Duke Huan of Qi asked **Guan Zhong**: *"What is the greatest affliction in ruling a state?"*
Guan Zhong replied: *"The most grievous affliction is the temple mice."*

*"Temple trees are painted and erected, and mice take shelter within the layers. If you fumigate, the wood burns; if you flood, the paint cracks. This is why temple mice are so troublesome."*

*"Now, the ministers at a ruler’s side—when they go out, they wield their influence to profit from the people; when they return, they conspire to conceal their misdeeds from the ruler, using flattery and slander to deceive him. If the ruler does not execute them, the laws are disordered; if he executes them, the ruler is endangered. They hold power and profit from it—these are also the temple mice of the state."*

*"Thus, when ministers monopolize authority and issue arbitrary prohibitions, those who benefit themselves are rewarded, while those who do not are harmed. This is also the fierce dog."*

*"If those at the ruler’s side act as temple mice and those in power act as fierce dogs, then his wisdom cannot prevail."*

---

Emperor Yao wished to pass the realm to Shun, but **Gun** advised: *"This is unauspicious! Who would pass the realm to a commoner?"*
Yao did not heed him, raised an army, and executed Gun at the outskirts of Yushan.

**Gonggong** also advised: *"Who would pass the realm to a commoner?"*
Yao did not heed him, and again raised an army to execute Gonggong in the city of You.

Thus, no one dared to speak against passing the realm to Shun.

**Zhongni** (Confucius) heard of this and said: *"Yao’s wisdom and Shun’s virtue were not difficult to achieve. The difficulty lay in this: when one reached the point of executing those who gave counsel, he would still pass the realm to Shun. This was the true difficulty."*

---

*"Not to let one’s doubts undermine one’s discernment is difficult."*

Duke Zhao of Liang established a law called the **Mao Men Decree**: *"If any minister, great officer, or prince enters court and the horses’ hoofs trample the steps, the court officials shall behead the carriage and execute the driver."*

---

*"Thus, the crown prince entered court, and the horses’ hoofs trampled the steps. The court officials beheaded his carriage and executed his driver. The crown prince, enraged, entered and wept before the king, saying: ‘You must surely execute and slay the court officials for me!’
The king replied: ‘The law exists to honor the ancestral temples and uphold the state. Therefore, those who can establish laws and follow orders with reverence for the state are the state’s loyal ministers—how could I execute them?’
To violate the law and disregard orders while failing to honor the state is for a minister to defy his sovereign and for the subjects to revere those who set themselves above the law. When a minister defies his sovereign, the ruler loses his authority; when subjects revere those who set themselves above the law, the high position becomes perilous. If authority is lost and the high position is in danger, the state cannot be preserved. How could I leave such a legacy for my descendants?’
Thus, the crown prince returned and fled, dwelling in exile and sleeping under the open sky for three days before kneeling in the north and repeatedly begging for his life. [Later account:]

The King of Chu urgently summoned the crown prince. The law of Chu forbade carriages from approaching the **Mao Men** (茅门).
Heavy rain fell, and the courtyard was flooded. The crown prince drove his carriage to the **Mao Men** anyway.
The court official said: ‘The carriage is not allowed to approach the **Mao Men**; this is against the law.’
The crown prince replied: ‘The king summoned me urgently, and I could not wait for the flood to subside.’
He drove forward. The court official raised his weapon and struck the horses, overturning the carriage.
The crown prince entered and wept before the king, saying: ‘The courtyard was flooded, so I drove my carriage to the **Mao Men**. The court official declared it unlawful and struck my horses, overturning my carriage. The king must surely execute him!’
The king said: ‘In the past, there was an elder sovereign who did not cross the threshold, and now there is a successor who does not obey the law—this is shameful. This is truly a minister who upholds the law.’
He then elevated the court official’s rank by two levels, opened a rear gate for the crown prince to exit, and forbade him from returning.

The successor of the Duke of Wei said to **Bo Yi** (薄疑): ‘You consider my realm too small to serve in, yet I can empower you to serve. I beg you to advance in rank and let you become the chief minister.’
He then granted Bo Yi ten thousand *qing* (顷) of land.
Bo Yi said: ‘My mother doubts me, believing that I am capable of what even the ministers of a great state cannot achieve.’
Yet, when Bo Yi’s mother had doubts about household matters, she would consult a soothsayer named **Cai Yu** (蔡妪), whom she deeply trusted and entrusted with family affairs. If Bo Yi’s mother agreed with his words, she would follow them; but if she had already spoken to the soothsayer, she would still seek confirmation from **Cai Yu**.
Thus, when assessing Bo Yi’s wisdom and ability, it was said that he could advise even the ministers of a great state, yet he was still unable to sway his mother.
When considering his filial piety, he was still unable to resolve doubts with his mother without consulting **Cai Yu**.
Now, Bo Yi stands before a sovereign, not his mother, and yet sovereigns all have their **Cai Yu**—those whom they hold in high regard. Those held in high regard by a sovereign are capable of private influence. The words of those who exercise private influence are beyond the reach of the law, while Bo Yi’s words are within the law. Private influence and the law are incompatible; they cannot coexist. [Later account:]

When the Duke of Wei journeyed to **Jin** (晋), he said to Bo Yi: ‘I wish for you to accompany me.’
Bo Yi replied: ‘My aged mother is still with me; I must first return home and consult her.’
The Duke of Wei personally invited Bo Yi’s mother, who said: ‘Bo Yi is your minister. If you have the intention to let him go, that is very good.’
The Duke of Wei said: ‘I have asked your permission, and you have consented to my request.’
When Bo Yi returned to speak to his mother, he said: ‘The Duke of Wei’s love for me—how does it compare to yours?’
She replied: ‘It is not as deep as my love for you.’
‘The Duke of Wei’s virtue in honoring me—how does it compare to yours?’
She replied: ‘It is not as great as my virtue in honoring you.’
After Bo Yi and his mother had decided on family matters and reached a conclusion, she would still consult the soothsayer **Cai Yu** before making a final decision. Now, the Duke of Wei has followed Bo Yi’s counsel and set out, yet even if Bo Yi and his mother had agreed, they would still be thwarted by another **Cai Yu**, and thus Bo Yi could not remain as a minister for long.

---

When teaching singing, one first instructs the students to loudly call out the **Gong** (宮) note and then corrects it, before gradually refining the **Zheng** (徵) note. If the voice does not return to clarity, it cannot be called teaching. [Later account:]

When teaching singing, one first measures the **Gong** (宮) note by rule, quickly calling out the central **Gong** (中宮), then slowly calling out the central **Zheng** (中徵). If the quick call does not hit the central **Gong** (宮), or the slow call does not hit the central **Zheng** (徵), it cannot be called teaching.

---

**Wu Qi** (吴起), a native of the **Zuo** (左氏) clan of **Wei** (卫), made his wife weave cloth to a narrow width, contrary to the standard measure.
Wu Qi ordered her to adjust it, but his wife replied: ‘Yes.’
After finishing, he measured it again, and it still did not conform to the standard.
Wu Qi grew furious, and his wife responded: ‘I initially followed your instructions, but I could not adjust it.’
Wu Qi banished her. His wife then sought her brother’s help to reclaim entry, and her brother said: ‘Wu Qi is a man of principle. His adherence to the law is such that he seeks to apply it to achieve great deeds for a great state. He must first practice it within his own household before enforcing it elsewhere. You, his mother, nearly lost your place within it.’
Wu Qi’s wife’s younger brother was held in higher regard by the Duke of Wei, so he used the duke’s influence to plead with Wu Qi. Wu Qi refused, and thus he left Wei and entered **Jing** (荆, Chu).

[Later account:]
Wu Qi showed his wife the loom and said: ‘Measure the cloth for me according to this standard.’
After she had woven it and presented it, the cloth was of superior quality.
Wu Qi said: ‘I ordered you to weave the cloth according to this standard, yet now it is of superior quality—why is this?’
His wife replied: ‘The effort and resources I expended were equal, but I applied greater care to its quality.’
Wu Qi said: ‘This was not what I instructed. Go and change your clothes.’
His father went to plead for her, but Wu Qi said: ‘My household does not tolerate idle words.’

---

The Duke of **Jin** (晋) asked **Hu Yan** (狐偃) and said: ‘I have prepared rich feasts in the hall, with wine and meat gathered in the palace. The wine is not strained, the fresh meat is not evenly sliced, and I slaughter one cow to distribute throughout the state. All the rewards of a year’s labor are used to clothe the soldiers. Is this sufficient to inspire the people to fight?’
Hu Yan replied: ‘It is not sufficient.’
The Duke of Jin said: ‘I have lifted the tolls on markets and lightened punishments. Is this sufficient to inspire the people to fight?’
Hu Yan replied: ‘It is not sufficient.’
The Duke of Jin said: ‘For those among my people who have lost relatives, I personally order the palace attendants to oversee funeral rites. If they are guilty of wrongdoing, I pardon them; if they are poor and cannot afford it, I provide the means. Is this sufficient to inspire the people to fight?’"
Hu Yan replied: ‘It is not sufficient.’

Hu Yan replied: ‘It is not sufficient. These are all measures to preserve life, but war is about killing. The people follow you because you preserve their lives, yet you then lead them to slaughter—you have lost the very reason they follow you.’

The Duke of Jin asked: ‘Then what *would* suffice to inspire the people to fight?’

Hu Yan replied: ‘Make it so that none dare avoid battle.’

The Duke of Jin said: ‘How can one ensure that none dare avoid battle?’

Hu Yan replied: ‘By enforcing rewards and punishments with absolute certainty—that will suffice to inspire them to fight.’

The Duke of Jin asked: ‘How severe must the punishments be?’

Hu Yan replied: ‘No favoritism toward kin or nobles—apply the law even to those you cherish.’

The Duke of Jin said: ‘Excellent.’

The next day, he issued an order for the people to till the fields at Pu Lu, setting noon as the deadline. Those who failed to meet it were to be tried by military law. Among those who arrived late was a favorite of the Duke’s, a man named Dian Jie. The officials petitioned for his punishment, and the Duke wept in sorrow.

The officials said: ‘We must carry out the law.’

They beheaded Dian Jie and displayed his corpse to the people, demonstrating the Duke’s resolve. Thereafter, the people trembled in fear, saying: ‘The Duke holds even Dian Jie—so esteemed and dear—as nothing when it comes to enforcing the law. What hope then is there for us?’

Seeing that his people were now ready to fight, the Duke of Jin raised his armies to the east, conquering Yuan. He attacked Wei, shifting its fields eastward, and seized Wulu. He struck Yang, defeated Guo, and subdued Cao. He marched south to besiege Zheng, then withdrew to its ramparts. He lifted the siege of Song and returned to clash with the Chu forces at Chengpu, defeating them utterly. He then established the Jian Tu Alliance and upheld the principles of Hengyong. In a single campaign, he achieved eight victories—all because of this: no other reason, no extraordinary means. He followed Hu Yan’s counsel and used Dian Jie’s execution as an example.

For when a boil festers, if you do not lance it to the bone and marrow, the pain will overwhelm the mind until it cannot endure. Only by doing so can you force a man to grind a half-inch whetstone against it. So too with governance: if the ruler does not know the people’s suffering, how can they be made to bear it?

To govern his state, one must not hesitate to heed the wise and punish treacherous ministers.

Treacherous ministers are always held in high regard.
Those held in high regard are always those whom the ruler holds most dear and loves.

Those whom the ruler holds most dear and loves—this is like clinging to the distinction between "firm" and "white."
With nothing but the commoner’s station, to seek to sever the ruler’s deep attachment to what he cherishes—this is like trying to persuade someone to sever their own left flank to please their right thigh: the body would surely die, and the persuasion would never take hold.

**One.**
When rewards and punishments are shared, prohibitions and commands fail to take effect. When ministers control them, they are said to be "shared." How do we know this? We see it in the cases of **Zàofù** and **Yúqí**. Though skilled in driving horses and able to endure thirst, when they grew greedy for a pig and drank to excess, they lost control. **Zǐhǎn** was made responsible for providing food, yet he failed to enforce discipline—so much so that the entire state feared him, and he eventually usurped the ruler. This was akin to the power derived from controlling the distribution of food. **Tián Dàn** managed the royal gardens and pools, arbitrarily granting rewards, and people flocked to him, leading him to assassinate **Jiǎn Gōng**. This too was a case of power arising from control over gardens and pools.

Thus, the rulers of **Sòng** and **Jiǎn** were slain. The danger lies in **Wáng Liáng** and **Zàofù** sharing the reins, or **Tián Lián** and **Chéng Xuè** sharing the zither. Though **Wáng** and **Zàofù** were indeed skilled charioteers, if they allowed others to hold the reins, the horses would not advance. Though **Tián Chéng** was a master musician, if another were allowed to pluck the strings, the melody would fail. So too when ministers share in rewards and punishments.

**Two.**
Strength and order arise from law, while weakness and chaos stem from favoritism. When laws are bent, disorder follows. If a ruler understands this, he will uphold rewards and punishments without relying on mere benevolence toward his subjects. Nobility and emoluments arise from merit; when merit is established, nobility follows. Executions and punishments arise from guilt; when guilt is proven, punishment follows. If ministers understand this, they will exert themselves to the utmost in loyalty to their ruler. When the ruler transcends mere benevolence and ministers transcend mere loyalty, then true kingship can be achieved.

**Zhāoxiáng** knew the ruler’s inclinations, yet he only sought to apply reason in his reproaches. The common people should merely follow their lord’s commands, requiring no excessive affection—thus, when the ruler fell ill and prayed for recovery, **Zhāowáng** held him accountable for the **Èr Jiǎ** incident, yet did not open the royal gardens. When **Yìng Hóu** wished to distribute vegetables and fruits to relieve the starving, **Zhāowáng** deemed it improper to reward without merit, and so stopped him.

**Tián Wěi** understood the minister’s nature, urging him only to establish merit—since private loyalty to the ruler was unnecessary. Hence, he instructed **Tián Zhāng**, saying: *"Enriching the state enriches oneself; benefiting the ruler benefits oneself."* Yet **Gōngyí** declined the fish, believing that accepting it for violating the law would mean losing it entirely, and thus refused.

**Three.**
A wise ruler learns from external affairs, and since external matters must succeed, **Sū Dài** was right not to trust **Qí Wáng**. Because **Sū Dài** ordered **Yàn Wáng** to entrust matters solely to him, the king did not do so, and thus failed to achieve hegemony.

A ruler must also discern the intentions of scholars, for those who reside in obscurity yet seek prominence will deceive. Thus, **Pān Shòu** spoke of **Yǔ’s** sentiments to flatter **Zǐ**, telling **Yàn Wáng** that **Yǔ** had passed his throne to **Yì**, only to have **Qǐ** seize it. The king then exalted **Zǐ** all the more.

If a ruler remains unaware, then how can he know that others may borrow his authority? Should he fear that his kin might share his garments, or that his ministers might borrow his power? If he knows that all men know themselves, he will not share his carriage with those who do not wear his garments, nor his household with those of the same clan—for fear they might exploit their kinship. How much more so with ministers borrowing the ruler’s authority?

**Wú Zhāng** understood this, so he feigned compliance, yet died while still relying on sincerity. **Zhào Wáng** hated the "tiger’s eye" and obstructed the ruler’s way, just as the royal gardener hated the tiger’s eye and had it removed. When courtiers spoke of **Píngyáng Jūn’s** eyes being worse than the tiger’s, the king ordered the speaker executed—just as **Zhōu’s** envoy rejected **Wèi Hóu** because his given name, **Bìqiáng**, was the same as the imperial title, and only allowed him to enter after he changed it.

**Four.**
A ruler is one who upholds the law, demands results, and establishes merit. Though officials may be corrupt, there are upright citizens who remain steadfast in their principles—such as the **Three Virtuous Men of Yīn** and **Lóng Fòng of Xià**. Yet there has never been a case of a corrupt official being alone in his governance. If **Zǐshuài** enforces order, who would dare to disobey? Thus, a wise ruler governs officials, not the people. When officials are governed, the people will be governed as well.

This is illustrated by the roots of a tree and the halter of a net: shaking the roots moves all the branches, pulling the halter opens all the meshes. When officials are upright, the state is governed. Hence, even the **shèifu** responsible for extinguishing fires must be held accountable.

Those who extinguish fires—if an official carries a bucket and runs to the blaze, his effort is that of one man alone. But if he wields a whip to command others, he mobilizes ten thousand. A wise ruler wields the **contracts (qì)** in the same way. Thus, when encountering a skilled horseman like **Zào Jiāo** facing a startled horse, pulling the horse and pushing the carriage would not advance them, but if another took the reins and held the whip, the horses would gallop forward.

Hence, the principle lies in straightening the crooked and leveling the uneven, in bending the warped and making the straight straight. If not, disaster follows—as when **Nào Chǐ** used **Qí’s** execution of **Mǐn Wáng**, or **Lǐ Duì** used **Zhào’s** famine to starve **Zhǔfù**.

**Five.**
By following the natural order of affairs, success comes without labor—thus **Zī Zhèng** sat on the chariot’s pole and sang of ascending the high beams. The danger lies in **Zhào Jiǎnzhǔ** consulting with tax officials about levies, where the ruler, desiring taxation, asked the officials about rates, yet failed to set clear limits himself, merely saying, *"Do not make them too light or too heavy."* The officials then arbitrarily enriched themselves. **Bó Yí**’s words led to the people being well-fed across the state. **Jiǎnzhǔ** rejoiced, yet the treasury was empty, while the people starved and corrupt officials grew rich.

Thus, **Huán Gōng** inspected the people, and **Guān Zhòng** examined the decaying wealth and the abandoned women. The duke, upon seeing a starving man and an elderly man without a wife, reported to **Zhòng**, saying: *"When the state has rotting wealth, the people starve; when the palace has abandoned women, the elderly have no wives."* Otherwise, as in **Yánlíng**, where the horses could not advance, **Zàofù** wept upon seeing it—obstructed by the decorations in front and the harness behind, unable to move forward or backward, and thus straying to the side. **Zàofù** wept at the sight, just as misplaced rewards and punishments inevitably lead to ruin.

**End of the Chapter.**

Zào Fǔ controlled the four horses, galloping swiftly in circles to indulge his desires in them. Whatever his will desired, the horses would follow without hesitation. His indulgence in the horses lay in his mastery over the reins and whip. With the reins and whip firmly in command, the horses did not disobey.

Yet when the horses were startled by the **#6出彘** (wild boar), Zào Fǔ could not restrain them, not because the reins and whip were insufficiently strict, but because his authority was undermined by the **#6出彘**. The **#6出彘** also made the horses something to be feared, hence the saying *"authority was undermined by the **#6出彘**."* The Prince of **Yúqī** served as the **bìngjià** (marriage escort) for the **Zhào** Duke, but he did not use the reins and whip, instead choosing to indulge the horses’ desires and monopolizing the advantage of fodder and water. Yet when the horses passed the **pǔchí** (ponds and fields), the **bìngjià** failed, not because the advantage of fodder and water was lacking, but because his **virtue/power** (德) was divided among the **pǔchí**.

Thus, **Wáng Liáng** and **Zào Fǔ**, though the finest charioteers in the world, could not make the horses move even ten *lǐ* when **Wáng Liáng** held the left reins and **Zào Fǔ** held the right, each issuing commands and whips—this was because of the divided authority. **Tián Lián** and **Chéng Qiào**, though the finest musicians in the world, could not compose a melody when **Tián Lián** played the upper strings and **Chéng Qiào** played the lower—this was also because of the divided effort.

How could the **Dao/Way** (道) of **Wáng Liáng** and **Zào Fǔ**, who were so skillful, succeed in controlling the horses if their authority was divided? How could the **Dao/Way** (道) of the ruler succeed in governing if he shared power with his ministers? The **Zhào** Duke said: *"Let me test your charioteering in the fields."* Zào Fǔ drove his carriage into the fields, and the horses, seeing the **pǔchí**, bolted, unable to be restrained. Though Zào Fǔ had long trained the horses to endure thirst, when they saw the **pǔchí**, they broke free and ran, and even Zào Fǔ could not control them. Now, **Jiǎn Gōng** had long restrained his people with laws, yet **Tián Chéng Héng** (田成恒) took advantage of them, just as **Tián Chéng Héng** tilted the **pǔchí** and tempted the thirsty people.

Another account says:
The Prince of **Yúqī** served as the **bìngjià** for the **Sòng** Lord in the pursuit of a thousand-*lǐ* horse race.

The carriage was already harnessed, and he examined the markings on the driver’s hands.
Just as he was about to depart, he urged the horses forward, but the wheels were bound by cords, which pulled them back, and the horses turned away.
He then released the cords and set them free, but the pig escaped from the hole, and the horses recoiled and retreated; though he urged them forward with the whip, they could not advance.
The horses reared and bolted, and though he tried to control them with the reins, he could not keep them straight.

Another account says:
**Sìchéng Zǐ Hǎn** told the **Sòng** Lord: *"To grant rewards and bestow favors is what the people delight in—let Your Majesty personally oversee these matters.
To impose punishments and execute criminals is what the people abhor—let me take responsibility for these."* Thus, the Lord executed commoners while **Zǐ Hǎn** was put to death for his crimes, and the Lord declared, *"This matter was decided by **Zǐ Hǎn**."*

After a year, the people came to know that the power of life and death rested with **Zǐ Hǎn**, and so the entire realm turned to him.
Thus, **Zǐ Hǎn** coerced the **Sòng** Lord and seized control of governance—**the law could not restrain him**.
Hence it is said that **Zǐ Hǎn** was the one who let the pig out, while **Tián Chéng Héng** was the one who tilted the **pǔchí**.

Now, if **Wáng Liáng** and **Zào Fǔ** were to share a chariot, each holding one rein as they entered the gates, the team would surely falter and the **Dao/Way** would not reach its destination.
If **Tián Lián** and **Chéng Xiào** were to share a zither, each plucking one string, the harmony would be ruined and the melody would fail to complete itself.

Two:
When **Qín Zhāo Wáng** was ill, the commoners bought cattle in their neighborhoods and prayed for the king’s recovery at home.
**Gōngsūn Shù** came out to see them, then entered to congratulate the king: *"The commoners have all bought cattle in their neighborhoods and prayed for Your Majesty’s recovery."*
The king sent someone to inquire about it, and indeed, it was so.
The king said: *"Bestow two ranks of reward upon them."*

The **punishment** is a **disparagement**. The king **rewarded** them with two ranks.

Indeed, those who prayed without being commanded were showing their love for me.
If they loved me, then I should **change the law** and align my heart with theirs—otherwise, the law would not stand.
If the law does not stand, then this is the path to **chaos and downfall**.
Yet instead of following this path, the king **punished** them with two ranks and then **rewarded** them to govern again.

Secondly:
When **King Xīn of Qín** was ill, the commoners prayed for him, and after his recovery, they sacrificed oxen to fulfill their prayers.
**Yàn Jué**, a **courtier**, and **Gōngsūn Yǎn** came out to see them and asked in surprise: *"This is not the time of the seasonal sacrifices—why are you slaughtering oxen and worshipping the ancestral mounds?"*
The commoners replied: *"The sovereign was ill, so we prayed for him. Now that he has recovered, we sacrifice oxen to fulfill our prayers."*
**Yàn Jué** and **Gōngsūn Yǎn** were **pleased** and went to see the king, bowing and congratulating him: *"Your Majesty has surpassed **Yáo** and **Shùn**!"*
The king was startled and asked: *"What do you mean?"*
They answered: *"**Yáo** and **Shùn**, their people did not even reach the point of praying for them. Now, Your Majesty was ill, and the people prayed with oxen, then slaughtered oxen to fulfill their prayers. Therefore, your subjects humbly believe that Your Majesty has surpassed **Yáo** and **Shùn**!"*
The king then asked why they came from certain neighborhoods, and **punished** their **ward leaders** and **five-family elders** with two ranks each.
**Lǚ** (屯) is also a **punishment**.
**Yàn Jué** and **Gōngsūn Yǎn** remained silent and dared not speak.

After several months, the king was **drunk on wine** and enjoying himself, so **Yàn Jué** and **Gōngsūn Yǎn** said to him: *"Earlier, your subjects humbly believed that Your Majesty had surpassed **Yáo** and **Shùn**, not merely daring to flatter you. **Yáo** and **Shùn**, when they were ill, their people did not even reach the point of praying for them. Now, Your Majesty was ill, and the people prayed with oxen, then slaughtered oxen to fulfill their prayers. Yet now you **punish** their ward leaders and five-family elders with two ranks each—your subjects humbly find this strange."*

The king said: *"Why do you not understand this?*
Those people serve me not because I love them, but because of my **power and authority**."*

「Chapter 2. **Releasing power and severing ties with the people**—this is also a form of punishment.
I abandon my **power and authority** to let the people drift away from me,
so if I do not **virtue/power** them with love, the people will not serve me.
Thus, I have completely abandoned the **Dao/Way** of love.

When there was a great famine in **Qín**, **Yìng Hóu** (應侯) petitioned: *"The grasses of the Five Parks (五苑) are sprouting—meaning the plants grow from the earth.
Vegetables, acorns, dates, and chestnuts are sufficient to sustain the people.
I request that they be distributed."*

King **Zhāoxiāng** (昭襄王) of **Qín** said: *"Our **Qín laws** state that the people receive rewards for their merits and punishments for their crimes.
If we distribute the grasses of the Five Parks, we would be rewarding both those who have merit and those who do not.
To reward both those with merit and those without is the **Dao/Way** of chaos.
If distributing the Five Parks leads to chaos, it is better to abandon the dates and vegetables and maintain order."*

**Another account says:**
*"If you order the distribution of the Five Parks’ vegetables, dates, acorns, and chestnuts—sufficient to sustain the people—
this would cause the people to compete for rewards, whether they have merit or not.
To create chaos by rewarding the living is worse than letting the people perish to maintain order.
Thus, the officials should abandon this plan."*

**Tián Wéi** (田鮒) instructed his son, **Tián Zhāng** (田章):
*"To enrich oneself and one’s family, one must first enrich the state and the ruler.
To enrich one’s family, one must first enrich the nation."*

**Another account says:**
*"Tián Wéi taught his son, Tián Zhāng:
‘When a ruler sells official positions and titles,
and a minister sells his wisdom and strength,
they rely on themselves, not on others.
**Gōngyí Xiū** (公仪休) served as chancellor of **Lǔ** while loving fish.
All across the state, people competed to buy fish and present it to him,
but Gōngyí’s son refused to accept it.
His younger brother remonstrated:
‘Master, you love fish yet refuse to accept it—why?’
He replied:
‘Because I love fish, I refuse to accept it.
If I accept fish, I must inevitably show favoritism to those who give it to me.
Showing favoritism would violate the law,
and if I violate the law, I would be removed from my position as chancellor.
Even if I love fish, I cannot guarantee that those who give it to me will always provide it,
nor can I guarantee my own supply of fish.
But if I do not accept fish, I can maintain my chancellor’s office indefinitely,
and though I love fish, I can still ensure a lasting supply for myself.
This demonstrates that relying on others is worse than relying on oneself,
and that others providing for you is worse than you providing for yourself.’"*

**Chapter 3.**
*"When you served as chancellor of **Yàn** (燕), you were honored yet refused authority.
**Sū Dài** (苏代), as an emissary from **Qí** (齐) to **Yàn**, was asked by the king:
‘How is the ruler of **Qí**?’
He replied:
‘He will never become a hegemon (霸).’
The **Yàn** king asked:
‘Why?’
He answered:
‘In the past, when **Huan Gōng** (桓公) was hegemon,
he entrusted domestic affairs to **Bào Shū** (鲍叔) and foreign affairs to **Guǎn Zhòng** (管仲).
**Huan Gōng** wore his hair unbound and attended to women,
spending his days leisurely in the marketplace.
Now, the ruler of **Qí** does not trust his great ministers.’
Thus, the **Yàn** king increased his trust in you.*

**Another account says:**
*"Sū Dài, as an emissary from **Qín** to **Yàn**, saw that if he did not praise you, he would not be able to complete his mission and return.
Moreover, his tribute and gifts would not be granted.
So when he saw the **Yàn** king, he praised the ruler of **Qí**.
The **Yàn** king asked:
‘If the ruler of **Qí** is so virtuous, will he not become king of all under heaven?’
Sū Dài replied:
‘He cannot even save his own state—how could he become king?’
The **Yàn** king asked:
‘Why?’
He said:
‘His distribution of favors is not equitable.’
The **Yàn** king asked:
‘Why will his state perish?’
He replied:
‘In the past, **Qián Gōng** (桓公) of **Qí** loved **Guǎn Zhòng**,
elevating him to the rank of *Zhòng Fù* (仲父),
entrusting domestic affairs to him and foreign affairs to his judgment.
The entire state followed him,
so he achieved one restoration of order under heaven (一匡天下)
and nine alliances among the feudal lords (九合诸侯).
Now, the ruler of **Qí** distributes his favors unequitably—
this is why I know his state will perish.’
The **Yàn** king said:
‘Now I entrust authority to you,
but the world has not yet heard of it.’
The next day, he publicly announced your appointment as chancellor and listened to your counsel.

**Pān Shòu** (潘寿) told the **Yàn** king:
‘Your Majesty should not do as I did—
you should instead grant the entire state to you.
The reason people call **Yáo** (尧) virtuous is that he ceded the **Mandate of Heaven** (天下) to **Xǔ Yóu** (许由),
who certainly refused it.
Thus, **Yáo** gained the reputation of ceding the **Mandate of Heaven** while in reality not losing it.
If Your Majesty grants the state to me,
I would certainly refuse it—
then Your Majesty would gain the reputation of ceding the state to me,
just as **Yáo** did.
Thus, the king of **Yàn** entrusted the entire state to you,
and you were greatly honored.’"*

**Another account says:**
*"Pān Shòu was a **recluse** (岩穴之士).
The **Yàn** king sent envoys to invite him.
When Pān Shòu saw the **Yàn** king, he said:
‘I fear that you will treat me as **Yì** (益) was treated.
The people feared that **Yì** would seize the **Mandate of Heaven** after **Yǔ** (禹) died,
so **Yǔ’s** descendants attacked **Yì** and established **Kāi** (启).
Now, Your Majesty trusts and loves me,
and you intend to pass the state to me.
All the **Tàizǐ’s** (太子) men have already taken their seals and left,
while none of my men remain in the court.
If Your Majesty, by misfortune, abandons the officials,
I would be like **Yì** as well.’
Thus, the **Yàn** king recalled the seals of office,
and from the rank of *sān bǎi dàn* (三百石) and above,
all officials pledged allegiance to you,
and you were greatly honored.

*"Now, the ruler’s means of self-reflection are the **scholars and followers** (士徒) of the feudal lords,
but today, the scholars and followers of the feudal lords are all factions of private doors.
The ruler’s means of self-examination are the **recluses and hermits** (岩穴之士),
but today, the recluses and hermits are all attendants of private doors.
What is this?
The resources of the state have been seized by you."*

Thus, **Wú Zhāng** said: *"The sovereign should not feign hatred or love for his subjects. If he feigns love, he cannot later hate; if he feigns hatred, he cannot later love."*

The **Yàn king** wished to pass the realm to **Zǐzhī**. When he consulted **Pàn Shòu**, the latter replied: *"Yǔ loved Yì and entrusted the world to him, yet later appointed Shìrén as his minister. When Yǔ grew old and deemed Shìrén unfit to rule, he passed the world to Yì—yet all power remained with Shìrén. Later, Shìrén and his faction attacked Yì and seized the world. Thus, Yǔ’s name passed the world to Yì, but in truth, he allowed Shìrén to take it himself. This shows Yǔ was inferior to Yáo and Shùn."*

Now, Your Majesty wishes to pass the realm to **Zǐzhī**, yet all officials are men of the **Tàizǐ**. This is to name it a transfer, yet in truth, it allows the **Tàizǐ** to take it himself.

The **Yàn king** thus recalled the seals of office, and all officials from the rank of *sān bǎi dàn* and above pledged allegiance to **Zǐzhī**, making him greatly honored.

**Wú Zhāng** then said: *"I have heard of the ancient rites: one does not ride in the same carriage as those of differing attire, nor share a household with those of differing lineage. How much less should the sovereign borrow another’s authority while excluding their influence!"*

**Wú Zhāng** told King **Chuān of Hàn**: *"The sovereign must not feign love for his subjects—once he does, he cannot later hate them. Nor must he feign hatred—once he does, he cannot later love them. If signs of feigned love or hatred appear, flatterers will exploit them to slander or praise, and even a wise sovereign cannot undo the damage, let alone one who relies on sincerity to borrow influence."*

The **Zhào king** strolled in his garden when his attendants presented a **pí** (菟) and a tiger, which they killed and displayed. The tiger’s eyes twitched angrily around its sockets. The king said, *"How detestable! The tiger’s eyes!"* The attendants replied, *"The eyes of **Píngyáng Jūn** are far more detestable than these. Seeing this tiger does no harm, but if one sees **Píngyáng Jūn’s** eyes like this, death will follow."* The next day, **Píngyáng Jūn** heard of this and had the speaker killed, yet the king did not punish him.

When the **Wèi lord** attended court at **Zhōu**, a **Zhōu official** asked his title. He replied, *"The lord of the feudal domains."* The **Zhōu official** rebuked him: *"Feudal lords may not share the title of the Son of Heaven. The title of one who opens and governs lands belongs to the Son of Heaven."* The **Wèi lord** then changed his reply: *"The lord who destroys the feudal domains."* **Kǒng Zǐ** heard of this and said: *"How distant are the restraints of propriety! Even empty titles are not lent to others—how much less real authority!"* The title *"Opens the domains"* does not guarantee the ability to govern them, hence it is called *"empty."*

**Shaking a tree** by plucking each leaf individually is laborious and incomplete, yet striking its trunk causes all leaves to tremble. Striking is to agitate violently. Standing by a precipice and shaking a tree startles the crows, who fly high, and terrifies the fish, which dive deep.

A skilled netter pulls the main rope—if one must pluck each mesh before catching fish, the effort is great and the task difficult. But by pulling the rope, the fish are already ensnared. Thus, officials are the fundamental ropes of the people, and so the sage governs through officials, not the people directly. Governing officials is like pulling the rope; managing the people is like setting the nets.

When saving a fire, ordering officials to carry jars and buckets is the work of one man, but wielding a whip and commanding others is to control thousands. Thus, the sage does not personally attend to minor matters, and the enlightened sovereign does not involve himself in trifles.

When **Zàofù** was plowing, a father and son in a carriage passed by. The horses panicked and refused to move. The son dismounted to lead the horses, and the father and son urged **Zàofù** to help push the carriage. **Zàofù** set aside his tools, loaded them, and took the son’s place in the carriage. Only then did he adjust the reins and whip—yet the horses still panicked. If **Zàofù** could not control them, even if he exerted himself to push the carriage, the horses would still not move.

Now, the sovereign labors in governance, yet entrusts authority to those of virtue and skill. Skill is the reins and whip of the realm. With skill, the sovereign rests in ease while achieving imperial achievements.

The **chuí** (椎) and **duàn** (锻) are used to flatten what is uneven; the **bàng** (榜) and **tíng** (檠) are used to straighten what is crooked. The sage’s laws are like these—used to flatten the uneven and straighten the crooked.

When **Dùnchǐ** served **Qí**, he severed **Mǐn Wáng’s** sinews; when **Lǐ Duì** served **Zhào**, he starved **Zhǔfù** to death. These two rulers could not wield the **chuí-duàn** and **bàng-tíng**, so they died in disgrace and became objects of ridicule.

Thus, it is said: *"When entering **Qí**, one hears only of **Dùnchǐ**, not of the **Qí king**; when entering **Zhào**, one hears only of **Lǐ Duì**, not of the **Zhào king**."* Therefore: *"If the sovereign does not wield skill, his authority is weak, and his ministers usurp his name."*

When **Tián Yīng** served as **Qí’s** chancellor, a man advised the king: *"If Your Majesty does not personally review the annual accounts within days, you cannot discern the officials’ deceit, gains, or losses."* The king agreed. **Tián Yīng** heard this and immediately requested an audience to present the accounts. The king was about to listen when **Tián Yīng** ordered the clerks to prepare the official seals, weights, and measures. The king reviewed them but found the records overwhelming, so he stopped eating. Later, when he resumed his seat, he did not eat again that evening. **Tián Yīng** then said: *"These are matters the ministers dare not neglect day or night. If Your Majesty reviews them in one evening, the ministers will be encouraged to excel."* The king agreed. Soon, however, he fell asleep, and the officials secretly shaved the seals and altered the weights and measures. When the king reviewed them again, chaos began.

**Wǔ Líng Wáng** entrusted governance to **Huì Wén Wáng** and made **Lǐ Duì** chancellor. Since **Wǔ Líng Wáng** did not personally wield the power of life and death, he was held hostage by **Lǐ Duì**.

Five. When **Zī Zhèngzǐ** attempted to pull the carriage up the steep slope of **Gāo Liáng**, he could not manage it. **Zī Zhèngzǐ** then squatted on the yoke and sang, halting those in front and urging those behind, and the carriage ascended.

Had **Zī Zhèngzǐ** lacked the skill to influence others, even if his own strength had been exhausted to the point of death, the carriage would still not have ascended. Yet now, without his body enduring hardship, the carriage ascended because he possessed the skill to influence others.

**Zhào Jiǎn Zhǔ** imposed taxes, and the officials requested permission to adjust their severity. **Zhào Jiǎn Zhǔ** replied: *"Do not make them too light, nor too heavy."* Heavy taxes benefited the ruler above, while light ones favored the people below. The officials, having no private gain, thus remained impartial.

**Bó Yí** suspected and told **Zhào Jiǎn Zhǔ**: *"Your realm is well-fed."* **Zhào Jiǎn Zhǔ**, delighted, asked: *"How so?"* **Bó Yí** answered: *"The royal treasury is depleted above, while the common people suffer hunger below—yet the corrupt officials grow wealthy."*

**Qí Huán Gōng** disguised himself and toured among the people’s homes. He encountered an elderly man who was self-sustaining, and the king inquired as to why. The man replied: *"Your servant has three sons, but our family is too poor to marry them off. My labor has not yet earned enough to repay even a single day’s wages."*

Upon returning, **Qí Huán Gōng** told **Guǎn Zhòng**, who said: *"If the stores are filled with rotting wealth, the people will starve. If the palace harbors unmarried women, the common folk will have none to wed."*

The king replied: *"Well said."* He then decreed that all unmarried women in the palace be sent away and ordered the people: *"Let men take wives at twenty and women be married at fifteen."* Another account states:

**Qí Huán Gōng**, in disguise, traveled among the people. He found a man named **Lùmén Jì**, aged seventy, yet without a wife. The king asked **Guǎn Zhòng**: *"Is there an elderly man without a wife among the people?"* **Guǎn Zhòng** answered: *"There is **Lùmén Jì**, now seventy years old, who has no wife."* The king asked: *"How can we ensure he has one?"* **Guǎn Zhòng** replied: *"I have heard that if the ruler accumulates wealth, the people and subjects will inevitably suffer want below. If the palace harbors unmarried women, there will be elderly men without wives."* The king replied: *"Well said."* He then commanded that all palace women who had not been bedded be married off and ordered: *"Let men take wives at twenty and women be married at fifteen."* Thus, within the palace, no women were left in despair, and among the people, no men were without wives.

**Yán Líng Zhuó Zǐ** rode in a carriage adorned with **Cānglóng** (Azure Dragon) and **Tiǎowén** (Filet) embellishments—meaning it was intricately decorated. The **gōushì** (hooks) at the front were adorned to restrain the horses, while the **cuòchā** (crossbars) and **guànchī** (sleeves) at the rear were decorated with gold. If the horses wanted to advance, the **gōushì** would hold them back; if they wanted to retreat, the **cuòchā** and **guànchī** would be applied, forcing them to veer aside. **Zhòufù** (the charioteer) passed by and wept, saying: *"The governance of ancient rulers was also thus."* Rewards were meant to encourage, yet their brilliance was overshadowed by corruption; punishments were meant to restrain, yet their severity was undermined by praise. The people stood bewildered, not knowing the true intent. When rewards were spoken of, they were tainted by corruption; when punishments were spoken of, they were accompanied by praise. Hence, the people did not understand their purpose.

This was also the reason why **sages** wept. Another account states:

The **延陵卓子** rode the **Chánglóng** (Azure Dragon) chariot alongside the **Zháiwén** (Zhai’s decorated) chariot, with horses bearing Zhai’s markings. At the front were the **cuòshì** (overlapping shackles), and at the rear were sharp **chā** (hooked blades). If the horses wanted to advance, they were pulled back by the **cuòshì**; if they wanted to retreat, they were prodded with the **chā**. The horses could neither push forward nor retreat, so they veered aside and galloped free, whereupon **Zhòufù** (the charioteer) drew his sword from below and severed his own feet.

**Zhòufù** saw this and wept, refusing to eat for the entire day. Then, gazing heavenward, he sighed and said:

*"The whip is meant to urge them forward, yet the **cuòshì** is placed at the front to restrain them; the hook is meant to make them retreat, yet the sharp **chā** is placed at the rear. Now, the ruler rewards his subjects for their **qīngjié** (purity and integrity), yet banishes them for failing to please his favorites; he praises their **gōngzhèng** (impartiality and justice), yet discards them for not obeying his commands. The people stand in fear, bewildered and unsure of their purpose—this is why the **sages** weep.

(*From the **Kǎnfēizǐ** (Han Feizi), Scroll Fourteen, the concluding section of the **Jìng** (Administration) chapter, with textual corrections as noted. Also found in Scroll Fifteen, **Nán Yī** (Difficult Discourse), the thirty-sixth section.*)

---
**Annotations (for reference, though not included in translation):**
- **Chánglóng (苍龙)**: Azure Dragon, a celestial chariot or metaphor for swift, powerful movement.
- **Zháiwén (翟文)**: Likely refers to a chariot or horse with Zhai’s decorative patterns (a historical figure or style).
- **Cuòshì (错饰)**: Overlapping shackles or restraints (from earlier context).
- **Chā (錣)**: Hooked blades (from earlier context).
- **Qīngjié (清洁)**: Purity and integrity (Daoist/Confucian virtue).
- **Gōngzhèng (公正)**: Impartiality and justice (Daoist/Confucian virtue).
- **Sages (圣人)**: Wise rulers or enlightened figures in Daoist/Confucian thought.

The ancient sages in their conduct sometimes acted contrary to reason, and Master Han established principles to challenge them. Duke Wen of Jin was about to engage in battle with the Chu, so he summoned his uncle, Cui Fan, and asked him: *"I am about to fight the Chu, whose forces outnumber ours. What should I do?"*

Cui Fan replied: *"I have heard that elaborate rites are for the virtuous gentleman, yet they do not exhaust loyalty and sincerity. Rites are elaborate and intricate, hence 'elaborate rites.' Only loyalty and sincerity can be cultivated through rites, hence 'they do not exhaust loyalty and sincerity.' In battle, there is no limit to deceit and guile. Without deceit, victory cannot be secured, hence 'there is no limit to deceit and guile.' My lord, you should rely on deceit alone."*

Duke Wen declined Cui Fan’s counsel and instead summoned Yong Ji, asking him the same question: *"I am about to fight the Chu, whose forces outnumber ours. What should I do?"*

Yong Ji answered: *"Burning forests to hunt is a temporary, expedient measure. Though the beasts may be many now, they will not last forever. If you deceive the people, you gain only a fleeting advantage, but in the end, there will be no more trust. If you rely on deceit to gain advantage, you will inevitably corrupt the people with deceit and guile, so there will be no more loyalty and sincerity."*

Duke Wen said: *"Well spoken."* After dismissing Yong Ji, he adopted Cui Fan’s strategy against the Chu, defeating them. Upon his return, he conferred ranks, placing Yong Ji above Cui Fan. The ministers protested: *"In the Battle of Chengpu, it was Cui Fan’s strategy that prevailed. How can you reward the one who spoke but place him below the one who acted?"*

Duke Wen replied: *"This is beyond your understanding. Cui Fan’s words were a measure for the moment, but Yong Ji’s words were for the benefit of ages to come."* Confucius, hearing of this, remarked: *"Duke Wen’s dominion was indeed fitting! He knew both the expedient of the moment and the benefit of ages."*

Some objected: *"Yong Ji’s response did not suit the duke’s question. When answering, one should consider the urgency and scale of the matter. If the question is grand, yet the answer is petty, the wise ruler will not accept it. Here, the duke asked about facing superior forces, yet Yong Ji spoke of future consequences—this was not the proper response."*

*"Moreover, Duke Wen did not grasp the expedient of the moment nor the benefit of ages. If he won the battle, his state would be secure, his army strong, and his reputation established. Even if there were future consequences, nothing surpasses this. How could the benefit of ages not follow? If he lost, his state would perish, his army weaken, and his name fade. The harm of today’s defeat outweighs any concern for the benefit of ages. The benefit of ages depends on today’s victory, and today’s victory depends on deceiving the enemy. Deceiving the enemy secures the benefit of ages. Thus, it is said that Yong Ji’s response did not suit the duke’s question."*

*"Furthermore, Duke Wen did not understand Cui Fan’s words. When Cui Fan spoke of 'no limit to deceit and guile,' he did not mean deceiving one’s own people—he meant deceiving the enemy. The enemy is the state we attack; even if there are future consequences, what harm is there?"*

*"If Duke Wen honored Yong Ji first because of his merit, then the victory over Chu and the defeat of their army were due to Cui Fan’s strategy. If it was for his wise words, then Yong Ji merely warned of future consequences—this was no virtue. Cui Fan, however, combined both. Cui Fan said that elaborate rites for the virtuous gentleman do not exhaust loyalty and sincerity—loyalty is to love one’s subjects, sincerity is to not deceive the people. Who could speak better than this? Yet he also said that deceit and guile are necessary in military strategy. Cui Fan had both wise words and battlefield victory, so he had two merits, while Yong Ji had none and was rewarded first. Was Duke Wen’s dominion not indeed fitting?"*

*"Confucius did not understand how to reward merit. If Confucius did not understand how to reward merit, how could he rashly praise 'fitting'?"*

There was a farmer from Mount Li who encroached on his neighbor’s fields. Shun went to till there, and within a year, the boundaries were properly marked. *Because they emulated each other’s humility, they were corrected.*

There were fishermen along the riverbank who clashed over their fishing platforms—*platforms being the raised areas in the water where fishermen stood*. Shun went fishing there, and within a year, they yielded precedence to the eldest.

There were potters among the Eastern Yi whose wares were poorly made. Shun went to work among them, and within a year, their vessels became sturdy. Confucius sighed and said: *"Plowing, fishing, and pottery-making were not Shun’s duties, nor were they the affairs of a great sage. Yet Shun took them up to rectify their flaws. Truly, Shun was a man of perfect virtue and benevolence! He personally toiled in hardship, and the people followed him—thus, the sage’s transformative power."*

Someone asked the Confucian scholars: *"At that time, where was Yao?"* The man replied: *"Yao was the Son of Heaven."* Then they asked: *"If Confucius considered Yao a sage, how does Shun’s conduct fit?"* If Yao, as a sage on high, governed with clarity, why were there still three men acting wickedly? If Shun’s transformative power was needed, then Yao must have failed in his governance. If Shun was virtuous, he replaced Yao’s clarity; if Yao was a sage, he negated Shun’s transformative power—both cannot coexist.*

There was a man from Chu who sold both shields and spears, praising them thus: *"My shield is unbreakable—nothing can pierce it."* And of his spear: *"My spear is unerring—nothing it strikes can it not pierce."* Someone asked: *"If you strike your shield with your spear, what happens?"* The man could not answer. A shield that cannot be pierced and a spear that can pierce anything cannot coexist in the world.

Now, Yao and Shun cannot both be praised—it is like the paradox of the shield and the spear. Moreover, Shun’s rectification took only a year, yet three years passed, and three men persisted in their flaws. Shun’s life was finite, as was his time. The world’s flaws could not be exhausted, yet he chased the inexhaustible with the finite—his efforts were few.

「Rewards and punishments must be enforced throughout the realm, and the decree was: *"Those who meet the standard are rewarded; those who fail are put to death."* The orders changed from morning to evening, and from evening to morning, and in ten days, the whole land was transformed. Why wait a full year?

Even Shun did not rely on this to persuade Yao to follow his own methods, but instead took personal action—was he not lacking in skill?

Moreover, one who takes bodily suffering as a means to transform the people is a challenge even for Yao and Shun. But a ruler of mediocre virtue finds it easy to indulge in arrogance while in power. If one seeks to govern the realm, abandoning what is easy for mediocre rulers and instead pursuing what is difficult for Yao and Shun, one cannot be said to be fit for politics.

When Guan Zhong was ill, Duke Huan of Qi went to inquire about him and said: *"Comrade Zhong, alas, you have succumbed to your fate. How shall I inform my humble self?"* Guan Zhong replied: *"Without your words, I would have already paid my respects to you. May I suggest that you dismiss the eunuch Ji Dan, remove Yi Ya, and distance yourself from the son of Wei, Kai Fang?"*

Yi Ya prepared the ruler’s favorite flavors; the ruler had never tasted human meat, so Yi Ya steamed his own son’s head and presented it to him. Now, no man fails to love his own child—if he does not love his own child, how could he love his ruler? The ruler expanded his desires and favored his concubines; Ji Dan managed the inner quarters from within the palace. No man fails to love his own body—if he does not love his own body, how could he love his ruler? Kai Fang served his ruler for fifteen years; between Qi and Wei, he could not even spare a few days to return home, abandoning his mother for long periods in office. His mother did not love him—how could he love his ruler?

I have heard it said: *"Falsehoods cannot endure, and hollow pretenses will not last long."* This means that deceitful schemes cannot persist in the long term. I wish you would dismiss these three men.

Guan Zhong died, yet Duke Huan did not act on his advice. When Duke Huan died, worms emerged from his corpse, and he was left unburied. Some said: *"The words Guan Zhong used to admonish Duke Huan were not the speech of a wise man."* The reason to dismiss Ji Dan and Yi Ya was that they did not love their own bodies, but instead catered to the ruler’s desires. If they did not love their own bodies, how could they love the ruler? Then again, if a subject exhausts his efforts to the point of death for his lord, he also does not love his own body. Guan Zhong intended to say that such a subject should not be employed. If one does not love his efforts to the death, how could he love the ruler? This would mean the ruler is dismissing loyal subjects. Moreover, if one judges that a subject does not love his own body to determine whether he does not love his ruler, then it is as if Guan Zhong were using his own inability to die for Prince Jiu to measure Duke Huan’s inability to die for him. Thus, Guan Zhong himself would also fall into the category of those to be dismissed.

The way of an enlightened ruler is not so. Instead, he sets forth what the people desire to elicit their service, hence offering noble titles and wealth to encourage them. He presents what the people abhor to restrain their deceit, hence establishing penalties and punishments to awe them. If rewards are trustworthy and punishments are certain, then the ruler can raise meritorious deeds among his subjects while their deceit is not tolerated by him. Those who have merit are rewarded, and naturally, the deceitful will not find favor. Even if there were a Ji Dan, what could he do to the ruler?

Moreover, a subject exhausts his efforts to the death to bargain with his ruler, while the ruler bestows titles and wealth to bargain in return. The relationship between ruler and subject is not that of father and son, but rather a matter of calculation and exchange. The ruler assesses the subject’s strength, while the subject assesses the ruler’s offerings. If the ruler is just, then the subject will exert his full strength, and deceit will not arise. If the ruler is unjust, then the subject will, on the one hand, obstruct the ruler’s clarity, and on the other, foster his own private interests.

Guan Zhong did not clearly explain this principle to Duke Huan. He merely suggested dismissing the three men, so it is said: *"Guan Zhong lacked foresight."* When Duke Shengzi was besieged at Jinyang, upon breaking the siege, he rewarded five men for their service, with Gao He as the first among them. Zhang Mengtan said: *"In the matter of Jinyang, He had no great merit, yet you reward him first—why?"* Duke Shengzi replied: *"In the matter of Jinyang, my state was in peril, and the altars of the land were nearly destroyed. My ministers all had thoughts of arrogance and disdain, but only He did not abandon the rites of ruler and subject. Therefore, I honored him first."*

Confucius heard of this and said: *"How well Shengzi knows how to reward! Reward one man, and no subject in the land dares to disregard propriety."*

Some said: *"Confucius does not understand how to reward well."* A truly skilled ruler in rewards and punishments ensures that no official dares to encroach upon another’s duties, and no subject dares to disregard propriety. If the ruler establishes his laws above while the subjects harbor no deceitful intentions below, then this can be called skilled in rewards and punishments. If Shengzi had issued no orders and enforced no prohibitions at Jinyang, then Shengzi would have no state, and Jinyang would have no ruler—who then would guard it?

Now, at Jinyang, Shengzi knew that the Zhi clan had surrounded him, that the mortar and hearth had produced tortoises, yet the people had no thoughts of rebellion. This was the virtue of close bonds between ruler and subject. Shengzi possessed the blessing of harmonious ruler-subject relations and held the methods of issuing orders and enforcing prohibitions, yet there were still subjects who were arrogant and disdainful—this was because Shengzi failed in his punishments.

For a subject, if he rides with his lord and achieves merit, he is rewarded. Now, He merely did not indulge in arrogance and disdain, yet Shengzi rewarded him—this was a misapplication of reward.

The lord had no arrogance, and He alone conformed to the proper conduct of a subject—this was not a case of rewarding virtue without merit.

A wise ruler does not bestow rewards upon those without merit, nor does he impose punishments upon those without guilt. Now Shengzi did not execute the arrogant and disdainful subjects, yet he rewarded He, who had no merit—where then was Shengzi’s proper application of reward?

Hence it is said: *"Zhongni did not know how to properly reward."*

During the time of Duke Ping of Jin, he feasted with his ministers. As the wine flowed freely, he sighed and exclaimed: *"There is no greater joy than being a ruler, for one’s words are never defied!"*

Shi Kuang was seated before him, plucked his zither and struck it against the wall, causing the duke to hastily pull aside his robes and avoid it, while the zither shattered against the wall.

The duke asked: *"Who struck the Master’s zither?"*
Shi Kuang replied: *"Just now, there was a petty man speaking at your side, so I struck it."*
The duke said: *"It was I."*
Shi Kuang said: *"Yǎ!"* (a sound of disapproval). *"This is the sigh of lamentation."*

This was not the speech of a true ruler. The duke’s attendants requested to remove him.
The duke said: *"Release him, and let this be a warning to me."*

Some said: *"Duke Ping lost the Way of the ruler, and Shi Kuang lost the conduct of a subject."*

To punish a subject not for his deeds but for his words—this is how a ruler treats his subject. To not reprove a subject’s words when he fails in conduct, but instead to dismiss him when his good counsel is unheeded—this is how a subject treats his ruler.

Now Shi Kuang did not reprove Duke Ping’s conduct, yet he acted as if to punish him like a ruler. He raised the zither and struck it near his body, thus reversing the proper order of ruler and subject and losing the conduct of a subject.

For a subject, if the ruler has faults, he should reprove them. If his reproof is not heeded, he should lightly regard his rank and salary in awaiting the ruler’s fate—this is the conduct and righteousness of a subject.

Now Shi Kuang did not reprove Duke Ping’s faults, yet he raised the zither and struck it near his body. Even a stern father would not do this to his son, yet Shi Kuang did it to his ruler—this was a most rebellious act.

The subject committed a grave rebellion, yet Duke Ping rejoiced and listened to him—this was losing the Way of the ruler.

Hence, Duke Ping’s actions cannot be clearly justified, for they made the ruler overindulgent in listening and unwise to his faults. Shi Kuang’s conduct also cannot be clearly justified, for it allowed a deceitful subject to seize the opportunity of giving counsel and to adorn the way of assassinating the ruler.

It cannot be said that both were clear in their actions—this was a case of two faults.

Hence it is said: *"Duke Ping lost the Way of the ruler, and Shi Kuang also lost the conduct of a subject."*

During the time of Duke Huan of Qi, there was a recluse named Xiaochén Jì. Duke Huan went to see him five times but could not obtain an audience.

Duke Huan said: *"I have heard that men of humble station do not lightly accept rank and salary, nor can they be exchanged for the lord of a thousand chariots. The lord of a thousand chariots, if he does not cherish virtue and righteousness, also cannot be served by men of humble station."*

Thus, he went a fifth time and finally obtained an audience.

Some said: *"Duke Huan did not know virtue and righteousness."*

Now, virtue and righteousness mean to grieve over the harm of the world and to hasten toward the calamities of a single state, without shunning humiliation—this is virtue and righteousness.

Hence, Yi Yin, seeing China in chaos, became a minister to Tang. Bǎilì Xī, seeing Qin in chaos, became a slave to Duke Mu of Qin. Both grieved over the harm of the world and hastened toward the calamities of a single state, without declining humiliation, and so they were called virtuous and righteous.

Now Duke Huan, with the power of a thousand chariots, descended to a commoner, intending to share in worrying over the state of Qi—but Xiaochén did not comply. This showed Xiaochén’s disregard for the people, and to disregard the people cannot be called virtue and righteousness.

Virtue and righteousness mean not to lose the conduct of a subject, nor to disrupt the proper order of ruler and subject. Therefore, within the four boundaries of the state, those who hold the *huì* (ritual assembly) and attend court are called subjects, while officials and ministers who receive their duties are called *míng* (minions).

Now Xiaochén was among the common people, yet he opposed the ruler’s desires—so he cannot be called virtuous and righteous.

Virtue and righteousness were not in him, yet Duke Huan honored him, allowing Xiaochén to have wisdom and ability while evading Duke Huan—this was to conceal himself.

If one cultivates virtue and conceals himself, he should not be employed as a subject—so it is fitting to punish him. If he had no wisdom or ability yet falsely boasted and flaunted himself before Duke Huan, this was deceit, and he should be put to death.

Xiaochén’s actions were either deserving of punishment or of execution. Duke Huan could not grasp the principle of ruler and subject, yet he honored one who should be punished or executed—this was teaching the people of Qi to treat their ruler lightly and disrespectfully, which was not the way to govern.

Hence it is said: *"Duke Huan did not know virtue and righteousness."*

In the campaign of Mí Jīng, Jin attacked Qi. Mí Jīng was the name of a mountain.

Han Xianzi was about to execute a man, and Zhi Xianzi heard of it. He drove his carriage to save the man, but by the time he arrived, he had already been executed.

Zhi Xianzi then said: *"Why not use him as a sacrificial victim?"*
His servant replied: *"You did not dare to save him earlier, why speak now?"*
Zhi Xianzi said: *"How dare I not separate the slander?"*

Some said: *"Zhi Xianzi’s words should not be lightly examined—this was not separating slander."*

The man whom Han Xianzi executed—if he was a criminal, then he could not be saved, for saving a criminal would cause the law to fail, and if the law fails, the state becomes disordered.

If he was not a criminal, then to encourage him to die as a sacrificial victim or to be used as a sacrificial victim was to compound the injustice—he had already been unjustly executed, and now to make him die as a sacrificial victim was another compounded injustice.

To compound injustice is how the people come to harbor resentment, and if the people resent, the state is in danger.

Zhi Xianzi’s words—if they did not bring about danger, then they caused disorder, and they should not be lightly examined.

Moreover, if the man whom Han Xianzi executed was indeed a criminal, why should Zhi Xianzi separate the slander? If he was not a criminal, then he had already been executed, yet Zhi Xianzi arrived—this was allowing Han Xianzi’s slander to become established, while Zhi Xianzi arrived too late.

When Zhi Xianzi said *"to use him as a sacrificial victim,"* this was insufficient to separate the slander of executing a man, yet it created another slander of sacrificial victims.

The sacrificial victim was not deserving of the injustice, yet another slander was added. This was separating slander.

In ancient times, Zhou executed the *páo luò* (searing punishment), and Chonghou and È Lái said to cut off the shins of those who crossed the river—why should Zhi Xianzi separate the slander of Zhou?

This was aiding in falsehood and adding to the slander.

Moreover, the people’s expectations of their ruler are great. If Han Xianzi could not obtain them, then executing him was not just.

The people also hoped that Zhi Xianzi would succeed. They hoped that Zhi Xianzi would correct Han Xianzi’s faults. Now that Zhi Xianzi has failed in both, the people have lost all hope in their ruler. If the ruler hears of evil deeds, what else can they hope for?

Thus it is said: Zhi Xianzi’s words did not separate slander—they only added to it. Moreover, when Zhi Xianzi went to rescue Han Xianzi from guilt, he deemed Han Xianzi wrong, yet he did not explain why he was wrong, but instead urged him to make a sacrificial offering. This only made Han Xianzi unaware of his own faults.

When a ruler causes the people to lose all hope, and a minister makes another unaware of his mistakes, I do not see how Zhi Xianzi separated slander.

Huan Gong released Guan Zhong from his bonds and made him his chancellor. Guan Zhong said: *"Your servant has been honored, yet I remain lowly."* The duke said: *"Let me place you above Gao and Guo."* Guan Zhong said: *"Your servant is now noble, yet I remain poor."* The duke said: *"Let me grant you a household of three returns."* Guan Zhong said: *"Your servant is now wealthy, yet I remain distant."* Thus, the duke established him as Zhongfu.

Xiao Lue said: Guan Zhong believed that being of low status made him unfit to govern the state, so he requested to be placed above Gao and Guo. He believed that poverty made him unfit to enrich the state, so he requested a household of three returns. He believed that being distant made him unfit to govern the close, so he was given the title Zhongfu. Guan Zhong was not greedy—he sought what would benefit governance.

Some said: If one commands a lowly official like Zang Ge to deliver orders to ministers and officials, none dare disobey. This is not because the ministers are lowly while Zang Ge is honored, but because the ruler’s authority is absolute. If Guan Zhong governed without relying on Huan Gong, that would be disloyal—it would mean issuing his own commands. A state without a ruler cannot be governed. If he relied on Huan Gong’s prestige and issued orders in his name, that is how Zang Ge’s authority would be established. Why wait for the honors of Gao, Guo, or Zhongfu before acting?

In the present age, the *dūchén* (imperial overseers) are lowly officials. When they issue decrees, they do not avoid the noble nor seek the lowly. Though these officials are lowly, when they deliver imperial commands, they are not shunned because of their status. Thus, when their actions are just, even a commoner trusts them as much as a minister. When their actions are unjust, even a high official bows to the will of the people.

Now Guan Zhong did not seek to honor the ruler or clarify the laws, but instead pursued greater honors and titles. This was not Guan Zhong’s greed for wealth and rank—it was his ignorance of proper governance. Thus it is said: Guan Zhong had flawed conduct, and Xiao Lue’s praise was excessive.

**Han Xuan Wang** asked Yue Liu: *"I wish to employ both Gong Zhong and Gong Chu—is this acceptable?"* Yue Liu replied: *"In the past, Wei employed both Lou and Di and lost the Western River. Lou was slow, and Di was treacherous. Chu employed both Zhao and Jing and lost Yan and Ying. Zhao and Jing were two powerful clans. Now, if Your Majesty employs both Gong Zhong and Gong Chu, they will surely compete for influence and seek private gain with neighboring states, showing favor to their own interests. This is called ‘external trade’—it will bring misfortune to the state."*

Some said: *"In the past, Qi Jing Gong employed both Guan Zhong and Bao Shu, and Shang Tang employed both Yi Yin and Zhong Hui. If a ruler employs two ministers, it is a cause for concern in the state. Then Huan Gong would not have become hegemon, and Shang Tang would not have become king."*

The ruler who employed Nao Chi was struck dead in the Eastern Temple, and the ruler who employed Li Dui was starved to death. A ruler with skill will not be troubled by employing two ministers, but one without skill will face contention and private dealings, or even tyranny and assassination. Now Yue Liu, lacking skill, advises the ruler to abandon employing two ministers in favor of one. If he does not face the misfortune of losing the Western River, Yan, or Ying, he will surely suffer the fate of death by starvation. Thus, Yue Liu’s counsel was not wise.

**Difficulties of the Second Kind, No. 37**
