Jing Gong passed by Yan Zi’s residence and said: *"Your home is small, near the market. I wish to relocate you to the gardens of Yu Zhang."* Yan Zi bowed twice and declined, saying: *"Your servant is poor and depends on the market for food. I must go there early and late—I cannot live far away."*

Jing Gong laughed and said: *"Your family is familiar with the market—do you know what is expensive and what is cheap?"* At that time, Jing Gong was excessive in his punishments, so Yan Zi replied: *"Sandals are expensive, and shoes are cheap."* Jing Gong asked: *"Why?"* Yan Zi answered: *"Because punishments are frequent."* Jing Gong then recalled an old man who had returned and said in alarm: *"Am I being tyrannical?"* Thus, he reduced punishments by five.

Some said: Yan Zi’s claim that sandals were expensive was not sincere—he only wanted to use this excuse to stop the excessive punishments. He was questioned and responded without deep thought. In a disordered state, the issue is not whether punishments are excessive but whether they are just or unjust. This is the danger of failing to discern proper governance.

Punishments, when just, should not be many; when unjust, they should not be few. Even if few, unjust punishments are still excessive. To argue that punishments are too many without considering their justice is the flaw of lacking skill. Armies defeated in battle are punished by the thousands and hundreds, yet they still do not stop fleeing. If punishments for restoring order are applied as if fearing they are insufficient, yet evil persists, then Yan Zi’s argument—focusing only on the number of punishments—is reckless.

To spare even the lowly grass harms the grain harvest. To show kindness to thieves harms the virtuous. Now, if one relaxes punishments and practices leniency, it benefits the wicked and harms the good. This is not the way to govern.

**Qi Huan Gong**, after drinking to excess, lost his hat and was ashamed, refusing to attend court for three days. Guan Zhong said: *"This is not a disgrace for a ruler. Will Your Majesty not rectify it through governance?"* The duke said: *"I will make it right."* He then opened the granaries, gave alms to the poor, reviewed the prisoners, and pardoned minor offenders. After three days, the people sang:

*"Oh Duke, why not lose your hat again?"*

Some said: Guan Zhong removed Huan Gong’s disgrace from the eyes of the common people, only to create a greater disgrace among the virtuous.

To compel **Huan Gong** to open the granaries and give alms to the poor, to review the prisoners and pardon minor offenders—this is not righteousness, nor can it be used to atone for disgrace. If righteousness must be performed only after the loss of one’s hat, then **Huan Gong**’s righteousness was not for the sake of his hat. Though this may atone for the disgrace of losing the hat in the eyes of the common people, it also leaves the disgrace of unrighteousness upon the virtuous.

Moreover, opening the granaries and giving alms to the poor is to reward those without merit. Reviewing prisoners and pardoning minor offenders is to fail to punish wrongdoers. To reward without merit, the people will grow lazy and hope for favors from above; losing the hat brings alms, so they always hope to lose the hat again. To fail to punish wrongdoers, the people will not be deterred and easily turn to wickedness—this is the root of chaos. How can it atone for disgrace?

In ancient times, **Wen Wang** encroached upon **Meng**, subdued **Jū**, and seized **Fáng**, three acts of aggression that made **Zhòu** detest him. **Wen Wang** then grew fearful and requested to be granted the land of **Luò Xī** and the state of **Chì Rǎng**, both of which were a thousand *lǐ* in extent, in exchange for lifting the punishment of *páo luò* (searing with fire and boiling in oil). All under heaven rejoiced. **Confucius** heard of this and said: *"How virtuous was **Wen Wang**! He lightly gave up a thousand *lǐ* of land to lift the punishment of *páo luò*. How wise was **Wen Wang**! By yielding a thousand *lǐ* of land, he won the hearts of all under heaven."*

Some said: *"Confucius deemed **Wen Wang** wise, but was he not mistaken?"* The wise know the places of calamity and avoid them, so their persons are not harmed. Was it because **Wen Wang** failed to win the hearts of the people that **Zhòu** hated him? Then even if he sought the people’s hearts to lift the hatred, it would still be acceptable. **Zhòu** hated him because he had gained the hearts of the people, yet **Wen Wang** again lightly gave up land to win their hearts—this only deepened their suspicions. It was inevitable that he would be bound and imprisoned in **Yǒu Lǐ**. The elder **Cháng** of **Zhèng** once said: *"To embody the **Dao**, one must act without imposing one’s will."* This was most fitting for **Wen Wang**, as it would not make the people suspect him. Confucius deemed **Wen Wang** wise, but his reasoning did not reach this level.

**Píng Gōng** of **Jìn** asked **Shù Xiàng** (Chuzi): *"In ancient times, **Huan Gong** of **Qí** united the nine **Hú** (clans) and rectified the **Dao** under heaven. Was this the power of the ruler, or the power of his ministers?"* **Shù Xiàng** replied: *"Guan Zhong was skilled at apportioning, while **Bīn Xū Wú** was not skilled at cutting and sewing—his words on increasing or decreasing were like those of a woman’s needlework, mending seams. **Zhì Péng** was skilled at pure embroidery—his words on embellishment were like those of a woman’s embroidery. When the garment was finished, the ruler raised it and wore it—this was also the ministers’ power, what power did the ruler have?"*

**Shì Guān** (the Blind Musician) struck his *qín* (zither) and laughed. The duke asked: *"Master **Shì Guān**, why do you laugh?"* **Shì Guān** replied: *"I laugh at **Shù Xiàng**’s response to Your Majesty. For one who serves as a minister, it is like a cook blending flavors and presenting them to the ruler. If the ruler does not eat them, who dares to force them upon him? Let me illustrate: the ruler is the soil, the ministers are the plants. Only when the soil is fertile can the plants grow luxuriantly—this is the ruler’s power, what power do the ministers have?"*

Some said: *"Both **Shù Xiàng** and **Shì Guān** spoke with partiality. To unite the nine **Hú** and rectify the **Dao** under heaven was the greatest achievement, not solely the ruler’s power nor solely the ministers’ power. In ancient times, **Gōng Zhī Qí** served **Yú**, and **Xī Nég Jiān** served **Cáo**—though both ministers were wise, their words struck true, and their deeds were effective, yet **Yú** and **Cáo** both perished. Why was this? It was because they had ministers but lacked rulers. Moreover, **Jiǎn Shū** served **Gān** and **Gān** perished, then served **Qín** and **Qín** became dominant—this was not because **Jiǎn Shū** was foolish toward **Gān** and wise toward **Qín**, but because **Qín** had a ruler while **Gān** lacked ministers. If it was not the ministers’ power, then why did **Huan Gong** prosper with Guan Zhong but decline with **Zhù Diào**? If it was the ruler’s power, then why did **Zhòu** not bring chaos when **Zhù Diào** served him?"*

In ancient times, **Wén Gōng** of **Jìn** admired the beauty of a **Qí** woman and forgot his duty to return. **Jiù Fàn** (Ziang) and **Qī Fàn** (Ji Fan) repeatedly remonstrated him, so he was able to return to **Jìn**. Thus, **Huan Gong** united the **Hú** through Guan Zhong, while **Wén Gōng** became dominant through **Jiù Fàn** and **Qī Fàn**—yet **Shì Guān** still said it was the power of the ruler. It is not so!

In the case of the five **Bá** (Hegemons), their ability to achieve success and renown under heaven was always due to both the ruler and the ministers having power. Therefore, it is said that both **Shù Xiàng** and **Shì Guān** spoke with partiality.

During the time of **Huan Gong** of **Qí**, a **Jìn**ese guest arrived. The officials requested permission to accord him proper rites, and **Huan Gong** said: *"I have three things to tell **Zhòng Fù** (Guan Zhong)."* The officials repeatedly asked, and each time, he said: *"Tell **Zhòng Fù**."* The jester laughed and said: *"How easy it is to be a ruler! The first thing is **Zhòng Fù**, the second thing is **Zhòng Fù**, and the third is **Zhòng Fù**. **Zhòng Fù** is the name of a musician—*yōu*, *pái yōu*, and *lè* (ease, leisure, and music)."*

**Huan Gong** said: *"I have heard that a ruler labors in seeking the people and rests in employing them. I already found **Zhòng Fù** with great difficulty—after obtaining him, why should it not be easy?"*

Some said: *"What **Huan Gong** answered the jester was not the speech of a ruler. If being a ruler means laboring in seeking the people, then why is seeking the people laborious? **Yī Yǎn** (Yi Yin) considered himself a minister serving **Tāng** (King Tang), while **Bǎi Lǐ Xī** (Bai Li Xi) considered himself a captive serving **Mù Gōng** (Duke Mu). Captives endure humiliation, and ministers bear shame—yet they endure these to serve their sovereigns. This is the urgent concern of the worthy for the world. Therefore, a ruler need only not oppose the worthy, and seeking the worthy is not difficult for a sovereign. Moreover, offices and ranks are established to employ the worthy, and titles and rewards are given to honor merit. When offices and ranks are set forth and titles and rewards displayed, the worthy will come of their own accord—what labor is there for the ruler? Employing the people is also not what brings ease, for a sovereign must employ them with measures and standards, using laws and punishments to guide their actions. If they conform to the law, they proceed; if they do not, they stop."*

If deeds match their words, reward them; if not, punish them.
Use laws and punishments to restrain ministers, and measures and standards to govern the people below.
This is not to be neglected—how then can a ruler find rest?

Seeking the worthy is not labor, employing the people is not ease, yet Duke Huan said, *"I labor in seeking the worthy and find ease in employing the people."* This is incorrect.

Moreover, Duke Huan obtaining **Guan Zhong** was not difficult—Guan Zhong did not die for his former lord before serving Duke Huan, and **Bao Shu** lightly relinquished his office and talents to entrust him. That Duke Huan obtained Guan Zhong was clearly not difficult.

After securing Guan Zhong, why should he have changed so swiftly?

Guan Zhong was not **Zhou Gong Dan**, who ruled as regent for seven years for King Cheng, passing authority when the king came of age—not for the sake of the world, but for his duty.

He who does not seize the throne while the heir lives would never abandon a dead lord to serve his enemy. One who abandons a dead lord to serve his enemy would never hesitate to seize the throne while the heir lives. One who does not hesitate to seize the throne while the heir lives would never hesitate to usurp his lord’s state.

Guan Zhong was a minister of **Prince Jiu**, plotting to assassinate Duke Huan but failing. His lord died, yet he served Duke Huan—his choices cannot be compared to Zhou Gong Dan’s.

If Guan Zhong were a great worthy, he would have been like **Tang** and **Wu**, who were ministers of **Jie** and **Zhou**. When Jie and Zhou rebelled, Tang and Wu overthrew them. Now Duke Huan sits in their place, acting as Jie and Zhou did over Tang and Wu—Duke Huan is in danger.

If Guan Zhong were unworthy, he would have been like **Tian Chang**, a minister of Duke Jian of Qi who killed his lord. Now Duke Huan sits in his place, as Duke Jian did over Tian Chang—Duke Huan is again in danger.

Guan Zhong was clearly not Zhou Gong Dan, yet whether he was like Tang and Wu or Tian Chang remains uncertain. Serving as Tang and Wu brought the peril of Jie and Zhou; serving as Tian Chang brought the chaos of Duke Jian.

After obtaining **Companion Zhong**, where could Duke Huan find safety?

If Duke Huan trusted Guan Zhong and knew he would not deceive him, then Guan Zhong was a minister who would not deceive his lord. Yet even if he knew Guan Zhong would not deceive him, Duke Huan entrusted him so completely that he relied on **Fian Dao** and **Yi Ya**, letting corpses rot unburied while maggots crawled from them—Duke Huan clearly did not know whether his ministers deceived him or not. Yet he trusted them so utterly, hence the saying: *"Duke Huan abandoned his lord."*

**Li Dui** governed Zhongshan, where the **Erling** officials submitted excessive reports of revenue. Li Dui said:
*"Eloquent speech that, when heard, does not conform to righteousness is called **tiao speech**—a hasty, deceitful thing.
Revenue without the benefits of mountains, forests, marshes, or valleys is called **tiao wealth**—a fraudulent abundance.
A gentleman does not listen to **tiao speech** or accept **tiao wealth**. You may now withdraw."*

Some said: *"Master Li framed his words thus: 'Eloquent speech that, when heard, does not conform to righteousness is called **tiao speech**. Eloquence lies in the speaker, persuasion in the listener—speech is not the listener, so eloquence is not persuasion. What is meant by "not conforming to righteousness" does not necessarily mean the listener must be blamed for what he hears.

The listener is either a petty man or a gentleman. A petty man has no righteousness and cannot measure it; a gentleman measures it and would not be persuaded by deceit.
When it is said that eloquent speech, when heard, does not conform to righteousness, it must be insincere speech.
Revenue without effort is not to be trusted—it is excessive, like **li** or **yang**, both meaning "abundant and unnatural."
Though revenue is excessive, what can be done?
If one attends to the harmony of yin and yang in undertakings, plants trees in accordance with the seasons, avoids early or late planting, and prevents cold or heat disasters, then revenue will be abundant.
If one does not let minor tasks hinder major duties, does not let private desires harm public affairs, if men fully engage in farming and women diligently weave, then revenue will be abundant.
If one attends to the principles of breeding livestock and understands the suitability of the land, if the six kinds of livestock thrive and the five grains flourish, then revenue will be abundant.
If one is skilled in strategy and familiar with terrain, if boats, carriages, and machines allow minimal effort for maximal gain, then revenue will be abundant.
If one facilitates commerce at markets and passes, can exchange what one has for what one lacks, if merchants come and foreign goods remain, if expenditures are frugal, clothing and food are moderated, and palaces and tools are sufficiently provisioned without indulging in luxuries, then revenue will be abundant.
All abundance comes from human effort.
If the heavens act rightly—wind and rain timely, cold and warmth balanced—and the land does not expand, yet there are years of plenty, then revenue will be abundant.
Human effort and natural work—both bring abundance, not the benefits of mountains, forests, marshes, or valleys.
To call revenue without such benefits **tiao wealth** is ignorant speech.

**Zhao Jianzi** besieged the outer walls of **Wei**, with **xi shields** and **xi rams** placed where arrows and stones could reach. Jianzi used the rams as pillows and lay down himself.
When the drums sounded, the soldiers did not rise. Jianzi threw down his embrace and said: *"Alas, my soldiers are exhausted!"*
The **Chengren** **Zhu** passed by in his **fu helmet** and replied: *"I have heard that it is not only the soldiers who are exhausted—it is also the lord’s inability to use them.
In the past, our ancestor **Xian Gong** conquered seventeen states and subjugated thirty-eight. He won twelve battles—this was the people’s strength.
When Xian Gong died, Duke Hui ascended, indulging in licentiousness and violence, favoring concubines, and letting Qin encroach until we retreated seventeen *li*—this too was the people’s strength."*

When Duke Hui died, the **Wen Gong** succeeded him. He besieged **Wei** and took **Yè**, and at the **Chéngpú Battle**, he defeated the **Jīng** five times, earning a name of honor throughout the lands—this too was the strength of the people.

Yet there are also rulers who cannot use them—soldiers are not exhausted.

Thus, **Jianzi** removed the **xi shields** and **xi rams**, standing where arrows and stones could reach. When the drums sounded, the soldiers charged, and they won a great victory.

**Jianzi** said: *"Gaining a thousand chariots of armored troops is not as valuable as hearing the words of the **Chéngren Zhu**."*

Some said: *"The **Chéngren** has not yet explained how this is so. He spoke of Duke Hui’s defeat through these people and Wen Gong’s hegemony through them, yet he has not shown how one should use them.

A ruler who can reward generously and punish decisively does not necessarily abandon the shields and stand personally where arrows and stones fly. **Jianzi** cannot yet swiftly remove the **xi shields** either.

**Yán Qīn** was surrounded, yet he lightly risked arrows and stones—this is what a filial son does for his parents. A filial son risks arrows and stones to save his parents because of love. Among a hundred filial sons, only one would dare to risk arrows and stones to save his parents. Among a hundred men, none would risk arrows and stones to save their parents—this is how rare filial piety truly is.

Now, if one assumes that when a man’s own body is in danger, others can still fight, then all the sons of the hundred clans above would be excellent filial sons who love their parents—this is the **Chéngren’s** falsehood. Those who can be filial to their parents are already rare, one in a hundred. How much rarer would they be in serving their lord among the hundred clans?

This is false.

All men love profit and hate harm. If rewards are heavy and trustworthy, men will lightly risk battle. If punishments are severe and certain, men will not retreat even when defeated. Leading troops, hundreds will not fail once. Men universally love profit and fear punishment.

A general who leads troops does not act beyond what all men naturally do, yet he achieves the feat of never losing a man among a hundred—men do not yet know the **Dao** of using troops.

---
*(End of annotation notes; translation continues as per guidelines.)*

The Duke Mu of Lu asked Zǐsī, saying: *"I have heard that the son of the Páng□ clan is unfilial—how was his conduct?"*

Zǐsī replied: *"A noble man honors the worthy to elevate virtue and commends the good to inspire the people. As for faults in conduct, these are matters known to the vulgar. I do not know."*

When Zǐsī left, Zǐfú Lì Bó entered and was asked about the son of the Páng□ clan. Zǐfú Lì Bó answered: *"His faults are three—all things the Duke has never heard of."* From that time onward, the Duke held Zǐsī in high regard while demeaning Zǐfú Lì Bó.

Some said: *"The Lu royal house was seized by the Jì clan for three generations—was this not inevitable? A wise ruler seeks the good and rewards it, seeks deceit and punishes it—this is how one attains it. Those who report the good are those who delight in aligning with the ruler’s virtue. Those who report deceit are those who delight in aligning with the ruler’s malice. This is where praise and reward exert their force. Both reporting the good and reporting deceit should be rewarded. Not reporting deceit is to differ from the ruler while secretly aiding the wicked below—this is where blame and punishment should apply. Now, Zǐsī did not report the faults, yet the Duke honored him, while Lì Bó reported deceit, yet the Duke demeaned him. Men universally prefer honor and despise disgrace, so the Jì clan’s rebellion flourished unchecked, and this was not reported to the Duke—this is how the Lu rulers were overthrown. Moreover, this was the custom of the ruined kings, the reason the people of Qǔ and Lu took pride in it, yet the Duke alone honored it—was this not perverse?"*

When Duke Wén fled, Duke Xiàn sent the palace attendant Bì to attack him at Púchéng. Bì severed his sacrificial vessel, and Wén fled to Zhì. When Duke Huì ascended the throne, he again sent Bì to attack him at Huìdòu, but failed. When Wén returned to the state, Bì sought an audience.

The Duke said: *"At Púchéng, the order was for one night’s stay, yet you arrived immediately. At Huìdòu, the order was for three nights, yet you arrived in one—how could you be so swift?"*

Bì replied: *"The Duke’s command was unchanging: to remove the Duke’s evils. If I could not endure that, what were the people of Pú and Zhì to me? At that time, the Duke was a man of Pú and Zhì, with no subject’s duty toward him—what were they to me? Now that you have ascended the throne, are you not like Pú and Zhì?"*

Moreover, Duke Huán of Qí placed Shègōu as minister to assist Guān Zhòng. The Duke received him. Some said: *"The houses of Qí and Jìn were extinguished—was this not inevitable? Duke Huán could use Guān Zhòng’s merit and forget the grudge of Shègōu. Duke Wén could heed the palace attendant’s words and abandon the crime of severing the sacrificial vessel. Huán and Wén could contain these two men. Later rulers were not as enlightened as these two, and later ministers were not as virtuous as these two. Disloyal ministers served unclear rulers. If the ruler did not know, there were Yàn Cāo and Zǐyú. There were Zǐhǎn and Tián Cháng’s treachery. If the ruler knew, he would use Guān Zhòng or the palace attendant to resolve it. The ruler would certainly not punish them, yet would take credit for the virtue of Huán and Wén—this is to harbor enmity while failing to discern clearly, relying on borrowed resources. If one assumes virtue without caution, even without heirs, is this not enough?"*

Moreover, the palace attendant’s words were merely a pretense of loyalty. When the Duke’s command was unyielding, this was loyalty to the Duke. To serve a later ruler after the Duke’s death without shame and yet still claim loyalty—this is not true loyalty unless one dies for it. Now, Huì died in the morning and served Wén in the evening—how could the palace attendant’s loyalty be measured?

A man set up a hidden trap for Duke Huán, saying: *"One danger, two dangers, three dangers—what are they?"* Duke Huán could not shoot, so he told Guān Zhòng. Guān Zhòng replied: *"The first danger is to favor jesters while distancing the worthy. The second danger is to exile the state’s people to the seas. The third danger is to delay naming a heir until the ruler is old."*

Duke Huán said: *"Good."* Without waiting for an auspicious day, he performed the ancestral rites to install the heir.

Some said: *"Guān Zhòng’s hidden trap was not achieved. The use of ministers does not depend on proximity or distance. Jesters and dwarves are naturally what a ruler enjoys with his guests. To favor jesters while distancing the worthy and call this governance is not truly difficult. To hold power yet fail to use what one possesses, and to not abandon the state when it is in turmoil—this is to restrain a whole state with one man’s strength. One who restrains a whole state with one man’s strength will rarely prevail. A ruler who can illuminate distant deceit and perceive hidden treachery, and whose orders are carried out, even if the ministers are distant as the seas, the state will surely have no internal rebellion. Thus, exiling the state’s people to the seas without rebellion is not truly difficult."*

King Chéng of Chǔ installed Shāng Chén as heir, yet also wished to install Gōngzǐ Zhí. Shāng Chén rebelled and killed Chéng. Gōngzǐ Zhāi, the son of the Zhou heir, and Gōngzǐ Gēn, who was favored, rebelled in the Eastern lands, splitting into two states. These were not the evils of delaying the heir.

If power is not divided, the lesser branches are humble, and favor is without basis, even if one is a high minister, delaying the heir is acceptable. Thus, delaying the heir while the lesser branches do not rebel is not truly difficult.

What is truly difficult is to borrow others’ strength to establish one’s own position without allowing it to harm oneself—this may be called the first danger. To not allow concubines to become two queens—this is the second danger. To not allow favored sons to endanger the legitimate heir, and to listen solely to one minister without daring to corner the ruler—this is what may be called the third danger.

Yè Gōngzǐ Gāo asked Confucius about governance. Confucius said: *"Governance lies in pleasing the near and attracting the distant."* Duke Aí of Lu asked Confucius about governance. Confucius said: *"Governance lies in selecting the worthy."* Duke Jǐng of Qí asked Confucius about governance. Confucius said: *"Governance lies in regulating expenditures."*

When the three rulers left, Zǐgōng asked: *"The three rulers asked the Master about governance, yet the Master’s answers differed—why was this?"*

Confucius said:
*"The city of Yè has a vast capital but a small state, and its people harbor disloyalty in their hearts. Therefore, governance lies in pleasing the near and attracting the distant—this is to teach the people to cherish favors. When governance relies on kindness alone, those without merit receive rewards, while those guilty escape punishment. This is why such laws fail. When laws fail, governance descends into chaos, and chaos leads to the ruin of the state and the people. This is not something one should pursue.

Moreover, if the people harbor disloyalty, it is because the ruler’s wisdom has its limits. If one does not emulate Yè Gōng’s clarity yet still advocates pleasing the near and attracting the distant, it is like abandoning the means by which one could restrain them, instead competing with kindness to win their hearts—this is not the way of one who can wield authority.

Even the virtue of Yáo, the most sagacious of the Six Sovereigns, could not prevent Shùn from seizing power and encompassing all under his rule. If a ruler lacks the means to restrain his subjects yet relies on Shùn’s virtue to retain his people, is this not a lack of means?

A wise ruler detects minor deceits before they grow, so the people dare not hatch grand conspiracies. He punishes small crimes before they fester, so the people dare not commit great rebellions. This is what is meant by addressing difficulties in their earliest forms and great matters in their smallest beginnings.

Now, if those with merit are always rewarded, the rewarded will not feel gratitude toward the ruler—they will see it as the result of their own efforts. If those who commit crimes are always punished, the punished will not harbor resentment toward the ruler—they will see it as the natural consequence of their deeds. When the people recognize that punishments and rewards stem solely from their own actions, they will strive for personal gain rather than accept favors from the ruler. Even the lowest of subjects may possess this understanding. How then can one speak of a people who cherish kindness?

A wise ruler’s subjects face no conflicts of interest. If one advocates pleasing the near and attracting the distant, it is better to abandon such counsel. Duke Aí of Lu had ministers who blocked outsiders from approaching and internally conspired to deceive their ruler, causing the ancestral temples to go unmaintained and the sacrificial rites to be neglected—these were undoubtedly his three ministers. Yet Confucius advised him to *select the worthy*, not to assess their actual deeds, but to choose those whom he personally deemed worthy. If Duke Aí had known of his three ministers’ external obstruction and internal deceit, they would not have lasted a single day in power. Since Duke Aí did not truly *select the worthy* but instead chose those he subjectively deemed worthy, the three ministers were able to hold their positions.

Yān Zǐ Guǎ was deemed worthy by his son but not by Sūn Qīng, and so he met his end in disgrace. Fú Chā was wise in appointing Guān Pí but foolish in trusting Zhū Xú, and thus his kingdom was extinguished by Yùe. Duke Aí of Lu did not need to *know the worthy*—yet Confucius advised him to *select the worthy*, risking the same fate as Fú Chā and Yān Zǐ Guǎ.

A wise ruler does not personally promote his ministers; instead, they advance through mutual recommendation. He does not claim personal virtue; instead, merit is demonstrated through deeds. One observes them in office, tests them in action, and evaluates them by their results. Thus, ministers govern impartially without favoritism, concealing neither the worthy nor advancing the unworthy. If this is so, why should the ruler labor over *selecting the worthy*?

Duke Jǐng of Qí bestowed estates of a hundred chariots, yet Confucius advised him to *regulate expenditures*—this would prevent the ruler from indulging in the extravagance of a Wèi Zhī while alone practicing frugality above, yet still risking poverty. If a ruler feeds his mouth and belly from a thousand *lǐ* of land, even Jié and Zhòu would not dare be extravagant. The state of Qí spanned three thousand *lǐ*, yet Duke Huán of Qí nourished himself from half of it—this was more extravagant than Jié and Zhòu, yet he became the leader of the Five Hegemons, for he understood the balance between excess and restraint.

If a ruler cannot restrain his subjects, it is called *banditry*. If he cannot adorn his inferiors while adorning himself, it is called *chaos*. If he cannot regulate his inferiors while regulating himself, it is called *poverty*.

A wise ruler ensures no one holds private gain, punishing those who deceive to eat. Those who exhaust their strength in service and return profit to the ruler will surely be heard, and those heard will surely be rewarded. Those who act in filth for private gain will surely be known, and those known will surely be punished. Thus, loyal ministers exhaust themselves for the public, commoners exert their strength for their families, and officials strive to the utmost for their superiors, refining themselves in integrity and frugality.

Duke Jǐng’s extravagance was not the state’s calamity. Even if he were as extravagant as this, it would not be the state’s calamity—so long as governance follows these principles. Therefore, advising him to *regulate expenditures* was not his most urgent concern.

With a single response to the three rulers, all three could have been free of calamity—this is what is meant by *knowing the people*. If one knows the people clearly, one restrains them in their earliest transgressions. If one restrains them in their earliest transgressions, deceit will not accumulate. If deceit does not accumulate, there will be no internal conspiracies. If there are no internal conspiracies, public and private interests will be distinct. If public and private interests are distinct, factions will disperse, and the evils of external obstruction and internal conspiracy will vanish.

If one knows the people clearly, one will discern their sincerity. If one discerns their sincerity, punishments and rewards will be clear. If punishments and rewards are clear, the state will not be impoverished. Therefore, it is said: *With one response, the three rulers would have been free of calamity—this is what is meant by knowing the people.*

Hán Zǐ wrote that Duke Huán’s extravagance, though comparable to Jié and Zhòu, did not diminish his virtue, and he immortalized this in his writings as a lesson—how utterly contrary to reason! That he did not meet his end in the Qin prisons may well have been due to this.

Zǐ Chǎn of Zhèng, as he left his palace in the morning, passed through the district of the rope-makers and heard a woman weeping. He patted his charioteer’s hand and listened closely. After a while, he sent an official to arrest and question her, and it was revealed that she had strangled her husband.

The next day, his charioteer asked: *"How did you know?"*
Zǐ Chǎn replied: *"Her voice was one of fear."*

In general, when people are with those they love, they first worry when they fall ill, fear when they face death, and grieve when they are gone. Now, this woman wept not in grief but in fear over one already dead—this is how I knew she had committed a crime.

Some said: *"Zǐ Chǎn’s governance was surely overzealous."*

「If one relies on personal wisdom rather than established laws and measures, one is indeed said to be overzealous. When crime must await the reach of one’s ears and eyes before it is known, then the number of criminals detected in Zhèng would surely be few.

Not entrusting officials with fixed responsibilities—*典* (diǎn) meaning "master" or charge—is to hold them solely accountable for results without regard for proper procedure. Not discerning the systematic governance of *参伍之政* (cānwǔ zhī zhèng), failing to uphold just standards of *度量* (dùliàng), and instead depending on sheer cleverness to outthink and detect crime—is this not a sign of lacking true skill?

Moreover, though the world is vast and human understanding limited, few cannot overcome many, nor can limited wisdom fully grasp all things. Thus, one must govern by aligning with the inherent nature of things—like using the *Dragon* to subdue *scaled insects* or the *Fèng* (phoenix) to control *winged birds*. When the multitude below is vast and the few above are limited, the few cannot fully know the many—so one must rely on people to understand people. Thus, the body need not labor while governance is effective, and wisdom need not be strained to detect crime.

Hence, the saying of the people of Sòng goes: *"If a sparrow flies past Yì (the legendary archer), he will surely hit it,"*—but this is false. Though Yì was skilled in archery, he could not always hit two sparrows in flight, so the claim is exaggerated. If he spread a net across the entire world, then no sparrow would escape. Likewise, detecting crime requires a vast net—one must not miss even a single instance. If one neglects proper governance and instead uses one’s own limited perception as bow and arrow, then Zǐ Chǎn’s judgment was indeed false.

Lǎo Zǐ (Laozi) said: *"To govern the state with cleverness is to be a thief to the state."*—This surely refers to Zǐ Chǎn.

Qín Zhāo Wáng (King Zhao of Qin) asked his attendants: *"Now, how do Hàn (Han) and Wèi (Wei) compare in strength to when they first rose?"*
His attendants replied: *"They are weaker than before."*
*"Now, how do Wèi Qi (魏齐) and Hàn Kāng (韩康) compare to the former Mèngxiáng (孟尝) and Máng Kuàng (芒卯)?"*
They answered: *"They are not as strong."*
The King said: *"Yet Mèngxiáng and Máng Kuàng, though powerful, could not harm me. Why should Hàn and Wèi now?"*
His attendants replied: *"Indeed so."*
Zhōngqī (中期) then plucked his zither and spoke: *"Your Majesty’s assessment of the world is mistaken. When the Six Jìn (六晋) were at their height, Zhī (知) was the strongest, annihilating Fàn (范) and Zhōngxíng (中行), then joining Hàn and Wèi to attack Zhào (赵), drowning their city—only three walls remained above water before it sank. Zhī Bó (知伯) led the charge, with Wèi Xuān Zǐ (魏宣子) driving the chariot and Hàn Kāng Zǐ (韩康子) as the side-riding officer. Zhī Bó said: ‘At first, I did not know water could destroy a state, but now I do.’ He declared, ‘The Fén Shuǐ (汾水) could drown Ānyì (安邑), and the Jiàng Shuǐ (绛水) could drown Píngyáng (平阳).’ Wèi Xuān Zǐ elbowed Hàn Kāng Zǐ, and Kāng Zǐ stepped on Xuān Zǐ’s feet—their elbows and feet met atop the chariot—yet Zhī was divided at Jìnyáng (晋阳) below. Though you are strong now, you are not yet as mighty as Zhī was. Though Hàn and Wèi are weak now, they are not yet as helpless as they were at Jìnyáng below. This is the time when the world is using elbows and feet to harm one another—may Your Majesty not alter this course."*

Some said: *"King Zhao’s inquiry was indeed mistaken, and his attendants’ and Zhōngqī’s responses were also flawed. In governing a state, a wise ruler must rely on its inherent strength. If that strength cannot be harmed, then even the mightiest cannot prevail against it—how much less could Mèngxiáng, Máng Kuàng, Hàn, or Wèi? If that strength can be harmed, then unworthy men like Wèi Qi and Hàn Kāng, or even Hàn and Wèi themselves, could still inflict damage. Thus, whether harm is done or not depends solely on oneself—why ask at all? If one depends on the belief that one cannot be harmed, then what difference does it make whether strong or weak? To ask how one can be harmed while relying on that belief is fortunate indeed."*

Shēn Zǐ (申子) said: *"To lose through calculation and seek through trust is to invite suspicion."*—This surely refers to King Zhao.

Zhī Bó had no restraint—he followed Hàn Kāng and Wèi Xuān, plotting to drown their state with water. This is why Zhī Bó’s state fell and he met his death, his head made into a drinking cup. Now King Zhao asks who is stronger, as if he fears those who might use water against him. Though his attendants are not the sons of Hàn and Wèi, how could there be matters of elbows and feet, yet Zhōngqī says not to change the course? This is empty talk.

Moreover, Zhōngqī’s position as keeper of the zither and bells—if the strings are out of tune and his playing unclear—then his duty is misplaced. This is how Zhōngqī served King Zhao. Though Zhōngqī was skilled at fulfilling his role, he did not satisfy the King, yet he spoke of what he did not truly know—was this not reckless?

His attendants answered that Hàn and Wèi were weaker now and did not match the former Mèngxiáng and Máng Kuàng—this was acceptable. But when they said *"Indeed so"* (甚然), it was flattery.

Shēn Zǐ said: *"Governance should not exceed one’s authority; even if one knows, one should not speak."* Now Zhōngqī, though unknowing, still spoke of it. Hence, it is said that King Zhao’s question was mistaken and his attendants’ and Zhōngqī’s responses were all flawed.

Guān Zǐ (管子) said: *"When one sees what can be praised with clear evidence and what can be punished with visible form, if rewards and punishments are given based on what is seen, even what is unseen will dare not be done. When one sees what can be praised without clear evidence and what can be punished without visible form, if rewards and punishments are not given based on what is seen, and one seeks to govern what is unseen, it cannot be achieved."*

Some said: *"In grand halls and formal courts, all are reverent. In private chambers alone, even trusted ministers like Zēng (曾) and Shǐ (史) may grow lax. Observing what makes people reverent is not a true measure of conduct. Furthermore, rulers above are often adorned by those below. If one’s likes and dislikes are shaped by what is seen, then ministers will surely embellish crimes to deceive their lord. Even a wise ruler cannot penetrate distant deceit—if one waits to observe their polished behavior before determining rewards and punishments, will this not lead to flaws in governance?"*

Guān Zǐ said: *"Speak of governance in the chamber and fill the chamber with its virtue; speak of it in the hall and fill the hall with its virtue—this is how one becomes the sovereign of the world."*

Some said: *"What Guān Zǐ means by ‘speak of governance in the chamber and fill the chamber with its virtue’ is not merely about idle words of feasting and amusement—it must refer to great matters. For a ruler, the greatest matters are not mere cleverness but the laws and measures themselves. Laws are the codified records and statutes set in official halls and disseminated among the people."*

「The *术* (art of governance) is what is kept hidden within one’s breast, used to align with myriad circumstances and subtly control the ministers. Hence, laws must be made manifest, while *术* should remain concealed.

Therefore, a wise sovereign speaks of laws, and even the humblest within the court knows them—it is not just those in the hall who are filled with their virtue. When employing *术*, even those closest and most beloved cannot hear it, so they cannot fill the chamber. Yet Guān Zǐ still says, *"speak of governance in the chamber and fill the chamber, speak of it in the hall and fill the hall,"*—this is not the speech of laws and *术*.

**Difficulties, Chapter Four, Part Thirty-Nine:**
When Sūn Wénzǐ of Wèi visited Lǔ, the Duke of Lǔ ascended the carriage with him. Chū Sūn Mùzǐ hurried forward and said, *"When the feudal lords meet, my lord has never allowed the Duke of Wèi to precede him. Now you do not yield even one rank to my lord, and I do not know what offense you have committed—pray, restrain yourself for a moment."*

Sūn Wénzǐ gave no reply, nor did he show any sign of remorse. Mùzǐ withdrew and told others, *"Sūn Wénzǐ will surely perish. A subject who does not yield to his lord, yet persists in his offense without repentance, is the root of ruin."* Some said, *"When a feudal lord loses the Dao/Way, the feudal lords punish him—thus we have Tāng and Wǔ. When a feudal lord loses the Dao/Way, the great ministers punish him—thus we have Qí and Jìn. If a subject punishes his lord, he will surely perish, then Tāng and Wǔ would not have reigned, and Jìn and Qí would not have been established."*

Sūn Wénzǐ was the lord of Wèi, yet when visiting Lǔ, he did not yield to the Duke of Lǔ as a subject should. The subject’s lord had faults, so the subject had cause to act. He did not perish under a lord who had faults, but under a subject who had cause to act—this is not discerning. Lǔ could not execute the great ministers of Wèi, yet the Duke of Wèi did not know how to punish a subject who was deceitful and unrepentant. Sūn Wénzǐ had these two failings, yet as a subject, he perished—his reason for ruin was that he failed to secure his lord’s favor.

Some said, *"The distribution of virtue and power between lord and subject is fixed. If a subject can seize power from his lord, it is because he has gained the favor of the ministers. Hence, one who takes what is not his rightful share will be stripped by the many, while one who declines what is his rightful share will be granted by the people."* Thus, Jié (Jìè) sought the daughter of Mín Shān, and Zhòu (Jiè) demanded the heart of Bǐ Gān, and the world abandoned them. Tāng changed his name by altering his body, and Wǔ received the mandate from Jìn, yet the whole land submitted. Zhào Jié fled to the mountains, and Tián Wài fell in battle, yet Qí and Jìn followed them. Then, the reason Tāng and Wǔ reigned and Qí and Jìn were established was not because of their lords, but because they gained the favor of the people and then treated their lords as such. Now, without having what is rightfully theirs, yet acting as if they already possess it, is to invert virtue and defy the Dao.

To invert virtue leads to the ruin of their undertakings. To defy the Dao gathers resentment. Yet why does the Duke of Lǔ not perceive these causes of ruin and defeat? Yáng Hǔ of Lǔ desired to attack the Sān Guān (Three Great Houses), but failing, he fled to Qí. Duke Jǐng of Qí honored him. Bào Wénzǐ advised against it, saying, *"Do not honor him. Yáng Hǔ was favored by the Jì Shì (House of Jì) and sought to attack Jì Sūn (House of Jì’s descendants). He coveted their wealth. Now you are wealthier than Jì Sūn, and Qí is greater than Lǔ—Yáng Hǔ will surely employ all his deceit."*

Duke Jǐng then imprisoned Yáng Hǔ. Some said, *"A family with a thousand gold pieces, if its son is not virtuous, shows how urgent the people’s pursuit of profit is. Duke Huán of Qí was the foremost of the Five Hegemons (Wǔ Bā), yet he killed his brother to seize the throne—his profit was immense. Between lord and subject, there is no kinship as between brothers. If one seizes power by force and then rules the myriad chariots, enjoying great profit, then which minister would not be like Yáng Hǔ? Matters succeed through cleverness and subtlety, yet fail through bluntness and clumsiness. The ministers have not yet rebelled because their preparations are not yet complete. All ministers have the heart of Yáng Hǔ, yet the lord above does not know—this is cleverness and subtlety. Yáng Hǔ coveted the world and sought to attack his lord—this is bluntness and clumsiness. If Duke Jǐng did not execute Yáng Hǔ for his clumsiness, then Bào Wénzǐ’s advice was in error. The loyalty or deceit of a subject lies in the actions of the lord. If the lord is wise and stern, the ministers will be loyal; if the lord is weak and lenient, the ministers will be deceitful. To know the subtle is wisdom; to show no mercy is sternness. Yet Duke Jǐng did not know of Qí’s clever ministers, yet he punished Lǔ’s instigator of rebellion—is this not reckless?"*

Some said, *"Loyalty and greed differ in their hearts. Thus, Gōngzǐ Mùyí declined the invitation from Sòng, yet Chǔ Shāng Chén killed his father. Zhèng Qùjì gave his younger brother the throne, yet Duke Huán of Lǔ killed his elder brother. Wú Bó united all under him, yet he used the laws of Duke Huán to rule the people—these were all lacking in steadfastness and integrity. Moreover, if a lord is wise and stern, the ministers will all be loyal. Yáng Hǔ caused rebellion in Lǔ, yet failing, he fled, and Qí did not execute him—this is to condone rebellion. A wise lord knows how to execute Yáng Hǔ to quell the rebellion—this is the wisdom of discerning subtleties. The saying goes: *"The feudal lords treat states as kin."* If the lord is stern, Yáng Hǔ’s crime cannot go unpunished—this is the sternness of showing no mercy. Then, executing Yáng Hǔ is the way to make the ministers loyal. Yet Duke Jǐng did not know of Qí’s clever ministers, and thus he discarded the punishment of a clear instigator of rebellion. He blamed what had not yet happened, yet did not punish the obvious crimes—this is reckless. Now, to execute Lǔ’s instigator of rebellion to threaten the ministers with deceitful hearts, yet also to gain the favor of Jì, Mèng, and Shū Sūn, how is Bào Wénzǐ’s advice reversed?"*

When Duke Zhāo of Zhèng intended to appoint Gāo Qúyí as a minister, Duke Jǐng of Zhèng disliked him, and though he repeatedly advised against it, he was not heeded. When Duke Jǐng ascended the throne, fearing that Gāo Qúyí would kill him, on the day of Xīn Mǎo, he assassinated Duke Jǐng and installed his son Dǎn.

The *junzi* (noble ones) said, *"Duke Jǐng knew what he disliked."* Gōngzǐ Yǔ said, *"Gāo Bó will surely be slaughtered—he has repaid evil to an extreme degree."* Some said, *"Gōngzǐ Yǔ’s words are not also reversed? Duke Jǐng’s meeting with misfortune was because he repaid evil too late. Yet Gāo Bó’s dying late was because he repaid evil to an extreme degree. A wise lord does not harbor lingering anger—he has anger, yet does not act upon it, and then he raises it again. Hence, it is said: *"to harbor lingering anger."*」

To harbor lingering anger makes the minister’s crime seem light in calculation, while acting upon it brings peril to the sovereign. Hence, the **Língtái** (灵台) feast—when Duke Wèi of Wèi was enraged yet did not punish, **Chǔ Shī** (褚师) instigated rebellion. The **yú** (鼋) soup—when the **Zhèng** (郑) lord was enraged yet did not punish, **Zǐ Gōng** (子公) assassinated the sovereign.

The *junzi* (noble ones) act upon knowing what they dislike, not to an extreme degree, but to demonstrate its clarity. Though they did not punish, the consequences led to death, thus proving their knowledge of what was disliked, and showing their lack of **quán** (权). The sovereign is not merely insufficient in foresight to avoid calamity—sometimes he is also unable to decide and act. Duke Zhèng saw the evil and the crime yet did not punish, allowing **Qúyí** (渠弥) to harbor resentment and fear death while gambling on luck, so he was not swift in killing. This was because Duke Zhèng’s retribution for evil was not extreme.

Some said: "The one who repaid evil to an extreme degree was the great punishment for minor crimes. The great punishment for minor crimes was the height of **yù** (狱). The disaster of **yù** (狱) did not lie in the act of punishment itself, but in the multitude of those seeking vengeance. Hence, Duke Lì of Jìn exterminated the **Sān Jiāo** (三郄) yet found joy, while **Zhōngxíng** (中行) rebelled. Duke Zǐdōu of Zhèng killed **Bó Jiān** (伯晅) yet consumed the tripod and sparked disaster. King Fú of Wú punished **Zǐ Xú** (子胥), allowing **Gōu Jiàn** (勾践) of Yùe to rise as a hegemon.

Thus, Duke Wèi’s exile and Zhèng’s **Líng** (灵) assassination were not due to **Chǔ Shī**’s survival or **Gōngfù**’s (公父) lack of punishment, but because they were not yet deserving of anger yet showed signs of it, not yet deserving of punishment yet held the intent of it. Though anger was justified by their crimes, punishment did not align with public sentiment—what harm could come from lingering resentment?

If one has committed a crime before ascending the throne, yet after taking office punishes past offenses, this is why the **Hú** (胡) of Qí perished. The sovereign’s actions, when carried out by his ministers, still leave lingering consequences—how much more so when a minister acts as the sovereign does?

If punishment is unjust yet done with the intent of thoroughness, it is as if he has made an enemy of the world—then even if he meets his end, is it not deserved?

In the time of Duke **Líng** (灵) of Wèi, **Mǐxí** (弥子瑕) was favored in the state. A **zhùróu** (侏儒) who had seen the duke said: *"My dreams are shallow, my lord."* The duke asked: *"What dream?"* The **zhùróu** replied: *"I dreamed of a stove—this means I will see you."* The duke was enraged and said: *"I have heard that a sovereign’s dreams involve the sun. Why would seeing me be a dream of a stove?"* The **zhùróu** said: *"The sun shines upon all under heaven—no single thing can obstruct it. A sovereign governs the entire state—no one can block his vision. Hence, to see a sovereign, one dreams of the sun.

But a stove, when one person warms himself by it, means others cannot see. Is it that one person warms the sovereign? Then though I dream of a stove, is it not also acceptable?"* The duke said: *"Well spoken."* He then removed **Yōng Kuì** (雍鉏) and demoted **Mǐxí**, employing **Sīkōng Gǒu** (司空狗) instead.

Some said: The **zhùróu** cleverly used dreams to convey the sovereign’s **dào** (道). Yet Duke Líng did not understand the **zhùróu**’s words. Removing **Yōng Kuì**, demoting **Mǐxí**, and employing **Sīkōng Gǒu** meant he discarded what he loved and used what he deemed virtuous. Duke Zǐdōu of Zhèng favored **Qìng Jiàn** (庆建) yet obstructed him; Duke Zǐ Kuài of Yàn favored **Zǐ Zhī** (子之) yet obstructed him. To discard what one loves and use what one deems virtuous does not guarantee that one person will not obstruct the sovereign. An unworthy one obstructing the sovereign is insufficient to harm the virtuous—yet now, without further understanding, the sovereign allows a virtuous one to obstruct himself, and thus he must be in danger.

Some said: **Qū Dào** (屈到) loved **jí** (芰), King Wén of Zhōu loved **chángpú** (菖蒲) and **yú** (菹)—these were not proper flavors, yet both sages esteemed them. The flavors they loved were not necessarily beautiful. Duke Líng of Jìn delighted in **Shēn Wúxù** (参无恤), and Duke Zǐ Kuài of Yàn favored **Zǐ Zhī**—these were not proper ministers, yet both sovereigns honored them. Those they deemed virtuous were not necessarily virtuous. If one honors the unvirtuous as if they were virtuous, it is the same as loving and employing them. If one truly knows virtue and employs it, it differs from using what is loved.

Hence, King Zhuāng of Chǔ employed **Shū Sūn** (叔孙) and became hegemon, while **Shāng Xīn** (商辛) employed **Fèi Zhōng** (费仲) yet perished—these were cases of employing the virtuous yet achieving opposite results. Duke Zǐ Kuài of Yàn, though he employed the virtuous yet acted like one who loved, was still far from **Mǐxí**’s (弥子瑕) folly. Thus, the **zhùróu**’s words were not yet fully understood.

The sovereign obstructed yet did not realize his obstruction—only after seeing it did he know. Thus, he demoted the obstructing minister, which was an act of further understanding. It was said: *"If one does not further understand yet allows a virtuous one to obstruct himself, he must be in danger."* Yet now, with further understanding, even if a virtuous one obstructs him, he will not be in danger.

*End of Chapter 40 of the "Difficulties of Power" (难势) from the* **Hán Fēi Zǐ** (韩非子).

Shēn Zǐ said: *"When the flying dragon rides the clouds and the soaring snake glides through the mist, yet when the clouds disperse and the mist clears, the dragon and snake become like the mole cricket and ant—this is because they have lost their means of ascent. Thus, a virtuous person who bows to one unworthy is because his power is light and his position low. An unworthy person who can subdue the virtuous is because his power is heavy and his position exalted. When Yáo was a commoner, he could not govern even three people, yet when Jié was a sovereign, he could bring chaos to all under heaven. From this, I know that power and position are sufficient to rely upon, yet virtue and wisdom are not sufficient to emulate."*

*Now, a weak crossbow may shoot arrows high, but it is because of the wind’s force. A person of unworthy character yet whose commands are obeyed is because he gains support from the multitude. Yáo taught among the commoners yet the people did not listen, but when he ascended the throne and faced south as sovereign over all under heaven, his commands were obeyed, his prohibitions enforced.* From this, it is clear that virtue and wisdom are not sufficient to subdue the people, yet power and position are sufficient to restrain even the virtuous.

In response, Shēn Zǐ said: *"When the flying dragon rides the clouds and the soaring snake glides through the mist, I do not say that the dragon and snake are without relying upon the power of the clouds and mists. However, if one selects the virtuous yet depends solely upon power, is that sufficient for governance? Then I have not yet seen it. If there is the power of the clouds and mists, yet the dragon and snake cannot ride or glide, it is because the dragon and snake lack the proper talent. Now, Jié and Zhòu ascended the throne and ruled all under heaven, wielding the sovereign’s might as their clouds and mists, yet they could not prevent the world from descending into great disorder—this is because Jié and Zhòu lacked the proper talent. Moreover, if one were to use Yáo’s power to govern the world, how would that differ from Jié’s power, which brought chaos? Power is not something that can necessarily compel the virtuous to use it in accordance with their own nature, nor prevent the unworthy from wielding it. If the virtuous use it, the world is governed; if the unworthy use it, the world descends into chaos. Among human inclinations, the virtuous are few and the unworthy are many, yet with the advantage of power and threat, those who use it to bring chaos outnumber those who use it to govern. Power is inherently conducive to governance yet also advantageous for chaos—thus the Book of Zhou states: 'Do not add wings to a tiger, lest it fly into the city and devour its people.' To ride upon the unworthy yet wield power is to add wings to a tiger. Jié and Zhòu built lofty towers and deep pits to exhaust the people’s strength, and used the torturer’s stake to harm their nature—yet Jié and Zhòu could enforce their four cruelties, for the sovereign’s might lent them wings. If Jié and Zhòu were commoners, they would not even have succeeded once before meeting their execution.’*

*Power nourishes the hearts of tigers and wolves, fostering deeds of violence and chaos—this is the world’s greatest affliction. In the matter of governance and chaos, power has its roots and branches, yet to speak only of power’s sufficiency to govern the world reveals a shallow understanding. A fine horse harnessed to a sturdy chariot, if driven by Zāng Huò, would be laughed at, yet if driven by Wáng Liáng, it would cover a thousand li daily. The chariot and horse are not different, yet one achieves a thousand li while the other is mocked—this shows the vast difference between skill and clumsiness. Now, if we take the state’s position as the chariot, power as the horse, decrees as the reins, and punishments as the whip, then Yáo and Shùn driving it would govern the world, while Jié and Zhòu driving it would bring chaos—this also shows the vast difference between virtue and unworthiness. To seek speed and distance yet not know how to entrust the task to Wáng Liáng, to seek profit and remove harm yet not know how to entrust the task to the virtuous and capable—this is the affliction of not recognizing analogies. Yáo and Shùn are indeed the Wáng Liáng of governance among the people."*

The interlocutor replied: *"If one relies solely on power to govern the state, and insists that governance must await the virtuous, that is incorrect. Power is something that, once established, transforms countless things under a single name. Power must arise from natural circumstances, then there is no need to speak of it as something set by man. What I speak of as power is what man establishes. Now, Yáo and Shùn obtained power and governed, while Jié and Zhòu obtained power and brought chaos—I do not say that Yáo and Jié were incorrect. However, this was not something that a single person could establish. If Yáo and Shùn were born into the sovereign’s position, even with ten Jiés and Zhòus they could not bring chaos—that is power governing. If Jié and Zhòu were born into the sovereign’s position, even with ten Yáos and Shùns they could not govern—that is power bringing chaos. Thus it is said: 'When power governs, chaos cannot prevail; when power brings chaos, governance cannot be achieved.' This is the natural power, not something man can establish. If what I speak of is power that man can wield, then virtue has no role to play! How can one prove this is so?"*

The interlocutor said: *"A man who sells both a spear and a shield praises the shield, saying, 'Its strength is unmatched—nothing can pierce it.' Then he praises the spear, saying, 'My spear’s sharpness is unmatched—nothing it cannot pierce.' When asked, 'If you pierce your shield with your spear, how will it fare?' the man cannot respond. The shield that cannot be pierced and the spear that can pierce anything cannot both be true in name. The power that cannot be restrained by virtue yet the Dao that cannot restrain power—this is the paradox of the spear and shield. It is clear that virtue and power cannot coexist. Moreover, Yáo, Shùn, Jié, and Zhòu emerge once in a thousand generations—this is like comparing shoulders and following footsteps in their birth. The world’s governance does not cease in between. What I speak of as power is the middle ground: those who neither reach the heights of Yáo and Shùn nor descend to the depths of Jié and Zhòu. If one adheres to the law and occupies a position of power, governance is achieved; if one abandons the law and forsakes power, chaos ensues."*

Now to abandon power and forsake the law while waiting for Yáo and Shùn—only when they arrive will governance be achieved—this is to have a thousand generations of chaos followed by one of order. To cling to the law and occupy power while waiting for Jié and Zhòu—only when they arrive will chaos ensue—this is to have a thousand generations of order followed by one of ruin. To have order for a thousand years and ruin for one, or ruin for a thousand years and order for one—this is like riding a swift steed and dividing the reins, the distance between the two being vast indeed.

To abandon the hidden and the measured laws, to discard the standards of measure and number, and yet command Xī Zhòng to forge a chariot—he could not even complete a single wheel. Without rewards to encourage and punishments to deter, relinquishing power and abandoning the law, even if Yáo and Shùn were to persuade at the door and debate with the people, they could not govern even three households. Thus, the efficacy of power is clear, yet to say that one must always await the virtuous is also untenable.

Moreover, to fast for a hundred days waiting for roasted meat is to starve the hungry to death. Now to await the virtue of Yáo and Shùn before governing the people of the present age is like waiting for roasted meat to save the starving—this is absurd.

They say a fine horse and sturdy chariot, yet if Zàng Gé drives it, he will be laughed at, but if Wáng Liáng drives it, he can cover a thousand *lǐ* in a day. I do not accept this. To await a man skilled in swimming from Yuè to save a drowning man from Zhōngguó—even if the Yuè man is skilled in swimming, the drowning man will still perish. To await an ancient Wáng Liáng to drive today’s horses is like the Yuè man’s attempt to save the drowning—its futility is clear.

A fine horse and sturdy chariot, with a relay station every fifty *lǐ*, even an average driver can pursue swiftness and achieve distance—thus, a thousand *lǐ* can be covered in a day. Why must one wait for an ancient Wáng Liáng? Moreover, if driving is not done by Wáng Liáng, then it must be ruined by Zàng Gé. If governance is not achieved by Yáo and Shùn, then it must be ruined by Jié and Zhòu. This is not like sweetening honey with *Lái Tíng* locusts—it is like mixing bitter and sweet without measure. This is a tangled web of arguments and lost reasoning, a debate of two extremes that cannot be reconciled—how can it be used to challenge the principles of governance?

The guest’s arguments did not reach this level of discussion.

**Chapter 41: On Debate**
A questioner asked: *"Where does debate arise?"*
The reply was: *"It arises from the ruler’s lack of clarity."*

The questioner said: *"How does the ruler’s lack of clarity give rise to debate?"*
The reply was: *"In a well-governed state, the most honored words are those of the ruler’s commands, and the most suitable affairs are those governed by law. Words cannot hold two honors, nor can laws serve two purposes—thus, those whose speech and actions do not conform to laws and commands must be prohibited. If there are no laws and commands yet one can deceive, adapt, create profit, and manipulate affairs, the ruler must adopt their words and demand their results. If their words are correct, they receive great rewards; if not, they face severe punishment. Thus, the foolish fear punishment and dare not speak, while the wise have no means to argue—this is why there is no debate."*

In chaotic times, it is not so. When the ruler issues orders but the people criticize them through literature, when officials enact laws yet the people privately alter them, the ruler hesitates in enforcing his laws while honoring the clever actions of scholars—this is why the world sees so much literary debate.

Speech and action must be aimed at practical utility. To sharpen an arrow and shoot at random, though it may strike the finest hair, cannot be called skillful shooting—there is no fixed target. Set a target five *cùn* wide at a distance of ten *bù*, and even Yì and Péng Mén cannot guarantee a hit without a fixed rule. Thus, with fixed rules, Yì and Péng Mén’s precision with a five-*cùn* target is skillful, but without them, hitting a hair by random chance is clumsiness.

Now, if the ruler listens to speech and observes actions without measuring them by public utility, even if the words are meticulously examined and the actions steadfast, it is like shooting at random. Thus, in chaotic times, what is considered perceptive is what is difficult to understand, and what is considered eloquent is vast learning. In observing actions, to withdraw from society is seen as virtue, and to defy the ruler is seen as resistance. The ruler delights in clever and perceptive speech, honors virtuous yet defiant actions—thus, those who establish laws and set standards of conduct, distinguishing between just and unjust arguments, are not corrected. Hence, those wearing scholar’s robes and carrying swords are many, while farmers and warriors are few. Empty, rigid words prevail, and constitutional laws fade away.

Thus it is said: *"When the ruler lacks clarity, debate arises."*

**Chapter 42: On Fields**
Xú Qú asked Tián Jiū:
*"I have heard that wise men do not serve beneath their superiors while seeking a ruler, and sages do not rely on merit to approach their superiors. Yet Yángchéng Yìqú, a capable general, was dismissed by Máo Bó. Gōngsūn Dǎn Huí, a sage advisor, was imprisoned in Zhōubù. Why is this?"*

Tián Jiū replied:
*"There is no other reason—it is because the ruler lacks measure and the sovereign lacks strategy."*

Moreover, have you not heard that the楚 general Sòng Jī lost his position, and the魏 advisor Féng Lí led to his state’s ruin? These two rulers were dazzled by empty words and eloquent arguments, failing to test their generals with Máo Bó or consult their advisors with Zhōubù—thus, they suffered the misfortunes of losing their positions and states. From this, it is clear that without testing by Máo Bó or consulting with Zhōubù, how can this be the preparation of an enlightened ruler?

Tángxī Gōng said to Hán Zǐ:
*"I have heard that observing rituals and practicing humility is the way to preserve oneself. Cultivating conduct and withdrawing wisdom is the path to fulfillment. Now, Master, you establish laws and set measures—yet I privately fear this endangers your person and risks your downfall. How can this be proven?"*

What I have heard from the ancient sovereigns’ strategies is this: *"Chǔ did not use Wú Qǐ, yet its chaos was diminished; Qín implemented Shāng Jūn, yet it became wealthy and strong. Though the words of these two men were correct, Wú Qǐ was dismembered and Shāng Jūn was torn apart by chariot—this was due to their misfortune in not encountering the right age or ruler. Encountering fortune cannot be guaranteed, and misfortune cannot be avoided. To abandon the path of preservation and fulfillment while indulging in perilous and reckless actions—I privately see no benefit for Master in this."*

Hanzi said: *"I fully comprehend Master’s words. Indeed, governing the realm and regulating the people’s livelihood is no simple task. Yet the reason we abandon the teachings of the ancient kings and adopt the methods of a lowly subject is that establishing laws and setting measures is the way to benefit the people and facilitate the common folk’s welfare.

Thus, though we risk the misfortune of serving a chaotic ruler or a dim-witted sovereign, we must still strive to harness the people’s resources for their benefit—this is the conduct of benevolence and wisdom. To fear the misfortune of serving a chaotic ruler or a dim-witted sovereign while avoiding the harm of death, yet neglecting the people’s resources for personal gain, is the behavior of greed and vulgarity. I cannot bear to adopt such vulgarity, nor dare I abandon the path of benevolence and wisdom.

The ancient kings had the intention of rewarding loyal ministers, yet in reality, they often harmed them greatly.

**Chapter 43: On Laws**
The questioner asked: *"Between Shen Buhai and Gongsun Yang, whose teachings are more urgent for a state?"*
The reply was: *"This cannot be measured. If a person does not eat for ten days, they will die; if the extreme cold comes and they have no clothing, they will also die. If asked which is more urgent for a person—clothing or food—then both are indispensable, both being means of sustaining life.

Now, Shen Buhai spoke of *shu* (strategic arts), while Gongsun Yang established *fa* (laws).
*Shu* refers to appointing officials based on their suitability, holding them accountable by their titles, wielding the power of life and death, and assessing the abilities of ministers—this is what the sovereign must grasp.
*Fa* refers to enacting statutes in the offices, ensuring punishments resonate in the people’s hearts, rewarding those who diligently uphold the laws, and punishing those who transgress them—this is what ministers must emulate.

Without *shu*, the sovereign falters above; without *fa*, the ministers rebel below. Both are indispensable, both being tools of imperial rule.

The questioner asked: *"What harm comes from relying solely on *shu* without *fa*, or solely on *fa* without *shu*?"*
The reply was: *"Shen Buhai served Duke Zhao of Han. Han was a branch state of Jin. The old laws of Jin had not yet faded, yet Han’s new laws were already emerging. The orders of the former ruler had not been fully implemented when those of the latter were already issued. Shen Buhai did not monopolize the laws nor unify the statutes, so deceit flourished. Thus, if the benefit lay in the old laws and earlier orders, he would advocate for them; if in the new laws and later orders, he would do the same. When the old and new laws conflicted, and the earlier and later orders clashed, even if Shen Buhai repeatedly urged Duke Zhao to employ *shu*, deceitful ministers would still find ways to manipulate words.

Thus, though Han possessed the strength of a great state, over seventy years passed before it achieved hegemony—because though *shu* was applied above, the laws were not meticulously refined in the offices.

When Gongsun Yang governed Qin, he established collective responsibility among officials and demanded accountability, linked households in groups of five for mutual surveillance, and made rewards substantial yet trustworthy while punishments severe and inevitable. Thus, the people exerted themselves tirelessly without rest, faced enemies boldly without retreat, and so Qin became wealthy and its army strong.

Yet without *shu* to detect deceit, its wealth and strength only benefited the ministers. When Duke Xiao of Qin died and King Hui ascended the throne, Zhang Yi betrayed Qin to aid Han and Wei. When King Hui died and King Wu ascended, Gan Mao betrayed Qin to aid Zhou. When King Wu died and King Zhao Shou ascended, Rang Hou attacked Han and Wei before turning east to assault Qi, yet after five years, Qin gained not an inch of territory and instead fortified the enclosure of Tao. Ying Hou attacked Han for eight years before securing the fief of Ru Nan. Since then, all who served Qin were of the sort like Ying and Rang.

Thus, when Qin won battles, its great ministers grew in power; when it gained land, private fiefs were established. The ruler lacked *shu* to discern deceit. Though Gongsun Yang refined his laws tenfold, the ministers merely exploited those resources. Thus, though Qin rode its strength for decades, it never attained imperial rule—because the laws were not meticulously refined in the offices, and the ruler lacked *shu* above.

The questioner asked: *"If the ruler employs Shenzi’s *shu* while officials implement Shangjun’s *fa*, would that suffice?"*
The reply was: *"Shenzi did not fully master *fa*. Shenzi said: ‘Do not overstep one’s office, even if one knows the truth, one must not speak.’ Governing without overstepping one’s office is acceptable, but knowing the truth yet remaining silent is not speaking out against wrongdoing. The sovereign, with the vision of a single state, sees clearly; with the hearing of a single state, hears keenly. If one knows yet remains silent, how can the sovereign rely on such counsel?

Shangjun’s laws stated: ‘For every severed head, one advances a rank; for those desiring office, they must achieve the rank of five *dan* (bushels of grain stipend). For two severed heads, one advances two ranks; for those desiring office, they must achieve the rank of one hundred *dan*. Thus, promotions in office correspond to the merit of severed heads.’ Now suppose there is a law: ‘Those who sever heads shall become physicians or artisans.’ Then houses would remain unfinished and illnesses would not be cured. Artisans are skilled with their hands, while physicians administer medicines. Yet applying the merit of severed heads to these roles is mismatched to their abilities.

Now, governing officials requires wisdom and ability, yet severing heads is a matter of courage and strength. Applying the merit of courage and strength to govern officials of wisdom and ability is like assigning those who sever heads to become physicians or artisans. Thus it is said: *Both Shenzi and Gongsun Yang were imperfect in their mastery of law and strategy.*

**Chapter 44: Resolving Doubts**
In governing broadly, it is not merely about rewards and punishments being just. Rewarding those without merit or punishing the innocent is not true clarity. Rewarding the meritorious and punishing the guilty without error is already within human capability—it is not what generates merit or stops wrongdoing.

Thus, the highest method of preventing deceit is to regulate the heart; the next is to regulate speech; the next is to regulate actions."*

**Chapter 44: Resolving Doubts (continued)**

Nowadays, all speak of honoring the sovereign and securing the state through *virtue/power* (仁義), wisdom, and ability, yet they fail to recognize that those who debase the sovereign and endanger the state also rely on *virtue/power* (仁義), wisdom, and ability. Therefore, the sovereigns of the Dao/Way distance themselves from *virtue/power* (仁義) and wisdom, and instead govern through law.

Thus, their reputation spreads wide and their name commands awe, the people are well-ordered and the state secure—this is because they know the method of using the people. All *strategy* (术) is what the sovereign employs to enforce control; law is what officials model themselves upon. Yet, if even the *ministers* (郎中) of the court daily hear the Dao/Way outside the palace gates, and if commoners within the borders daily see the law, this is not truly difficult.

In ancient times, the House of You had *Huxu* (有扈氏) who lost proper measure, *Gongtou* (讙兜氏) with a lone male heir, the *Sanmiao* (三苗) had *Chengju* (成驹), *Jie* (桀) had *Houchi* (侯侈), *Zhou* (纣) had *Chonghouhu* (崇侯虎), and *Jin* (晋) had *You Shi* (优施). These six were ministers who brought ruin to their states.

To affirm what is false and deny what is true, to harbor internal treachery against their lord, to feign prudence while plotting harm, to distort righteous paths and pursue private gain—these are the actions of ministers who cling to their lord while undermining his governance, who favor inferiors while scheming against superiors. They do not hesitate to employ external forces to disrupt internal affairs. Such ministers, though *ancient sage-kings* (先古圣王) could not endure them, are yet found in this age. Who would dare use them?

As for *Guan Longfeng* (关龙逢), *Wangzi Bidang* (王子比干), *Sui Jiliang* (随季梁), *Chen Xie* (陈泄冷), *Chu Shenxu* (楚申胥), and *Wu Zixu* (吴子胥)—these six ministers all fiercely contended and gave strong counsel to their lords, even when it meant opposing them. If their words were heeded and their deeds followed, they acted like teacher and disciple. But if their words were not heard and their actions not taken, they would assail their lord with reproach, and even lay down their lives to prove their point. Though their families might be destroyed and their limbs scattered, they would not falter. Such ministers, even *ancient sage-kings* (先古圣王) could not tolerate them. In this age, who would dare employ them?

Yet there were *Xu You* (许由), *Xu Ya* (续牙), *Jin Boyang* (晋伯阳), *Qin Dian* (秦颠颉), *Wei Qiao* (卫侨如), *Hufuji* (狐不稽), *Chongming* (重明), *Dong Bulijie* (董不识), *Bian Sui* (卞随), *Wu Guang* (务光), *Bo Yi* (伯夷), and *Shu Qi* (叔齐)—these twelve ministers, though, all saw no joy in personal gain, nor fear in facing danger. Some refused the offer of the world even when given to them, and though they bore the name of suffering humiliation, they did not delight in the pleasures of grain. To see no joy in gain, even if the lord bestows lavish rewards, he cannot sway them. To face danger without fear, even if the lord applies harsh punishments, he cannot intimidate them. These are called *uncommandable* (不令) subjects.

These twelve perished—some buried alive in caves, others withered to death in the wilderness, some starved in mountains and valleys, others drowned in streams and springs. Even *ancient sage-kings* (先古圣王) could not make them their ministers; in this age, who would dare use them?

Now, there were *Zhou Huazhi* (周滑之), *Zheng Wangsun Shen* (郑王孙申), *Chen Gongsun Ning* (陈公孙宁), *Yi Xingfu* (仪行父), *Jing Gan* (荆竿) *Yin Shenhai* (尹申亥), *Sui Shaoshiji Yue* (随少师越), *Zhong Gan* (种干), *Wu Wangsun E* (吴王孙额), *Jin Yang Chengxie* (晋阳成泄), *Qi Shu Tiao* (齐竖刁), and *Yi Ya* (易牙)—these twelve ministers all sought petty gain while disregarding law and *virtue/power* (義). When advancing, they concealed the virtuous and the able, casting shadows upon their lord. When in retreat, they sowed chaos among the officials, bringing calamity. They all aided their lord and shared his desires; if they gained even a single word of favor from the king, they would not hesitate to destroy the state and slaughter the people.

Even if such ministers were under a *sage-king* (圣王), there would still be fear of losing them; how much more so under a feeble and disordered lord, who could avoid such ruin? Those who serve thus are all met with death and the downfall of their states, becoming the laughingstock of the world. Hence, *Zhou Weigong* (周威公) was killed and his state divided in two; *Zheng Ziyang* (郑子阳) was killed and his state split into three; *Chen Linggong* (陈灵公) died at the hands of *Xia Zhengshu* (夏征舒氏); *Criminal King Ling* (刑灵王) perished on the *Ganxi* (乾溪) plain; *Sui* (随) was extinguished by *Jing* (荆); *Wu* (吴) was swallowed by *Yue* (越); *Zhi Bo* (知伯) was annihilated beneath *Jinyang* (晋阳); and *Huan Gong* (桓公) lay unburied for seven days.

Thus it is said: *Flatterers and sycophants are known only by sage-kings, while disordered lords draw them near—hence, they lead to death and the ruin of the state.*

Thus, the sage-kings and enlightened rulers act differently: when recommending officials from within, they do not spare their kin; when recommending officials from without, they do not spare their enemies. If one is worthy, they promote him; if one is unworthy, they punish him. Therefore, the virtuous advance while the wicked retreat, and with a single act they can bring the feudal lords into submission.

Consider their records: Emperor Yao had Dan Zhu, yet Shun had Shang Jun; Emperor Ku had Wu Guan, King Tang had Tai Jia; King Wu had Guan and Cai. These five kings’ condemned subjects were all their own close relatives—yet why did these kings destroy their bodies and break their families? Because they harmed the state, injured the people, and violated the laws.

Observe whom they raised: some were found among the mountains, forests, marshes, and caves; others were in prisons, shackled in chains and ropes; still others were engaged in butchering, chopping firewood, feeding cattle, or slaughtering oxen. Yet the enlightened rulers did not shame them for their lowly status, for their ability could illuminate the laws, benefit the state, and aid the people—thus they were promoted, securing their own safety and exalted reputation.

Disordered lords act otherwise: they do not understand their ministers’ intentions or actions, yet entrust them with the state. Thus, the lesser ones suffer name and territory erosion, while the greater ones see their states perish and their lives end—all because they fail to discern how to use their ministers.
