They have no measure to judge their ministers, only relying on the common people’s praise or blame. If the crowd praises one, they delight in him; if the crowd condemns one, they resent him. Hence, ministers who shatter families and harm their lords, foster internal factions and ally with external cliques to earn favor, secretly conspire to strengthen their own positions, and falsely bestow ranks and riches upon one another—saying:

*"Those who aid me shall I benefit, those who oppose me shall I harm."* The crowd, greedy for gain and intimidated by power, follows their lead. If they truly rejoice, they can profit themselves; if they harbor grudges and anger, they can harm themselves. The people flock to them while the citizens abandon their lords, filling the state with their praise. When this reaches the ruler, he cannot discern their true motives and thus deems them virtuous.

These ministers further employ cunning deceivers: outwardly, they pretend to be favored envoys of the feudal lords, provided with carriages and horses, entrusted with auspicious symbols and seals, bolstered by eloquent arguments, and supplied with wealth and silks. They make the feudal lords indulge in flattery, subtly advancing private agendas under the guise of public discourse.

The envoys they send are rulers of other states, and the advisors they employ are the ruler’s own attendants. The inner and outer factions speak in unison with the ruler’s entourage, using the same insinuating words. The greater ones do not hesitate to humble themselves and exalt their positions to serve them, while the lesser ones offer high ranks and abundant wealth to secure their loyalty.

When wicked ministers hold heavy ranks and gather more factions, they become even more rebellious and persuasive, saying:

*"What the ancients called sage-kings and enlightened rulers were not those who merely upheld seniority or weakness, nor those who followed order. Rather, they were those who fostered factions, gathered allies, and overthrew their lords to seize power for themselves."*

They ask: *"How do you know this?"* And the answer comes:

*"Shun overthrew Yao, Yu overthrew Shun; Tang exiled Jie, King Wu attacked Zhou; these four rulers were ministers who killed their lords, yet the world praises them. Examine their motives: they were driven by greed for power. Assess their actions: they wielded violent and chaotic forces. Yet these four rulers, through their broad strategies, earned the world’s acclaim as great. Through their self-promotion, they were honored as enlightened. Thus, their power was sufficient to dominate the world, their influence enough to overshadow all—so the world followed them."*

They further say:

*"In our time, we hear of Tian Chengzi taking Qi, Si Chengzi Han taking Song, Tai Zai Xin taking Zheng, the Shans taking Zhou, Yi Ya taking Wei, and the three sons of Han—Wei, Zhao, and Jin—dividing Jin. These six men were ministers who killed their lords and seized states. Wicked ministers, hearing this, furrow their brows and raise their ears, believing it true. Thus, they foster internal factions and seize external cliques, gauge the times to act, and with a single stroke take the state. Moreover, within they use factions and threats to kill their lords, and without they exploit the feudal lords’ favor to arrogantly undermine their states. They hide their true intentions, manipulate justice, suppress their lords, and obstruct the officials—such cases are innumerable. Why is this? Because the lords fail to discern how to choose their ministers."*

The records state:

*"Since the reign of King Xuan of Zhou, tens of states have perished, and many ministers have killed their lords to seize power—half the ruin comes from within, half from without. Even those who exhaust all the people’s strength to destroy states and kill their lords are still counted as virtuous rulers. But those who pervert the laws, alter their positions, preserve the people while ruining the state—these are the most grievous afflictions of all.*

For a ruler, if he truly understands his ministers’ words, then even if he indulges in hunting, racing chariots, striking bells, and dancing girls, his state may yet endure. If he does not understand his ministers’ words, then even if he practices frugality, toils diligently, wears coarse cloth, and eats simple food, his state will still perish.

Take the case of Zhao’s ancestral lord, Jing Hou: though he did not cultivate virtue or conduct, he loved unrestrained indulgence, seeking comfort for his body and pleasure for his senses. In winter, he exhausted himself with hunting; in summer, he wallowed in licentiousness. He spent nights reveling, feasting for days without pause, and had wine poured into the mouths of those who could not drink. He beheaded those who failed to bow properly or respond with reverence. Yet, despite such excessive living and wanton punishment, Jing Hou ruled his state for decades, his armies never faltered against enemy states, his lands never diminished by neighboring powers. Within, there was no disorder among the sovereign and his hundred officials; without, no troubles from the feudal lords or neighboring states. He understood how to employ his ministers.*

The lord of Yan, Zi Kuai, a descendant of Shao Gong Si, controlled thousands of square miles of land and commanded tens of thousands of soldiers. Yet he did not seek the pleasures of his children, nor did he listen to the sounds of bells and chimes. Within, he did not indulge in filth or pollute his halls and pavilions; without, he did not exhaust himself with hunting or falconry. Instead, he personally worked the unharvested fields to improve the irrigation. His labor for the people and his care for the world were so profound that even the ancient sage-kings and enlightened rulers could not match his diligence."*

Yet Zi Kuai died and his state perished, seized by his son, and the world mocked him. Why was this so? Because he did not understand how to employ his ministers.

Thus it is said: There are five forms of deceit among ministers, yet the ruler does not know them. For those who rule, there are those who lavishly spend wealth and bribes to gain favor, those who indulge in feasts and rewards to sway the masses, those who cultivate factions and flatter clever scholars to arrogantly assert themselves, those who grant pardons and absolve crimes to wield their authority, and those who flatter the common people with extravagant speech and grand attire to dazzle their senses. These five are what the enlightened ruler suspects, and what the sage sovereign prohibits.

Abandoning these five, then deceitful schemers dare not stand respectfully to speak, and those whose words exceed their deeds—those who do not conform to the law—dare not falsely claim loyalty to discuss matters. Thus, the ministers, when at rest, cultivate their virtue, and when in action, fulfill their duties. Without the ruler’s command, they dare not act rashly in speech or falsely in deed. This is how the sage-king shepherds his ministers.

The sage sovereign and enlightened ruler do not probe their ministers with suspicious objects. If there is no retraction when suspicion is raised, such cases are rare in the world.

Thus it is said: A concubine may have a son resembling the heir, a secondary wife may have a concubine resembling the primary wife, a courtier may resemble the chancellor, and a minister may be favored like the ruler himself. These four are the dangers of a state.

Thus it is said: When inner favorites rival the queen, when outer favorites rival the prime minister, when illegitimate sons rival the heir, and when great ministers rival the ruler, these are the paths to chaos. Therefore, the *Zhou Records* states: Do not exalt concubines while belittling wives, do not exalt illegitimate sons while belittling the heir, do not exalt mediocre ministers while equating them to high-ranking officials, nor exalt great ministers to rival their ruler. If these four parallels are broken, then the ruler has no schemes and the subjects have no strange notions. If these four parallels are not broken, then the ruler’s person and state will perish.

The forty-fifth chapter of *Guai Shi* states: The three principles by which the Sage governs are: *li* (benefit), *wei* (authority), and *ming* (reputation). Benefit is what gains the people; authority is what enforces commands; reputation is the shared path of ruler and subjects. Without these three, even the most urgent matters may be neglected.

Now, though benefit exists, the people are not transformed; though authority exists, the subjects do not obey; though offices have laws, governance does not align with reputation. Though these three exist, why does the world alternate between order and chaos?

The ruler’s cherished rewards contradict what he uses to govern. Establishing titles is meant to distinguish rank, yet those who slight their superiors and avoid audiences are called virtuous. Authority and benefit are meant to enforce commands, yet those who disregard benefit and scorn authority are called weighty. Laws are meant to govern, yet those who disregard laws for private gain are called loyal. Offices and ranks are meant to encourage the people, yet those who seek fame and avoid office are called heroic. Punishments are meant to wield authority, yet those who scorn laws and avoid capital crimes are called brave.

The people’s obsession with reputation is extreme, and their pursuit of benefit is thus—how could those who hunger and suffer not endure hardship to vie for reputation in the world? Therefore, the reason the world is disordered is not the fault of the subjects; the ruler has lost his Way.

He constantly values what brings chaos while devaluing what brings order. Thus, the desires of the subjects are always contrary to what the ruler uses to govern.

Now, if a subject obeys his ruler, the ruler’s priority is clear. Yet those who are sincere, upright, and single-hearted are called destitute. Those who uphold the law, obey commands, and judge carefully are called foolish. Those who respect their ruler and fear punishment are called cowardly. Those who speak appropriately and act moderately are called unworthy. Those without private schemes or secret learning, who listen to officials and follow instruction, are called vulgar. Difficulty in granting is called upright. Difficulty in giving is called virtuous.

«Difficulty in restraint is called *Qí* [齐]. To have a decree yet not listen or comply is called *yǒng* [勇]. To lack profit for one’s ruler is called *yuàn* [愿].

To have few desires and broad benevolence, practicing *virtue/power* [德] in moderation, is called *rén* [仁]. To be grave, dignified, and self-respecting is called *zhǎngzhě* [长者].

To study privately and form groups is called *shītú* [师徒]. To dwell in quietude and repose is called *yǒusī* [有思]. To forsake *virtue/power* [仁] and pursue profit is called *jí* [疾].

To be treacherous, impulsive, and fickle is called *zhì* [智]. To act for others before oneself, to be widely loved and called *shèng* [圣], and to embrace all under Heaven with universal compassion is called *shèng*.

To speak of the great principles yet be impractical, and to act contrary to the world, is called *dàrén* [大人]. To hold low rank yet remain unyielding to one’s ruler is called *jié* [杰].

If those below gradually act thus—within, they disturb the people; without, they cause inconvenience. The ruler ought to restrain their desires and erase their traces, yet does not. Instead, he honors them, thus teaching those below to disrupt and those above to be misled for the sake of harmony.

What governs the realm is *punishment* [刑罚], yet those who practice private *righteousness/virtue* [义] are now honored. The state’s stability depends on *tranquility and order* [安静], yet those who are impulsive, treacherous, and flatterers are entrusted.

Within the four borders, obedience stems from *trust and virtue/power* [信与德], yet those who are deceitful, scheming, and subvert are employed. Decrees are enacted, and authority is established through *reverence, frugality, and listening to one’s ruler* [恭俭听上], yet those who dwell in rigid, unworldly ways are exalted.

The granaries are filled through the fundamental labor of *farming* [耕农], yet those who engage in *luxurious embroidery and trivial scribblings* [綦组锦绣刻书] are deemed prosperous. Renown is achieved, and cities are fortified through *warriors* [战士], yet those who are orphaned and starving beg at the Way’s side, while idle drinkers and sycophants ride in carriages and wear silk.

*Rewards and stipends* [赏禄] are meant to exhaust the people’s strength and ease their deaths, yet those who conquer and take by force labor without receiving due reward, while those who practice divination, read omens, and engage in petty fortune-telling are granted lavish praise.

The ruler wields the *measures and standards* [度量] to monopolize the power of life and death, yet those who uphold the measures and standards wish to serve with loyalty yet are not permitted to be seen, while those who employ clever speech and profit-driven arguments to deceive the world are repeatedly favored.

To adhere to the law with direct speech, to ensure punishments correspond to crimes, to follow the *plumb line and ink line* [绳墨], and to exterminate the wicked—this is how the ruler governs, yet such officials are increasingly distanced.

Those who flatter, curry favor, and follow desires to endanger the world are kept close. All taxes and labor are exacted to prepare for difficulties and stock the granaries, yet soldiers flee their duties, conceal their service, and take refuge under powerful patrons to avoid *corvée taxes* [傜赋], numbering in the tens of thousands of whom the ruler knows nothing.

Indeed, the fields and houses are granted to *encourage warriors and soldiers* [战士卒], yet those who are dismembered and left to rot in the wild have no home to shelter them, and their bodies die while their lands are seized.

Meanwhile, concubines and favored women, great ministers and courtiers without merit, select houses to dwell in and fields to eat from.

Rewards and profits originate solely from the ruler, whom they monopolize, yet warriors of integrity gain no office, while idle officials are held in esteem. If the ruler teaches thus, how can rank remain noble? How can position remain secure?

Those who hold low rank yet scorn the law are those who do not follow decrees, harbor *doubt* [二心], and lack private study, yet rebel against the world. If the ruler does not restrain their actions or dismantle their factions, but instead honors them, the officials are at fault.

The reason *shame and propriety* [廉耻] were established was to bind those below. Now, scholars and officials do not blush at filth or disgrace yet seek office, and women of private virtue enter official doors without proper order.

Honorable titles and rewards are meant to be grave, yet warriors who fight with merit are poor and lowly, while sycophants and idle favorites receive promotions.

*Sincere names and trustworthy reputation* [名号诚信] are meant to spread authority, yet the ruler conceals and obstructs them.

Close associates and favored women move freely together, and ministers control appointments and transfers of others. The officials are at fault.

Great ministers and officials conspire with those below, comparing strategies even if they do not act according to the law. Though their *virtue/power and profit* [威利] lie with the people, the ruler is debased while the ministers grow heavy.

Indeed, the purpose of establishing laws and decrees is to abolish private schemes. When laws and decrees are enforced, private ways are abolished. Private schemes are the source of disorder, yet scholars have *doubt* [二心], study privately in secluded places, harbor deep schemes, and either oppose the world or mislead those below—some grandly, some subtly.

If the ruler does not restrain them but instead honors those who transform *names into reality* [以名化之以实], this is how the idle gain prominence and the unworthy grow rich.

Thus, scholars with *doubt* [二心] will inevitably harbor deep schemes, feign knowledge, and slander laws and decrees to seek their own ends, opposing the world.

All who disrupt the ruler and rebel against the world are scholars with *doubt* [二心]. Therefore, the original saying declares: What brings order is the law; what brings chaos is private scheming. When the law is established, none can act privately.

Thus it is said: Those who follow the *Dao/Way* [道] of private scheming bring chaos; those who follow the *Dao/Way* [道] of the law bring order. If the ruler lacks the *Dao/Way* [道], then the wise have private arguments, and the virtuous have private intentions.

When the ruler has private favors, those below have private desires. Groups of *sages and clever schemers* [圣智] arise, crafting words and arguments to oppose the law and present them to the ruler. If the ruler does not restrain them but instead honors them, this teaches those below not to listen to the ruler or follow the law.

Thus, the virtuous gain renown yet remain distant, while the wicked rely on rewards to grow rich. The virtuous gain renown yet remain distant, while the wicked rely on rewards to grow rich—this is how the ruler is overcome by those below.»

---
(*Annotations for overlapping terms preserved as per prior context: 道 [Dao/Way], 氣 [qi] omitted, 德 [virtue/power] contextualized where relevant. Technical terms like 刑罚 [punishment], 傜赋 [corvée taxes], 绳墨 [plumb line and ink line—symbolizing strict standards], and 圣智 [sages and clever schemers] retained as-is.*)

「Thus, the ruler’s authority is undermined by those below, and the state falls into chaos.

**Chapter Forty-Six: *Six Reversals* (六反)**
*(From *Hanfeizi*, Volume Eighteen)*

---
*(Editorial notes omitted; text follows corrected readings as specified.)*

---
**Original Context (for continuity):**
The ruler who fails to govern with strict standards and just punishments invites rebellion. When clever schemers manipulate the ruler, the virtuous withdraw, the wicked prosper, and the state collapses—**this is how the ruler is overcome by those below.**»

「To fear death is difficult, yet those who submit to defeat are called *honorable* by the world as "men who cherish life." Those who study the Dao and establish doctrines are *renegades* who abandon the law, yet the world honors them as "scholars of literature." Those who wander and live in luxury, seeking food, are *parasites* of the people, yet the world honors them as "men of talent." Those who twist their words to gain knowledge are *deceitful*, yet the world honors them as "men of eloquence and wisdom." Those who wield blades to assault and kill are *brutish and reckless*, yet the world honors them as "men of daring valor." Those who shelter criminals and harbor deceit are *deserving of death*, yet the world honors them as "men of integrity."

These six types of people are those whom the world praises.

Yet those who rush into danger and die for their convictions—*men of righteous resolve*—the world scorns them, calling them "fools who lack calculation." Those who hear little but follow orders, upholding the law, are *law-abiding* people, yet the world scorns them as "crude and unsophisticated." Those who labor with strength to earn their food are *productive* people, yet the world scorns them as "men of limited ability." Those who are upright and pure in conduct, yet live in humble circumstances, are *honest and thrifty*, yet the world scorns them as "fools in appearance." Those who value life and ponder matters carefully are *respectful* of their superiors, yet the world scorns them as "cowardly and intimidated." Those who thwart criminals and suppress deceit are *wise* in serving their superiors, yet the world scorns them as "men who provoke slander."

These six types of people are those whom the world condemns.

The six *deceitful and harmful* types bring no benefit, yet the world praises them as such. The six *virtuous and useful* types contribute to wealth and strength, yet the world condemns them as it does. This is what is meant by *Six Reversals*.

Commoners follow private gain and are praised accordingly; rulers heed empty reputation and honor them in turn. Where honor is bestowed, advantage follows. The people follow private harm and denounce them; rulers are ensnared by common opinion and degrade them. Where degradation is imposed, harm follows.

Thus, titles and rewards are given to those who act from private malice and deserve punishment, while condemnation and harm are inflicted upon those who act from public virtue and should be rewarded. Seeking to enrich and strengthen the state in this way is impossible.

The ancients had a saying: "To govern is like washing hair—though some hair may be discarded, the act must be performed." They loved the expense of discarding hair but forgot the benefit of long hair, not understanding the principle of *balancing priorities*.」

The **plucking of boils** is painful, and **taking medicine** is bitter. Yet, because of the weariness from suffering, if one does not pluck the boils nor take the medicine, the body will not recover and the disease will not cease.

Now, when **superiors and inferiors** interact, there is no **filial affection** as between father and son, yet they wish to use **moral conduct** to restrain inferiors. This will inevitably lead to **obstruction** in their relations.

Moreover, when **parents** regard their **children**, they rejoice if a son is born and kill the daughter if one is born. These both come from the parents’ embrace, yet the son receives praise while the daughter is slain—because they consider the future **convenience** and calculate **long-term advantage**. Thus, even **parents** treat their children with **self-interested calculation**, yet **clay figures** have no filial affection?

Now, scholars in discussing **rulers** all abandon the **heart of seeking advantage** and advocate the **Dao of mutual love**, demanding that rulers exceed **parents’ love** for their children. This is a **misunderstanding of the principles of恩 (benevolence) and诈 (strategic deception)**, hence a **slanderous accusation**. Therefore, **enlightened rulers** do not accept it.

The **Sage’s governance** relies on **clear laws and prohibitions**. If laws and prohibitions are manifest, then **officials and institutions** will be established. If **rewards and punishments** are not **partial**, then the people will **comply**.

When **officials govern well**, the state grows **wealthy**, and wealth brings **strong armies**, thus completing the **achievements of a霸王 (Hegemon-King)**. The **Hegemon-King** is the **greatest advantage** for a ruler. When a ruler **holds this advantage** to govern, his **appointment of officials** is based on **merit**, and his **rewards and punishments** are without **partiality**.

If the people **understand this clearly** and **exert their utmost strength** even unto death, then **military victories and noble titles** can be secured, and when **titles and wealth** are attained, the **pursuit of prosperity and honor** is fulfilled. **Prosperity and honor** are the **greatest advantages** for subjects. When subjects **hold this advantage** in their endeavors, they **face dangers even unto death**, their **strength is fully expended**, and they **do not harbor hopes of personal gain**.

This is why it is said: **"If the ruler is not仁 (benevolent), the subject is not忠 (loyal), then one cannot achieve the Hegemon-King’s glory."**

**Deceit must be known** to **prepare defenses**, and **must be punished** to **stop its spread**. If it is **unknown**, then deceit **flourishes**; if it is **not punished**, then deceit **persists**.

If **light valuables** are displayed in **secluded places**, even **Zengzi and Shiyin** might **doubt them**. If **a hundred gold pieces** are hung in the marketplace, even **great thieves** will not take them. If deceit is **unknown**, then **Zengzi and Shiyin** might **doubt the valuables** in secluded places; if it is **known**, then **great thieves** will not take the gold hung in the marketplace.

Therefore, in **enlightened governance**, the ruler **multiplies vigilance** and **heightens penalties**, ensuring the people **comply with laws** rather than **halt from廉 (moral scruples)**.

**Mothers love their children more than fathers**, and **fathers’ commands** carry **ten times the weight** of **mothers’ affection**. **Officials’ authority** over the people **exceeds even that of fathers**, for their commands are **a million times stronger**.

**Mothers accumulate affection** while their **commands are exhausted**; **officials employ威严 (awe and majesty)**, and the people **obey**. Thus, the **strategies of威 (awe) and爱 (affection)** can be **resolved** accordingly.

Moreover, when **parents** seek **advantages** for their children, they **act to ensure their safety and prosperity**, and **guide them to avoid crimes**. When **rulers** regard the people, they **demand their deaths** in times of **difficulty** and **exhaust their labor** for **peace and stability**.

**Parents** use **deep affection** to **bind their children** to **safety and prosperity**, yet the children **disobey**. **Rulers** use **no affection** but **demand the people’s deaths and labor**, yet their **commands are followed**. **Enlightened rulers** understand this, so they **do not cultivate benevolent affection** but **augment the power of awe and majesty**.

Thus, when **mothers’ love is excessive**, their children **often fail**; this is **excessive affection**. **Fathers’ love is sparse**, yet their **discipline with penal rods** produces **many virtuous children**; this is **strict governance**.

Now, when **household members** manage their **property**, they **endure hunger and cold** together, **strengthen themselves** through **bitter toil**. Even if they **encounter military conscription or famine**, those who enjoy **warm clothes and fine food** are **certainly from a harmonious household**. If they **share concern in clothing and food**, and **extend kindness in leisure and joy**, yet in times of **famine or disaster** they **sell wives and children**, these are **certainly from a broken household**.

Thus, **law as the Dao** brings **immediate suffering** yet **long-term advantage**. **Benevolence as the Dao** offers **temporary pleasure** but **ultimate ruin**. The **Sage weighs the light and heavy**, **discards minor advantages**, and **pursues the greatest benefit**—therefore, he **applies the law’s discipline** while **abandoning the people’s reliance on benevolence**.

Scholars’ words all speak of **"light punishments"**, but this is the **method of chaos and destruction**. The **essential nature of rewards and punishments** is to **encourage good and restrain evil**.

**Generous rewards** make what is **desired swiftly attainable**, and **severe punishments** make what is **prohibited urgently avoided**. Those who **seek advantage** must **hate harm**, for harm is the **opposite of advantage**, and if it **contradicts what is desired**, how can they **not hate it**?

Those who **seek governance** must **hate disorder**, for disorder is the **opposite of governance**. Therefore, the more one **desires strict governance**, the **greater their rewards must be**, and the more one **hates disorder**, the **heavier their punishments must be**.

Now, if one **adheres to light punishments**, it means they **do not deeply hate disorder** nor **strongly desire governance**. This is not only **lacking in strategy** but also **lacking in execution**. Thus, the **distinction between贤 (virtuous) and不肖 (unworthy)**, between **wise and foolish**, lies in the **lightness or severity of rewards and punishments**.

As for **heavy punishments**, they are **not intended for criminals alone**. The **enlightened ruler’s laws** are **measured and just**. To **punish bandits** is not to **punish what is just**; to **punish what is just** is to **punish the corrupt and compliant**. Therefore, it is said: **"To impose heavy penalties on deceit and stop all evil within the borders"**—this is the **true meaning of governance**.

**Heavy punishments** deter **thieves and bandits**, while **fearful obedience** comes from **good citizens**. Why should one **hesitate** about **heavy punishments** for governance?

As for **generous rewards**, they are **not only for merit** but also to **encourage the entire realm**. Those who **receive rewards** delight in their **benefits**, and those who **have not yet received them** admire the **achievements**. Thus, **rewarding one person’s merit** **inspires the masses within the borders**—why should one **hesitate** about **generous rewards**?

Now, those who **do not understand governance** all say that **heavy punishments harm the people**, and **light punishments can stop deceit**—why impose **heavy penalties** at all? This is **not examining governance closely**.

If **heavy punishments** are used to **stop deceit**, it is **not certain** that **light punishments** will **stop it**. If **light punishments** are used to **stop deceit**, then **heavy punishments** will **definitely stop it**.

Thus, when **heavy punishments** are **established beforehand**, deceit **is entirely halted**—how can they **harm the people**? What is called **"heavy punishments"** is that **the gains from deceit are slight**, yet **what the ruler imposes is severe**.

The people do not risk great crimes for small gains, so deceit is **definitely** halted.

What is called **"light punishments"** is that **the gains from deceit are great**, yet **what the ruler imposes is slight**.

The people covet its gains and scorn its crimes, so deceit **does not cease**.

Thus, the ancient sages had a saying: *"One does not stumble upon mountains, but upon molehills. Mountains are great, so people avoid them; molehills are small, so people easily step on them."*

Now, if punishments are light, the people will **definitely** disregard them.

To commit crimes and yet not punish them is to **drive the state into ruin and abandon it**. To punish those who commit crimes is to **set traps for the people**.

Thus, **"light punishments"** are the people’s molehills. If one does not **establish light punishments as the Dao/Way for the people**, then it is either **to create traps for them**—and this can be called **harming the people**.

Now, scholars all speak of the praises in the classics and strategies, yet they do not **examine the actual circumstances of the present age**. They say: *"If the ruler does not cherish the people, and taxes and levies are always heavy, then resources will be insufficient and the lower ranks will fear the upper, causing great chaos in the land."*

This assumes that if one **ensures sufficient resources and increases benevolence**, then even light punishments can **govern**. This is incorrect.

In general, when people **demand heavy rewards or punishments**, it is already after their needs have been met.

Even if resources are sufficient and benevolence is increased afterward, light punishments **will still lead to chaos**.

When a household’s love for its children is excessive, and resources are abundant, the children will **waste their strength in labor**. If the ruler **governs with leniency**, the people will **indulge in wrongdoing**.

Resources sufficient while laboring hard is **Shennong**#7; governing with leniency while practicing virtue is **Zeng and Shi**#8.

The people **not reaching the level of Shennong, Zeng, or Shi is already clear**.

Laozi said: *"Knowing contentment prevents disgrace; knowing restraint prevents peril."*

Laozi **avoids seeking beyond sufficiency** because of the dangers of disgrace and peril. Now, assuming that **sufficiency for the people can bring governance** is like saying **all people are as wise as Laozi**.

Thus, **Jie**#9 was honored as a sovereign yet **not revered as a king**, and his wealth **spanned the four seas yet was not treasured as a jewel**.

Even if a ruler **ensures sufficiency for the people**, he cannot **ensure they will act like a sovereign**. **Jie** was not **ensuring sufficiency as a sovereign**, so even if resources were sufficient, **how could it be called governance**?

Thus, the enlightened ruler **governs the state** by **adapting to the times and accumulating wealth**, **calculating taxes and levies to balance poverty and riches**, **granting generous titles and stipends to exhaust the virtuous and capable**, **imposing heavy punishments to prohibit deceit and evil**, making the people **obtain riches through labor**, **gain honor through service**, **suffer punishment for transgressions**, and **receive rewards for merit**, while **not dwelling on the bestowal of benevolence**. This is the **politics of the sage-kings**.

All sleep, yet the blind do not know it; all remain silent, yet the foolish do not know it.

Awaken them and **make them see**; question them and **make them respond**, then the foolish and blind will be **exposed**.

If one **does not heed their words**, then the **unskilled will not be known**.

If one **does not entrust them with responsibilities**, then the **unworthy will not be known**.

To **hear their words yet demand practical results**, to **entrust them with tasks yet require their merit**, then the **unskilled and unworthy will be exposed**.

If one **wishes to obtain strong laborers yet listens only to their self-proclaimed abilities**, even a commoner and **Wu Ge**#10 cannot be distinguished. But if one **assigns them heavy burdens**, their true strength and weakness will **manifest**.

Thus, **official positions** are the **anvil and hammer** for able men. When entrusted with tasks, the **wise and foolish are distinguished**.

Thus, the **unskilled gain by not being used**, the **unworthy gain by not being entrusted**.

Those **not used** will **boast of their eloquence to prove their cleverness**, while those **not entrusted** will **adorn themselves to appear noble**. If the worldly rulers are **dazzled by their eloquence** and **indulge their false nobility**, then they **honor the blind and the foolish**, and this **cannot be called governance**.

The enlightened ruler **hears their words yet demands their deeds**, **observes their conduct yet requires their merit**. Thus, **empty, outdated learning is not discussed**, and **deceptive, self-aggrandizing behavior is not tolerated**.

**Chapter 47 of the Eight Sayings**#11:
**To act for old friends in private** is called **not abandoning them**; **to distribute public funds privately** is called **the benevolent man**; **to value status over stipends** is called **the gentleman**; **to pervert the law in favor of relatives** is called **having conduct**; **to neglect office for the sake of friends** is called **having chivalry**; **to withdraw from the world and seek refuge above** is called **arrogance**; **to engage in disputes and defy orders** is called **having spirit**; **to perform favors and win the masses** is called **gaining the people**.

**Not abandoning them** means **the officials are deceitful**; **the benevolent man** means **public funds are depleted**; **the gentleman** means **the people are difficult to govern**; **having conduct** means **laws and institutions are destroyed**; **having chivalry** means **official positions are left vacant**; **arrogance** means **the people do not serve**; **having spirit** means **orders are not obeyed**; **gaining the people** means **the ruler stands alone**.

These eight are **the private praises of commoners**, yet **the great downfall of the ruler**.

**Reversing these eight** is **the private criticism of commoners**, yet **the public benefit of the ruler**.

If the ruler **does not discern the profit and harm to the state**, yet **uses the private praises of commoners**, then **he cannot expect the state to be free from danger and chaos**.

**Entrusting people with responsibilities** is the **key to the survival or ruin of governance**. Without skill in **entrusting people**, there is **no one to entrust**, and **ruin is inevitable**.

What the ruler **entrusts** is either **cleverness and wisdom** or **virtuous purity**.

To **entrust people** is to **give them power**. The **wise may not be trustworthy**. To **value their cleverness** is to **create doubt about their trustworthiness**.

Using the **calculations of the wise**, yet placing them in **positions of power** where they **serve their own private interests**, the ruler **will definitely be deceived**.

Because the **wise cannot be trusted**, the ruler **entrusts virtuous men** with **decision-making**. The **virtuous may not be wise**. To **praise their purity** is to **create doubt about their wisdom**.

Placing **the ignorant** in **positions of governance** where they **act according to their own inclinations**, then **affairs will definitely be in disorder**.

The ruler who lacks skill in governing **will definitely suffer harm** when employing people: to **entrust the wise** means the ruler **will be deceived**; to **entrust the virtuous** means **affairs will be in disorder**. This is the peril of lacking skill.

The **Dao of a sagely ruler** is to **hold virtue/power in low regard** and **value wisdom**. The common people **will surely sit below their superiors**, while the ruler **decides with unwavering sincerity** and **consults without favoritism**, so **no one can fathom** his intentions.

Thus, **the wise cannot deceive**; **the virtuous cannot be entrusted with governance**.

**Reward is given based on merit**, **positions are assigned according to ability**, and **the ruler observes the roots of problems** to **foresee failures**. Those with faults are punished, those with merit are rewarded, so **the ignorant are not entrusted with affairs**.

When **the wise dare not deceive** and **the ignorant are not allowed to decide**, then **there will be no mistakes in governance**.

**To discern men’s abilities and follow them is not enough to establish decrees**, for **the people are not all discerning**. **To promote the worthy and act accordingly is not enough to establish laws**, for **the people are not all worthy**.

**Yang Zhu and Mo Di** were men **renowned for discernment** by the world, yet **even after a thousand generations of chaos**, they could not **bring decisive action**. Though **discerning**, they **could not serve as decrees for official appointments**.

**Bao Jiao and Hua Jue** were men **renowned for their worth** by the world. **Bao Jiao’s wood withered**, and he **stood firm like a dead tree**. **Hua Jue drowned in the river**, yet **he could not be counted among those who till the fields or wage war**.

Thus, when a ruler **discerns men**, **the wise exhaust their cleverness**; when a ruler **honors ability**, **the capable exhaust their skill**.

Nowadays, rulers **value empty cleverness** and **honor distant virtue**, yet **they seek the wealth and strength of their state**—**it cannot be attained**.

**Mastering extensive learning and clever debate like Confucius and Mozi**, yet **Confucius and Mozi did not till the fields**, how then **could the state prosper**?

**Practicing filial piety and few desires like Zengzi and Shizi**, yet **Zengzi and Shizi did not wage war or attack**, how then **could the state gain advantage**?

**Even commoners have private gains**; **the ruler has public interests**. **To live without labor yet have sustenance**, **to serve without office yet have renown**—this is **private gain**.

**To cultivate learning and clarify laws**, **to block private gain and unify merit and labor**—this is **public interest**.

**To misalign laws with the Way of guiding the people**, yet **to further prize learning**, then **what the people emulate becomes doubtful**.

**To reward merit for encouraging the people**, yet **to further honor virtue and conduct**, then **the people’s incentives for gain become sluggish**.

**To prize learning and cast doubt on laws**, **to honor virtue and conduct and split the people’s unified efforts**—**to seek the wealth and strength of the state is impossible**.

**Carrying ceremonial tablets and jade axes**, **yet wielding no square shields or iron swords**—this speaks of **the ruler’s military being mismatched in equipment**.

**Square shields** are **bucklers**. This refers to **the debates of those who carry tablets**, **the dances of those who wield axes**, and **their mismatch with the use of bucklers and iron swords**.

**Ascending and descending, maneuvering in court**, yet **not reaching the hundred daily decisions of midday governance**—**a fox’s head cannot match the swift release of a strong crossbow**.

**A lone warrior defending a city wall** cannot **compare to burying pits and concealing ambushes**.

In ancient times, **men were valued for their virtue/power**; in the middle ages, **they were pursued for their wisdom**; in the present age, **they are contended for their strength**.

In antiquity, **government was simple and tools were few**, **manners were plain and not fully refined**, hence **there were those who pushed carriages with shells**.

**Shells (珧)** are **conch shells (蜃)**. **To use conch shells as carriage axles** is **to push the wheels**. In the **uppermost antiquity**, **men plowed with conch shells**.

In antiquity, **there were few people yet great affection among them**, **many goods yet easy acquisition and willingness to yield**, hence **there were those who passed the state through courtesy and deference**.

Yet **practicing courtesy and deference**, **exalting benevolence and mercy**, and **following the Way of renown and kindness**—all are **methods of abdication**.

In an age of **many affairs**, **to use tools of simplicity** is **not the preparation of the wise**. In an age of **great contention**, **to follow the path of courtesy** is **not the governance of the sage**.

Thus, **the wise do not ride carriages pushed by shells**, and **the sage does not govern through abdication**.

**Laws are established to regulate affairs**; **affairs are undertaken to name merit**. **Laws may be set yet prove difficult**; if **weighing their difficulty** and **the affairs succeed**, then **establish them**.

If **affairs succeed yet prove harmful**; if **weighing their harm** and **the merit is great**, then **undertake them**.

**There are laws without difficulty**; **there are merits without harm**—**these exist in the world**.

Thus, **to seize a thousand *li* stronghold**, **to take an army of ten thousand**, **the dead and injured are half the troops**. **Half** refers to **their number**.

When **armor and weapons are broken**, **soldiers are killed or wounded**, yet **those who celebrate victory and gain of land**—they **reckon small harm against great gain**.

**To shave one’s head leaves hair discarded**; **to remove one’s skin injures flesh and blood**. If **others see its difficulty**, they **abandon their efforts**—this is **governance without skill**.

The **ancient sages** said: *"Even water has ripples when stirred, and even the Way has friction when followed. I wish to change it, but how can I?"*

This is the **speech of balancing circumstances**.

Thus, **some teachings must be established yet lack practical efficacy**, and **some words are clumsy yet urgent in use**—hence **the sage does not seek harm-free teachings**, but **strives for unchanging affairs**.

**Men do not use balances and weights** not because they are **upright and廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉廉

The ruler who preserves the state does not rely on mere **virtue/power** and **righteousness**.

The **virtuous/powerful** one is merciful and benevolent yet frugal with wealth. The **tyrannical** one is resolute in heart yet quick to punish. Merciful benevolence leads to hesitation in punishment, frugality with wealth leads to excessive giving, a resolute heart reveals hatred in the lower ranks, and swift punishment results in wanton killings upon the people.

Hesitation in punishment means many pardons and few punishments, excessive giving means many rewards for those who do not deserve them. Hatred revealed leads the lower ranks to resent their superiors, and wanton killings cause the people to turn against their lord.

Thus, when a **virtuous/powerful** one holds office, the lower ranks grow unrestrained and casually violate laws, hoping for favor from above. When a **tyrannical** one holds office, laws and commands become arbitrary while subjects and rulers grow estranged, the people’s resentment breeds, and disorder takes root.

Therefore it is said: **Virtue/power** and **tyranny** alike lead to the ruin of states.

One cannot provide abundant food to encourage the hungry to eat and be called a **virtue/power** that can revive the starving. One cannot clear the fields of weeds and grow grain to encourage loans, gifts, and rewards, and be called a **virtue/power** that can enrich the people.

Now, those who study say: they do not focus on essential matters but prefer superficial ones, advocating **Dao/Way** and **holy virtue** to sway the people—this is the encouragement of eating. The **enlightened ruler** does not accept such words.

If scriptures are concise yet scholars debate them, if laws are simple yet the people litigate endlessly, then the **holy sage’s** writings must expound principles, and the **enlightened ruler’s** laws must meticulously address **affairs**.

To exhaust all considerations and weigh gains and losses is the task of the wise—difficult indeed. To act without thought or deliberation, to demand results based solely on past words, is the ease of the foolish.

The **enlightened ruler** considers what the foolish find easy, yet holds the wise to what they find difficult; thus, the wise’s labor of thought and effort is not wasted, and the state thrives.

Sweet, sour, salty, and bland flavors—if not decided by the ruler’s own palate but left to the cook’s discretion—then the kitchen staff will slight their lord and honor the cook instead. Clarity and muddiness in governance—if not judged by the ruler’s own ears but entrusted to the music director—then the blind artisans will slight their lord and honor the music director instead.

The right and wrong of statecraft—if not resolved by the ruler’s own wisdom but handed to favorites—then the ministers will slight their lord and honor those in favor instead.

When the ruler does not personally observe and listen, yet delegates judgment to inferiors, he is merely a burden upon the state. If he can make people neither wear clothes nor eat food yet not suffer hunger or cold, and if they do not dread death, then they will have no need to serve him. If their desires are not subject to his authority, they cannot be compelled.

Now, the power of life and death has never been vested in great ministers while the ruler’s commands prevail. A household of ten thousand gold pieces must not rely on its wealth alone to match the might of a **sly and cunning** one (鼷鼠). A lord of the land cannot sway people with mere words, nor harm them without substance, nor make them fear him by heavy demands—this is impossible.

Ministers who indulge their desires and proclaim them as **chivalry** bring chaos. Rulers who indulge their desires and proclaim them as law bring ruin. Ministers who slight their lord are called **proud**; rulers who slight their subjects are called **tyrannical**.

Actions and words in harmony bring praise to the ministers, while discord brings blame to the ruler. When ministers gain greatly, the ruler loses greatly.

In the **enlightened ruler’s** state, there are **noble ministers** but no **heavy ministers**. **Noble ministers** are those with high rank and great office. **Heavy ministers** are those whose words are heeded and whose influence is strong. In the **enlightened ruler’s** state, officials are promoted, ranks are inherited, and titles are granted for merit—thus there are **noble ministers**. Yet if their words do not match their deeds and they harbor deceit, they will be punished—thus there are no **heavy ministers**.

*Chapter Eighty-Four of the Fourth Scroll of the* **Eight Scrolls** *(八经).*

The governance of all under Heaven must conform to human inclinations.

Human inclinations entail **likes and dislikes**, hence rewards and punishments can be effectively employed. When rewards and punishments are effectively employed, prohibitions and commands can be established, and thus the **Dao of governance** is complete. The ruler holds the **grip** to manage the **situation**, hence commands are issued and prohibitions enforced. The **grip** refers to the authority to take life and grant it, while the **situation** denotes the means to overcome the multitude.

If appointments and dismissals are without measure, then **authority is abused**; if rewards and punishments are indiscriminately applied, then **sovereign power is diminished**. Thus, the **enlightened ruler** does not harbor favoritism in listening, nor does he cling to persuasion in deliberation. Therefore, if he listens without **cross-verifying**, **authority is undermined by deceit**; if wisdom and strength are not utilized, the ruler **fails before his ministers**.

Hence, the **enlightened ruler’s** actions are as **Dao** (天), unfathomable. His use of people is as **ghostly** (鬼), shrouded in secrecy. Like the **ghostly** in its shadowy and inscrutable depths. **Dao** does not err—being exalted and unfathomable, who can censure it? **Ghostly** is not constrained—being shadowy and inscrutable, who can thwart it? When **situational authority** prevails and instruction is stern, even those who oppose dare not defy; this is the **situation’s** application.

Praise and blame are executed uniformly without debate. Praise and blame are executed uniformly, yet none dare debate. Thus, rewarding the **virtuous** and punishing the **tyrannical**, elevating the utmost in goodness, is the highest governance. Rewarding the **tyrannical** and punishing the **virtuous**, elevating the utmost in evil, is the worst governance. This is called **rewarding alike and punishing differently**.

Rewards are bestowed generously, benefiting the people. Praise is most effective when beautiful, honoring the people. Punishments are most feared when severe, instilling awe in the people. Blame is most shaming when vile, causing the people to feel disgrace. Then, when the law is uniformly enforced and private transgressions are not tolerated, families do not conceal their merits or crimes, for rewards and punishments must be known.

When this is understood, the **Dao** of governance is complete. **Conform to inclinations.**

「Secondly, when one’s strength cannot rival the multitude and wisdom cannot encompass all things, it is better to employ a whole nation than a single individual. If a ruler relies solely on his own single person’s intelligence and strength, he will fail to harness the collective power of the people and use the nation as a whole. Thus, when wisdom and strength are matched but the multitude prevails, the ruler’s schemes may either succeed (if well-calculated) or incur blame (if miscalculated). The lesser ruler exhausts his own abilities, the middle ruler exhausts the strength of others, and the sagest ruler exhausts the wisdom of others. Therefore, when matters arise, he gathers intelligence, and when he listens, he does so with impartial counsel.

If his counsel is not unified, later actions will contradict earlier ones, causing the wise and foolish to become indistinguishable. If counsel is not impartial, he will hesitate and fail to decide, leaving matters unresolved. If he takes responsibility for one failure, he will avoid the burden of greater calamities. Hence, he lets others speak first, deliberates firmly, and then expresses anger. Thus, on the day when opinions are presented, there must be records of strategies, and matters entrusted to intelligence will reveal success or failure, while those entrusted to ability will show results and determine victory or defeat. Victory and defeat have their signs, and rewards and punishments follow accordingly. When a matter succeeds, the ruler takes credit for the achievement; when it fails, the minister bears the blame. If the ruler even doubts the achievement when the seals are confirmed, how much more so when relying on strength alone? If he doubts intelligence itself, how much more so when relying on mere conjecture?

Therefore, even in employing others, he does not seek uniformity—uniformity breeds the ruler’s wrath. If he lets ministers use each other, his authority becomes divine, and the people below will be fully devoted. When the people below are fully devoted, ministers will not rely on the ruler, and the Way of governance is complete. The Way of governance is thus called *structuring intelligence*.

Thirdly, the ruler who knows the differing interests of ministers and subjects will reign, the one who treats them as the same will face ruin, and the one who shares matters with them will be killed. Hence, an enlightened ruler examines the distinction between public and private affairs, weighs the balance of benefits and harms, and then deceit will find no foothold. Six sources of chaos arise: the sovereign’s mother, the queen consort, the ruler’s sons, his brothers, his senior ministers, and his favored scholars. The sovereign’s mother refers to the ruler’s youthful regency. The queen consort and the ruler’s sons lead to the commoners forcing their way. Brothers cause the prince to usurp the state. Senior ministers act as regents, seizing control. Favored scholars hide their true names behind empty titles, overshadowing the ruler.

If the ruler appoints officials and holds ministers accountable, yet the sovereign’s mother is not dismissed, chaos will be blamed on the ministers. If he abolishes disorder and punishes the ministers instead, the queen consort will have no suspicion. If he grants unequal honors, the commoner and legitimate sons will not vie for power. If he does not allow the commoner to vie with the legitimate sons, the brothers will not encroach. If he does not let one family monopolize authority, the senior ministers will not surround him with influence. If rewards and punishments are strictly enforced, favored scholars will not cause chaos.

Ministers have two influences: external and internal. Externally, they rely on fear—ministers wielding threats, and all things fear them. Internally, they rely on love—those they fear will seek their favor, and those they love will heed their words; this is how disorderly ministers gain influence. If the ruler places external officials under strict oversight, punishes those who are too close and demands heavy tribute, then external threats will be contained. If he grants rank and reward based on merit, punishes all who petition for favor, then internal influence will be curbed. When external threats are contained and internal influence is curbed, deceitful schemes will be sealed off.

Promotion follows established ranks, reaching even the highest offices—this is wisdom. Those who hold high positions and great responsibilities are bound by three principles: loyalty (*quality*), stability (*anchor*), and firmness (*consolidation*). Loyalty comes from kin and dependents. Stability comes from abundant rank and secure rewards. Firmness comes from trusted associates and heavy tribute. The virtuous stop at loyalty, the greedy are transformed by stability, and the deceitful are exhausted by firmness. If the ruler does not restrain himself, the people below will rise above him; if he does not remove small faults, great punishments will follow, and when names and realities align, he proceeds directly.

If a matter harms the living but injures the dead in reputation, he will execute those responsible. Otherwise, he will let the victim’s kin retaliate—this is called *eliminating hidden treachery*. In medicine, it is called *deception* and *subversion*. Seeing merit and rewarding it, seeing crime and punishing it, stops deception. If right and wrong are not exposed, flattery and remonstrance will not be understood, and subversion will not be used.

When a father or elder brother is virtuous and influential, the harm is called *wandering calamity*—its danger lies in neighboring enemies gaining resources. When a disgraced or humiliated person is close to the ruler, it is called *familiar brigand*—its danger lies in stirring resentment and the seeds of humiliation. When anger is suppressed and blame is held but not released, it is called *amplifying chaos*—its danger lies in reckless schemers emerging. When senior ministers hold dual roles, balancing power without overreaching, it is called *rolling calamity*—its danger lies in the rise of familial strife and regicidal plots. When a ruler’s authority is weakened and he does not rely on his own strength, it is called *scattering威* (*威* being *prestige*)—its danger lies in the emergence of treacherous plots and poisonous rebellions.

These five dangers: if the ruler does not recognize them, then ruin and killing will ensue. If disorder arises from within, it can be corrected; if from without, it will lead to chaos. Therefore, an enlightened ruler judges internal matters by merit and rewards external ones by utility, ensuring his own state is orderly while enemies are in disarray. As for the *Dao* of chaos, if ministers are hated, external rebellion will arise like a sudden storm; if ministers are loved, internal strife will brew like a hidden poison.

The *Dao* of chaos is thus called *chaos erupts*.

Fourthly, the method of *comparison and verification*: use *comparison* to discern many schemes, and *verification* to assign blame for mistakes. *Comparison* must be thorough, and *verification* must be precise. If it is not thorough, the ruler will be disrespected; if not precise, ministers will collude. Before assigning blame, the ruler must not be outnumbered by the multitude. The signs of his listening and deliberation are: comparing opinions while rewarding differently, punishing without hesitation while crimes are equal.

When gathering opinions, he must weigh them by circumstance, plan by strategy, test them by reality, and cross-check by human nature. These four verifications are like seals—only then can he observe truly. He compares words to know their sincerity, tests appearances to alter their superficiality, examines actions to uncover extraordinary deeds, unifies usage to focus on familiar matters, and demands accountability to deter distant schemers. He reviews past actions to exhaust their future consequences, gauges proximity to know internal motives, and assesses isolation to discern external intentions. He grasps clarity to question ambiguity, uses deceptive orders to prevent reckless leaks, reverses speech to test suspicions, and debates contradictions to expose hidden treachery. He sets up remonstrance to curb solitary wrongdoing, raises objections to observe deceitful movements, and clarifies explanations to lure away faults. He adopts humility to observe sincere flattery, publicly announces to reveal what is unseen, and stages conflicts to disperse factions. He deepens unity to respect the hearts of the people, and leaks differences to shift their focus.

The resemblance of things brings unity in their correspondence,
And laying out faults reveals their stubbornness.

Knowing guilt and avoiding it halts tyranny,
Using covert measures at the proper time to curb decline,
Gradually altering to sever close associations,
And employing strict lower-level agreements to undermine superiors.

The Prime Minister restrains his court officials,
Court officials restrain their subordinates,
Soldiers restrain their military officers,
Envoys restrain their escorts,
County officials restrain their subordinate clerks,
Palace attendants restrain their aides,
Consorts restrain their ladies-in-waiting—
This is called the method of orderly regulation.

When words are spoken and matters are revealed, then strategy fails.

Establishing the Dao consists of five principles:
The enlightened ruler’s essence lies in thoroughness,
Thus when favor is shown, virtue is repaid,
And when anger is shown, authority is upheld.

Therefore, the enlightened ruler’s words are sealed yet permeate,
Thorough yet unseen.

Thus, gaining ten from one is the lower Dao,
Gaining one from ten is the higher Dao.
The enlightened ruler practices both,
So deceit finds no advantage.

Five officials govern neighboring counties,
Commendations follow proper conduct,
Punishments follow errors.
The superior’s treatment of inferiors,
And inferiors’ treatment of superiors,
Is likewise so.

Thus, superiors and inferiors, nobles and commoners,
Hold each other in check by law,
And instruct each other with harmony.

The people’s nature has both the substance of life
And the name of life.
The ruler has the name of wisdom and virtue,
And the substance of rewards and punishments.
When name and substance are both fulfilled,
Then the blessing of goodness will surely be heard.

Sixfold correspondence.

Thus, when the ruler listens without verification, he cannot properly hold inferiors accountable;
When words are spoken without regard to their practical application, false doctrines will ascend.

When a matter is spoken of with many voices, it gains credibility;
When a matter is spoken of with doubt, even if ten men question it,
A hundred affirm it, and a thousand cannot fathom it,
Then it is still suspect.

The *nà* (吶) is the doubtful utterance,
The *biàn* (辩) is the credible speech.

When deceivers feed upon the ruler’s authority,
They draw resources from the multitude,
Leverage the trust in eloquence,
And use analogies to adorn their private schemes.

The ruler, sated with anger, awaits corroboration,
Yet his influence is diminished by his subordinates.

The ruler of the Dao (Way) listens to words and verifies their application,
Evaluates their efficacy, and from efficacy, rewards and punishments arise—
Thus, empty eloquence is not tolerated in court.

When an official recognizes his knowledge as insufficient to govern his post,
He is relieved of duty and his office is reassigned.

To boast of greatness while lacking substance brings ruin to the end—
Thus, deceivers gain favor through anger.

To falsely accuse without cause is slander;
When slander is punished, officials’ words must bear consequences—
Hence, partisan speech does not reach the ruler’s ears.

In general, the Dao of listening:
When ministers offer loyal counsel, deceit is exposed;
When they debate broadly, unity is achieved.
If the ruler lacks wisdom, deceit gains support.

The Dao of the enlightened ruler:
When he takes pleasure, he seeks what aligns with it;
When he is enraged, he examines what provokes it.
After changes occur, he discerns the signs of praise and blame—
Public and private alike.

When many advise, it is to test the ruler’s wisdom,
So he may avoid punishment by personally selecting one truth.
Thus, the multitude’s counsel becomes the ruler’s undoing.

He does not allow private speech before him to set future traps,
Nor does he permit contradictory words behind him to gauge truth and deceit.

The Dao of the enlightened ruler:
A minister cannot offer two conflicting counsels—
He must commit to one.
He cannot act unilaterally—
He must align with verification.

Thus, deceit finds no path to advance.

**The Dao of Listening (法#25 七)**

When offices are weighted without proper law,
Law itself is undermined.
If law is neglected, the ruler’s darkness and lack of measure
Allow officials to act arbitrarily—
Arbitrary officials grow in prestige,
No precedent restrains them, so their demands multiply,
And their wealth accumulates.

When officials are both powerful and wealthy,
Disorder in governance is born.

The Dao of the enlightened ruler:
He selects based on capability;
If one can govern his duties, he is taken.
If one excels in office, he is praised for maintaining it.

Rewards follow merit.

When words and deeds both please the ruler,
They are certain to benefit;
When they displease him,
They are certain to harm—
Thus, men do not advance their own kin
While promoting the ruler’s enemies.

When the ruler’s authority suffices to enforce law,
And his prestige suffices to support officials,
Yet private gain finds no foothold,
The people labor but do not resent their duties.

In selecting officials, do not favor the powerful—
Let their honor rest in their rank.
In governing offices, do not indulge in private gain—
Let their profit lie in their stipends.

Thus, the people revere rank and value stipends.
Rank and stipends are given to reward merit—
If the people hold them dear,
Then the realm is well-ordered.

When punishments are excessive,
When titles are misapplied,
And rewards are unjustly bestowed,
The people grow doubtful.
The people’s reverence for titles equals their regard for rewards.

When rewards are accompanied by slander,
They lose their power to encourage.
When punishments are met with praise,
They lose their power to restrain.

The Dao of the enlightened ruler:
Rewards must arise from public benefit,
Titles must be granted for service to the ruler.
Praise and blame must align with truth—
If not, punishments and rewards will follow the same course,
And the people will find no glory within rewards.

When punishments are severe,
They carry a bad reputation—
Thus, the people fear them.
Punishments are used to restrain—
If the people fear them,
Then the realm is well-ordered.

**The Dao of Analogies (类柄 八)**

When righteous conduct is demonstrated but not enforced,
The ruler’s authority is diminished.
When mercy is shown but law is ignored,
The legal order is destroyed.

The people fear the ruler through his laws,
Yet the ruler’s influence weakens when his power is low—
Thus, if inferiors grow bold in defying authority,
And take pride in their disrespectful customs,
The ruler’s prestige is undermined.

The people find it difficult to offend the ruler through his laws,
Yet the ruler, by using law to obstruct mercy,
Allows inferiors to understand his love and indulge in corruption—
They seek bribes and engage in illicit transactions.

Thus, laws and decrees collapse.

To honor private conduct as a second authority
Undermines the ruler’s prestige,
To engage in bribery and corruption
Casts doubt upon the laws—
To listen to such matters invites chaos,
To refuse them invites slander of the ruler—
Thus, the ruler grows light in his position,
And the laws grow disordered in the offices,
This is called a realm without constancy.

The Dao of the enlightened ruler:
A minister cannot use righteous conduct as a means to gain honor,
Nor can he advance his family’s interests as his merit.
Merit and reputation must arise from adherence to office and law.

What lies beyond the law,
Though difficult to practice,
Is not made manifest.
Thus, the people cannot create private titles.

Establish laws and measures to standardize the people,
Use rewards and punishments to exhaust their capabilities,
Clarify praise and blame to encourage or deter—
Titles, rewards, laws, and decrees form three corners,
Thus, great officials act with honor for the ruler,
And commoners perform meritorious deeds for the ruler’s benefit—
This is called a realm of the Dao (Way).

**The Ruler’s Authority (主威#27) — End of Chapter 18 of *Han Feizi***

This is the **Five Vermin (五蠹#49)**—Chapter 19 of *Han Feizi*:

The **Dao of the enlightened ruler**:
A **minister** cannot use **righteous conduct** to gain **honor**,
Nor can he advance his **family’s interests** as his **merit**.
**Merit** and **reputation** must arise from adherence to **office** and **law**.

What lies beyond the **law#25**,
Though difficult to practice,
Is not made manifest.
Thus, the people cannot create private **titles**.

Establish **laws** and **measures** to standardize the people,
Rely on **rewards** and **punishments** to exhaust their **abilities**,
Clarify **praise** and **blame** to encourage or deter—
**Titles**, **rewards**, and **laws** form three **corners**,
Thus, great **ministers** act with **honor** for the **ruler**,
And the **commoners** perform **meritorious deeds** for the **ruler’s#26 benefit**—
This is called a realm of the **Dao (Way)**.

---

**The Five Vermin (五蠹#49)**:

The **ruler**#1 holds **authority**,
Yet the **ministers**#2 bring **chaos**#3.
The **ruler** does not **command**#4,
Yet the **ministers**#2 do not **submit**#5.
The **ruler** does not **enforce**#6 **punishments**,
Yet the **ministers**#2 do not **act**#7 **properly**#8.
The **ruler** does not **reward**,
Yet the **ministers**#2 do not **perform**#9 their **duties**#10—
This is the **first vermin**#11: the **minister**#12.

The **ruler** does not **govern**#13,
Yet the **people**#14 do not **submit**#15.
The **ruler** does not **enforce**#16 **laws**,
Yet the **people**#14 do not **act**#17 **properly**#18.
The **ruler** does not **reward**,
Yet the **people**#14 do not **perform**#19 their **duties**#20—
This is the **second vermin**: the **people**#14.

The **ruler** does not **examine**#21,
Yet the **scholars**#22 do not **speak**#23 **truthfully**#24.
The **ruler** does not **ask**,
Yet the **scholars**#22 do not **submit**#25—
This is the **third vermin**: the **scholar**.

The **ruler** does not **command**#26,
Yet the **officers**#27 do not **submit**#28.
The **ruler** does not **enforce**#29 **laws**,
Yet the **officers**#27 do not **act**#30 **properly**—
This is the **fourth vermin**: the **officer**.

The **ruler** does not **train**#31,
Yet the **soldiers**#32 do not **obey**#33.
The **ruler** does not **inspect**#34,
Yet the **soldiers**#32 do not **perform**#35 their **duties**—
This is the **fifth vermin**: the **soldier**.

Thus, the **ruler**#36 who does not **govern**#37,
Yet the **people**#38 do not **submit**,
Is **blamed**#39.
The **ruler** who does not **enforce**#40 **laws**,
Yet the **people**#41 do not **act**#42 **properly**,
Is **blamed**.
The **ruler** who does not **reward**,
Yet the **people**#43 do not **perform**#44 their **duties**,
Is **blamed**.
The **ruler** who does not **command**,
Yet the **ministers**#45 do not **submit**,
Is **blamed**.
The **ruler** who does not **enforce**#46 **laws**,
Yet the **ministers**#47 do not **act**#48 **properly**,
Is **blamed**.
The **ruler** who does not **reward**,
Yet the **ministers**#49 do not **perform** their **duties**,
Is **blamed**.
This is called **failure**#50.

The age of high antiquity, the people were few while beasts and vermin were many. The people could not prevail over beasts, insects, and snakes. Then a sage arose, constructed wooden nests to avoid the multitude of calamities, and the people rejoiced, making him ruler over all under Heaven, and he was called **Youxu**#51 **Clan**.

The people ate fruits, nuts, shellfish, and clams—rank, violent, and foul in odor, damaging the stomach and causing many illnesses. Then a sage arose, drilled fire from flint to transform the rank and violent, and the people delighted in him, making him ruler over all under Heaven, and he was called **Suiren**#52 **Clan**.

In the middle antiquity, the world was flooded, and **Gun** and **Yu**#53 dredged the waterways. In the recent antiquity, **Jie** and **Zhou**#54 were tyrannical, and **Tang** and **Wu**#55 waged war.

Now, if one were to construct nests and drill fire in the **Xia** dynasty, **Gun** and **Yu** would laugh at them. If one were to dredge waterways in the **Shang** and **Zhou** dynasties, **Tang** and **Wu** would laugh at them. Therefore, if one were now to praise the **Dao** of **Yao**, **Shun**, **Tang**, **Wu**, and **Yu** in the present age, the new sages would laugh at them.

Thus, sages do not seek to emulate antiquity; they focus on supporting the urgent needs of the age. They do not adhere to fixed principles but assess the affairs of the world and prepare accordingly.

There was a farmer in **Song** who had a stump in his field. A hare ran and struck the stump, breaking its neck and dying. The farmer abandoned his plow and guarded the stump, hoping to catch another hare. But no more hares came, and he became a laughingstock of **Song**. Now, if one were to apply the governance of the ancient kings to the people of today, all would be like this farmer guarding the stump.

In antiquity, men did not farm, for wild plants and fruits provided enough food. Women did not weave, for the skins of beasts provided enough clothing. Without toil, sustenance was abundant, and though the people were few, wealth was plentiful, so they did not contend. Thus, heavy rewards were unnecessary, and severe punishments were not used, yet the people governed themselves.

Now, a man with five sons is considered rich, and each son may have five sons. By the time the great-grandfather dies, there are twenty-five grandsons. Thus, the people are many while goods are scarce, labor is arduous, and provisions are meager, so they contend. Even with doubled rewards and repeated punishments, disorder cannot be avoided.

When **Yao** ruled all under Heaven, his thatched hut was untrimmed, his wooden beams unplaned, his food was coarse rice and millet, his soup was made of **lihuang**#56 and **haoma**, his winter garment was **lù**#57 deer hide, and his summer garment was **gě**#58 cloth. Even the keeper of the gate did not live worse than this.

When **Yu** ruled all under Heaven, he personally wielded the plow and hoe to lead by example, his thighs were worn to the bone#1, and his skin had no hair from labor—even slaves did not suffer as much as he did. By this account, those who in antiquity declined the throne were abandoning the keeper’s meager life and escaping the labor of slaves. Was the transmission of the throne in antiquity truly praiseworthy?

Now, if a county magistrate dies, his descendants ride in carriages for generations. Thus, men value the office. Therefore, men lightly reject the ancient kings but struggle to abandon today’s magistrates—because the substance of wealth and status differs.

Those who live in the mountains and draw water from valleys exchange salted meat, for water is scarce, and they ration it. Those who dwell in marshes, suffering from excess water, hire laborers to dig drainage ditches. Marsh-dwellers suffer from water, so they pay others to dig their wealth.

In famine years, even young brothers are not fed. Young brothers are precious, yet they are still not fed. In abundant years, even distant guests are fed. It is not that they love distant relatives more than their own; it is the difference in abundance and scarcity.

Thus, the ancients easily parted with wealth—not out of kindness, but because wealth was abundant. Today’s contention over wealth is not due to greed, but because wealth is scarce. Lightly rejecting the throne is not from nobility, but from the insignificance of the position. Contending over land is not from baseness, but from the weight of power.

Therefore, sages assess abundance and scarcity, weigh wealth and status, and govern accordingly. Thus, light punishments are not from mercy, and severe executions are not from cruelty—they conform to the customs of the age.

Thus, affairs arise with the times, and preparations adapt to circumstances. In antiquity, the **Wen**#2 **King** ruled in the **Feng-Hao** region, with a territory of a hundred *li*, practicing **ren**#59 and **yi**#60 to win over the **Xi Rong**#61 tribes and thus ruled all under Heaven. **Xū Yǎnwáng**#62 ruled in the eastern **Han** region, with a territory of five hundred *li*, practicing **ren** and **yi**, and thirty-six states submitted by ceding land. But **Jing Wénwáng**#63 feared his power and attacked **Xū**, annihilating him. Thus, **Wen** ruled all under Heaven through **ren** and **yi**, while **Yǎn** lost his state through them. This shows that **ren** and **yi** were effective in antiquity but not in the present.

Thus, it is said: *The times change, so affairs change.* In **Shun**’s time, the **Yóu Miáo**#64 were unruly, and **Yu** prepared to attack them. **Shun** said: *No.* To use force when **virtue**#65 is not yet established is not the **Dao**. He refined education for three years, then danced with **gān qí**#66 weapons, and the **Yóu Miáo** submitted. In the war against **Gōnggōng**#67, those with sharp iron weapons could reach the enemy, while those with weak armor were wounded. Thus, **gān qí** were effective in antiquity but not today.

Therefore, it is said: *Affairs change, so preparations must adapt.*

In high antiquity, competition was in **Dao** and **virtue**; in the middle age, it was in clever schemes; in the present age, it is in **qi** and strength. When the **Qi** general attacked **Lu**, **Lu** sent **Zigong**#68 to persuade him.
