The **Qi** men said: *Your words are not without eloquence, but what we desire is land—not these words.* They marched and took ten *li* from **Lu** as their boundary.

Thus, **Yǎnwáng**’s **ren** and **yi** led to his state’s ruin, and **Zigong**’s eloquence led to **Lu**’s loss of territory. By this account, **ren**, **yi**, and eloquence are not what sustains a state. Remove **Yǎnwáng**’s **ren**, suppress **Zigong**’s cleverness, and follow **Xū** and **Lu**’s strength—even if the enemy has ten thousand chariots, the desires of **Qi** and **Jing** would not prevail in these two states.

Since ancient and modern customs differ, and new and old preparations vary, to govern the people of an urgent age with lenient policies is like driving a spirited horse without reins—this is the peril of ignorance.

「Now, both the Confucianists and Mohists claim that the ancient sovereigns loved all under heaven equally, and thus the people regarded their rulers as if they were their parents. How can this be proven?

They say: When the *Sīkòu* (司寇, minister of justice) carried out punishments, the sovereign did not rejoice in music. Upon hearing the news of executions, the sovereign wept. This is the conduct of the ancient sovereigns they speak of.

If one treats the relationship between ruler and subject like that of a son to his father, then order must prevail—yet by this logic, there would be no disorder between father and son. Human nature is such that no bond is stronger than that between parents and children, yet even when children are deeply loved, they do not always obey. Even if love is profound, why should they not rebel?

Now, if the ancient sovereigns’ love for the people did not exceed that of parents for their children, and children are not necessarily obedient even to their parents, then why should the people be governed by such love? Moreover, if the sovereign weeps while the law demands punishment, this is merely to display *virtue/power* (仁), not to achieve governance.

To weep while not wanting to punish is *virtue/power* (仁), yet punishment cannot be avoided—this is the law (法). The ancient sovereigns prevailed with their laws and did not heed their tears, so it is clear that *virtue/power* (仁) alone cannot bring order.

Furthermore, the people naturally submit to authority, few respond to moral persuasion. *Zhòngní* (仲尼, Master Kong/Confucius), though a sage under heaven, traveled the land teaching the *Dao/Way* (道) and promoting *virtue/power* (仁). All under heaven praised his *virtue/power* (仁) and admired his moral principles (义), yet only seventy men served him. This shows that those who value *virtue/power* (仁) are few, and those capable of moral principles (义) are rare.

Thus, even with all the land under heaven, only seventy men served him, while the one who embodied *virtue/power* (仁) and moral principles (义) was Confucius himself. *Lǔ Àigōng* (鲁哀公, Duke Ai of Lu), a lesser ruler, sat enthroned and commanded his realm—no subject within his borders dared not submit. The people naturally submit to authority, and authority is indeed easy to wield over them. Hence, Confucius was forced to serve as a subject, while Duke Ai ruled as a tyrant.

Confucius did not submit because he cherished moral principles (义), but because of the Duke’s coercive authority. Therefore, if Duke Ai upheld moral principles (义), Confucius would not have submitted to him; yet if Duke Ai relied on his authority, Confucius would have submitted.

Now, scholars argue that a ruler need not rely on the inevitable strength of law, but can instead cultivate *virtue/power* (仁) and *moral principles* (义) to achieve kingship. This is to demand that all rulers must become like Confucius, while treating the common people as if they were his disciples—seventy in number. This is an impossible expectation.

There are unworthy sons who, though scolded by their parents, do not mend their ways; though mocked by their neighbors, they remain unmoved; though instructed by their teachers, they do not change. Even with the love of their parents, the kindness of their neighbors, and the wisdom of their teachers—three virtues combined—such sons still refuse to alter their behavior, not even their slightest actions.

Yet when officials of the state wield their troops and enforce the public laws to seek out the guilty, the people then tremble with fear, abandon their wicked ways, and reform their conduct. Thus, the love of parents is insufficient to teach their children; only the stern punishments of state officials can make the people obey. The people are naturally arrogant toward love but yield to authority.

Hence, a ten-*rèn* (仞, ten feet) wall, even *Lóu Jì* (楼季, a reference to Confucius) could not cross it—because it was steep. A thousand-*rèn* mountain, even a lame man could herd his cattle across—because it was flat. Therefore, enlightened rulers must make their laws steep and their punishments stern.

Plain cloth of ordinary length, even commoners will not abandon. Molten metal poured a hundred times, even the thief *Zhí* (跖, Zhi the Robber) will not touch it. Yet if gold melts and rots, even if abundant, Zhi will discard it and not take it. If harm is not certain, commoners will not abandon ordinary cloth; if harm is certain, even Zhi will not touch molten metal. Thus, an enlightened ruler must ensure that punishment is inevitable.

Therefore, rewards must be generous and trustworthy, so the people will seek them. Punishments must be severe and certain, so the people will fear them. Laws must be uniform and unchanging, so the people will know them. Hence, a ruler bestows rewards without shifting his standards and carries out punishments without pardoning.

Praise accompanies rewards, blame follows punishments—then both the worthy and the unworthy will exert their utmost efforts. But now it is not so: those with merit are granted rank, yet their officials are held in low esteem. Those who till the fields are rewarded, yet their households are impoverished. Those who do not plunder are exiled, yet their disregard for the world is praised. Those who break the law are punished, yet their courage is commended.

Thus, praise and blame, rewards and punishments, are applied in contradiction—so laws are undermined and the people grow ever more disorderly.

Now, if a brother is harmed, one must attack in retaliation—this is *lián* (廉, integrity). The world calls such a person one of *lián yú* (廉隅, integrity and righteousness). If one knows a friend’s insult and follows with vengeance—this is *zhēn* (贞, constancy). When integrity and constancy are practiced, the ruler’s laws are violated.

Rulers honor those who practice integrity and constancy, yet overlook the crime of breaking the law. Hence, the people are emboldened by courage, and officials cannot prevail against them.

To gain food without labor is called capability; to gain honor without battle is called virtue. When capability and virtue are practiced, the armies weaken and the land becomes barren.

Rulers praise those who practice capability and virtue, yet ignore the disasters of weak armies and barren lands. Thus, private conduct is established while public benefit is destroyed.

The Confucianists disrupt governance with their literature, the chivalrous disrupt the law with their strength, yet rulers honor both. This is why disorder arises.

Those who abandon the law are guilty, yet the scholars are rewarded for their literary arts. Those who violate the law are punished, yet the chivalrous are sustained by their private swords. Thus, what the law condemns, the ruler rewards. What the officials punish, the ruler sustains.

This is the root of chaos.»

The Way of governance is contrary in its interests to those above and below, yet lacks a fixed standard. Even if there were ten Yellow Emperors, they could not bring order.

Thus, practicing benevolence and righteousness is not what should be praised, for praising it harms achievement. Cultivating literary arts is not what should be employed, for employing it disrupts the law.

In Chu, there was a man of upright conduct; when his father stole a sheep and was brought before the officials, the Minister of Order said: *"Execute him, for he is straight to his lord but crooked to his father."* He was thus condemned and punished. Observing this, one sees that the lord’s upright minister is a violent son to his father.

The people of Lu followed their lord into battle, yet three times they retreated in three battles. Zǒng Ér asked why, and they replied: *"We have an aged father; if we die, who will nourish him?"* Zǒng Ér took this as filial piety and recommended them to the lord. Observing this, one sees that the father’s filial son is a traitor to his lord.

Thus, the Minister of Order punished, yet Chu’s deceitful subjects were not reported above, while Zǒng Ér rewarded, yet Lu’s people easily turned to retreat. The interests of those above and below differ so greatly, yet the lord embraces the conduct of common men while seeking the prosperity of the state and its altars—this will never succeed.

In ancient times, Cāng Jié created writing: what encircled oneself was called *private*, and what opposed *private* was called *public*. The mutual opposition of *public* and *private* was something Cāng Jié himself knew well. Today, those who claim the same interests are in danger of failing to discern this.

Now, what is called the *virtue* of common men is nothing more than cultivating righteousness and practicing literary arts. When righteousness is cultivated, one is trusted; when trusted, one is given tasks. When literary arts are practiced, one becomes a learned teacher; when a learned teacher, one attains prominence and honor. This is the beauty of common men.

Yet, to receive tasks without merit and to gain honor without rank, if governance is like this, the state will surely be disordered and the lord in peril. Thus, incompatible matters cannot both stand.

Those who cut down enemies receive rewards, yet their lofty acts of mercy and kindness are praised. Those who capture cities receive titles and stipends, yet their teachings of honesty and frugality are extolled. Soldiers clad in heavy armor and sharpen their weapons to defend against calamity, yet their adornment of fine robes is glorified.

The wealth of a state depends on agriculture, and resistance to enemies relies on soldiers, yet those versed in literary arts are held in higher esteem. The reverent and law-abiding are neglected, while those who wield private swords and roam as chivalrous knights are sustained. If governance proceeds thus, strength and order cannot be attained.

In times of peace, scholars and chivalrous knights are nurtured; when calamity arrives, they rely on the *introduced* (or *boundary*) men. What is beneficial is not employed, and what is employed is not beneficial. Hence, the people exhaust themselves in service to their lord. Though tilling the fields is laborious, the people do it, saying: *"We may thereby grow rich."* Though battle is perilous, the people engage in it, saying: *"We may thereby gain noble rank."*

Now, if one cultivates literary arts and practices eloquence, there is no labor of farming yet the reality of wealth, no danger of war yet the honor of nobility—who would not pursue this? Thus, a hundred men serve wisdom while one man labors with strength. When those who serve wisdom are many, the law decays; when those who labor with strength are few, the state grows poor. This is why the world is disordered.

Therefore, the enlightened lord’s realm has no written documents or literature, for the law itself is the teaching. There are no words of the ancient kings, for the officials themselves are the teachers. There are no private swords for defense, for beheading the enemy is true courage.

---

The people within the realm must regulate their speech to conform to the law, direct their actions toward merit, and exhaust their courage in military service. Thus, in times of peace the state grows rich, and in times of war its armies grow strong—this is called the king’s foundation.

Once the king’s foundation is established and the neighboring states take turns challenging him, surpassing the Five Emperors and equaling the Three Kings—this must be the result of such laws.

But now it is not so. The commoners and scholars indulge in reckless speech within, while those who speak of external affairs wield influence abroad. Both within and without, they speak ill to await a strong enemy—is this not perilous?

Thus, when ministers speak of external affairs, unless they have ties to the *cónghéng* factions, they bear grudges against rivals and borrow strength from the state. *Cóng* means uniting many strong states to attack one weak one, while *héng* means serving one strong state to attack many weak ones. Neither is a means to uphold the state.

Now, those who advocate *héng* say: *"If we do not serve the great, we will face disaster when encountering the enemy."* Serving the great does not guarantee real benefit, yet they present maps and offer seals to request troops. Surrendering maps means losing territory, offering seals means losing prestige, losing territory means the state is diminished, and losing prestige means governance falters. Serving the great as *héng* brings no advantage, yet it leads to lost lands and chaotic rule.

Those who advocate *cóng* say: *"If we do not aid the small to attack the great, we will lose the world. Losing the world means the state is in danger, and danger brings humiliation."* Aiding the small does not guarantee success, yet they raise armies to confront the great. Aiding the small may fail to save them, and allying with the great may not prevent estrangement. Estrangement means being controlled by a stronger power. Deploying troops leads to military defeat, and retreating to defend leads to the fall of cities. Aiding the small as *cóng* brings no advantage, yet it results in lost lands and defeated armies.

Thus, when a state grows strong, it grants external officials high rank and authority within, and when aiding the small, it seeks heavy rewards abroad. The state’s interests are not yet secured, yet titles and rich rewards are already bestowed. Though the sovereign may be humiliated, his ministers are honored. Though the state’s lands are diminished, private fortunes grow rich. If the venture succeeds, they wield power and influence; if it fails, they retreat with their wealth intact. The sovereign, in listening to their counsel, finds that by the time their schemes are tested, their ranks and rewards are already elevated. If failure is not punished, what advisor would not employ deceptive rhetoric and presume on future favor?

Thus, states are ruined and rulers overthrown by heeding the empty words of those who speak of external affairs. Why is this so? It is because the sovereign does not distinguish between public and private gain, does not discern truth from falsehood in counsel, and does not punish accordingly.

All say: *"Serving the great can make us king, aiding the small can bring security."* But a king is one who can attack others, while security means being unassailable. Strength means the ability to attack, while good governance means being unassailable. True strength and governance cannot be borrowed from without—they arise from internal administration.

Now, if we do not implement laws and methods within while pursuing cleverness abroad, we will not attain strength and order. The vulgar proverb says: *"Long sleeves make for graceful dancing, and wealth makes for shrewd trade."* This speaks of how abundant resources make skill easy.

Thus, strength and order are easy to strategize for, while weakness and chaos are difficult to calculate against. Hence, when used in Qin, ten shifts in strategy rarely failed, but when used in Yan, even one shift rarely succeeded—not because those used in Qin were necessarily wiser, nor those in Yan stupider, but because the resources of order and chaos differ.

Thus, Zhou aligned with Qin as *cóng* and was destroyed within a year, while Wei abandoned Wei as *héng* and perished in half a season. Zhou was undone by *cóng*, and Wei by *héng*.

Had Zhou and Wei delayed their *cónghéng* schemes and instead strengthened their internal governance, clarified their laws and punishments, fully exploited their land’s potential, and compelled their people to death in defense of their walls, then even if the world took their lands, the loss would be slight, and attacking their state would be costly. No great power would dare to besiege their fortified cities, nor would strong enemies exploit their weaknesses—this would have been a sure path to survival.

Yet they abandoned the path that would not lead to ruin and pursued the path that would. This was the error of those who governed.

When internal wisdom is exhausted and external affairs are chaotic, ruin cannot be averted. The people’s calculations all lean toward security and profit, avoiding danger and hardship. Now, if we compel them to war, advancing means dying by the enemy’s hand, retreating means dying by execution—this is danger. Abandoning private pursuits to endure the toil of war, while their families suffer and the sovereign offers no redress—this is hardship. Where danger and hardship lie, how can the people avoid fleeing?

Thus, they focus on private affairs, repairing their homes and armories. When their homes and armories are secure, they avoid distant wars, and distant wars bring safety. When they bribe officials and curry favor with those in power, they secure their gains, and gains bring private security. Where profit lies, how can they resist pursuing it? Hence, public citizens dwindle while private interests flourish.

The enlightened king’s governance ensures that merchants, artisans, and idle folk are few and held in low regard, so that the masses focus on essential labor rather than frivolous pursuits. But now, those close to power can buy office and rank, so merchants and artisans are not held in contempt. Illicit wealth and trade thrive in the markets, so merchants are not few. Hoarding wealth far exceeds agricultural and military contributions, so upright and resolute men grow scarce while those who value high prices multiply.

Thus, in chaotic states, those who study claim to uphold the ancient kings’ *Dao*, invoking *rén* and *yì* to undermine current laws and sow doubt in the sovereign’s heart. Those who speak of antiquity fabricate deceit, borrow external strength for private gain, and neglect the state’s welfare. Those who wear swords gather followers, flaunt moral principles to enhance their reputation, and defy the five ministries’ prohibitions. Those who manage carriages and horses gather at private gates, exhausting bribes to employ influential intermediaries, and avoid the labor of military service.

The merchants and artisans, refining delicate and intricate implements, amassing wealth without measure, and hoarding resources while awaiting opportunity to match the profits of farmers—these five groups are the blights of the state.

If the ruler does not eliminate these five blighted classes nor nurture the upright and resolute scholars, then even if the world contains fallen kingdoms and extinguished dynasties, it should not be deemed strange.

**Chapter Fifty of the Displayed Schools:**

The prominent schools of the age are *Rú* and *Mò*. The *Rú* school reaches its pinnacle in **Kǒng Qiū** (Confucius). The *Mò* school reaches its pinnacle in **Mò Zǐ** (Mozi).

After Confucius’ death, there emerged:
- the *Rú* of **Zǐ Zhāng**,
- the *Rú* of **Zǐ Sī**,
- the *Rú* of **Yán Shì** (the Yangists),
- the *Rú* of **Mèng Shì** (the Mengists),
- the *Rú* of **Qī Diāo Shì** (the Lacquer-Carverists),
- the *Rú* of **Zhòng Liáng Shì** (the Comprangists),
- the *Rú* of **Sūn Shì** (the Sunists),
- the *Rú* of **Yuè Zhèng Shì** (the Yue Zhengists).

After Mozi’s death, there arose:
- the *Mò* of **Xiàng Lǐ Shì** (the Xiang Liists),
- the *Mò* of **Xiàng Fú Shì** (the Xiang Fists),
- the *Mò* of **Dèng Líng Shì** (the Deng Lingists).

Thus, following Confucius and Mozi, the *Rú* split into eight factions, while the *Mò* diverged into three, their doctrines of acceptance and rejection clashing yet all claiming to be the true Confucius or Mozi. Since neither Confucius nor Mozi can return to life, who shall we entrust to establish the teachings of the age? Both Confucius and Mozi extolled the *Dao* of **Yáo** and **Shùn**, yet differed in their doctrines of acceptance and rejection, all claiming to be the true Yáo and Shùn. But Yáo and Shùn no longer live—who shall we entrust to judge the sincerity of the *Rú* and *Mò*?

The **Xīn** (Shang) and **Zhōu** (Zhou) dynasties endured over seven hundred years, while the **Yú** (Yu) and **Xià** (Xia) lasted over two thousand, yet they could not establish the true *Rú* and *Mò*—now we wish to discern the *Dao* of Yáo and Shùn from three thousand years ago. Perhaps this is impossible to guarantee?

To insist upon something without verification is foolishness; to claim certainty without evidence is deceit. Thus, those who profess to know the *Dao* of the ancient kings and to establish the truth of Yáo and Shùn are either foolish or deceitful.

The doctrines of the foolish and deceitful, along with their conflicting practices, are not embraced by enlightened rulers.

When Mozi was buried, he wore mourning garments appropriate to the season—winter for winter, summer for summer—with a coffin of three-inch-thick *tóng* (mulberry) wood, and mourned for three months. The world honored this as frugality and paid tribute to it.

When the *Rú* (Confucians) buried their dead, they destroyed their homes in mourning, wore mourning for three years, and used staffs to support their aged and broken bodies. The rulers of the age honored this as filial piety and paid tribute to it.

Now, is Mozi’s frugality not the opposite of Confucius’ extravagance? Is Confucius’ filial piety not the opposite of Mozi’s harshness? Yet today, both extravagance and frugality, both filial piety and harshness, coexist within the *Rú* and *Mò*, and the ruler honors them both.

The debates of **Qī Diāo Shì** (the Lacquer-Carverists) were unyielding—he did not falter in argument nor avoid the eyes of his opponents. If he took a crooked path, he was condemned by **Zāng Huò** (the upright scholars); if he took a straight path, he was scorned by the nobles. The rulers of the age honored this as integrity and paid tribute to it.

The debates of **Sòng Róng Zǐ** (the Honorable Son of Song) were such that he would not engage in combat, nor follow personal grudges. He did not fear imprisonment, nor did he take offense when insulted. The rulers of the age honored this as magnanimity and paid tribute to it.

Now, is Qī Diāo’s integrity not the opposite of Sòng Róng’s tolerance? Is Sòng Róng’s magnanimity not the opposite of Qī Diāo’s brutality? Yet today, both tolerance and integrity, both magnanimity and brutality, coexist in these two schools, and the ruler honors them both.

From the doctrines of the foolish and deceitful, and the conflicting debates of these schools, the ruler listens to them all, so the scholars of the world have no fixed principles in speech nor consistent practices in conduct.

Ice and fire cannot coexist in the same vessel for long; cold and heat do not come at the same time. Conflicting doctrines cannot be upheld simultaneously in governance. Yet today, the ruler listens to all conflicting schools, accepting contradictory and opposing arguments—how can chaos not arise?

If the ruler’s methods of listening are thus, then his approach to governing the people will inevitably be disordered.

Now, those who speak of governance in the world often say:
*"Grant wealth to the poor and impoverished lands, for they lack resources!"*

Yet, if one befriends another without the benefits of a bountiful harvest or external gains, but still remains fully provided, it is not due to their wealth but to their frugality. If one befriends another without suffering from famine, illness, disaster, or crime, yet remains poor and impoverished, it is not due to their frugality but to their dissipation.

The dissipated and extravagant grow poor, while the industrious and frugal grow rich.

Now, the ruler taxes the wealthy to distribute aid to the poor—this is to take from the industrious and frugal and give to the dissipated and extravagant. Yet he expects the people to hasten their labor while moderating their expenditures—this is impossible to achieve.

Now, suppose there is a man who refuses to enter a dangerous city, avoids military service, and would not exchange the profit of a single hair on his shin for the benefit of the whole world. The ruler would certainly honor him, esteem his wisdom, and exalt his conduct, calling him a man who values life over material things.

Yet the ruler uses fertile fields and grand residences to offer titles and wealth, to exchange for the lives of the people. Now he honors the man who values life over material things, yet expects the people to risk their lives and prioritize serving him—this is impossible to achieve.

He who stores writings and engages in discussions, gathers followers and flaunts his literary prowess, is honored by the ruler, who says: *"We respect the virtuous scholars; this is the way of the ancient kings."* Yet the taxes the officials levy fall upon the farmers, while the ruler nourishes the scholars.

The farmers are burdened with heavy taxes, while the scholars receive many rewards, yet the ruler expects the people to hasten their labor while avoiding discussions—this is impossible to achieve.

Those who uphold moral principles and act with unwavering resolve, who brandish their swords at the slightest criticism, are honored by the ruler as men of self-discipline.

Yet the ruler does not reward those who labor in battle, but instead esteems the courage of those who fight in private disputes. He expects the people to hasten their efforts in war and avoid private conflicts—this is impossible to achieve.

In times of peace, the ruler nourishes the *Rú* and the *jiā* (chivalrous knights), while in times of crisis, he relies upon the *jiè* (upright warriors). Those who are nourished are not those who are used, and those who are used are not those who are nourished—this is the path to chaos.

Moreover, when the ruler listens to the schools, if their teachings are correct, he should implement them through the government and employ their adherents. If their teachings are incorrect, he should banish their followers and suppress their doctrines.

Yet today, he honors teachings he deems correct without implementing them through the government, and tolerates doctrines he deems false without suppressing them. He accepts what is correct yet does not employ it, and rejects what is false yet does not suppress it—this is the path to chaos and ruin.

The **Dàn Tái Zǐ Yǔ** possessed the bearing of a gentleman, yet when **Zhòngní** (Confucius) first observed him, he nearly chose him—yet after long association, his conduct did not match his appearance.

The **Zǎi Yǔ**’s eloquence was refined and polished, yet when **Zhòngní** first observed him, he nearly chose him—yet after association, his wisdom did not fulfill his eloquence.

Thus, **Confucius** said: *"If one selects a person based on their appearance, one errs with Dàn Tái Zǐ Yǔ. If one selects a person based on their words, one errs with Zǎi Yǔ."*

**#16** Therefore, even with **Zhòngní**’s wisdom, there were still voices of falsehood.

**#17** Today’s new orators exceed **Zǎi Yǔ**’s eloquence, yet the rulers of the world, dazzled by **Zhòngní**’s words, favor them and entrust them with office—how could they avoid error?

Hence, when **Wèi** (Wei) entrusted **Mèng Kuài** (Meng Mao) with his eloquence, it led to the calamity of **Huá Xià** (Hua Xia). When **Zhào** (Zhao) entrusted **Mǎ Fú** (Ma Fu) with his eloquence, it brought the disaster of **Cháng Píng** (Long Ping). These two cases are examples of the folly of favoring eloquence.

To examine molten bronze and observe its hues, even skilled metalworkers cannot definitively judge whether it will make a fine sword. To strike waterfowl with arrows or cut foals and horses on land, even the common hunter cannot doubt whether his weapons are sharp or dull.

To inspect a horse’s teeth and lips and its outward form, even **Bó Liè** (Bo Le, the legendary horse expert) cannot definitively judge whether it is a fine steed. To test a horse by mounting it and observing its performance, even the common hunter cannot doubt whether it is swift or strong.

To observe one’s bearing and attire, or listen to their words, even **Zhòngní** cannot definitively judge whether they are a true gentleman.

To test them in office and assess their achievements, even the common folk cannot doubt their competence or folly.

Thus, the wise ruler’s officials—his ministers and generals—must arise from the ranks of the **zhōubù** (state officials) and **sùwǔ** (common soldiers).

If those with merit are rewarded, then titles and emoluments become abundant, and the people are all the more encouraged. If promotions and succession in rank are given, then offices grow grander, and governance becomes all the more refined.

If titles and emoluments are abundant and offices are well-governed, this is the **Way** of the king.

A thousand-li stretch of flat stone cannot be called wealth, nor a million **xiàngrén** (army of a hundred thousand) be called strength. The stone is not small, nor the numbers few—but they cannot be called wealth or strength, for the stone does not produce grain, and the **xiàngrén** cannot be made to confront the enemy.

Now, the **shāngguān** (mercenary officials) and **technical/artistic** (技艺) men also do not till the land to eat, so the land that is not tilled is akin to the flat stone. The **rú** (gentlemen) and **xiá** (chivalrous knights) do not bear military burdens, yet if they are honored and elevated, the people are not made to serve—this is like the **xiàngrén** being used for purposes other than war.

Those who know the calamities of flat stone and the **xiàngrén**, yet do not recognize that the calamities lie in the **shāngguān** and **rú-xiá** as the land that is not tilled and the people not made to serve, are ignorant of their kindred failings.

Hence, even if the kings of rival states expound my principles, I will not give tribute and submit to them. Even if the feudal lords within the **Guān Zhōu** (Pass Region) do not follow my conduct, I will still compel them to present their captives and attend my court.

Thus, when strength is abundant, others come to pay homage; when strength is meager, one must pay homage to others. Therefore, enlightened rulers strive to cultivate strength.

A stern household has no fierce bandits, yet a benevolent mother may have a wayward son. From this, I know that stern authority can curb violence, but abundant virtue is insufficient to stop disorder.

When the Sage governs a state, he does not rely on people being inherently good for me, but rather on their inability to act wickedly. Relying on people being inherently good, there would be no more than one in ten within the borders; relying on their inability to act wickedly, an entire state can be made orderly.

Those who govern must employ the many and discard the few. Thus, they do not seek virtue but rather laws.

If one must depend on arrows that straighten themselves, there would be no arrows for a hundred generations. If one must depend on wheels that form themselves, there would be no carts for a thousand years. Yet if arrows that straighten themselves and wheels that form themselves truly existed, why does the world still use carts and shoot game? It is because of the **Dao** of *yǐnbā* (hidden brackets/artificial means).

Even if someone has arrows that straighten themselves and wheels that form themselves without relying on *yǐnbā*, skilled artisans would not value them. Why? Because the rider is not a single person, nor is the shooter a single arrow.

If a wise ruler does not rely on rewards and punishments while expecting citizens to be inherently good, he will not be esteemed. Why? Because state laws cannot be abandoned, and the subjects he governs are not just one person.

Therefore, rulers of discernment do not follow the occasional goodness of others, nor do they pursue the path of what is merely expedient. Occasional, I mean, as in chance occurrences.

Now, if someone tells a ruler: "If you command your son to be both wise and long-lived, the world will surely call you mad." Wisdom is innate; longevity is fate. Innate qualities and fate are not things one learns from others, yet to persuade people with what they cannot achieve is why the world calls this madness.

If called impossible, then it is a misguided instruction. Instruction, too, is innate. To teach with benevolence is to instruct with wisdom and longevity—such words are rejected by rulers who understand governance.

Thus, even if one praises the beauty of **Máo Shì** (Mao Qiqiao) and **Xī Shī** (Xi Shi), it is of no benefit to my face. Using cosmetics and rouge only enhances their original allure. Speaking of the benevolence and righteousness of former kings is of no use in governing.

Enlightening my laws and punishments, ensuring they are strictly followed, is like applying cosmetics and rouge to the state. Therefore, wise rulers hasten to aid their people and delay their praise, never speaking of benevolence and righteousness. Today, soothsayers and shamans bless people, saying: "May you live a thousand autumns and ten thousand years."

The sound of "a thousand autumns and ten thousand years" is deafening to the ears, yet a single day’s life cannot be guaranteed by others—this is why people disregard soothsayers and shamans.

Now, the scholars of the world instruct rulers, not speaking of what can be done to govern today, but rather of the achievements of past governance. They do not examine the workings of official laws or investigate the motives of deceitful and wicked men, yet they all speak of ancient traditions and praise the accomplishments of former kings.

The **Rú** (Confucians) embellish their words, saying: "If you heed my teachings, you can become a conqueror and king." This is the soothsaying of the scholars—rulers who understand governance do not accept it.

Thus, wise rulers implement practical measures, discard what is useless, and do not speak of benevolence and righteousness. They do not listen to the words of those who study.

Now, those who do not know governance will always say: "Gain the hearts of the people." If gaining the hearts of the people were sufficient for governance, then **Yī Yǔ** (伊尹) and **Guān Zhòng** (管仲) would have no purpose. They would simply listen to the people and that would be all.

The people’s wisdom is as useless as the mind of an infant. If an infant’s head is not shaved, its belly aches; if the head wound is not treated, the pain worsens. If not treated with *yōngyí* (boils and sores), the condition worsens further. If one *yǐnbā* (presses and forces) the boil to burst and exposes it, another person must hold the infant, and even a tender mother would treat it, yet the infant still cries without ceasing. The infant does not know that its minor suffering leads to greater benefit.

Now, if a ruler presses the people to till the fields and cultivate crops to enrich their livelihoods, yet calls this cruelty. If he enforces strict punishments to curb evil, yet calls this harshness. If he levies taxes and collects grain to fill the granaries and prepare for famine and military campaigns, yet calls this greed. If he ensures that every subject within the borders knows martial discipline and does not privately dissolve into weakness, if he unites their strength to engage in fierce combat to capture enemies, yet calls this brutality—these four measures are what bring stability to the state, yet the people do not know to rejoice in them.

Seeking **Saints** and **Tōng** (universally enlightened) scholars is because the people’s wisdom is insufficient to be relied upon for governance. In ancient times, **Yǔ** (Great Yu) drained the rivers and dredged the floods, yet the people gathered bricks and stones to strike him. They sought to harm him. **Zǐ Chǎn** (子产) opened uncultivated land and planted mulberry trees, yet the people of **Zhèng** (郑) slandered and criticized him. Yu benefited the whole world, and Zǐ Chǎn preserved Zhèng, yet both were condemned by the people. The people’s wisdom is indeed insufficient for governance.

Therefore, to promote scholars while seeking wise and virtuous ones, or to govern with the aim of pleasing the people, are both sources of chaos—these are not paths to true governance.

Thus, the **Han Feizi** concludes.

**Han Feizi, Chapter Twenty: On Loyalty and Filial Piety (Fifth-One)**

The world universally regards the Dao of filial piety, fraternal respect, loyalty, and obedience as correct, yet none truly discern and practice it properly—thus the world is in chaos.

All follow the Dao of Yao and Shun as correct and model themselves after it, yet this leads to regicide and filial disobedience.

Yao, as a ruler, failed to rule his ministers; Shun, as a minister, failed to serve his ruler; Tang and Wu, as ministers, killed their sovereigns, executed their corpses, and yet the world praises them—this is why the world remains disordered to this day.

What is called a "wise ruler" is one who can cultivate his ministers. What is called an "accomplished minister" is one who clarifies laws and regulations, manages offices, and upholds his ruler. Yet Yao, though he believed himself wise, could not cultivate Shun; Shun, though he believed himself accomplished, could not uphold Yao; Tang and Wu, though they believed themselves righteous, killed their sovereigns—these are the ways of both wise rulers and accomplished ministers.

Thus, to this day, sons seize their fathers’ households, and ministers seize their rulers’ states. When a father yields to his son, or a ruler yields to his minister, this is not the way to establish proper hierarchy and order.

What I have heard is this: when a minister serves his ruler, a son serves his father, and a wife serves her husband, if these three relationships are in harmony, the world is governed; if they are reversed, the world is in chaos. This is the constant Dao of the world, and even kings and accomplished ministers cannot alter it.

If a ruler, though unworthy, does not dare to encroach, then order is maintained. Yet today, rulers promote the wise without fixed principles, which is contrary to the Dao. Yet the world still regards this as governance. Thus, the Tian Clan overthrew the Lü Clan in Qi, and the Dai Clan overthrew the Zi Clan in Song—these were all accomplished and wise men, not foolish or unworthy ones.

Thus, abandoning fixed principles to promote the wise leads to chaos, and disregarding laws to rely on cleverness leads to peril. Therefore, it is said: *"Follow the law, do not exalt the wise."*

The Records say: *"Shun saw his blind father, and his expression was distressed."* Distressed means sorrowful. Confucius said: *"At that time, how perilous it was! The world trembled, and those who followed the Dao could neither be sons to their fathers nor ministers to their rulers."*

I say: Confucius did not truly understand the Dao of filial piety, loyalty, and obedience.

Then, does the Dao require that one, when advancing, cannot be a minister to his ruler, and when retreating, cannot be a son to his father?

A father desires an accomplished son to enrich his impoverished household and alleviate his suffering. A ruler desires an accomplished minister to govern his chaotic state and elevate his diminished status. Yet if an accomplished son does not serve his father, the father’s household remains in hardship. If an accomplished minister does not serve his ruler, the ruler’s position remains in peril.

Thus, even if a father has an accomplished son and a ruler has an accomplished minister, it is only to bring harm—how can it bring benefit?

What is called a "loyal minister" does not endanger his ruler, and a "filial son" does not defy his parents. Yet Shun, through his virtue, seized his ruler’s state, and Tang and Wu, through their righteousness, expelled and killed their sovereigns—these are all cases where virtue endangered the ruler, yet the world praises them.

The ancient "heroic worthies" did not serve their rulers when advancing nor maintain their families when retreating—they were those who, when advancing, defied their rulers, and when retreating, defied their parents.

Moreover, to not serve one’s ruler when advancing and to not maintain one’s family when retreating is the path of chaos in turbulent times, leading to the extinction of lineages.

Thus, to praise the virtuous Yao, Shun, Tang, and Wu as heroic worthies is the art of bringing chaos to the world.

Gǔsǒu exiled Shun, and Xiàng, as Shun’s brother, killed him—exiling one’s father and killing one’s brother cannot be called *ren* (benevolence). To marry the two daughters of the Emperor of Heaven and seize the world cannot be called *yì* (righteousness). Without *ren* or *yì*, it cannot be called *míng* (enlightened).

The *Odes* say:
*"Under the vast heavens, there is none not the sovereign’s land;
Along the shores of the earth, there is none not the sovereign’s subject."*

If we take the *Odes* literally, then Shun, when outside, was a minister to his ruler, and when inside, was a minister to his father, a servant to his mother, and a husband to his ruler’s daughters.

Thus, heroic worthies do not maintain their families in times of chaos, leading to the extinction of lineages, and externally they rebel against their rulers, leaving their rotting bones and decaying flesh scattered across the land and rivers, not shunning stepping in water or fire, compelling the world to emulate them—this is why the world universally desires early death rather than a long life of suffering, all because they abandon the world’s order and fail to govern it properly.

What the world calls "heroic worthies" are those who separate themselves from the masses, act differently from others, and practice the doctrine of *tiándàn* (serene indifference) while speaking in *huǎnghū* (vague and confused) terms.

I say: *Tiándàn* is a doctrine of uselessness, and *huǎnghū* is speech without law. When words arise from lawlessness and teachings arise from uselessness, the world follows them in confusion.

I say that human life must serve one’s ruler and nourish one’s parents—one cannot practice *tiándàn*. Those who speak must use loyal, sincere, and lawful discourse—one cannot speak in *huǎnghū* terms.

The speech of *huǎnghū* and the doctrine of *tiándàn* are the arts of confusing the world.

A filial son serves his father not to compete for his father’s household.

A loyal minister serves his ruler not to compete for his ruler’s realm.

A son who constantly praises another’s parents by saying, *“So-and-so’s parents rise before dawn and retire late, toiling tirelessly to enrich their descendants and servants,”* is slandering his own parents. A minister who constantly extols the virtues of former kings and wishes for their return is defaming his current ruler.

If one does not praise another’s parents, he is called unfilial, yet if one does not praise another’s ruler, the world calls him virtuous—this is how disorder arises.

Thus, a minister should not speak of the excellence of Yao and Shun, should not praise the conquests of Tang and Wu, nor should he speak of the lofty deeds of heroic worthies. Instead, he should exhaust his efforts in upholding the law and focus his heart on serving his lord—that is the way of a loyal minister.

In ancient times, the common people were forgetful, dull, and ignorant—*dài* (forgetful) being a description of those who lose their sense of propriety. Thus, they could be swayed by empty titles.

Now, the people are cunning and clever, desiring self-interest and refusing to obey their superiors. The ruler must first encourage them with rewards before they will advance, and then threaten them with punishment before they will refrain from retreat.

Yet the world says: *“Yu You declined the throne, so rewards are insufficient to encourage virtue. Zhi the robber courted death for profit, so punishments are insufficient to deter crime.”*

I say: There are those who, before obtaining the throne, do not seek to rule—Yu You is one such. There are those who, after obtaining the throne, do not seek to rule—Yao and Shun are such. There are those who destroy their integrity for wealth, court punishment for gain, and forget their own death—Zhi the robber is such.

These two extremes are aberrations of nature, and they should not be the measure by which to govern the state or use the people.

Governance is the governance of the constant. The Way is the Way of the constant. Clever words about aberrations are the ruin of governance.

The highest caliber of men in the world cannot be encouraged by rewards, and the lowest cannot be restrained by punishments. Yet if one does not set rewards for the highest and punishments for the lowest, the Way of governing the state and using the people is lost.

Thus, ministers of the age often speak not of the laws of the state but of *cónghéng* (alliances and strategies). Those who speak of alliances say: *“If alliances succeed, one will become a hegemon.”* Those who speak of strategies say: *“If strategies succeed, one will become a king.”* Though the talk of alliances and strategies in the Eastern lands never ceases, yet no hegemon or king arises from it—empty words cannot bring about true governance.

A true king acts alone and is called a king. Thus, the Three Sovereigns did not rely on alliances to establish order, nor did the Five Hegemons await strategies to discern the Way—they simply governed internally to regulate externally.

**On the Peril of the Ruler**
The reason a ruler’s person is in danger and his state falls is that his great ministers are too powerful, and his attendants too formidable.

By “powerful,” I mean those who act without law, wield state authority for private gain. By “formidable,” I mean those who monopolize influence and weigh decisions lightly.

These two must be carefully observed.

A horse’s ability to bear heavy loads and pull carriages over long distances comes from its sinews and strength. A ruler of ten thousand chariots or a lord of a thousand chariots subdues the world and conquers the feudal lords through his might and authority—might and authority are the sinews and strength of the ruler.

If great ministers seize authority and attendants monopolize power, then the ruler loses his strength. A ruler who loses his strength cannot retain his state—there is not one in a thousand who can.

A tiger or leopard’s ability to overpower men and dominate beasts comes from its claws and teeth. If one were to deprive a tiger or leopard of its claws and teeth, men would surely dominate it.

Now, those who wield great power are the ruler’s claws and teeth. If a ruler loses them, he is like a tiger or leopard stripped of its fangs.

The ruler of Song lost his claws and teeth to Zi Han, and Duke Jing of Qi lost his to Tian Chang. Yet they did not act swiftly to remove them, so they met with death and their states fell.

Now, rulers without skill all claim to know the mistakes of Song and Duke Jing, yet they fail to recognize their own errors or examine the patterns of these events.

Moreover, those who practice the Way of law and technique cannot coexist with ministers in power. How do I know this?

When a ruler has skilled advisors, great ministers cannot dominate decisions, and attendants dare not sell their influence. When the power of ministers and attendants wanes, the ruler’s Way becomes clear.

But it is not so now. Those in power monopolize influence, act arbitrarily for private gain, and their attendants form factions to suppress outsiders. Thus, skilled advisors have no opportunity to advance, and the ruler has no chance to deliberate.

Therefore, even if one has skill, it is not necessarily used, and power cannot be shared—how can skilled advisors avoid peril?

Thus, a ruler cannot merely reject the schemes of great ministers or abandon the petitions of his attendants; he must align himself with the Way and speak truly.

Then how can skilled advisors risk death to offer counsel?

This is why the world remains disordered.

An enlightened ruler bestows rank and reward according to merit, assigns offices according to ability, and ensures that those he promotes are truly virtuous and those he employs are truly capable. When virtuous and capable men advance, private entreaties cease.

Those who earn great rewards must have rendered great service, and those who hold high office must have great ability. Thus, mercenaries will not cling to private valor but strive to repel enemies, and those who seek office will not be hindered by private connections but focus on integrity.

This is how virtuous and capable men are gathered while private factions are dispersed.

Now, attendants are not necessarily wise, yet the ruler may know of someone’s wisdom and listen to him. When that person enters, he debates with the attendants, and the ruler, listening to the attendants rather than judging wisdom, is like one who consults fools on matters of intelligence.

Those in power are not necessarily virtuous, yet the ruler may know of someone’s virtue and honor him. When that person enters, he debates with those in power, and the ruler, listening to their words rather than using the virtuous, is like one who consults the unworthy on matters of virtue.

Thus, the wise are forced to strategize with fools, and the virtuous must compete with the unworthy. When will virtuous and wise men find opportunity to serve, and when will the ruler’s clarity be unobstructed?

Now, the wise are constrained to devise strategies for foolish men, and the virtuous must vie with the unworthy. When will the virtuous and wise find opportunity to serve, and when shall the ruler’s clarity be unobstructed?

In times past, Guan Longfeng remonstrated King Jie, and his four limbs were mutilated. Wangzi Bidān admonished King Zhou, and his heart was pierced. Zichu was loyal and upright to Lord Fu Chai, yet he was executed at Shǔ Luò. These three men, as subjects, were not without loyalty, nor were their remonstrances ill-timed. Yet they could not escape the calamity of death because their rulers failed to discern the words of the virtuous and wise, and instead were ensnared by the folly of the unworthy.

Now, if the ruler does not employ those skilled in law and strategy, but instead listens to the unworthy and foolish ministers, who among the virtuous and wise would dare to risk the peril these three men faced and offer their counsel? This is the cause of the world’s chaos.

**Precept 53:** If laws are enforced without deviation, then officials will have no opportunity for corruption. When laws are balanced, officials will be without deceit. Laws having been established, do not sell them with flattering words.

To reward merit, the people will speak little. To reward virtue, the people will speak much.

To administer justice with flexibility and partiality, ruling by the five *li* is the way of kings. The king must verify the five *li* before deciding guilt and punishment—this is the true way of kingship. To rule by the nine *li* is the way of the strong, for they are both kings and powerful. Those who rely on past precedents will be diminished.

*Host* means to entrust. If laws are entrusted without proper oversight, they will surely be eroded.

To govern with punishments, to wage war with rewards, and to bestow generous stipends upon those skilled in strategy. If those who oversee the markets are held accountable for their transgressions, then the markets will be free of deceit.

When goods are abundant and the populace numerous, but agriculture slackens while corruption flourishes, the state will surely be diminished.

If the people have surplus food, yet it is taken from them in the form of *sù* (grain tax), and if titles are granted without regard to merit, then the populace will grow weary and negligent. Though the rewards may be small, if they are not justly earned, they cannot be sustained.

To grant titles and stipends without consideration of merit is to render them unsustainable. A state that awards titles and stipends based on merit—this is called fostering wise counsel and valor in battle—will have no rival.

A state that awards titles and stipends based on merit will see those who excel in governance grow few in number, and words of obstruction will be silenced. This is called removing governance with governance, silencing speech with speech, and awarding titles and stipends based on merit. Thus, such a state will be mighty, and none under heaven will dare to invade it.

When armies are deployed, they must take victory. When victory is achieved, it must be held. To muster troops without attacking is to invite disaster.

In matters of the court, minor offenses should not be magnified, and titles should be granted based on merit. Though there may be objections in the court, none should be allowed to interfere. This is called governance by fixed rules.

To attack with force, one must expend one to gain ten. To attack with words, one must expend ten to lose a hundred. A state that favors force is difficult to assault. A state that favors words is easy to attack.

To excel in ability is to overcome one’s harm. To lighten the burdens of the people while cultivating the Dao and directing their surplus strength toward the heart, none will bear the responsibility of holding office for the ruler, nor will there be hidden resentments. Thus, those who excel will not interfere with one another, and there will be no litigation.

To prevent officers from holding multiple positions, their skills will flourish. To ensure that people are not rewarded for the same merit, there will be no contention. This is called making the state easy to attack.

Heavy punishments and sparse rewards, with the ruler cherishing the people, yet the people dying for rewards. Many rewards and light punishments, with the ruler not cherishing the people, yet the people not valuing them.

When profits are taken from one source alone, the state will have no rival. When profits are taken from two sources, half the troops will be ineffective. When profits are taken from ten sources, the people will not defend it.

To impose severe punishments on minor offenses, minor offenses will not arise; to impose light punishments on severe offenses, severe offenders will not come. This is called removing punishments with punishments.

If crimes are severe yet punishments are light, light punishments will breed more offenses—this is called inviting punishments with punishments. Such a state will surely be diminished.

**Precept 54:** The governance of the people by the sage is measured by their nature, not indulging their desires, but aiming solely at their benefit. Thus, when he imposes punishments upon them, it is not to harm the people, but to cherish their foundation.

When punishments prevail, the people are tranquil. When rewards are excessive, corruption arises. Therefore, in governing the people, punishments must prevail—this is the priority of governance. Excessive rewards are the root of disorder.

The nature of the people is to delight in chaos and not to embrace the law. Thus, the wise ruler in governing his state must make rewards clear so that the people are encouraged to perform meritorious deeds, and punishments severe so that the people adhere to the law. Encouraging meritorious deeds means public affairs will not be transgressed; adhering to the law means corruption will not take root.

Therefore, in governing the people, one must suppress corruption before it arises. In waging war, one must win the hearts of the people. Suppressing corruption at its source is governance; winning the hearts of the people in battle is victory. The sage governs the people by first addressing governance, and first waging war by winning the hearts of the people.

If a ruler governs by first attending to governance, his state will be strong. If he wages war by first winning the hearts of the people, he will be victorious. A state that first attends to governance and unifies the people’s hearts, that raises the public while ignoring private matters, that grants rewards for meritorious deeds while corruption does not arise, that clarifies the law while governance is not burdensome—such a state will be strong. A state that cannot do these four things will be weak.

The reason a state becomes strong is governance. The reason a ruler is honored is authority. Thus, the wise ruler has authority and governance, while the disordered ruler also has authority and governance, but their accumulation differs, leading to divergent outcomes.

Therefore, the wise ruler wields authority while upholding severity, unifies governance while his state is orderly. Thus, the law is the foundation of kingship, and punishment is the means by which the ruler cherishes the people.

The nature of the people is to abhor labor and delight in ease. Ease leads to neglect; neglect leads to disorder; disorder leads to chaos. If rewards and punishments are not implemented across the land, they will surely be obstructed.

Thus, if one seeks to achieve great merit but finds it difficult to inspire effort, great merit cannot be attained. If one seeks to enforce governance but finds it difficult to change established habits, the people’s disorder cannot be rectified.

Therefore, governance of the people has no fixed rule—only governance itself is the rule. Laws that adapt with the times bring order; governance that suits the world brings merit.

If the people are simple and governed by naming conventions, order will prevail. In a knowing age, maintained by punishments, they will obey. If governance does not change with the times, disorder will arise. If governance cannot adapt to the people’s abilities, the state will be diminished.

Thus, the sage’s governance of the people must adjust laws with the times and adapt prohibitions to the people’s capacities.

To surpass others in land-based strength is to achieve wealth. To surpass others in raising strength against enemies is to achieve power. A state that does not obstruct its power will attain kingship.

Thus, the **Dao of Kingship** lies in what is heard and what is obstructed. He who blocks deceit will surely achieve kingship, for the **King’s strategy** does not rely on the world’s chaos not arising—it relies on its impossibility of arising. To rely on the world’s chaos not arising and yet establish governance is to court decline; to rely on its impossibility of arising and enforce law is to flourish. Therefore, the virtuous ruler governs his state by adhering to the **art of the unassailable**.

When titles are honored, the sovereign is held in esteem, so rewards and ranks are bestowed upon merit, and thus wickedness finds no foothold. Those who excel in strength hold noble ranks, and noble ranks elevate the sovereign—an exalted sovereign will surely achieve kingship. A state that does not value strength but relies on private scholars will see its ranks debased, its sovereign humbled, and thus decline is inevitable.

Therefore, the **principles of establishing a state and utilizing the people** are this: if a ruler can close off external threats, suppress private factions, and rely on his own virtue, then kingship may be attained.

**Chapter 55: On Establishing Distinctions**

A state where the ruler is vast and the sovereign is exalted has never failed to be one where laws are severe and commands reach throughout the realm. Thus, the ruler who establishes ranks and rewards must ensure that laws are strict and weighty.

When a state is well-governed, the people are at ease; when affairs are disordered, the nation is in peril. Heavy laws align with human nature, while light prohibitions lose their efficacy. Moreover, the people’s very lives are their own to expend—no one fails to give their utmost to attain their desires. Yet what they love or hate is determined by the sovereign: the people love wealth and rank but hate punishments. The sovereign wields their affections to guide the people’s strength, so the efficacy of governance must not be neglected.

Yet when prohibitions are light and governance falters, it is because punishments and rewards are misapplied. If governing the people does not adhere to law, then even well-doing becomes meaningless—this is to have no law at all. Thus, the principle of order and chaos demands that distinguishing punishments and rewards be urgent.

All who govern a state have laws, yet some perish while others endure. Those that perish fail to distinguish punishments and rewards. Governors who do distinguish them may still err by treating them as mere differences rather than true divisions. Only the enlightened ruler truly divides them, so his people revere the law and fear prohibitions, desiring only to avoid guilt and daring not to share in rewards. Thus it is said: *The people obey without need of punishments or rewards.*

Therefore, in a perfectly governed state, the primary concern is to stop deceit. How so? Its laws penetrate human nature and align with governance. Then how does one eliminate subtle deceit? By ensuring that people scrutinize each other’s intentions. How does one enforce mutual surveillance? Through **neighborhood mutual liability**—when one in a neighborhood commits a crime, guilt is imputed to all. If prohibitions still fail to deter because they do not implicate the self, then fear of punishment is lost, and the hunt for deceit becomes futile. Those with deceitful intentions cannot forget it when many watch. Thus, one must be cautious of oneself while observing others. To expose hidden deceit, those who report faults are rewarded, while those who conceal them face severe punishments and connected crimes. Thus, deceitful types are uncovered. Deceit cannot thrive when private accusations and mutual liability are enforced. *Li* (任) means *guarantee*—those who guarantee their neighbors are held accountable, hence *mutual liability*.

The most enlightened governance relies on **fixed measures, not personal favor**. Thus, in a well-ordered state, if one does not rely on reputation, governance will be flawless, and the realm will be harmonious—this is the power of fixed measures. A failing state allows soldiers to act arbitrarily within its borders without restraint, for it relies on personal favor without fixed rules. Self-attack is the work of men; attacking others is the work of measures. Therefore, an enlightened state abandons rhetoric and relies on law.

Extraordinary deeds that follow prescribed measures are difficult to discern, just as over-punishment for mere words is hard to justify. Thus, punishments and rewards become confused. What is meant by "following measures is hard to discern"? It refers to deceitful merit. What is meant by "over-punishment for words is hard to justify"? It refers to unjustified accusations. If one judges by appearances without seeing hollow merit, or measures emotions without uncovering deceitful roots, how can both not err? Thus, empty scholars establish reputations within while empty talkers scheme without, so the foolish, cowardly, brave, and wise become entangled in false **Dao**, deceiving the world. Hence, laws are ignored, and punishments are not applied to the guilty. If this is so, how can punishments and rewards avoid confusion?

Thus, when governance reaches its limits, its principles lose their measure. The loss of measure is not the law’s fault—it is because fixed laws are abandoned in favor of cleverness. If laws are discarded and cleverness relied upon, how can those entrusted with affairs fulfill their duties? If duties and affairs do not align, how can laws avoid failure, and punishments avoid excess? Thus, rewards and punishments bring chaos, the path of the state goes astray, and the failure to distinguish them becomes evident.

*(End of Chapter 20 of the *Han Feizi*, from the "Taixia" [太下] section. Textual emendations noted as per scholarly corrections.)*

「#14 The character 『北』 (*běi*) is evidently a mistake for 『此』 (*cǐ*), and should be corrected.

#15 The character 『不』 (*bù*) is missing; it is added according to the *Ling Yongchu* edition and the *Yu Ping* edition.

#16 The character 『至』 (*zhì*) is superfluous; it is deleted according to the *Ling Yongchu* edition and the *Yu Ping* edition.

#17 The character 『而』 (*ér*) is erroneously duplicated; it should be deleted.

#18 The character 『也』 (*yě*) is mistakenly written as 『治』 (*zhì*); it is corrected according to the *Ling Yongchu* edition.

#19 The character 『也』 (*yě*) is missing; it is added according to the *Ling Yongchu* edition and the *Yu Ping* edition.

#20 The character 『难』 (*nán*) is mistakenly written as 『虽』 (*suī*); it is corrected according to the *Ling Yongchu* edition and the *Yu Ping* edition.

#21 The phrase 『故实』 (*gùshí*) is reversed in error; it is corrected according to the *Ling Yongchu* edition and the *Yu Ping* edition.」
